<seg id="1"> Over the last decade or so, behavioral economics has fundamentally changed the way economists conceptualize the world.</seg>
<seg id="2"> Behavioral economics is an umbrella of approaches that seek to extend the standard economics framework to account for relevant features of human behavior that are absent in the standard economics framework.</seg>
<seg id="3"> Typically, this calls for borrowing from the neighboring social sciences, particularly from psychology and sociology.</seg>
<seg id="4"> The emphasis is on well-documented empirical findings:</seg>
<seg id="5"> at the core of behavioral economics is the conviction that making our model of an economic man more accurate will improve our understanding of economics, thereby making the discipline more useful.</seg>
<seg id="6"> It is natural for such an endeavor to begin as a subdisciplineˇŞone that catalogs anomalies and explores alternative ways to model choice, with applications illustrating the workings of such models.</seg>
<seg id="7"> A more ambitious role for behaviorally based insights is to effect how researchers in applied fields make both positive and normative analyses.</seg>
<seg id="8"> By and large, this is the arena in which the usefulness of new ideas is eventually evaluated.</seg>
<seg id="9"> In the long run, one expects the arguments, if useful, to be integrated into the mainstream literature.</seg>
<seg id="10"> An example of such development in the behavioral context is finance.</seg>
<seg id="11"> Success of behavioral finance, a thriving area which has produced enough material to warrant a handbook treatment , is partly explained by the fact that the conflict between the standard benchmark model and a rich supply of data is particularly clear.</seg>
<seg id="12"> Accounting for behavioral tendencies fills a disturbing gap in understanding financial markets, and institutions therein.</seg>
<seg id="13"> While other applied fields have not gone nearly so far, there is no reason why behavioral ideas could not, and should not, be applied to other domains too.</seg>
<seg id="14"> Indeed, behavioral tendencies concern human behavior in general and there is no reason to tie the arguments to a particular field.</seg>
<seg id="15"> It is hoped that this volume will contribute to the integration of behavioral insights into applied fields.</seg>
<seg id="16"> The contributors to this volume take for granted the fact that behavioral ideas have an important future in economics and hope, through this book, to promote developments that will make good use of them.</seg>
<seg id="17"> Our aim is not to engage in the debate between the standard modeling and behavioral approaches, but to move on to applications.</seg>
<seg id="18"> The chapters in this volume examine behavioral dimensions of six fields of economics (public economics, development, law and economics, health, wage determination, and organization economics) in which behavioral argumentation has proven to be useful but has not yet been integrated as a part of the established framework.</seg>
<seg id="19"> We have left out finance as being beyond the phase where contributions such as the ones in this volume can shift a field.</seg>
<seg id="20"> Interest in behavioral economics has been stimulated by accumulating evidence that the standard model of consumer decision-making provides an inadequate positive description of human behavior for some questions.</seg>
<seg id="21"> According to the evidence (and contrary to the standard economic model), individuals are bounded in many dimensions, in particular in their rationality, self-control and self-interest.Bounded rationality manifests itself in incomplete information processing ability.</seg>
<seg id="22"> Individuals appeal to heuristics and rules of thumb when making their decisions.</seg>
<seg id="23"> They make biased probability judgments and are often overconfident.</seg>
<seg id="24"> Moreover, individuals tend to anchor to seemingly irrelevant information or to the status quo, and they are loss aversive.</seg>
<seg id="25"> In general, they do not maximize expected utility.</seg>
<seg id="26"> Incomplete self-control refers to the tendency of economic agents to make decisions that are in conflict with their long-term interest.</seg>
<seg id="27"> Self-control problems may lead to addictive behavior, undersaving, or procrastination.</seg>
<seg id="28"> As opposed to the neoclassical view, restricting the choice set can be beneficial for an agent with bounded willpower.</seg>
<seg id="29"> Lack of self-interest refers to the idea that preferences have a social dimension.</seg>
<seg id="30"> Individuals care, or act as if they care, about other individualsˇŻ well-being.</seg>
<seg id="31"> They are also reciprocal: they care about being treated fairly and want to treat others fairly if those others are themselves behaving fairly.</seg>
<seg id="32"> As a result, agents are both nicer and (when they are not treated fairly) more spiteful than postulated by the neoclassical theory.</seg>
<seg id="33"> Beyond this familiar trilogy of bases for deviations from the standard model, economists are exploring additional psychological and sociological factors that shape economic decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="34"> They are also examining decision processes to view and model the black box of human decision-making;</seg>
<seg id="35"> very recent work in neuroeconomics focuses directly on the question of how decisions are made.</seg>
<seg id="36"> This is an important development, since it may help to address the fundamental difficulty of constructing welfare criteria based on individual choices.</seg>
<seg id="37"> In addition to revised models of individual choice, alternative behavioral models of individual choice can help us to understand the functioning of economic institutions.</seg>
<seg id="38"> On the normative side, behavioral modeling can help us to design better institutions.</seg>
<seg id="39"> This can take place not only through better understanding of how the institutions work, but also through better understanding of individual needs and the concept of welfare.</seg>
<seg id="40"> One cannot evaluate the ultimate goodness of a behavioral model of an economic man without seeing how useful the model is in structuring our thinking of general economics.</seg>
<seg id="41"> Applicability requires that the models and stylized facts compound to an integrated theory that is flexible, adequately parsimonious, and permits us to construct testable hypotheses.</seg>
<seg id="42"> This suggests enhancing communication between applications and the underlying theory.</seg>
<seg id="43"> To develop the theory further it helps to have feedback from areas where the theory could be applied.</seg>
<seg id="44"> Studying applications may give a sharper view of the behavioral tendencies that really matter.</seg>
<seg id="45"> The chapters in this volume describe both realized and potential opportunities for applications of behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="46"> Each chapter includes updated versions of a presentation at the conference and the remarks of the discussants.</seg>
<seg id="47"> The final chapter consists of a modified transcript of a round-table discussion.</seg>
<seg id="48"> Summaries of the highlights of the general discussion have been prepared by Botond Koszegi and Emmanuel Saez.</seg>
<seg id="49"> This volume consists of the following chapters.</seg>
<seg id="50"> Douglas Bernheim and Antonio Rangel discuss emerging methods for normative policy analysis in behavioral economics, with a particular focus on issues in public economics.</seg>
<seg id="51"> They argue against the view that a departure from the doctrine of revealed preference, which is unavoidable in the presence of bounded rationality, necessarily renders welfare analysis infeasible or entirely subjective.</seg>
<seg id="52"> Instead, they argue that it is sometimes possible to replace revealed preference by other compelling normative principles.</seg>
<seg id="53"> For example, if one knows enough about the nature of decision-making malfunctions, it may be possible to recover tastes by relying on a selective application of the revealed-preference principle.</seg>
<seg id="54"> Accordingly, practicing behavioral economics requires one to modify, not abandon, the key methodological principles of modern economics.</seg>
<seg id="55"> The chapter considers three areas: addiction, saving, and contributions for public goods.</seg>
<seg id="56"> The discussants are Nicholas Stern and Emmanuel Saez.</seg>
<seg id="57"> Sendhil Mullainathan gives an overview of potential applications of behavioral ideas to economic development.</seg>
<seg id="58"> He argues that the work in the behavioral literature on savings and bounded willpower can be translated into understanding savings institutions and behavior in developing countries.</seg>
<seg id="59"> Additionally, the insights about self-control have some direct links to understanding education, and the behavioral approach also appears to add some insight to the large body of research on the diffusion of innovation.</seg>
<seg id="60"> The question of how (and when) to evaluate the impact of development policies can also be better understood.</seg>
<seg id="61"> Mullainathan speculates about specific areas where psychology could be useful in the future:</seg>
<seg id="62"> poverty traps, conflict, social preferences, corruption, and research on the psychology of the poor.</seg>
<seg id="63"> The discussant is Anne Case.</seg>
<seg id="64"> Christine Jolls discusses applications of behavioral economics to law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="65"> She describes some of the central attributes of behavioral law and economics and outlines an emerging focus on prospects for ˇ°debiasingˇ± individuals through legal structures.</seg>
<seg id="66"> She argues that using the vehicle of ˇ°debiasing through law,ˇ± behavioral law and economics may open up a new space for legal interventions that recognize human limitations and attempt to steer individuals away from mistakes without taking the steering wheel from the individualˇŻs own hands.</seg>
<seg id="67"> Because, however, debiasing through law cannot be applied in every context, Jolls suggests that future work in behavioral law and economics should seek to refine and strengthen analyses on how to structure legal rules when debiasing is not feasible.</seg>
<seg id="68"> The discussants are Ian Ayres and Christoph Engel.Truman F.</seg>
<seg id="69"> Bewley studies the origins of wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="70"> He reports the implications of interviews with company managers and labor leaders in the northeast of the United States during the early 1990s when unemployment was high because of a recession.</seg>
<seg id="71"> During this era, standard economic arguments would have predicted wage cuts, but they never came.</seg>
<seg id="72"> Quite surprisingly, the primary resistance to wage reduction comes from upper management, not from employees.</seg>
<seg id="73"> Bewley finds that the main reason for avoiding pay cuts is that they damage morale.</seg>
<seg id="74"> Morale has three components.</seg>
<seg id="75"> One is the identification with the firm and an internalization of its objectives.</seg>
<seg id="76"> Another is trust in an implicit exchange with the firm and with other employees;</seg>
<seg id="77"> employees know that aid given to the firm or to coworkers will eventually be reciprocated, even if it goes unacknowledged.</seg>
<seg id="78"> The third component is a mood that is conducive to good work.</seg>
<seg id="79"> The mood need not be a happy one;</seg>
<seg id="80"> good morale is not equivalent to happiness or job satisfaction.</seg>
<seg id="81"> Workers may be content, simply because they do nothing.</seg>
<seg id="82"> Good morale has to do with a willingness voluntarily to make sacrifices for the company and for coworkers.</seg>
<seg id="83"> Thus, this chapter is an example of the adaptation of an organization to the behavioral traits displayed by economic agents working in the organization.</seg>
<seg id="84"> This issue in general, and not just in wage setting, is the focus of the chapter on organizations.</seg>
<seg id="85"> The discussant is Seppo Honkapohja.</seg>
<seg id="86"> Richard Frank argues that the health sector is full of institutions and decision making circumstances that involve friction in markets and cognitive errors by decision makers.</seg>
<seg id="87"> Stress of decision-making, anxiety, professionalism, insurance coverage, and lack of information make decision-making in health-related questions particularly relevant for behavioral analysis.</seg>
<seg id="88"> He concludes that at the heart of the matter is the doctor-patient relationship, where trust plays the key role.</seg>
<seg id="89"> Indeed, the field has drawn heavily on nonstandard arguments put forward by Arrow and may be ripe for expansion using the wider range of behavioral insights now available .</seg>
<seg id="90"> Frank also discusses normative issues.</seg>
<seg id="91"> He argues that the demand functions in health-care markets cannot be given the standard normative interpretation and, hence, that they cannot be taken as the definite guideline for policy analysis.</seg>
<seg id="92"> He expresses skepticism over whether the prevailing cornerstones of the U.S. health policyˇŞto increase information and the degree of available choicesˇŞwill improve the quality health production.</seg>
<seg id="93"> The discussants are Jacob Glazer and Botond Koszegi.</seg>
<seg id="94"> Colin Camerer and Ulrike Malmendier analyze, on the one hand, how behavioral economics can be applied to organizations and, on the other, how behavioral analysis of individuals can be enriched by thinking about the economic questions associated with economic organizations.</seg>
<seg id="95"> Biases in behavior within organizations give rise to the question of how organizations should be designed in order to repair these mistakes or to exploit them, or how firms organize around them if they represent genuine regret-free preferences rather than errors.</seg>
<seg id="96"> A lot of psychology and sociology is involved when workers team up in an organization:</seg>
<seg id="97"> social comparison, changes in identity, camaraderie, attribution and diffusion of credit and blame, and so forth.</seg>
<seg id="98"> This kind of behavioral analysis has played a small role in behavioral economics in recent years but looms large when thinking about organizations.</seg>
<seg id="99"> The authors lay down an agenda for further research.</seg>
<seg id="100"> Moreover, the study of institutions can provide important feedback for the analysis of behaviorally bounded individuals.</seg>
<seg id="101"> The discussant is Michael Cohen.</seg>
<seg id="102"> The wrap-up panel consisted of Eldar Shafir, Jean Tirole, Tim Wilson, and Peter Diamond.</seg>
<seg id="103"> Their remarks and the following discussion are presented in the final chapter.</seg>
<seg id="104"> Public economics has positive and normative objectives;</seg>
<seg id="105"> it aims both to describe the effects of public policies and to evaluate them.</seg>
<seg id="106"> This agenda requires us to formulate models of human decision-making with two components: one describing choices, and the other describing well-being.</seg>
<seg id="107"> Using the first component, we can forecast the effects of policy reforms on individualsˇŻ actions, as well as on prices and allocations.</seg>
<seg id="108"> Using the second component, we can determine whether these changes benefit consumers or harm them.</seg>
<seg id="109"> Traditionally, economists have made no distinction between the behavioral and welfare components of economic models.</seg>
<seg id="110"> Such a distinction has not been necessary because standard welfare analysis is grounded in the doctrine of revealed preference.</seg>
<seg id="111"> That is, we infer what people want from what they choose.</seg>
<seg id="112"> When evaluating policies, we attempt to act as each individualˇŻs proxy, extrapolating his or her likely policy choices from observed consumption choices in related situations.</seg>
<seg id="113"> Interest in behavioral economics has grown in recent years, stimulated largely by accumulating evidence that the standard model of consumer decision-making provides an inadequate positive description of human behavior.</seg>
<seg id="114"> Scholars have begun to propose alternative models that incorporate insights from psychology and neuroscience.</seg>
<seg id="115"> Some of the pertinent literature focuses on behaviors commonly considered ˇ°dysfunctional,ˇ± such as addiction, obesity, risky sexual behavior, and crime.</seg>
<seg id="116"> However, there is also considerable interest in alternative approaches to more standard economic problems involving, for example, saving, risk-taking, and charitable contributions.</seg>
<seg id="117"> Behavioral economists have proposed a variety of models that raise difficult issues concerning welfare evaluation.</seg>
<seg id="118"> No consensus concerning appropriate standards and criteria has yet emerged.</seg>
<seg id="119"> Broadly speaking, there are two main schools of thought.</seg>
<seg id="120"> One school of thought insists on strict adherence to the doctrine of revealed preference for the purpose of economic policy evaluation.</seg>
<seg id="121"> In this view, observed ˇ°anomaliesˇ± should be explained, when possible, by expanding the preference domain.</seg>
<seg id="122"> Indeed, in the view of some economists, the only legitimate objective of behavioral economics is to identify preferences that robustly rationalize choices.</seg>
<seg id="123"> This perspective maintains the tight correspondence between the behavioral and welfare components of economic models.</seg>
<seg id="124"> The second school of thought holds that behavioral economics can in principle justify modifying, relaxing, or even jettisoning the principle of revealed preference for the purpose of welfare analysis.</seg>
<seg id="125"> A number of possibilities have been explored.</seg>
<seg id="126"> If people make systematic mistakes in identifiable circumstances, it may be appropriate to apply the principle of revealed preference selectively rather than systematically.</seg>
<seg id="127"> If an individualˇŻs choices reveal several distinct sets of mutually inconsistent preferences, then normative evaluation may require the adoption of a particular perspective.</seg>
<seg id="128"> If choices do not reveal coherent preferences, then perhaps normative evaluations should emphasize other aspects of well-being, such as opportunities.</seg>
<seg id="129"> To pursue any of these possibilities, one must formulate separate, and potentially divergent, positive and normative models.</seg>
<seg id="130"> Adopting alternatives to the principle of revealed preference allows economists to engage on issues that specialists in other fields, as well as the public at large, regard as central policy concerns.</seg>
<seg id="131"> For example, they can meaningfully address the ˇ°self-destructiveˇ± behavior of addicts or make sense of the claim that Americans save ˇ°too littleˇ± for retirement.</seg>
<seg id="132"> However, there is also a danger.</seg>
<seg id="133"> Revealed preference is an attractive political principle because it guards against abuse (albeit quite imperfectly in practice).</seg>
<seg id="134"> Once we relax this doctrine, we potentially legitimize government condemnation of almost any chosen lifestyle on the grounds that it is contrary to a ˇ°naturalˇ± welfare criterion reflecting the individualˇŻs ˇ°trueˇ± interests.</seg>
<seg id="135"> If we can classify, say, the consumption of an addictive substance as contrary to an individualˇŻs interests, what about choices involving literature, religion, or sexual orientation?</seg>
<seg id="136"> If choices do not unambiguously reveal an individualˇŻs notions of good and bad, then ˇ°true preferencesˇ± become the subject of debate, and every ˇ°beneficialˇ± restriction of personal choice becomes fair game.</seg>
<seg id="137"> Given these dangers, if we are to relax the principle of revealed preference when evaluating public policy, it behooves us to set a high scientific threshold for reaching a determination, based on objective evidence, that a given problem calls for divergent positive and normative models.</seg>
<seg id="138"> It is important to emphasize that any justification for modifying or replacing the principle of revealed preference must necessarily appeal to evidence other than observations of choice.</seg>
<seg id="139"> After all, in the absence of additional assumptions, it is impossible to disprove the hypothesis that people prefer what they choose simply by examining their choices.</seg>
<seg id="140"> As we argue in detail below, this is one respect in which direct evidence on the neural mechanisms of decision-making is beginning to prove valuable.</seg>
<seg id="141"> Unfortunately, behavioral economists have typically been somewhat cavalier in adopting normative criteria.</seg>
<seg id="142"> For example, in the literature on quasi-hyperbolic discounting, it is now standard practice to adopt the ˇ°long-runˇ± perspective (p = 1) for welfare analysis, rather than the perspective that governs ˇ°short-runˇ± choices (p &lt; 1).</seg>
<seg id="143"> This approach has been criticized on the grounds that, according to the principle of revealed preference, the short-run perspective also has status as a welfare criterion.</seg>
<seg id="144"> The arguments that have been offered in defense of the ˇ°long-runˇ± perspective have not convinced skeptics that it is appropriate to attach absolutely no normative significance to short-run preferences.</seg>
<seg id="145"> The foundations for welfare analysis therefore require closer attention.</seg>
<seg id="146"> This chapter has two goals.</seg>
<seg id="147"> First, we discuss emerging methods for normative policy analysis in behavioral economics, as well as potentially fruitful lines of inquiry.</seg>
<seg id="148"> We explicitly argue against the view that any departure from the doctrine of revealed preference renders welfare analysis either infeasible or entirely subjective.</seg>
<seg id="149"> We argue instead that it is sometimes possible to replace revealed preference with other compelling normative principles.</seg>
<seg id="150"> For example, if one knows enough about the nature of decision-making malfunctions, it may be possible to recover tastes by relying on a selective application of the revealed-preference principle.</seg>
<seg id="151"> Accordingly, practicing behavioral economics requires us to modify, not abandon, the key methodological principles of modern economics.</seg>
<seg id="152"> Second, we review a collection of applications of behavioral economics to the field of public economics.</seg>
<seg id="153"> In preparing this selective review, we have intentionally favored depth over breadth in the hope of providing a substantive discussion of welfare issues and policy implications.</seg>
<seg id="154"> We focus on three specific policy issues: saving, addiction, and public goods.</seg>
<seg id="155"> each literature is still in its infancy.</seg>
<seg id="156"> The remainder of this chapter is organized as follows.</seg>
<seg id="157"> Section 2.2 discusses alternative approaches to the problem of welfare.</seg>
<seg id="158"> This section is an abbreviated version of Bernheim and Rangel, to which we refer the reader for additional details.</seg>
<seg id="159"> Sections 2.3-2.5 survey applications to, respectively, saving, addictive substances, and public goods.</seg>
<seg id="160"> Section 2.6 provides a brief discussion of the future of behavioral public economics.2.2</seg>
<seg id="161"> CONCEPTUALIZING AND MEASURING WELFAREWelfare analysis has two main components.</seg>
<seg id="162"> First, one determines how policies affect the well-being of each individual.</seg>
<seg id="163"> Second, one aggregates across individuals.</seg>
<seg id="164"> As is well-known, the second step involves some thorny issues (e.g., those raised by ArrowˇŻs Impossibility Theorem).</seg>
<seg id="165"> However, since these are common to both neoclassical and behavioral approaches, we will say no more about them.</seg>
<seg id="166"> Instead, we will focus on the assessment of each individualˇŻs well-being.</seg>
<seg id="167"> There is widespread agreement that normative criteria should respect the principle of individual sovereignty, which holds that notions of good and bad for society should be rooted in the notions of good and bad held by the affected individuals.</seg>
<seg id="168"> This principle instructs policy analysts to act as each individualˇŻs proxy when comparing alternative policies.</seg>
<seg id="169"> It precludes the analyst from imposing his or her own value judgments.</seg>
<seg id="170"> Our focus here is, in effect, on the meaning of the phrase, ˇ°acting as each individualˇŻs proxy.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="171"> In the neoclassical paradigm, the analyst attempts to determine which policy choice the individual would make, given the opportunity.</seg>
<seg id="172"> This is obviously difficult, since the policy choices under consideration differ considerably from the private choices that people ordinarily make.</seg>
<seg id="173"> The beauty and power of standard consumer theory resides in the fact that it allows us to extrapolate choices among public policy outcomes from observations of private choices.</seg>
<seg id="174"> One common interpretation of the neoclassical approach is that people have well- defined preference rankings, which the analyst discovers by examining evidence on choices (through the principle of revealed preference).</seg>
<seg id="175"> These rankings are then taken as the basis for welfare evaluations.</seg>
<seg id="176"> As detailed in Bernheim and Rangel (2005a), this interpretation rests on the following four assumptions.Assumption 2.1 (coherent preferences).</seg>
<seg id="177"> Each individual has coherent, well- behaved preferences.</seg>
<seg id="178"> Assumption 2.2 (preference domain).</seg>
<seg id="179"> The domain of each individualˇŻs preference rankings is the set of lifetime state-contingent consumption paths.</seg>
<seg id="180"> Assumption 2.3 (fixed lifetime preferences).</seg>
<seg id="181"> Each individualˇŻs ranking of lifetime state-contingent consumption paths remains constant across time and states of nature.</seg>
<seg id="182"> Assumption 2.4 (no mistakes).</seg>
<seg id="183"> Each individual always selects the most preferred alternative from the feasible set.</seg>
<seg id="184"> It is important to emphasize that Assumption 2.3 does not rule out the possibility that tastes vary over time or across states of nature.</seg>
<seg id="185"> To illustrate, consider the following problem: choose either an immediate five-day vacation, or a ten-day vacation after a three month delay.</seg>
<seg id="186"> Assumption 2.3 allows for the possibility that the preferred choice changes with age, or fluctuates randomly with mood.</seg>
<seg id="187"> For example, if an individual is under stress, the immediate vacation may be more attractive.</seg>
<seg id="188"> The assumption does not, however, allow for the possibility that, while in a relaxed mood, the individual would wish to prescribe for himself a different choice than he would actually make at other points in time while in an stressed mood.</seg>
<seg id="189"> On the contrary, while in a relaxed mood, he should regard the decisions he makes at other points in time while in stressed moods as optimal.</seg>
<seg id="190"> Though he is willing to make different trade-offs at different points in time and in different states of nature, his notion of a ˇ°life well-livedˇ± remains fixed.</seg>
<seg id="191"> Another interpretation of the neoclassical approach, discussed at greater length in Bernheim and Rangel, holds that revealed preferences are merely constructs for systematizing information concerning choices.</seg>
<seg id="192"> This view does not require one to take a position as to whether people actually have preferences, or whether revealed preferences coincide with ˇ°trueˇ± preferences.</seg>
<seg id="193"> Rather, it posits that people act as if they optimize given particular preferences, and uses this representation to extrapolate choices among policy alternatives.</seg>
<seg id="194"> According to this view, the neoclassical paradigm is only about choice.</seg>
<seg id="195"> Throughout the remainder of this section, we adopt the perspective that preferences are ˇ°realˇ± objects.</seg>
<seg id="196"> In our view, the concept of preference is something that we all understand in concrete terms.</seg>
<seg id="197"> Even if we are limited to inferring othersˇŻ preferences from their choices, this does not call the existence of preferences into question.</seg>
<seg id="198"> After all, most of us believe we can learn much about our own preferences from introspection.</seg>
<seg id="199"> None of us have ever chosen between spending two weeks on Maui and two years in prison,</seg>
<seg id="200"> yet we know we would be happier with the first alternative;</seg>
<seg id="201"> we do not need to infer this preference from an actual choice.</seg>
<seg id="202"> From this perspective, the discovery of true preferences is a central objective of welfare economics.</seg>
<seg id="203"> One can think of the various approaches to welfare analysis that have appeared in the behavioral literature as efforts to grapple with the distinctive issues that arise when we relax each of the four assumptions listed above.</seg>
<seg id="204"> We will consider each of them in turn.2.2.1</seg>
<seg id="205"> Relaxing the First Assumption (Coherent Preferences)</seg>
<seg id="206"> Assumption 2.1 holds that people have well-defined, coherent preferences.</seg>
<seg id="207"> If observed choices are highly context dependent, with significant decisions turning on minor and seemingly irrelevant aspects of framing, then it may be appropriate to assume that people have poorly behaved or incoherent preferences (or possibly no preferences at all).</seg>
<seg id="208"> In this case, how does one evaluate an individualˇŻs well-being?</seg>
<seg id="209"> One possibility is to abandon the principle that the welfare criterion used to evaluate public policy should be based on individual notions of good and bad allocations.</seg>
<seg id="210"> Unlike the standard approach, this leads to a sharp separation between positive models describing choice and normative models describing welfare.</seg>
<seg id="211"> One interesting example of this approach appears in Sugden (2004), who argues for a notion of welfare based on opportunities.</seg>
<seg id="212"> Sugden formulates a rigorous welfare criterion along these lines, and proves a counterpart to the first welfare theorem.</seg>
<seg id="213"> There are many practical and philosophical reasons to consider welfare standards based on opportunities rather than allocations.</seg>
<seg id="214"> This certainly simplifies some aspects of measurement, and it avoids the need to systematize behavioral observations by imposing untested assumptions.</seg>
<seg id="215"> Yet we suspect that most economists will resist such a radical departure from the standard approach.</seg>
<seg id="216"> Even if we acknowledge that opportunities are important, people also appear to care a great deal about allocations and subjective perceptions of well-being.</seg>
<seg id="217"> And while there is some evidence of context dependence and incoherence, we doubt anyone would claim that preferences are entirely incoherent (e.g., one cannot induce the typical person to exchange two weeks at a resort in Maui for two years in prison by manipulating framing).</seg>
<seg id="218"> An approach based exclusively on opportunities would appear to ignore this potentially valuable information.</seg>
<seg id="219"> 2.2.2
<seg id="220"> Some behavioral anomalies that defy explanation within the standard approach may become explicable if we expand the preference domain.</seg>
<seg id="221"> Conceptually, this permits us to conduct welfare analysis by applying the principle of revealed preference, as in the standard approach</seg>
<seg id="222"> (that is, we can use essentially the same model to describe choices and welfare).</seg>
<seg id="223"> We discuss two examples.</seg>
<seg id="224"> The first example involves temptation and self-control.</seg>
<seg id="225"> Motivating behavioral anomalies include evidence of apparent time-inconsistency and various forms of precommitment.</seg>
<seg id="226"> Gul and Pesendorfer (2001) argue that it is possible to account for a range of otherwise puzzling behavioral observations if preferences are defined over both allocations and choice sets</seg>
<seg id="227"> If some choices feel tempting when they are available, and if this detracts from well-being, then an individual may prefer small choice sets to large ones.</seg>
<seg id="228"> This provides a reason to constrain future alternatives even when constraints have no impact on choices.</seg>
<seg id="229"> In the Gul-Pesendorfer framework, a desire to constrain future choices does not imply that preferences change over time.</seg>
<seg id="230"> On the contrary, as in the standard framework, the individual applies the same set of lifetime preferences at every moment in time.</seg>
<seg id="231"> Even though, at time t, he might wish to constrain his available options for time s &gt; t, he nevertheless approves of the choice he would actually make at time s in the absence of this constraint</seg>
<seg id="232"> (because he understands the significance of temptation).</seg>
<seg id="233"> In this framework, if one imposes suitable structure on choice data, one can discover lifetime preferences over allocations and choice sets by applying the principle of revealed preference, and one can use these preferences to make welfare evaluations, just as in the standard approach.</seg>
<seg id="234"> The second example involves social preferences.</seg>
<seg id="235"> Motivating behavioral anomalies include, among others, a tendency to give money away in settings where there is no room for reciprocity an apparent aversion to inequality</seg>
<seg id="236"> and a desire to conform to group norms (see Jones (1984) for a review of pertinent evidence).</seg>
<seg id="237"> For the purpose of positive modeling, behavioral economists frequently assume that preferences are defined not only over an individualˇŻs own consumption bundle,</seg>
<seg id="238"> but also over social outcomes, such as the consumption bundles of others.</seg>
<seg id="239"> If one imposes suitable structure on choice data, one can once again discover these tastes by applying the principle of revealed preference.</seg>
<seg id="240"> These preferences provide a foundation for normative evaluation (in other words, one again uses essentially the same model to describe choices and welfare).</seg>
<seg id="241"> Relaxing the Third Assumption (Fixed Lifetime Preferences)</seg>
<seg id="242"> The third assumption states that preferences over lifetime state-contingent consumption paths do not change over time or across states of nature.</seg>
<seg id="243"> Behavioral anomalies motivating relaxation of this assumption include, again, evidence of apparent time- inconsistency and various forms of precommitment.</seg>
<seg id="244"> From a positive perspective, a common modeling strategy involves endowing the individual with different well- behaved lifetime preferences at different points in time</seg>
<seg id="245"> one could, of course, also allow lifetime preferences to vary across states of nature.</seg>
<seg id="246"> Assuming we have measured these preferences properly, welfare analysis requires us, in effect, to adjudicate conflicts among them.</seg>
<seg id="247"> The problem is analogous to welfare aggregation involving many individuals;</seg>
<seg id="248"> here, we aggregate over multiple ˇ°selves.ˇ±One branch of the literature exploits this analogy.</seg>
<seg id="249"> Effectively, it envisions person A at time t as the ˇ°childˇ± of person A at time t ˇŞ 1.</seg>
<seg id="250"> It then applies standard multi-person welfare principles.</seg>
<seg id="251"> One possibility is to apply the Pareto criterion.</seg>
<seg id="252"> The main problem with this approach is that the criterion is not very discerning.</seg>
<seg id="253"> As a result, it is often impossible to rank interesting classes of policies.</seg>
<seg id="254"> One usually ends up being able to offer policy makers little in the way of clear guidance.</seg>
<seg id="255"> A second possibility is to aggregate preferences through the application of some welfare function.</seg>
<seg id="256"> As in problems with multiple consumers, one can write down a class of well-behaved aggregators (i.e., the analog of Samuelson-Bergson social welfare functions) and attempt to derive general results.</seg>
<seg id="257"> However, unless one has a basis for making specific assumptions about the aggregator, this approach fails to sharpen the prescriptions generated from application of the Pareto criterion.</seg>
<seg id="258"> Alternatively, one could in principle provide the policy maker with a mapping from properties of the aggregator (e.g., welfare weights) to prescriptions.</seg>
<seg id="259"> A second branch of the literature makes welfare evaluations based on some reasonably stable component of preferences.</seg>
<seg id="260"> For example, OˇŻDonoghue and Rabin (1999b) argue for the application of a ˇ°long-runˇ± welfare criterion (p = 1) in models with quasi-hyperbolic discounting.</seg>
<seg id="261"> In Bernheim and Rangel (2005a), we provide a formal justification of this criterion based on aggregation principles.</seg>
<seg id="262"> In particular, we demonstrate that if the consumerˇŻs horizon is sufficiently long,</seg>
<seg id="263"> and if the policy analyst applies any member of a large class of well-behaved aggregators, the resulting welfare criterion is ˇ°closeˇ± to long-run preferences.</seg>
<seg id="264"> The intuition for this result is that the consumer judges trade-offs between period t and t + 1 by exactly the same criteria in all periods but one,</seg>
<seg id="265"> and the influence of any one ˇ°selfˇ± must decline to zero as the number of selves becomes large.</seg>
<seg id="266"> One can make a similar point concerning states of nature.</seg>
<seg id="267"> To illustrate, consider an individual who lives in continuous time.</seg>
<seg id="268"> Choices are essentially instantaneous but have long-lasting consequences (as an example, think of drug use).</seg>
<seg id="269"> The individualˇŻs mental state is either ˇ°cold,ˇ± which corresponds to one set of lifetime preferences, or ˇ°hot,ˇ± which corresponds to another.</seg>
<seg id="270"> Normally, the individual operates in a cold mode.</seg>
<seg id="271"> At each moment, there is some chance that he enters the hot state, which has a fixed duration of e.</seg>
<seg id="272"> Suppose we model the arrival of the hot state as a failure time process, with a fixed hazard parameter.</seg>
<seg id="273"> As e approaches zero, the fraction of time spent in the cold state converges to unity.</seg>
<seg id="274"> Accordingly, if we aggregate preferences according to the frequency with which they prevail, we end up using the cold preferences for normative analysis.</seg>
<seg id="275"> Even so, cold preferences do not describe behavior in this limit.</seg>
<seg id="276"> Since hot states can create ˇ°momentary lapsesˇ± with long- lasting effects, the appropriate positive and normative models diverge.</seg>
<seg id="277"> See Bernheim and Rangel (2005a) for a formal treatment.</seg>
<seg id="278"> Relaxing the Fourth Assumption (No Mistakes)</seg>
<seg id="279"> Assumption 2.4 holds that choice and preferences do not diverge.</seg>
<seg id="280"> Gul and Pesendorfer (2004a) defend this assumption as follows:Revealed-preference theory defines the interest of people to be what they do.</seg>
<seg id="281"> Since there is no objective standard of self-interested behavior it is unclear what it would mean for an agent to act against his self-interest.</seg>
<seg id="282"> Yet there are clearly situations where virtually everyone would agree that divergence does occurˇŞwhere a choice is obviously not in someoneˇŻs interest.</seg>
<seg id="283"> There are also situations in which most would agree that public policy should recognize these divergences.</seg>
<seg id="284"> Consider the following example.</seg>
<seg id="285"> American visitors in London suffer numerous injuries and fatalities because they often look only to the left before stepping into streets, even though they know traffic approaches from the right.</seg>
<seg id="286"> This is a systematic pattern;</seg>
<seg id="287"> one cannot dismiss it as an isolated incident.</seg>
<seg id="288"> A literal application of the revealed preference compels us to conclude either that these people simply have a very strong preference to look left, or that they are masochistic.</seg>
<seg id="289"> If we use these revealed preferences for welfare analysis, there is no legitimate basis for preventing someone from stepping in front of a truck.</seg>
<seg id="290"> And yet it is safe to say that, after recognizing the purpose of the intervention, anyone would be grateful.</seg>
<seg id="291"> The pedestrianˇŻs objectiveˇŞto cross the street safelyˇŞis clear, and the decision is plainly a mistake.</seg>
<seg id="292"> As another example, consider the treatment of children.</seg>
<seg id="293"> Few economists would apply notions of consumer sovereignty and revealed preference to evaluate the welfare of a child.</seg>
<seg id="294"> We acknowledge that children do not know what is best, and that their actions often fail to reflect valid preferences, probably because they give insufficient weight to consequences.</seg>
<seg id="295"> Policies prohibiting the sale of cigarettes and alcohol to minors are therefore relatively uncontroversial.</seg>
<seg id="296"> And yet it is difficult to justify, objectively, the sense in which the revealed preferences of an irresponsible nineteen- year-old are legitimate, whereas those of a fourteen-year-old are not.</seg>
<seg id="297"> While turning eighteen has profound legal significance, it does not discontinuously change the mechanics of decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="298"> There are other contexts for which revealed preference seems untenable as a guiding principle for public policy evaluation.</seg>
<seg id="299"> For example, when people have sufficiently severe diagnosed psychiatric disorders, the state can and should step in to protect them.</seg>
<seg id="300"> Eating disorders, while not quite as extreme, provide another illustration.</seg>
<seg id="301"> For the purpose of public policy, we probably should not proceed on the assumption that an anorexicˇŻs refusal to eat is just an expression of valid preferences.</seg>
<seg id="302"> On the contrary, we should and generally do regard this as dysfunctional.</seg>
<seg id="303"> These examples are instructive because they suggest that, in some circumstances, it is reasonable to use evidence of brain process malfunctionsˇŞsomething other than choice dataˇŞto trump the principle of revealed preference.</seg>
<seg id="304"> In these situations, denying the possibility of mistakes while rigidly adhering to the principle of revealed preference guarantees the use of an improper welfare criterion.</seg>
<seg id="305"> So far, we have confined our discussion to ˇ°dysfunctionalˇ± choices.</seg>
<seg id="306"> More generally, almost any behavioral anomaly motivating some relaxation of the first three assumptions can also motivate relaxation of the fourth.</seg>
<seg id="307"> For example, evidence of time-inconsistent present-bias may reflect a systematic tendency to ˇ°over consume.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="308"> Likewise, people may make precommitments to prevent themselves from repeating a pattern of mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="309"> A natural analytic strategy involves endowing the individual with well-behaved lifetime preferences, while simultaneously specifying a decision process (or decision criterion) that does not necessarily involve selecting the maximal element in the preference ordering.</seg>
<seg id="310"> To conduct positive analysis, one employs a model of the decision process (or criterion).</seg>
<seg id="311"> To conduct normative analysis, one uses a model of lifetime preferences.</seg>
<seg id="312"> In contrast to the standard approach, these positive and normative models potentially diverge.</seg>
<seg id="313"> Our model of addiction (Bernheim and Rangel 2004), discussed in greater detail below, exemplifies this approach.</seg>
<seg id="314"> We assume that people attempt to optimize given their true preferences,</seg>
<seg id="315"> but randomly encounter conditions that trigger systematic mistakes, the likelihood of which evolves with previous substance use.</seg>
<seg id="316"> One can also interpret the familiar model of quasi-hyperbolic discounting along similar lines (indeed, many of those who advocate this model favor this interpretation).</seg>
<seg id="317"> In this interpretation, present-biased behavior is a mistake that results from the decision?making processesˇŻ tendency to place too much weight on immediate rewards relative to future rewards.</seg>
<seg id="318"> In justifying and implementing this approach, we encounter two critical and difficult issues.</seg>
<seg id="319"> First, how do we know that choices and preferences diverge?</seg>
<seg id="320"> That is, what is the basis for overturning the principle of revealed preference?</seg>
<seg id="321"> Second, if we find compelling evidence of divergence, how do we identify preferences empirically?</seg>
<seg id="322"> Both questions are addressed in the literature, though not in a single paper.</seg>
<seg id="323"> Criteria for Overturning Revealed Preferences.</seg>
<seg id="324"> With respect to the first issue, it is important to acknowledge that, strictly speaking, it is impossible to overturn the principle of revealed preference using only observations of choices.</seg>
<seg id="325"> While choice experiments can overturn specific structural assumptions, overturning the principle itself necessarily requires other types of evidence.</seg>
<seg id="326"> It is always possible to rationalize choice data by assuming that tastes are sufficiently context-specific.</seg>
<seg id="327"> One promising approach is to use evidence from neuroscience and psychology on the neural processes at work in decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="328"> For example, if it is possible to isolate a process that provides inputs for decision-making, and to show either that this process has substantive limitations, or that it malfunctions under identifiable circumstances,</seg>
<seg id="329"> then the evidence may provide a foundation both for asserting the existence of errors, and for a particular reduced-form model of the error-producing mechanism.</seg>
<seg id="330"> In this regard, brain processes of particular interest include those involved in anticipating and evaluating the outcomes of different choices, remembering pertinent information (memory), and attending to relevant data and options (attention).</seg>
<seg id="331"> An example of this approach appears in Bernheim and Rangel (2004), where we argue that addictive substances interfere with the proper operation of an automatic neural forecasting system, thereby skewing decisions.</seg>
<seg id="332"> We elaborate on this example in Section 2.4.5, below.</seg>
<seg id="333"> Strategies for Identifying Preferences.</seg>
<seg id="334"> With respect to the second issue, it may be possible in a given instance to identify preferences by interpreting the available data through the lens of structural modeling.</seg>
<seg id="335"> This approach requires one to formulate two tightly parameterized models: one for preferences, and one for choices.</seg>
<seg id="336"> Ideally, it should be possible to justify the major structural assumptions of the decision-making model through the type of neurological and psychological evidence used to establish the existence of a discrepancy between preferences and choices.</seg>
<seg id="337"> As long as true preferences influence choices, even if the individual does not optimize, there will be some relationship between the parameters of the positive and normative models, and this will be useful for purposes of identification.</seg>
<seg id="338"> Indeed, for the two examples mentioned so far</seg>
<seg id="339"> the parameters of the normative model are a subset of the parameters of the positive model (certain parameters describe true preferences, and others describe discrepancies between choices and preferences).</seg>
<seg id="340"> Consequently, by estimating the positive model, one can recover preferences under the maintained hypothesis that the structural assumptions are correct.</seg>
<seg id="341"> Ideally, the assumed structure should subsume the possibility that there is no discrepancy between preferences and choices, so that it is possible to test this hypothesis.</seg>
<seg id="342"> Both of the examples considered above satisfy this requirement.</seg>
<seg id="343"> Identification of Preferences through Choice Data.</seg>
<seg id="344"> As long as the parameters of the normative model are a subset of the parameters of the positive model, one can in principle estimate these parameters using data on choices, and nothing else.</seg>
<seg id="345"> For example, Laibson et al. (forthcoming) use consumption data to parameterize a model with quasi-hyperbolic discounting.</seg>
<seg id="346"> This in turn implies that it is possible to test the hypothesis of no mistakes (e.g., p = 1 in the context of quasi- hyperbolic discounting) without considering anything other than choices.</seg>
<seg id="347"> This statement seems inconsistent with the principle that it is impossible to falsify the principle of revealed preference with choice data alone.</seg>
<seg id="348"> The explanation for this apparent inconsistency is that one tests the hypothesis of no mistakes jointly with the assumptions of the structural model.</seg>
<seg id="349"> Even if this joint hypothesis is rejected, there is some other structural model for which the hypothesis of no mistakes would not be rejected.</seg>
<seg id="350"> When interpreting the results, one therefore necessarily relies on the nonchoice evidence used to justify the assumed structure.</seg>
<seg id="351"> Accordingly, the reliability and strength of this nonchoice evidence limits the force of oneˇŻs conclusions.</seg>
<seg id="352"> The observations in the preceding paragraph remain valid even if one uses data on nonstandard types of choices, such as decisions made in advance of consequences, precommitments, and expenditures on self-control.</seg>
<seg id="353"> For any given structural decision-making model, this type of evidence may prove extremely useful from the perspective of estimating parameters precisely and convincingly,</seg>
<seg id="354"> and it may allow one to reject the hypothesis of no mistakes for a much broader class of preferences (e.g., any preference for which the decision maker would exhibit time-consistent behavior).</seg>
<seg id="355"> However, stepping outside of the assumed structure, there will always be other formulations of preferences that can explain the choice data without assuming a divergence between preferences and decisions.</seg>
<seg id="356"> Of course, any such formulation will necessarily diverge from the standard model (as in Gul and Pesendorfer 2001), and, in any given case, rationalization of the data may require strange assumptions about preferences.</seg>
<seg id="357"> It is worth emphasizing that the estimation of separate positive and normative models does not require us to abandon the principle of revealed preference completely.</seg>
<seg id="358"> Instead, one implicitly invokes a principle of selectively revealed preference.</seg>
<seg id="359"> Depending on the structural model, identifiable decisions (e.g., in the context of quasi-hyperbolic discounting, choices well in advance of consequences)</seg>
<seg id="360"> may, by assumption, reveal preferences with certainty, or there may be uncertainty as to whether a given decision conforms to preferences (as in models with stochastic mistakes).</seg>
<seg id="361"> In the latter case, one can model this uncertainty explicitly, proceeding, for example, as in the literature on switching regimes.</seg>
<seg id="362"> Identification of Preferences through Both Choice and Nonchoice Data.</seg>
<seg id="363"> Another largely unexplored possibility would involve the use of both choice and nonchoice data in structural estimation.</seg>
<seg id="364"> Data of potential interest could include self-reported information about preferences and/or well-being, as well as measures of physical states such as arousal and stress.</seg>
<seg id="365"> These additional data could facilitate more precise and reliable estimation of key structural parameters.</seg>
<seg id="366"> One might, for example, use self-reported data on preferences along with choice data to estimate the parameters of a normative model.</seg>
<seg id="367"> In principle, the normative model could even include parameters that do not appear in the positive model.</seg>
<seg id="368"> Likewise, nonchoice data may prove useful in identifying circumstances in which choices reliably reflect preferences, and those in which they do not.</seg>
<seg id="369"> If, for example, there is reason to believe that people are more prone to make mistakes when they are under stress, data on cortisol levels might help to identify choices that reveal preferences more reliably.</seg>
<seg id="370"> The use of nonchoice data raises several concerns.</seg>
<seg id="371"> First, one can interpret the data through the lens of structural modeling only if one is willing to make additional assumptions, for example, about how the nonchoice data relate to decision-making processes.</seg>
<seg id="372"> Advocates of the revealed-preference approach view these assumptions with considerable suspicion.</seg>
<seg id="373"> However, an emerging theme in behavioral economics is that it is possible to justify, defend, and test these assumptions through the careful use of data from psychology and neuroscience.</seg>
<seg id="374"> Furthermore, in practice the revealed-preference approach relies on assumptions that are not directly supported by choice data</seg>
<seg id="375"> (e.g., structural estimation always entails untested restrictions on the form of preferences) and people have different opinions as to which of these assumptions are most ˇ°reasonableˇ± in a given instance.</seg>
<seg id="376"> To the extent that we judge an assumption as reasonable based on evidence not involving choice, it behooves us to make the basis of our inference explicit, regardless of whether we follow the standard approach or a behavioral alternative.</seg>
<seg id="377"> One cannot claim an advantage for the standard approach simply by sweeping the implicit reliance on nonchoice evidence under the rug, or by theorizing about an idealized procedure that is impossible to follow in practice</seg>
<seg id="378"> Second, economists generally view nonchoice data as significantly less reliable and considerably more ambiguous than information on choices.</seg>
<seg id="379"> In part, this view is justified by evidence indicating that certain types of self-reported data are unreliable</seg>
<seg id="380"> In our view, this deficiency is exaggerated, particularly with regard to evidence concerning limitations and malfunctions of specific brain processes involving forecasting, memory, and attention (as discussed above).</seg>
<seg id="381"> There is every reason to believe that the quality of this and other nonchoice evidence, as well as our ability to interpret it, will improve with time.</seg>
<seg id="382"> Furthermore, given the potential value of nonchoice data, concerns about the quality of this information should motivate the development of better procedures for acquiring and interpreting it, rather than a policy of ignoring it on ˇ°conceptualˇ± grounds.</seg>
<seg id="383"> We conclude this section by acknowledging two further concerns.</seg>
<seg id="384"> First, the feasibility and value of the empirical approach to measuring welfare discussed in this section has yet to be established through a series of persuasive applications.</seg>
<seg id="385"> Only a few studies (discussed below) have made a start in this direction.</seg>
<seg id="386"> There are many unresolved issues, e.g., those concerning how to elicit and use data on self-reported preferences.</seg>
<seg id="387"> Nevertheless, at a conceptual level, it does appear that one can meaningfully conduct empirical welfare analysis allowing for some types of divergences between preferences and choices.</seg>
<seg id="388"> Second, there are significant political dangers associated with the research agenda described in this section.</seg>
<seg id="389"> As we mentioned in Section 2.1, revealed preference is an attractive political principle because it prevents critics of any particular choice (e.g., concerning literature, sexual orientation, or religion) from condemning it on the grounds that it is contrary to a ˇ°naturalˇ± welfare criterion reflecting the individualˇŻs ˇ°trueˇ± interests.</seg>
<seg id="390"> While we do not condone casual departures from this principle, we do think it is possible to insist on a high standard of proof, based on scientific evidence.</seg>
<seg id="391"> In classifying certain behavioral patterns, such as psychoses, eating disorders, and addiction, as mental illnesses, the medical profession has grappled with essentially the same issues.</seg>
<seg id="392"> While there have certainly been some dubious decisions (e.g., the classification, until relatively recently, of homosexuality as a psychiatric disorder), the process has, on the whole, reflected the balanced application of sound scientific principles.</seg>
<seg id="393"> SAVINGFor more than fifty years, the framework of intertemporal utility maximization has dominated economistsˇŻ thinking about personal saving.</seg>
<seg id="394"> This framework traces its roots to Fisher (1930), and lies at the heart of the Life-Cycle Hypothesis articulated by Modigliani and Brumberg (1954).</seg>
<seg id="395"> In recent years it has become controversial, and an increasing number of economists have expressed doubts concerning its general validity.</seg>
<seg id="396"> In this section, we survey some of the pertinent empirical evidence motivating the growing interest in alternatives to the standard model, describe some leading behavioral models, and explore some of their key policy implications.</seg>
<seg id="397"> Our objective is to cover central themes.</seg>
<seg id="398"> Given the size and rapid growth of this literature, we make no attempt to be comprehensive.</seg>
<seg id="399"> Also, in describing competing models of saving, we focus on basic formulations, and ignore complications arising from liquidity constraints, intertemporal complementarities, and uncertainty about length of life and market parameters.</seg>
<seg id="400"> The Policy IssuesThe last few decades have witnessed sharp declines in rates of saving for many developed countries.</seg>
<seg id="401"> For example, according to statistics from the National Income and Product Accounts (NIPA), the rate of net national saving for the United States dropped from 8.3% of net national product in 1980 to 1.8% in 2003.</seg>
<seg id="402"> Low rates of saving have created widespread concern over investment, growth, the balance of payments, and the financial security of individual households.</seg>
<seg id="403"> As a result, policymakers worldwide have become increasingly interested in developing strategies for stimulating thrift.</seg>
<seg id="404"> Public policies affecting private saving are highly contentious.</seg>
<seg id="405"> In the United States, policy makers are currently debating a variety of critical questions.</seg>
<seg id="406"> Should the United States partly replace its traditional social security system with individual savings accounts?</seg>
<seg id="407"> If so, how should we structure the new system?</seg>
<seg id="408"> Should the government impose more stringent regulations on defined-contribution pension plans, which appear to be replacing defined-benefit plans at a steady rate?</seg>
<seg id="409"> Should we create or expand tax-deferred savings accounts for special needs, such as medical care and education?</seg>
<seg id="410"> Or should we consider more fundamental tax reform that would reduce or eliminate the tax burden on capital income across the board?</seg>
<seg id="411"> To answer these and other critical questions, public economists require a theory of personal financial decision-making that can explain observed behavior and generate credible out-of-sample predictions.</seg>
<seg id="412"> It must also provide clear answers to normative questions, such as whether people save enough for retirement, and whether they invest their savings wisely.</seg>
<seg id="413"> The Neoclassical Perspective on SavingWe begin by reviewing a simple version of the standard model.</seg>
<seg id="414"> In practice, policy makers worry that people are not saving enough for their own security and future well-being.</seg>
<seg id="415"> This is part of the motivation for proposals involving subsidized saving and/or mandatory accumulation.</seg>
<seg id="416"> The standard model does not, however, recognize the legitimacy of this concern (except insofar as it results from a market failure).</seg>
<seg id="417"> Under this view, saying that someone saves ˇ°too littleˇ± is comparable to asserting that he or she does not listen to enough classical music:</seg>
<seg id="418"> thrift is simply a matter of taste.</seg>
<seg id="419"> In contrast, if households potentially make systematic mistakes, the adequacy of saving becomes a well-posed and important empirical issue.</seg>
<seg id="420"> In the ensuing sections, we review some of the evidence that calls the legitimacy of the standard approach into question, and we explore the implications of several emerging alternatives.</seg>
<seg id="421"> Some Problematic ObservationsIn some respects, saving behavior conforms reasonably well to the predictions of the Life-Cycle Hypothesis.</seg>
<seg id="422"> For example, most people tend to accumulate wealth, broadly defined to include things like pension and social security entitlements, over the course of their working lives, and use either some or all of it to finance consumption after retirement.</seg>
<seg id="423"> Yet there are also sound reasons to question the general applicability of this model and to examine alternatives.</seg>
<seg id="424"> Here we list a number of problematic patterns identified in the literature.</seg>
<seg id="425"> While it may be possible to account for some of these within the context of the Life-Cycle framework, collectively they pose a serious challenge to this approach.</seg>
<seg id="426"> Changes in Consumption Near Retirement.</seg>
<seg id="427"> The standard framework implies that people should smooth consumption, avoiding sudden and predictable changes in living standard.</seg>
<seg id="428"> Yet a variety of studies have found that consumption declines sharply at retirement, when households experience a predictable decline in disposable income.</seg>
<seg id="429"> The decline in consumption is strongly correlated with accumulated wealth;</seg>
<seg id="430"> those who accumulate less experience larger declines.</seg>
<seg id="431"> One can try to account for this pattern within the standard model in several ways.</seg>
<seg id="432"> First, retirement may be associated with a decline in work-related expenses and/or consumption goods that are substitutes for leisure.</seg>
<seg id="433"> If these effects are anticipated, and if their magnitudes vary across the population, then people who plan for larger spending cuts after retirement will intentionally accumulate less wealth.</seg>
<seg id="434"> Yet the evidence does not support this interpretation, as the effect is equally strong for categories of spending that would appear complementary to leisure and unrelated to work.</seg>
<seg id="435"> Second, for those who stop working earlier than expected (e.g., due to disability), retirement reflects ˇ°bad news,ˇ± to which consumption must adjust.</seg>
<seg id="436"> Moreover, these same individuals find themselves with less-than-average wealth at retirement.</seg>
<seg id="437"> However, even when the effects of unexpected retirement are removed through statistical procedures, one still observes both a decline in consumption at retirement, and a strong correlation between the size of this effect and accumulated wealth.</seg>
<seg id="438"> Notably, the sharp drop in consumption at retirement is also larger for households with lower rates of income replacement from social security and pension plans.</seg>
<seg id="439"> Once again, this pattern is observed even when the effects of unexpected retirement are removed.</seg>
<seg id="440"> Since income replacement rates are easily anticipated, and since this variable is not likely to be strongly correlated with work- related expenses or a preference for leisure substitutes, standard theory is hard- pressed to account for the evidence.</seg>
<seg id="441"> This evidence would appear to indicate that people reduce consumption at retirement because they are surprised, either by the decline in their disposable income or by the inadequacy of their accumulated wealth.</seg>
<seg id="442"> Yet other evidence suggests that the decline in consumption at retirement is anticipated.</seg>
<seg id="443"> The explanation for this apparent puzzle remains an open question.</seg>
<seg id="444"> Self-Reported Mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="445"> Several studies document large gaps between self-reported behavior and self-reported plans and/or preferences.</seg>
<seg id="446"> A large fraction of the population reports saving too little (that is, significantly less than planned, or less than appropriate) for retirement.</seg>
<seg id="447"> The reported gap is quite large, and few people report saving too much.</seg>
<seg id="448"> Of those who express an intention to increase their saving, only a small fraction follow through.</seg>
<seg id="449"> Taking these self-reports literally, one would conclude that pro-saving policies are potentially welfare-improving.</seg>
<seg id="450"> Skeptics counter that people are inclined to report ˇ°idealˇ± or ˇ°virtuousˇ± behavior in answer to questions about plans or preferences;</seg>
<seg id="451"> they might well also report that they watch too much television.</seg>
<seg id="452"> This is a serious concern.</seg>
<seg id="453"> However, the finding appears to be robust across samples, contexts, and phrasing of the pertinent questions.</seg>
<seg id="454"> While the evidence is imperfect, in our view it should not be dismissed.</seg>
<seg id="455"> Others minimize the significance of the self-reported savings gap on the grounds that carefully calibrated life-cycle models can replicate data on wealth accumulation.</seg>
<seg id="456"> We find this line of argument unconvincing.</seg>
<seg id="457"> At most, it supports an ˇ°as-ifˇ± interpretation of the life-cycle model.</seg>
<seg id="458"> This does not rule out the possibility that people actually do make mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="459"> Within the standard framework, one can rationalize a systematic tendency to consume too much as impatienceˇŞthat is, a low value of S.</seg>
<seg id="460"> However, if overconsumption is indeed a mistake, then the true value of S is higher than the as-if value, and this rationalization leads to an inappropriate welfare criterion.</seg>
<seg id="461"> In addition, the models used to ˇ°explainˇ± the level and distribution of wealth have other counterfactual implications (e.g., they produce no decline in consumption at retirement).</seg>
<seg id="462"> Limited Planning Skills.</seg>
<seg id="463"> Most people are poorly equipped to engage in life-cycle planning without assistance.</seg>
<seg id="464"> Collectively, existing studies paint a rather bleak picture of economic and financial literacy.</seg>
<seg id="465"> For example, only 20% of adults can determine correct change using prices from a menu, and many have trouble determining whether a mortgage rate of 8.6% is better or worse than 81%.</seg>
<seg id="466"> People tend to underestimate the power of compound interest, and many poorly understand common financial instruments.</seg>
<seg id="467"> In principle, financially illiterate individuals could seek guidance from experts.</seg>
<seg id="468"> In practice, somewhere in the neighborhood of 60% of virtually every population subgroup relies primarily on parents, relatives, friends, and personal judgment.</seg>
<seg id="469"> People with less education are actually more likely to rely on their own judgment.</seg>
<seg id="470"> Only a minority consults financial professionals or print media.</seg>
<seg id="471"> Moreover, in some cases, financial professionals rely on simple rules of thumb, and even their relatively sophisticated tools conflict in some ways with sound life-cycle planning principles.</seg>
<seg id="472"> Financial literacy is strongly related to behavior.</seg>
<seg id="473"> Those who are less financially literate also tend to save less.</seg>
<seg id="474"> Moreover, measures designed to address financial illiteracy appear to have significant effects on choices.</seg>
<seg id="475"> Policies mandating financial education for high school students result in higher asset accumulation once exposed students reach adulthood.</seg>
<seg id="476"> Likewise, financial education in the workplace increases participation in employee-directed pension plans and stimulates saving.</seg>
<seg id="477"> Failure to Formulate Sophisticated Plans.</seg>
<seg id="478"> Under an ˇ°as-ifˇ± interpretation, the standard model implies nothing about the process by which an individual arrives at consumption and saving decisions.</seg>
<seg id="479"> Yet it is difficult to see how someone would formulate coherent life-cycle choices without extensive and deliberate planning.</seg>
<seg id="480"> In practice, many people report spending little if any effort formulating long-range financial plans; moreover, those who fail to plan tend to save less.</seg>
<seg id="481"> When they exist, financial plans tend to be relatively unsophisticated.</seg>
<seg id="482"> Many people establish saving targets, and in most cases think of these targets as percentages of income.</seg>
<seg id="483"> However, the targets appear to reflect rough rules of thumb:</seg>
<seg id="484"> in the vast majority of cases, they are integer multiples of 5%, and they vary neither with stated expectations about earnings growth nor with age.</seg>
<seg id="485"> In addition, important financial decisions often appear to turn on arguably irrelevant considerations.</seg>
<seg id="486"> People are significantly more likely to make tax-deductible Individual Retirement Arrangement (IRA) contributions if they owe the Internal Revenue Service money at the end of the tax year.</seg>
<seg id="487"> There is a striking tendency for a household to make an IRA contribution equal to the single-person limit, even when they are eligible to contribute more.</seg>
<seg id="488"> And IRA participation rates rose sharply when the system was expanded in 1982, even among groups that had been eligible prior to the expansion, and fell sharply once the system was scaled back in 1986, even among groups that remained eligible.</seg>
<seg id="489"> The Importance of Default Options.</seg>
<seg id="490"> We use the term ˇ°default optionˇ± to signify the outcome resulting from inaction.</seg>
<seg id="491"> For a neoclassical consumer, choices depend only on preferences and constraints.</seg>
<seg id="492"> Consequently, in the absence of significant transaction costs, default options should be inconsequential.</seg>
<seg id="493"> Yet, in the context of decisions concerning saving and investment, they appear to matter a great deal.</seg>
<seg id="494"> With respect to 401(k) plans, there is considerable evidence that default options affect participation rates, contribution rates, and portfolios.</seg>
<seg id="495"> Also, automatic cash distributions for terminated employees with small balances reduce retirement account balances, even though these employees are free to roll their funds into an IRA.</seg>
<seg id="496"> Effects of defaults on portfolio allocation have also been documented in the context of the recent privatization of social security in Sweden.</seg>
<seg id="497"> The dissemination of information about investment alternatives appears to counter this effect.</seg>
<seg id="498"> In the standard framework, defaults can matter if other choices are associated with significant transaction costs.</seg>
<seg id="499"> Yet in the contexts described above, transactions costs are presumably quite low.</seg>
<seg id="500"> Alternatively, the effect of a default option may be related to the costs of decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="501"> In pressing this explanation, one must explain why these costs favor the default option over other alternatives (e.g., the simplest or most transparent choices).</seg>
<seg id="502"> One possibility is that people believe the default conveys information about the wisdom of a particular choice.</seg>
<seg id="503"> This may be a plausible assumption in the context of portfolio allocation within 401(k) plans, where the employer has a fiduciary responsibility to its employees in its role as plan sponsor.</seg>
<seg id="504"> In any case, even if default options are viewed as informative, their strong effects tell us that people regularly make significant decisions concerning saving on the basis of precious little information.</seg>
<seg id="505"> Inefficient Choices.</seg>
<seg id="506"> In the standard framework, consumers always choose alternatives on the efficient frontiers of their constraint sets.</seg>
<seg id="507"> When evaluating evidence pertaining to this implication, it would be unfair to interpret it too literally.</seg>
<seg id="508"> In some instances (e.g., failure to engage in sophisticated tax arbitrage), squeezing out the last dime involves complex arrangements and potentially high transaction costs,</seg>
<seg id="509"> so the appearance of inefficiency may be illusory.</seg>
<seg id="510"> However, in some cases, people select alternatives far from the efficient frontiers of their choice sets in settings in which superior alternatives are clearly available.</seg>
<seg id="511"> Examples include failures to take advantage of low-interest loans available through life insurance policies (War- shawsky 1987), naive diversification strategies (Bernartzi and Thaler 2001), the tendency to invest 401(k) balances heavily in the stock of oneˇŻs employer (Holden et al. 2001; Bernartzi 2001),</seg>
<seg id="512"> the proclivity to maintain substantial balances on high- interest credit cards, and the inclination to delay IRA contributions until the end of the tax year.</seg>
<seg id="513"> Insights from PsychologyA number of the empirical puzzles described in the previous section may be related to problems involving the exercise of self-control.</seg>
<seg id="514"> There is a sizable and rapidly growing literature in psychology and neuroscience concerning the properties, development, and limitations of self-control processes.</seg>
<seg id="515"> In this section we provide a brief introduction to this literature by summarizing some of the evidence most relevant for savings.</seg>
<seg id="516"> See Frederick et al. (2002) and Loewenstein et al. (2003b) for more comprehensive reviews of the literature.</seg>
<seg id="517"> Evidence of Dynamically Inconsistent Choice.</seg>
<seg id="518"> Saving reflects a decision to accept a lower level of consumption in one period in exchange for a higher level of consumption in another.</seg>
<seg id="519"> The standard model assumes that the individual evaluates a trade-off involving consumption at two future fixed points in time, say s and t (with s &lt; t), precisely the same way at every moment r.</seg>
<seg id="520"> Yet a large body of evidence finds that this evaluation in fact depends on the proximity of r to s.</seg>
<seg id="521"> In particular, when s is sufficiently proximate, people tend to favor consumption in the closer period s.</seg>
<seg id="522"> The direct evidence for this proposition is experimental.</seg>
<seg id="523"> The typical experiment involves two treatments.</seg>
<seg id="524"> In the first, subjects are offered a small prize in s days, or a large prize in t days.</seg>
<seg id="525"> In the second, they are offered the same small prize in s + d days, or the same large prize in t + d days, for some d &gt; 0 (where we interpret d as ˇ°delayˇ±).</seg>
<seg id="526"> When s = 0 (that is, the subject decides between an immediate reward and a delayed one in the first treatment),</seg>
<seg id="527"> a significantly larger fraction of subjects choose the small prize in the first treatment than in the second (see, for example, Ainslie and Haendel (1983), or, for a recent review of the evidence, Frederick et al. (2002)).</seg>
<seg id="528"> For relatively small values of s (on the order of seven days), this differential disappears (Coller et al. 2003).</seg>
<seg id="529"> The simple experiment described in the previous paragraph potentially suffers from a variety of confounds.</seg>
<seg id="530"> An immediate reward is usually distinguished by more than just its immediacy.</seg>
<seg id="531"> Arguably, it is less risky (that is, less likely to be forgotten by the subject or neglected by the experimenter), and it involves lower transaction costs.</seg>
<seg id="532"> However, the discrepancy between the two treatments persists even when reasonable steps are taken to eliminate these confounds.</seg>
<seg id="533"> Another concern is that, with state-contingent utility, evaluations of trade-offs may depend on ˇ°moods.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="534"> For an immediate reward, mood is known, while for a future reward it is not.</seg>
<seg id="535"> Under appropriate (if somewhat special) assumptions, this can account for the observed pattern.</seg>
<seg id="536"> Notably, similar results are obtained regardless of whether the reward consists of money or a consumption good.</seg>
<seg id="537"> This is surprising in that, for a wide range of standard and nonstandard behavioral theories, the best choice with monetary rewards involves the maximization of present discounted value (at least in the absence of binding liquidity constraints), which means it should not vary with d.</seg>
<seg id="538"> Precommitment.</seg>
<seg id="539"> People who understand that their behavior is dynamically inconsistent might want to exercise self-control through the use of precommitment devices.</seg>
<seg id="540"> There is evidence that this occurs in practice.</seg>
