<seg id="541"> For example, Ariely and Wertenbroch (2002) study a field experiment in which students are allowed to self- impose deadlines on assignments.</seg>
<seg id="542"> They find that many subjects choose these constraints.</seg>
<seg id="543"> Wertenbroch (1998) discusses suggestive evidence that people attempt to control their consumption of ˇ°temptingˇ± foods by purchasing small packages, even when the unit price is lower for larger packages.</seg>
<seg id="544"> The Role of Cues and Cognitive Processes in Self-Control.</seg>
<seg id="545"> In an influential study, Shiv and Fedorikhin (1999) show that cognitive load can affect self-control.</seg>
<seg id="546"> Subjects are given a number to memorize, and are asked to report it in another room.</seg>
<seg id="547"> In some cases the number has two digits, and in others it has seven.</seg>
<seg id="548"> Before reporting the number, they are asked to choose between two deserts, chocolate cake and fruit salad, which are physically present.</seg>
<seg id="549"> Individuals in the seven-digit treatment are roughly 50% more likely to choose the chocolate cake.</seg>
<seg id="550"> This suggests that self-control requires cognitive effort, and that this becomes more difficult when cognition is engaged in other tasks.</seg>
<seg id="551"> Shiv and Fedorikhin (1999) also consider a variation of this experiment in which the deserts are not physically present; instead, subjects are shown pictures.</seg>
<seg id="552"> The differential in choices between the two treatments disappears.</seg>
<seg id="553"> This suggests that cues can impair self-control.</seg>
<seg id="554"> To account for this effect, psychologists hypothesize that self-control is difficult when the individuals enter strong ˇ°visceral states,ˇ± and that the real items are more likely than pictures to trigger such states.</seg>
<seg id="555"> These findings are consistent with the work of Mischel and coauthors, which shows that self-control is affected by the deployment of attention and the presence of cues.</seg>
<seg id="556"> In a typical experiment, a subject (often a child) is placed in a room and is offered a choice between an inferior and a superior prize (one or two pieces of candy).</seg>
<seg id="557"> Subjects can obtain the inferior prize at any time by calling the experimenter, but must wait until he returns to obtain the superior prize.</seg>
<seg id="558"> In practice, the childˇŻs ability to wait depends crucially on whether the inferior prize is visible.</seg>
<seg id="559"> Merely covering the object significantly enhances self-control.</seg>
<seg id="560"> More generally, in MischelˇŻs experiments, the deployment of attention emerges as a key determinant of self-control.</seg>
<seg id="561"> Any stimulus that focuses attention on the ˇ°temptingˇ± features of the inferior prize increases the likelihood that the children will select it.</seg>
<seg id="562"> Children are significantly more likely to wait if they are advised to distract themselves by thinking about something else, or if they are provided with a toy, even when children in a control group show no interest in the toy.</seg>
<seg id="563"> Discussion.</seg>
<seg id="564"> The evidence suggests that exercising self-control is sometimes difficult.</seg>
<seg id="565"> The amount of effort devoted to imposing self-control appears to depend on a variety of environmental and contextual factors that are arguably unrelated to true preferences.</seg>
<seg id="566"> Accordingly, lapses in self-control are potentially associated with divergences between choices and true preferences (i.e., mistakes).</seg>
<seg id="567"> Moreover, one expects such lapses to arise probabilistically, as the result of chance encounters with cues and stimuli outside the individualˇŻs control.</seg>
<seg id="568"> The models of decision-making described in the next two sections attempt to capture these ideas in different ways.</seg>
<seg id="569"> They make different assumptions about the nature of the processes responsible for the mistakes associated with self-control lapses, and they employ different reduced-form representations of these processes.</seg>
<seg id="570"> Models of Saving with Quasi-Hyperbolic DiscountingA naive individual acts as if his future selves will be willing to follow through on his current plans.</seg>
<seg id="571"> In this case, one determines behavior by solving a sequence of optimization problems.</seg>
<seg id="572"> In each period, the naive self divides his resources between current consumption and saving, anticipating that he will use his wealth to finance his desired consumption path for the rest of his life.</seg>
<seg id="573"> He never actually follows this plan because, in the next period, he again attaches disproportionate weight to the present.</seg>
<seg id="574"> The naive individual does not understand his self-control problem, and makes no attempt to manage it.</seg>
<seg id="575"> A sophisticated decision maker perfectly anticipates his future actions.</seg>
<seg id="576"> In particular, he knows that, given the opportunity in any future period, he will consume a larger fraction of his resources than he would like.</seg>
<seg id="577"> Under this assumption, one determines behavior by solving for the subgame perfect equilibria of the dynamic game played between multiple selves.</seg>
<seg id="578"> Frequently, this setting gives rise to multiple equilibria, which means behavior is indeterminate unless one applies a selection criterion or refinement.</seg>
<seg id="579"> In contrast to naive decision makers, a sophisticated decision maker understands his self-control problem perfectly, and may attempt to manage anticipated lapses of self-control by limiting future choices.</seg>
<seg id="580"> Finally, a partially sophisticated decision maker understands that he will have a self-control problem in the future, but underestimates its magnitude.</seg>
<seg id="581"> OˇŻDonoghue and Rabin(1999b, 2001)parameterize the degree of sophistication to create a continuum between the two extreme cases of complete naivete and perfect sophistication. (See their papers for details, as well as for further discussion of the relationships between these assumptions.)</seg>
<seg id="582"> Models of Savings with Cue-Triggered MistakesIn the functional mode, the decision maker is sophisticated about his self-control problem:</seg>
<seg id="583"> he selects the optimal level of current consumption recognizing the probabilities and consequences of entering the faulty mode in the future, as well as the manner in which his actions affect the distribution of future decision modes.</seg>
<seg id="584"> In the faulty mode, he ˇ°binges.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="585"> This response is mechanical, reflecting simple impulses.</seg>
<seg id="586"> In the simplest versions of the model, the size of the binge is proportional either to intended consumption</seg>
<seg id="587"> (e.g., because he has chosen to shop in an expensive store),</seg>
<seg id="588"> or to remaining lifetime resources</seg>
<seg id="589"> (where the factor of proportionality is sufficiently large to ensure that the binge exceeds intended consumption).</seg>
<seg id="590"> In either case, the size of the binge is constrained by his available liquid resources.</seg>
<seg id="591"> The model has two straightforward implications.</seg>
<seg id="592"> First, precommitment technologies are valuable because they can reduce the size of a mistake when the faulty mode is triggered.</seg>
<seg id="593"> Second, the consumer can actively manage his self-control problem, for example, by choosing activities that reduce the likelihood of encountering cues that trigger binges.</seg>
<seg id="594"> If the size of the binge is related to intended consumption, he can also reduce the size of mistakes, when they occur, by planning to consume less (e.g., lapses are less costly if he shops at less expensive stores).</seg>
<seg id="595"> Other implications of the model are less immediate.</seg>
<seg id="596"> While an increase in the probability or size of a binge always reduces welfare, it can either increase or decrease the level of saving (depending on parameter values).</seg>
<seg id="597"> Additional saving becomes more attractive because it allows the individual to self-insure against future mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="598"> However, it also becomes less attractive because it leads to greater waste.</seg>
<seg id="599"> The net effect on savings depends on the balance of these two forces.</seg>
<seg id="600"> The model also predicts the existence of low-asset traps.</seg>
<seg id="601"> For an individual with few assets, the size of a binge is constrained by liquid resources.</seg>
<seg id="602"> If he saves an additional dollar and then experiences a binge, the entire dollar is wasted.</seg>
<seg id="603"> For an individual with substantial wealth, the size of a binge is ordinarily not constrained by liquid resources.</seg>
<seg id="604"> If he saves an additional dollar and then experiences a binge, only a fraction of the dollar is wasted.</seg>
<seg id="605"> Consequently, saving is relatively less attractive when wealth is low.</seg>
<seg id="606"> Gul and Pesendorfer emphasize that their approach is conceptually consistent with the method of revealed preference.</seg>
<seg id="607"> Supposedly, this eliminates the need for nonchoice data, and prevents the policy analyst from imposing his or her own judgments when evaluating welfare.</seg>
<seg id="608"> We disagree.</seg>
<seg id="609"> Practical implementation of the revealed-preference methodology requires the analyst to make assumptions about the data generating process (e.g., about functional forms, or similarities across individuals).</seg>
<seg id="610"> There are always untested assumptions, which the analyst selects based on other information, instinct, introspection, or fuzzy notions of ˇ°reasonableness.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="611"> We believe it is fair to say that these assumptions are not chosen exclusively on the basis of choice data.</seg>
<seg id="612"> Moreover, as all veterans of empirical policy debates are aware, the analystˇŻs judgments about untested assumptions translate directly into judgments about welfare.</seg>
<seg id="613"> There are also theoretical considerations, which we discuss at length in Bernheim and Rangel (2005b).</seg>
<seg id="614"> Assuming one restricts oneˇŻs attention to data on choices over allocations and constraint sets, both the standard theory and Gul and PesendorferˇŻs model are observationally equivalent to other models with different welfare implications.</seg>
<seg id="615"> Hence, the analystˇŻs judgment, expressed through axioms and assumptions, is unavoidable.</seg>
<seg id="616"> 2.3.8
<seg id="617"> Even so, the models described in this section have already provided valuable insights.</seg>
<seg id="618"> We conclude this section with a brief description of some important open questions.</seg>
<seg id="619"> The models described in this survey provide an explanation for some of the patterns described in Sections 2.3 and 2.4, including time inconsistency, self-reported mistakes, and some types of inefficient financial choices.</seg>
<seg id="620"> However, it is not clear that they can adequately account for other patterns, such as the discontinuity of consumption near retirement, the role of default options, the failure to plan, and the use of rough rules of thumb.</seg>
<seg id="621"> None provides a fully satisfactory explanation for the success of the Saving for Tomorrow Savings Plan designed by Thaler and Benartzi (2004), which relies on framing effects instead of changes in budget constraints.</seg>
<seg id="622"> Nor do they incorporate limitations on financial skills.</seg>
<seg id="623"> In focusing on self-control problems, they ignore issues associated with the complexity of financial decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="624"> Likewise, the theoretical work described in the previous sections has formalized only a few of the behavioral channels through which public policy could affect choices and welfare.</seg>
<seg id="625"> It is important to study other behavioral mechanisms with the same level of rigor.</seg>
<seg id="626"> Interesting possibilities include the following.</seg>
<seg id="627"> The Role of Financial Professionals.</seg>
<seg id="628"> Many people rely on advice from financial professionals.</seg>
<seg id="629"> One can therefore potentially learn about behavior by studying the methods used to generate this advice (see, for example, Bernheim et al. 2002).</seg>
<seg id="630"> For example, the most common retirement planning technique involves setting some fixed target for retirement (usually derived from an arbitrary earnings replacement rate) and computing the annual inflation-adjusted contribution to savings sufficient to achieve this target .</seg>
<seg id="631"> This generates a negative interest elasticity of saving because higher rates of return make it easier to accumulate the resources required to reach the target.</seg>
<seg id="632"> Social Influences.</seg>
<seg id="633"> When saving incentives are in place, boundedly rational individuals may be more likely to learn that others regard the benefits of saving as important.</seg>
<seg id="634"> For example, the availability of a 401(k) in an employment setting may stimulate conversations about contributions and investments, and thereby produce ˇ°peer groupˇ± influences involving both demonstration and competition.</seg>
<seg id="635"> The very existence of a pro-saving policy may indicate that ˇ°authoritiesˇ± perceive the need for greater thrift, or endorse a particular level of saving (e.g., the contribution limit).</seg>
<seg id="636"> Keeping Score.</seg>
<seg id="637"> By segmenting retirement saving from other forms of saving, certain kinds of tax-favored accounts may make it easier to monitor progress towards long-term objectives.</seg>
<seg id="638"> Information on total accumulated balances is usually provided automatically, or is readily available.</seg>
<seg id="639"> This gives individuals a convenient yardstick for measuring the adequacy or inadequacy of their thrift.</seg>
<seg id="640"> This may have the effect of making the costs of short-sightedness more explicit.</seg>
<seg id="641"> It could also help people to formulate goals and simple behavioral rules.</seg>
<seg id="642"> According to Thaler and Shefrin (1981): ˇ°simply keeping track seems to act as a tax on any behavior which the planner views as deviant.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="643"> Intrinsic Motivation.</seg>
<seg id="644"> Scitovsky (1976) has raised the possibility that some individuals may view saving as a virtuous activity in and of itself, without any explicit contemplation of future consequences.</seg>
<seg id="645"> Pro-saving policies may promote this outlook by reinforcing the notion that, as something worthy of encouragement, saving is intrinsically rewarding and immediately gratifying.</seg>
<seg id="646"> Intrinsic Gratification from Tax Avoidance.</seg>
<seg id="647"> We have noted that people are more likely to contribute to IRAs if they owe money at the end of the tax year.</seg>
<seg id="648"> This suggests that immediate tax avoidance is intrinsically gratifying.</seg>
<seg id="649"> If so, ˇ°front-loadedˇ± plans, wherein contributions are deductible and withdrawals are fully taxable, may be more effective in stimulating saving than ˇ°back-loadedˇ± plans, wherein contributions are not deductible and withdrawals of principal are not taxable.</seg>
<seg id="650"> Mental Accounting.</seg>
<seg id="651"> Thaler and Shefrin (1981) and Prelec and Loewenstein (1998) argue that people exercise self-control by separating resources into ˇ°mental accounts,ˇ± each associated with a different objective.</seg>
<seg id="652"> IRAs and 401(k)s may reinforce the discipline of mental accounting by earmarking certain resources for retirement, particularly in the presence of penalties for early withdrawal.</seg>
<seg id="653"> Education and Promotion.</seg>
<seg id="654"> The existence of tax-deferred savings accounts may stimulate promotional activities and advertisements by financial services firms.</seg>
<seg id="655"> PoliciesˇŻ that favor the development of employee-directed pensions (like the 401(k)) may encourage employers to provide retirement education.</seg>
<seg id="656"> While advertising and education appear to affect financial decisions, the precise mechanisms are poorly understood.</seg>
<seg id="657"> These types of considerations potentially have important implications for critical policy questions, such as the choice between broad-based policies for promoting saving (e.g., consumption taxation) and more targeted strategies (e.g., IRAs).</seg>
<seg id="658"> From a behavioral perspective, narrow measures can focus attention on a single issue</seg>
<seg id="659"> (such as the adequacy of saving for retirement),</seg>
<seg id="660"> expose individuals to information concerning the importance of saving, provide a natural context for the development and enforcement of private rules, and promote the growth of pro-saving institutions.</seg>
<seg id="661"> Contribution limits may actually stimulate saving if they validate specific targets, provide natural focal points for the formation of private rules, or make it easier to monitor compliance with these rules.</seg>
<seg id="662"> 2.4 ADDICTION</seg>
<seg id="663"> Although more than four million chemical compounds have been cataloged to date, only a few score are classified as addictive by clinical consensus.</seg>
<seg id="664"> These include alcohol, barbiturates, amphetamines, cocaine, caffeine and related methyl xanthine stimulants, cannabis, hallucinogens, nicotine, opioids, dissociative anesthetics, and volatile solvents.</seg>
<seg id="665"> There is also some debate as to whether other substances, such as fats and sugars, or activities, such as shopping, shoplifting, sex, television viewing, and internet use, are clinically addictive.</seg>
<seg id="666"> These substances and activities pose challenges both for public policy, and for standard economic analysis.</seg>
<seg id="667"> This section reviews the distinctive behavioral patterns associated with the consumption of addictive substances, describes the neuroscientific foundations of addiction, summarizes several competing economic models, and reviews their policy implications.</seg>
<seg id="668"> 2.4.1
<seg id="669"> Readily available statistics for the United States illustrate the scope of the phenomenon.</seg>
<seg id="670"> Estimates for 1999 place total expenditures on tobacco products, alcoholic beverages, cocaine, heroin, marijuana, and methamphetamines at more than $150 billion.</seg>
<seg id="671"> During a single month in 1999, more than 57 million individuals smoked at least one cigarette, more than 41 million engaged in binge drinking (involving five or more drinks on one occasion), and roughly 12 million used marijuana.</seg>
<seg id="672"> In 1998, slightly more than 5 million Americans qualified as ˇ°hard-coreˇ± chronic drug users.</seg>
<seg id="673"> Roughly 4.6 million persons in the workforce met the criterion for a diagnosis of drug dependence and 24.5 million had a history of clinical alcohol dependence.</seg>
<seg id="674"> In 1998, additional social costs resulting from health-care expenditures, loss of life, impaired productivity, motor vehicle accidents, crime, law enforcement, and welfare totaled $185 billion for alcohol and $143 billion for other addictive substances.</seg>
<seg id="675"> Smoking killed roughly 418,000 people in 1990, alcohol accounted for 107,400 deaths in 1992, and drug use resulted in 19,277 deaths during 1998.</seg>
<seg id="676"> Alcohol abuse contributed to 25-30% of violent crimes.</seg>
<seg id="677"> Even within jurisdictions, public policy toward various addictive substances is far from uniform, despite the commonalities suggested by their shared clinical classification.</seg>
<seg id="678"> Policies range from laissez faire to taxation, subsidization (e.g., of rehabilitation programs), regulated dispensation, criminalization, product liability, and public health campaigns.</seg>
<seg id="679"> Each alternative policy approach has passionate advocates and detractors.</seg>
<seg id="680"> Despite sharp disagreements about the ideal treatment of addictive substances, there is reasonably widespread agreement that most existing policies work poorly.</seg>
<seg id="681"> The United StatesˇŻs ˇ°War on Drugsˇ± is, for example, often labeled a ˇ°failed policy.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="682"> Use of banned substances remains widespread, and the resulting health costs are high.</seg>
<seg id="683"> Prohibitions on certain substances, like marijuana, lack credibility among younger Americans, who fail to see why alcohol is singled out as socially acceptable.</seg>
<seg id="684"> While the incidence of criminal activity among drug addicts is relatively high, it is important to acknowledge that drug-related crime is, to a significant extent, a consequence of current policy, rather than a justification for it.</seg>
<seg id="685"> Criminalization promotes black markets, fosters organized crime, enriches criminals, and contributes to a culture of violence.</seg>
<seg id="686"> As a result, more than 625,000 citizens were incarcerated for drug-related offenses during 1999.</seg>
<seg id="687"> These people were disproportionately poor, black, and among societyˇŻs most economically vulnerable members.</seg>
<seg id="688"> While existing policies have serious drawbacks, alternatives are also potentially problematic.</seg>
<seg id="689"> For example, the high incidence of alcohol abuse and smoking, along with the attendant social costs, at a minimum raise serious concerns about the potential consequences of across-the-board legalization.</seg>
<seg id="690"> The apparent intractability of social problems related to addiction underscores the importance of creatively and openly rethinking policy strategies.</seg>
<seg id="691"> 2.4.2
<seg id="692"> It was widely believed that people start using drugs to achieve a pleasurable ˇ°high,ˇ± and continue using them despite a deterioration of the high (a phenomenon known as ˇ°toleranceˇ±) to avoid unpleasant feelings associated with cravings and withdrawal.</seg>
<seg id="693"> These hedonic properties are easily incorporated into standard models of consumer choice.</seg>
<seg id="694"> Early work in this tradition includes papers by Stigler and Becker (1977), Iannacone (1986), and Becker and Murphy (1988).</seg>
<seg id="695"> The last of these is widely viewed as the definitive articulation of the neoclassical perspective on addictive behavior, also known as the theory of ˇ°rational addiction.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="696"> In Becker and MurphyˇŻs model, the individualˇŻs well-being depends on consumption of an addictive good, consumption of a nonaddictive good, and a state variable summarizing past consumption of the addictive good.</seg>
<seg id="697"> This addictive state rises with use of the substance and falls with abstinence.</seg>
<seg id="698"> To model tolerance, one assumes that utility declines as the addictive state rises.</seg>
<seg id="699"> To model the effects of cravings and the pain of withdrawal on the inclination to use a substance, one assumes that the marginal utility of the addictive good rises with the addictive state.</seg>
<seg id="700"> This assumption is necessary (but not sufficient) to generate a property known as ˇ°adjacent complementarity,ˇ± which means that greater current consumption leads to greater consumption in the future.</seg>
<seg id="701"> According to Becker and Murphy, this is the distinguishing feature of an addictive substance.</seg>
<seg id="702"> Becker and MurphyˇŻs model generates a variety of interesting positive results regarding the use of addictive substances.</seg>
<seg id="703"> For example, with appropriate parameterizations, the model generates behavior that is consistent with aspects of bingeing cycles and abrupt withdrawals.</seg>
<seg id="704"> It distinguishes between conditions that lead to certain behaviors that they associate with addiction, and conditions that do not.</seg>
<seg id="705"> It also predicts that an anticipated future increase in the price of an addictive substance leads to an immediate decrease in drug use (see Gruber and Koszegi (2001) and Chaloupka and Warner (2001) for a review of supporting evidence).</seg>
<seg id="706"> From a normative perspective, the theory of rational addiction makes no distinction between addictive substances and other goods.</seg>
<seg id="707"> Accordingly, the standard welfare theorems apply.</seg>
<seg id="708"> It follows that government intervention is justified only if markets for addictive substances function imperfectly.</seg>
<seg id="709"> There are two main concerns in this regard.</seg>
<seg id="710"> First, if people are either poorly informed or misinformed about the effects of addictive substances, they may make poor decisions.</seg>
<seg id="711"> As long as the government can provide relevant information more effectively and efficiently than private markets, educational policies (e.g., public health campaigns) are potentially beneficial.</seg>
<seg id="712"> Second, the consumption of addictive substances may generate externalities.</seg>
<seg id="713"> For example, driving under the influence leads to accidents, addicts commit crimes to support their habits, and addiction can be devastating to family members.</seg>
<seg id="714"> The standard policy prescription for externalities involves a Pigouvian tax per unit of the substance equal to the marginal external damage that it imposes on others.</seg>
<seg id="715"> Since the publication of Becker and MurphyˇŻs paper, others have extended the theory of rational addiction in a variety of ways, mainly to account for other observed features of addictive behavior.</seg>
<seg id="716"> For example, in Orphanides and Zervos (1995,1998), different people have different susceptibilities to addiction, which they discover through experimentation.</seg>
<seg id="717"> The paper shows that a highly susceptible individual can control his addictive tendencies if he discovers his susceptibility quickly, but not if he discovers it slowly.</seg>
<seg id="718"> The authors briefly discuss a few policy implications.</seg>
<seg id="719"> Clearly, consumers benefit from accurate information concerning the distribution of susceptibilities.</seg>
<seg id="720"> Moreover, since people are uncertain about their addictive susceptibilities, imperfections in private markets for rehabilitation insurance can leave them with residual risk, which potentially creates a role for government as a provider of social insurance.</seg>
<seg id="721"> Other contributions include (but are not limited to) Dockner and Feichtinger (1993), who show how the theory of rational addiction can account for cyclical consumption patterns,</seg>
<seg id="722"> and Orphanides and Zervos (1998), who introduce impulsiveness by allowing the consumerˇŻs discount rate to depend (in a time- consistent way) on use.</seg>
<seg id="723"> 2.4.3
<seg id="724"> A number of studies have shown that aggregate drug use responds both to prices and to information about the effects of addictive substances.</seg>
<seg id="725"> For example, an aggressive U.S. public health campaign is widely credited with reductions in smoking rates.</seg>
<seg id="726"> There is also evidence that users engage in sophisticated forward- looking deliberation, reducing current consumption in response to anticipated price increases.</seg>
<seg id="727"> What, then, makes addiction a distinctive phenomenon?</seg>
<seg id="728"> Bernheim and Rangel (2004) list five important behavioral patterns distilled from the extensive body of research on addiction in neuroscience, psychology, and clinical practice.</seg>
<seg id="729"> Unsuccessful Attempts to Quit.</seg>
<seg id="730"> Addicts often express a desire to stop using a substance permanently and unconditionally but are unable to follow through.</seg>
<seg id="731"> Short-term abstention is common, while long-term recidivism rates are high.</seg>
<seg id="732"> For example, during 2000, 70% of current smokers expressed a desire to quit completely and 41% stopped smoking for at least one day in an attempt to quit, but only 4.7% successfully abstained for more than three months</seg>
<seg id="733"> This pattern is particularly striking because regular users experience painful withdrawal symptoms when they first attempt to quit, and these symptoms decline over time with successful abstention.</seg>
<seg id="734"> Thus, recidivism often occurs after users have borne the most significant costs of quitting, sometimes following years of determined abstention.</seg>
<seg id="735"> Cue-Triggered Recidivism.</seg>
<seg id="736"> Recidivism rates are especially high when addicts are exposed to cues related to past drug consumption.</seg>
<seg id="737"> Long-term usage is considerably lower among those who experience significant changes of environment</seg>
<seg id="738"> Treatment programs often advise recovering addicts to move to new locations and to avoid the places where previous consumption took place.</seg>
<seg id="739"> Stress and ˇ°primingˇ± (exposure to a small taste of the substance) have also been shown to trigger recidivism.</seg>
<seg id="740"> Self-Described Mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="741"> Addicts often describe past use as a mistake in a very strong sense: they think that they would have been better off in the past as well as the present had they acted differently.</seg>
<seg id="742"> They recognize that they are likely to make similar errors in the future, and that this will undermine their desire to abstain.</seg>
<seg id="743"> When they succumb to cravings, they sometimes characterize choices as mistakes even while in the act of consumption.</seg>
<seg id="744"> It is instructive that the twelve-step program of Alcoholics Anonymous begins: ˇ°We admit we are powerless over alcoholˇŞthat our lives have become unmanageable.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="745"> As an example, Goldstein (2001, p. 249) describes an addict who had been. . . suddenly overwhelmed by an irresistible craving, and he had rushed out of his house to find some heroin...</seg>
<seg id="746"> it was as though he were driven by some external force he was powerless to resist, even though he knew while it was happening that it was a disastrous course of action for him [our italics].</seg>
<seg id="747"> Self-Control through Precommitment.</seg>
<seg id="748"> Recovering users often manage their tendency to make mistakes by voluntarily removing or degrading future options.</seg>
<seg id="749"> They voluntarily admit themselves into ˇ°lock-upˇ± rehabilitation facilities, often not in order to avoid cravings but precisely because they expect to experience cravings and wish to control their actions.</seg>
<seg id="750"> They also consume medications that either generate unpleasant side effects, or reduce pleasurable sensations, if the substance is consumed subsequently.</seg>
<seg id="751"> Severe addicts sometimes enlist others to assist with physical confinement to assure abstinence through the withdrawal process.</seg>
<seg id="752"> Self-Control through Behavioral and Cognitive Therapy.</seg>
<seg id="753"> Recovering addicts attempt to minimize the probability of relapse through behavioral and cognitive therapies.</seg>
<seg id="754"> Successful behavioral therapies teach cue-avoidance, often by encouraging the adoption of new lifestyles and the development of new interests.</seg>
<seg id="755"> Successful cognitive therapies teach cue-management, which entails refocusing attention on alternative consequences and objectives, often with the assistance of a mentor or trusted friend or through a meditative activity such as prayer.</seg>
<seg id="756"> Notably, these therapeutic strategies affect addictˇŻs choices without providing new information.</seg>
<seg id="757"> The clinical definition of addiction makes reference to some of these patterns.</seg>
<seg id="758"> Substance addiction is said to occur when, after significant exposure, users find themselves engaging in compulsive, repeated, and unwanted use despite clearly harmful consequences, and often despite a strong desire to quit unconditionally.</seg>
<seg id="759"> From the perspective of traditional economic analysis, each of the patterns listed above is at least somewhat puzzling.</seg>
<seg id="760"> The rational consumers of economic textbooks have no trouble following through on plans, and therefore should manifest neither of the first two patterns.</seg>
<seg id="761"> Contrary to the third pattern, rational consumers always choose what they want, so, armed with good information, they cannot make systematic mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="762"> The notion that someone might be powerless over a consumption good is an anathema to a neoclassical economist.</seg>
<seg id="763"> The standard theory of consumer behavior embraces the principle that expanding or improving the set of available alternatives necessarily makes an individual better off, so precommitments can only be counterproductive, contrary to the fourth pattern.</seg>
<seg id="764"> Finally, since in the standard model individuals never make mistakes, there is no role for expenditures on self-control.</seg>
<seg id="765"> Creative extensions of the basic model may provide rationalizations for some of these patterns without overturning the basic paradigm.</seg>
<seg id="766"> For example, Laibson (2001) has proposed a variant of the Becker-Murphy framework in which preferences become state-contingent with experience, and which can in principle account for cue management and avoidance.</seg>
<seg id="767"> Even so, the five patterns described above collectively pose a serious challenge to neoclassical perspective, and provide motivation for economists to think ˇ°outside the box.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="768"> 2.4.4
<seg id="769"> It now appears that addiction does not result primarily from the pleasurable effects of substances on the hedonic system.</seg>
<seg id="770"> Instead, the new view of addiction holds that certain substances interfere with the proper operation of a neural system that plays an important role in learning.</seg>
<seg id="771"> This is not to say that pleasure is unimportant.</seg>
<seg id="772"> However, the key feature of addiction appears to be the fact that addictive substances cause a specific learning process to malfunction.</seg>
<seg id="773"> Figure 2.1 shows, at a high level of abstraction, how the brain normally makes decisions about standard consumption goods.</seg>
<seg id="774"> Our senses provide us with information about environmental conditions.</seg>
<seg id="775"> We process this information, along with information about our internal statesŇ»things like hunger, fatigue, and so forthŇ»and this results in a decision.</seg>
<seg id="776"> The decision is followed by experience, including rewards.</seg>
<seg id="777"> The experienced relationship between environmental conditions, decisions, and rewards induces learning, which normally improves the quality of future decisions.</seg>
<seg id="778"> On left-hand side of this diagram, we have broken out an important component of the decision-making system, which we have labeled the ˇ°basic forecasting mechanism.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="779"> This is a hard-wired system for measuring correlations between conditions, decisions, and short-term rewards.</seg>
<seg id="780"> It does not involve higher reasoning; in fact, it is present in lower life forms as well as humans.</seg>
<seg id="781"> For nonaddictive substances, the basic forecasting mechanism learns with experience to construct an accurate forecast of the subsequent hedonic experiences.</seg>
<seg id="782"> It is worth emphasizing that the brain appears to have a variety of mechanisms for forecasting the possible consequences of decisions.</seg>
<seg id="783"> Some involve higher cognition (represented on the right-hand side of the diagram);</seg>
<seg id="784"> for example, we sometimes develop causal models of the world and reason out the implications of our actions.</seg>
<seg id="785"> Some, like the basic forecasting mechanism, are more mechanical.</seg>
<seg id="786"> Both types of forecasting mechanisms play a role in decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="787"> Sometimes we act based on the ˇ°gut reactionsˇ± generated by the basic forecasting mechanism.</seg>
<seg id="788"> Sometimes higher cognition overrides a gut reaction.</seg>
<seg id="789"> This is how the brain works.</seg>
<seg id="790"> Each process has its advantages and disadvantages.</seg>
<seg id="791"> The basic forecasting mechanism is very fast, but it is inflexible and unsophisticated.</seg>
<seg id="792"> Higher cognition is flexible and sophisticated, but comparatively slow.</seg>
<seg id="793"> When we have to make decisions quickly, we rely on our gut reactions.</seg>
<seg id="794"> When there is no time pressure, we take the time to think things through.</seg>
<seg id="795"> A balance between these systems emerged through evolution as natureˇŻs compromise.</seg>
<seg id="796"> Consequently, the mere fact that we rely in some instances on impulses and gut reactions rather than reasoned deliberation does not mean that our choices are irrational or dysfunctional.</seg>
<seg id="797"> For nonaddictive substances, these mechanisms, operating in parallel, typically produce reasonable decisions.</seg>
<seg id="798"> Figure 2.2 shows how addictive substances interfere with the proper operation of these decision-making processes.</seg>
<seg id="799"> In a nutshell, the problem with the addictive substances is that they act directly on the learning process underlying the basic forecasting mechanism, short circuiting the neurological process by which this mechanism discovers correlations between environmental conditions, decisions, and rewards.</seg>
<seg id="800"> As a result, the mechanism massively overstates the correlation between drug use and actual experienced pleasure.</seg>
<seg id="801"> Loosely speaking, drugs fool a subconscious, hard-wired brain process into anticipating an exaggerated level of pleasure.</seg>
<seg id="802"> An addict can try to compensate for this effect by exercising cognitive control, but he cannot consciously correct the malfunction of the basic forecasting mechanism.</seg>
<seg id="803"> More specifically, the available neurological evidence supports four specific hypotheses that justify the new view of addictive substances.</seg>
<seg id="804"> First, the mesolimbic dopamine system (MDS) serves, at least in part, as a basic forecasting mechanism which, with experience, learns to produce a response to situations and opportunities, the magnitude of which constitutes a forecast of near term pleasure</seg>
<seg id="805"> Second, MDS forecasting does not appear to directly produce or reflect the experience of pleasure.</seg>
<seg id="806"> Indeed, the human brain appears to contain a separate hedonic system that is responsible for producing sensations of ˇ°well-beingˇ±</seg>
<seg id="807"> Third, MDS-generated forecasts directly influence choices</seg>
<seg id="808"> In an organism with a sufficiently developed frontal cortex, higher cognitive mechanisms can override impulses resulting from MDS forecasts,</seg>
<seg id="809"> for example, by identifying alternative courses of action or projecting the future consequences of choices.</seg>
<seg id="810"> The outcome depends on the intensity of the MDS forecast and on the ability of the frontal cortex to engage the necessary cognitive operations.</seg>
<seg id="811"> A strong MDS forecast can impair this ability by influencing attention to stimuli, cognitive focus, and memory.</seg>
<seg id="812"> Thus, a more attractive MDS-generated forecast makes cognitive override less likely.</seg>
<seg id="813"> We emphasize that the basic forecasting mechanism and higher cognitive processes are not two different sets of ˇ°preferencesˇ± or ˇ°selvesˇ± competing for control of decisions.</seg>
<seg id="814"> Hedonic experiences are generated separately, and an individual maximizes the quality of these experiences by appropriately deploying both forecasting processes to anticipate outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="815"> Fourth, addictive substances act directly on the basic forecasting mechanism, disrupting its ability to construct accurate hedonic forecasts and exaggerating the anticipated hedonic benefits of consumption.</seg>
<seg id="816"> Although addictive substances differ considerably in their chemical and psychological properties, there is a large and growing consensus in neuroscience that they share an ability to activate the firing of dopamine into the nucleus accumbens with much greater intensity and persistence than other substances.</seg>
<seg id="817"> They do this either by activating the MDS directly, or by activating other networks that have a similar effect</seg>
<seg id="818"> For nonaddictive substances, the MDS learns to assign a hedonic forecast that bears some normal relation to the subsequent hedonic experience.</seg>
<seg id="819"> For addictive substances, consumption activates dopamine firing directly, so the MDS learns to assign a hedonic forecast that is out of proportion to the subsequent hedonic experience.</seg>
<seg id="820"> This not only creates a strong (and misleading) impulse to seek and use the substance, but also undermines the potential for cognitive override.</seg>
<seg id="821"> Cognitive override still occurs, but in a limited range of circumstances.</seg>
<seg id="822"> The preceding discussion implies that, in some circumstances, drug use can literally be a mistake, in the sense that the brain is fooled into making a choice.</seg>
<seg id="823"> It does not, however, imply that drug use is always a mistake.</seg>
<seg id="824"> Even if the integrity of the basic forecasting mechanism is compromised, higher cognition can still either agree with it or override it.</seg>
<seg id="825"> In different people, brain chemistry appears to strike different balances between these mechanisms.</seg>
<seg id="826"> This may explain why some people become addicts, while others use repeatedly without becoming addicted.</seg>
<seg id="827"> Use can be rational in some instances and irrational in others.</seg>
<seg id="828"> It is important to bear this point in mind when evaluating public policy alternatives.</seg>
<seg id="829"> In emphasizing the effects of addictive substances on decision processes, we do not mean to discount the significance of their hedonic effects.</seg>
<seg id="830"> The typical user is initially drawn to an addictive substance because it produces a hedonic ˇ°high.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="831"> Over time, regular use leads to hedonic and physical tolerance.</seg>
<seg id="832"> That is, the drug loses its ability to produce a high unless the user abstains for a while, and any attempt to discontinue the drug may have unpleasant side effects (withdrawal).</seg>
<seg id="833"> Cue-conditioned ˇ°cravingsˇ± may have hedonic implications as well as nonhedonic causes.</seg>
<seg id="834"> All of these effects are clearly important.</seg>
<seg id="835"> However, there is an emerging consensus in neuroscience and psychology that decision-process effects, rather than hedonic effects, provide the key to understanding addictive behavior</seg>
<seg id="836"> 2.4.5
<seg id="837"> The theory is based on the following three main premises:</seg>
<seg id="838"> (i)
<seg id="839"> (ii)
<seg id="840"> (iii)
<seg id="841"> The first two premises are justified by the body of research described in Section 2.3.4, which shows that, after repeated exposure to an addictive substance, the brain tends to make skewed hedonic forecasts upon encountering environmental cues that are associated with past substance use.</seg>
<seg id="842"> The third premise is justified by behavioral evidence indicating that users are often surprisingly sophisticated and forward-looking.</seg>
<seg id="843"> For example, they reduce current consumption in response to expected future price increases.</seg>
<seg id="844"> Some also enter detox not because they intend to remain sober, but rather because they want to increase the intensity of the next high.A Summary of the Model.</seg>
<seg id="845"> The formal model in Bernheim and Rangel (2004) envisions an individual who makes a sequence of decisions regarding lifestyle, the use of an addictive substance, and the consumption of nonaddictive substances.</seg>
<seg id="846"> It assumes that, at any point in time, the individual operates in one of two modes:</seg>
<seg id="847"> a ˇ°coldˇ± mode, in which properly functioning decision-making processes lead to the selection of his most preferred alternative, and a dysfunctional ˇ°hotˇ± mode, in which decisions and preferences may diverge</seg>
<seg id="848"> (because he responds to distorted MDS-generated forecasts).</seg>
<seg id="849"> The hot mode is transient, but always results in use of the substance.</seg>
<seg id="850"> The likelihood of entering the hot mode at any moment depends on the individualˇŻs history of substance use, his chosen lifestyle</seg>
<seg id="851"> (e.g., partying exposes the individual to more intense substance-related cues)</seg>
<seg id="852"> , and random events</seg>
<seg id="853"> (e.g., the frequency and intensity of recently encountered environmental cues to which he has been sensitized through prior use).</seg>
<seg id="854"> The history of use is summarized through the notion of an addictive state.</seg>
<seg id="855"> Use moves the individual to a higher addictive state, and abstention moves him to a lower addictive state.</seg>
<seg id="856"> An increase in the addictive state raises the likelihood of entering the hot mode at any moment</seg>
<seg id="857"> (e.g., because it implies increased sensitivity to randomly occurring environmental cues).</seg>
<seg id="858"> Higher addictive states are also associated with lower baseline well-being (e.g., due to deteriorating health)</seg>
<seg id="859"> , lower financial resources (due to decreased productivity, absenteeism, and out-of-pocket medical expenses), and possibly a greater ˇ°boostˇ± from consuming the addictive substance.</seg>
<seg id="860"> By varying assumptions about the properties of the substance in question, the model can replicate a wide range of observed behaviors.</seg>
<seg id="861"> In particular, it can account for each of the patterns discussed in Section 2.4.3 (see Bernheim and Rangel (2004) for details and Bernheim and Rangel (forthcoming) for simulations of the model).</seg>
<seg id="862"> Policy Implications.</seg>
<seg id="863"> This theory admits two classes of rationales for government intervention.</seg>
<seg id="864"> First, as in the theory of rational addiction, intervention may be justified to correct market failures involving addictive substances</seg>
<seg id="865"> (i.e., the government can address externalities, misinformation, and ignorance).</seg>
<seg id="866"> Second, policies may also affect the frequency and consequences of mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="867"> This consideration gives rise to a number of nonstandard policy implications.</seg>
<seg id="868"> Limitations of Informational Policy.</seg>
<seg id="869"> In practice, public education campaigns (such as the U.S. anti-smoking and anti-drug initiatives) have achieved mixed results.</seg>
<seg id="870"> The process-malfunction theory of addiction highlights a fundamental limitation of informational policy:</seg>
<seg id="871"> contrary to standard theory, one cannot assume that even a highly knowledgeable addict always makes informed choices.</seg>
<seg id="872"> Information about the consequences of substance abuse may affect initial experimentation with drugs, but cannot alter the neurological mechanisms through which addictive substances subvert deliberative decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="873"> Counterproductive Disincentives.</seg>
<seg id="874"> Policies such as ˇ°sin taxesˇ± and criminalization strive to discourage use by making substances costly.</seg>
<seg id="875"> As we have noted, this is potentially justifiable on the grounds that use generates negative externalities.</seg>
<seg id="876"> In the context of the theory described in this section, even higher taxes (whether implicit or explicit) might be justified if they reduce excessive use in ˇ°hotˇ± decision states.</seg>
<seg id="877"> Unfortunately, it is likely that compulsive use of addictive substances is much less sensitive to costs and consequences than is deliberative use.</seg>
<seg id="878"> Consequently, imposing costs in excess of external diseconomies is likely to distort cold-state choices detrimentally, without significantly reducing problematic hot-state usage.</seg>
<seg id="879"> Indeed, policies that impose high costs on use may thwart social insurance objectives by exacerbating the consequences of uninsurable risks associated with the use of addictive substances.</seg>
<seg id="880"> Accordingly, the optimal rate of taxation for addictive substances may be significantly lower than that implied by externalities (see Bernheim and Rangel (forthcoming) for simulation results).</seg>
<seg id="881"> Supply Disruption.</seg>
<seg id="882"> Standard reasoning suggests that taxation is preferable to criminalization.</seg>
<seg id="883"> Both impose costs, but taxes generate revenues, while criminalization dissipates social resources.</seg>
<seg id="884"> In the context of the theory discussed in this section, criminalization offers an offsetting benefit: it disrupts supply, making it particularly difficult for users to obtain a banned substance on short notice.</seg>
<seg id="885"> The effect on use is likely to be larger in hot states, when people act impulsively, than in cold states, when people plan deliberatively.</seg>
<seg id="886"> This is exactly what one would hope to achieve, and precisely opposite the effect of a tax.</seg>
<seg id="887"> To put it somewhat differently, criminalization may help some addicts impose self-control, without (as a practical matter) preventing deliberate use.</seg>
<seg id="888"> There is, however, an associated disadvantage:</seg>
<seg id="889"> while in the hot state, addicts may engage in costly and potentially dangerous search activity.</seg>
<seg id="890"> Harm Reduction.</seg>
<seg id="891"> If addiction results in significant part from randomly occurring mistakes, various interventions can serve social insurance objectives by ameliorating some of its worst consequences.</seg>
<seg id="892"> For instance, subsidization of rehabilitation centers and treatment programs (particularly for the indigent) can moderate the financial impact of addiction and promote recovery.</seg>
<seg id="893"> Likewise, the free distribution of clean needles can moderate the incidence of diseases among heroin addicts.</seg>
<seg id="894"> In some cases, it may even be beneficial to make substances available to severe addicts at low cost.</seg>
<seg id="895"> As is usually the case, one must trade off the benefits from insurance against incentive effects: by moderating consequences, harm-reducing policies could in principle encourage casual use and experimentation.</seg>
<seg id="896"> Policies Affecting Cues.</seg>
<seg id="897"> Since environmental cues frequently trigger addictive behaviors, public policy can also influence use by changing the cues that people normally encounter.</seg>
<seg id="898"> One approach involves the elimination of problematic cues.</seg>
<seg id="899"> For example, advertising and marketing restrictions of the type imposed on sellers of tobacco and alcohol suppress one possible artificial trigger for compulsive use.</seg>
<seg id="900"> Since one personˇŻs decision to smoke may trigger anotherˇŻs, confining use to designated areas may reduce unintended use.</seg>
<seg id="901"> A second approach involves the creation of counter-cues.</seg>
<seg id="902"> For example, Brazil and Canada require every pack of cigarettes to display a prominent, viscerally charged image depicting some deleterious consequences of smoking, such as lung disease and neonatal morbidity.</seg>
<seg id="903"> In principle, a sufficiently strong counter-cue could trigger thought processes that induce users to resist cravings, even though the same information is ineffective when offered in a less provocative format.</seg>
<seg id="904"> Policies that eliminate problematic cues or promote counter-cues are potentially beneficial because they combat compulsive use, while imposing minimal inconvenience and restrictions on deliberate rational users.</seg>
<seg id="905"> Facilitation of Self-Control.</seg>
<seg id="906"> The process-malfunction theory of addiction places a high value on policies that provide better opportunities for self-regulation without making particular choices compulsory.</seg>
<seg id="907"> This could potentially help those who are vulnerable to compulsive use, without encroaching on the freedoms of those who would deliberately choose to use.</seg>
<seg id="908"> Laws that limit the sale of a substance to particular times, places, and circumstances frequently provide limited opportunities along these lines</seg>
<seg id="909"> Well-designed policies could in principle accomplish this objective more effectively.</seg>
<seg id="910"> For example, a number of U.S. states have enacted laws allowing problem gamblers to voluntarily ban themselves from casinos.</seg>
<seg id="911"> Alternatively, if a substance is available only by prescription, and if prescription orders are filled on a ˇ°next dayˇ± basis, then deliberate forward- looking planning becomes a prerequisite for availability.</seg>
<seg id="912"> Recovering heroin addicts could self-regulate problematic compulsive use by carefully choosing when, and when not, to file requests for refills.</seg>
<seg id="913"> 2.4.6
<seg id="914"> In contrast to the theory of rational addiction, the consumer acts as ifhe attaches disproportionate importance (1/p)to current well-being when making decisions about current consumption.</seg>
<seg id="915"> Gruber and Koszegi use this model to compute optimal cigarette taxes.</seg>
<seg id="916"> When evaluating individual welfare, they assume that true preferences correspond to standard exponential discounting.</seg>
<seg id="917"> Implicitly, they adopt the interpretation of quasi- hyperbolic discounting discussed in Section 2.4.4: true preferences are standard, but the decision-making process leads individuals to make present-biased mistakes, which the (p, 5)-model captures in reduced form.</seg>
<seg id="918"> In principle, one could defend this interpretation with reference to the evidence described in Section 2.4.</seg>
<seg id="919"> Unfortunately, the model does not fit these facts in two important respects.</seg>
<seg id="920"> First, the evidence indicates that mistakes are domain-specific.</seg>
<seg id="921"> In contrast, the proclivity to make present-biased mistakes in the (p, 5)-model cuts across all domains.</seg>
<seg id="922"> Second, the evidence indicates that mistakes are triggered by intermittent environmental cues.</seg>
<seg id="923"> In contrast, the decision maker always suffers from present-bias in Gruber and KoszegiˇŻs framework.</seg>
<seg id="924"> One could, of course, formulate a variant of Gruber and KoszegiˇŻs model with narrow-domain, cue-triggered present-bias.</seg>
<seg id="925"> The resulting model would be a close cousin of the process-malfunction theory of addiction discussed in the previous section.</seg>
<seg id="926"> However, one significant difference would remain.</seg>
<seg id="927"> In the (p, 5)-framework with the proposed modifications, the decision maker would remain sophisticated, forward-looking, and responsive to economic incentives even when suffering from present-bias.</seg>
<seg id="928"> In contrast, the process-malfunction theory holds that mistakes result from simple impulses generated by a hard-wired process that encompasses a limited range of consequences.</seg>
<seg id="929"> In some respects, the policy implications of this approach are similar to those discussed in the preceding section.</seg>
<seg id="930"> Informational policy alone is limited because it cannot address the causes of present-bias.</seg>
<seg id="931"> Supply disruption is potentially beneficial, as are policies that facilitate the exercise of self-control.</seg>
<seg id="932"> In other respects, the policy implications described by Gruber and Koszegi differ sharply from those discussed in the preceding section.</seg>
<seg id="933"> Most notably, the (p, 8)- framework provides a rationale for ˇ°sin taxesˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="934"> When making decisions, the consumer always puts too little weight on future consequences, including those resulting from adjacent complementarities.</seg>
<seg id="935"> The government can address these ˇ°internalitiesˇ± (externalities imposed on future selves) by imposing a Pigouvian tax on current consumption.</seg>
<seg id="936"> Accordingly, the rate of taxation for addictive substances should be higher than that justified by marginal externalities.</seg>
<seg id="937"> For example, according to Gruber and KoszegiˇŻs simulations, the optimal tax on each pack of cigarettes is at least a dollar higher than would be justified by externalities alone.</seg>
<seg id="938"> Why do the models of Bernheim and Rangel and Gruber and Koszegi lead to sharply differing conclusions concerning substance taxation?</seg>
<seg id="939"> The answer lies in two of the issues discussed above.</seg>
<seg id="940"> First, Gruber and Koszegi assume that consumers always make present-biased mistakes, while Bernheim and Rangel assume that mistakes occur only in the presence of intermittent environmental cues.</seg>
<seg id="941"> Accordingly, social insurance can enhance the consumerˇŻs well-being in Bernheim and Rangel, but not in Gruber and Koszegi.</seg>
<seg id="942"> In other words, Gruber and KoszegiˇŻs assumptions eliminate the factor that argues against high tax burdens in Bernheim and RangelˇŻs model.</seg>
<seg id="943"> Second, Gruber and Koszegi assume that the decision maker remains sophisticated, forward-looking, and responsive to economic incentives even while committing errors, whereas Bernheim and Rangel assume that errors result from a mechanical and largely inflexible process.</seg>
<seg id="944"> Accordingly, taxation directly reduces decision errors in Gruber and Koszegi, but has a limited effect along these lines in Bernheim and Rangel.</seg>
<seg id="945"> 2.4.7
<seg id="946"> Following their earlier work on temptation, they assume that the consumerˇŻs preferences are defined both over consumption bundles and over the sets from which these bundles are chosen.</seg>
<seg id="947"> In each period of life, the consumer divides his resources between two goods, one of which is addictive, with the object of maximizing an intertemporal utility function.</seg>
<seg id="948"> This function is standard in all respects, except that it is modified to include, for each period, a penalty representing net temptation from the most tempting unchosen alternative in the choice set.</seg>
<seg id="949"> Even though the consumer applies the same lifetime preferences at every moment in time and makes no mistakes, precommitments are still potentially valuable because they reduce the unpleasant feelings associated with the temptation to consume addictive substances.</seg>
<seg id="950"> Gul and PesendorferˇŻs model invokes a number of important assumptions.</seg>
<seg id="951"> The following three deserve emphasis.</seg>
<seg id="952"> First, the level of temptation associated with an alternative depends only on the level of the addictive good, and not at all on the level of the nonaddictive good.</seg>
<seg id="953"> Second, recent consumption of the addictive substance increases the weight given to temptation, but does not enter the ˇ°standardˇ± portion of the utility function.</seg>
<seg id="954"> According to this assumption, as long as an individual is forced to abstain from the addictive substance, his experienced well-being is unrelated to his past consumption.</seg>
<seg id="955"> As a result, this assumption is in sharp conflict with evidence on cravings and withdrawal.</seg>
<seg id="956"> Third, the consumer only experiences temptation with respect to current choices.</seg>
<seg id="957"> For example, when deciding whether to enter rehabilitation for the next period, he is not tempted by the prospect of future drug use.</seg>
<seg id="958"> In the Gul-Pesendorfer model, private markets tend to work poorly relative to the first-best.</seg>
<seg id="959"> Markets provide people with choices, and choices create costly temptation.</seg>
<seg id="960"> Unless it is possible to irrevocably lock in all choices in advance, a consumer is typically happier with the first-best consumption trajectory when someone else chooses it for him, than when he chooses it himself in ˇ°real timeˇ±</seg>
<seg id="961"> Ł¨it is first-best in the first instance, but not in the second).</seg>
<seg id="962"> Even though the laissez faire solution is inefficient, the optimal rate of taxation or subsidization for an addictive good is zero.</seg>
<seg id="963"> The same result holds in standard models of commodity taxation (when the government has no revenue requirement), for essentially the same reasons.</seg>
<seg id="964"> However, we conjecture that this is a knife-edge case, driven by the first assumption mentioned above.</seg>
<seg id="965"> It would appear that if, contrary to the assumption, temptation depends, at least to some extent, on immediate rewards from the nonaddictive good (in addition to consumption of the addictive substance), the optimal rate of sin taxation is strictly positive.</seg>
<seg id="966"> Other policy implications resemble those discussed in previous sections.</seg>
<seg id="967"> Informational policy alone is limited because it cannot address the causes of temptation.</seg>
<seg id="968"> Supply disruption is potentially beneficial because it removes tempting alternatives.</seg>
<seg id="969"> Policies that facilitate self-control can also enhance welfare by allowing consumers to eliminate alternatives that would otherwise prove tempting in the future.2.4.8</seg>
<seg id="970"> Looking AheadThe case of addiction exemplifies the potential for improving policy analysis through the integration of psychology, neuroscience, and economics.</seg>
<seg id="971"> Though progress is evident, much work remains.</seg>
<seg id="972"> We close this section with a brief discussion of some important open questions.Estimation and Testing of Competing Behavioral Models.</seg>
<seg id="973"> Almost all of the existing empirical work on addictive behavior is either a theoretical (i.e., it documents factual patterns) or based on the framework of rational addiction.</seg>
<seg id="974"> So far, research on behavioral alternatives has been almost exclusively theoretical.</seg>
<seg id="975"> It is important to explore the feasibility of estimating parsimonious structural versions of the various competing behavioral models, using both choice data and a combination of choice and nonchoice data.</seg>
<seg id="976"> Insights from ongoing research in neuroscience should be exploited to develop procedures for acquiring and using new types of pertinent nonchoice data (e.g., on physical states).</seg>
<seg id="977"> Future research should compare the performance of the models in explaining observed behavior, and examine testable implications that distinguish between them.</seg>
<seg id="978"> Empirical research can potentially shed light on the relative importance of the various forces at work in these models.</seg>
<seg id="979"> Imperfect Foresight.</seg>
<seg id="980"> Most of the economic literature on addiction assumes that people perfectly understand the benefits and costs of substance use, including its effects on future tastes and decision-making processes.</seg>
<seg id="981"> The evidence suggests that this extreme assumption is unrealistic.</seg>
<seg id="982"> For example, in a study of high-school seniors who smoked cigarettes, 56% predicted that they would not be smoking in five years, but in fact only 31% were able to quit.</seg>
<seg id="983"> Under the assumption that decision makers are completely or partially naive, models with quasi-hyperbolic discounting incorporate imperfect self-understanding.</seg>
<seg id="984"> While this represents a step in the right direction, further work is clearly needed.</seg>
<seg id="985"> Models of naive behavior should draw on new and existing empirical research concerning the nature of unsophisticated decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="986"> They should allow for the possibility that people lack perfect foresight not only with respect to their own future tastes and choices, but also with respect to other consequences, such as health effects.They should also introduce the possibility that people learn about their self-control problems with experience.</seg>
<seg id="987"> The literature on ˇ°projection biasˇ± illustrates the potential to discover important regularities concerning the structure of naive decision-making through empirical research.</seg>
<seg id="988"> This phrase refers to the tendency for people to assume that their future likes and dislikes will be more similar to their current likes and dislikes than is actually the case.</seg>
<seg id="989"> Loewenstein et al. (2003a) briefly and informally discuss several provocative implications for addiction.</seg>
<seg id="990"> Victims of projection bias are more likely to become addicted against their interests because they underestimate both the effects of habit formation and the degree to which current consumption has negative consequences for future health.</seg>
<seg id="991"> Once addicted, they are more likely to try to quit when they are not experiencing cravings, because they underestimate future cravings.</seg>
<seg id="992"> Conditional on attempting to quit, they are also more likely to ˇ°fall off the wagonˇ± because, upon experiencing cravings, they overestimate the difficulty of continued abstention in the future.</seg>
<seg id="993"> Differences across Substances and Populations.</seg>
<seg id="994"> It is important to emphasize that there is no single combination of policies that is ideal for all addictive substances.</seg>
<seg id="995"> For example, while alcohol and crack cocaine are both addictive, public policy should (and does) treat them differently.</seg>
<seg id="996"> A number of factors affect the relative desirability of the various policy alternatives, including (but not limited to) the typical individualˇŻs susceptibility to addiction, the responsiveness of compulsive and deliberative use to prices and other incentives, and the magnitude of the externalities imposed on third parties.</seg>
<seg id="997"> It is also important to stress that the ideal policy regime for any particular substance may evolve over time as our ability to treat, control, and/or predict addiction develops.</seg>
<seg id="998"> Ideally, economists should attempt to estimate parametric behavioral models for a wide range of substances and populations, and to use these estimates as a basis for determining the best policy for each substance.</seg>
<seg id="999"> 2.5
<seg id="1000"> In this section, we review the contributions of behavioral public economics to our understanding of public goods.</seg>
<seg id="1001"> As in previous sections, we identify the key policy issues, summarize the standard approach, and discuss empirical evidence that calls this approach into question.</seg>
<seg id="1002"> We then review the leading behavioral alternative and discuss its implications.</seg>
<seg id="1003"> 2.5.1
<seg id="1004"> A large number and wide variety of public policy issues from the environment to school finance, and from the war on poverty to the financing of basic research involve the provision of public goods.</seg>
<seg id="1005"> Funding for these goods flows from both public and private sources.</seg>
<seg id="1006"> At the community level, philanthropic activities in the United States address a large class of socially valuable activities, from assisting the poor to financing cultural events.</seg>
<seg id="1007"> Andreoni (2006) reports that, for the United States, contributions to the philanthropic sector totaled 240.3 billion dollars in 2003;</seg>
<seg id="1008"> moreover, in 1997, roughly 45,000 charitable, religious, and other non-for-profit organizations were registered with the government.</seg>
<seg id="1009"> Voluntarily provided public goods also play important roles in smaller groups, such as families.</seg>
<seg id="1010"> In each of these domains, public goods give rise to a common problem: how can the group best overcome free riding and provide funding at an appropriate level?</seg>
<seg id="1011"> Should the group provide its members with incentives to contribute (e.g., tax breaks)?</seg>
<seg id="1012"> Should it require mandatory contributions (e.g., through taxes)?</seg>
<seg id="1013"> Is it best to have a hybrid system that draws on both public and private contributions?</seg>
<seg id="1014"> To answer these questions, economists require a theory of public goods that explains observed patterns of voluntary giving.</seg>
<seg id="1015"> The theory must explain why people give, how they select the causes to which they contribute, and how their contributions respond to economic variables, government policies, and the behavior of others.</seg>
<seg id="1016"> It should also account for the existence of philanthropic organizations, and explain how the activities of these entities respond to government policy.</seg>
<seg id="1017"> 2.5.2
<seg id="1018"> First, individuals only care about their consumption of private and public goods.</seg>
<seg id="1019"> They do not benefit directly from making contributions, nor do they care about othersˇŻ consumption or well-being.</seg>
<seg id="1020"> Second, individuals do not care about the process through which allocations are determined.</seg>
<seg id="1021"> For example, they are indifferent between public and private provision as long as the level of private consumption and public good provision is the same in both instances.</seg>
<seg id="1022"> Notice also that, in this simple model, there is no obvious role for charitable fund-raising.</seg>
<seg id="1023"> For example, since people are fully informed about the public good, there is no reason for charities to disseminate information.</seg>
<seg id="1024"> This model has featured prominently in several important strands of the literature. These include work on optimal tax and regulatory policy in the presence of externalities, the design of efficient mechanisms for public goods problems, and political economy models of public goods provision.</seg>
<seg id="1025"> From a positive perspective, the model has a number of sharp, testable implications, including the following</seg>
<seg id="1026"> Only the Wealthy Contribute.</seg>
<seg id="1027"> In large groups only the very upper tail of the income distribution contributes to the public good.</seg>
<seg id="1028"> Furthermore, as the population grows (fixing the distribution of wealth), contributors account for a smaller fraction of the population.</seg>
<seg id="1029"> As a result, the effect of population size on total contributions converges to zero for sufficiently large populations.</seg>
<seg id="1030"> Unless the group is small, the level of public goods depends only on the wealth of the very rich: changes in wealth for the rest of the population have no impact on total provision.</seg>
<seg id="1031"> Neutrality of Public Provision.</seg>
<seg id="1032"> Public provision of public goods financed through lump-sum taxation is neutral as long as no individual pays a lump-sum tax greater than the contribution he would make in the absence of government intervention.</seg>
<seg id="1033"> In this case, public contributions fully crowd out private contributions.</seg>
<seg id="1034"> While the conditions required for neutrality seem stark, the result generalizes to other environments.</seg>
<seg id="1035"> For example, Bernheim (1986) and Andreoni (1988) have shown that the total level of the public goods is invariant, or approximately invariant, with respect to public provision financed by distortionary taxes, and with respect to subsidized giving.</seg>
<seg id="1036"> These results build on earlier work by Warr (1982) and Roberts (1984).</seg>
<seg id="1037"> Contributions from External Sources (Almost) Fully Crowd Out Internal Funding.</seg>
<seg id="1038"> In a large economy, exogenous contributions to the public good (made by someone outside the group, say a higher level of government) have a negligible impact on the level of provision.</seg>
<seg id="1039"> In other words, external funding almost fully crowds out private contributions.</seg>
<seg id="1040"> It follows, for example, that contributions from a higher level of government to a local charity cannot measurably increase total funding, assuming that the number of contributors is reasonably large.</seg>
<seg id="1041"> Neutrality of Redistribution.</seg>
<seg id="1042"> Redistributing wealth among contributors has no effect on the total level of contributions.</seg>
<seg id="1043"> In contrast, redistributing wealth from the group of contributors to the group of noncontributors decreases the total level of the public good.</seg>
<seg id="1044"> These results are valuable because they provide stark and robust testable implications of the standard model.</seg>
<seg id="1045"> How well do they match the data?</seg>
<seg id="1046"> 2.5.3
<seg id="1047"> In contrast to the bulk of the literature that preceded it, this chapter studies contributions to a particular public goodˇŞthe operation of public radio stationsˇŞrather than aggregate contributions.</seg>
<seg id="1048"> The narrow focus is desirable because, when analyzing aggregates, it is difficult to harmonize the scope of data pertaining to public and private contributions.</seg>
<seg id="1049"> Moreover, a high rate of giving in the aggregate may mask low rates of giving to individual causes.</seg>
<seg id="1050"> The paper uses a unique cross-sectional dataset on the funding sources and member contributions to sixty-six public radio stations across the United States serving nonoverlapping markets.</seg>
<seg id="1051"> It has two main findings.</seg>
<seg id="1052"> First, about half of the subjects in the sample (who were recruited for a study of listening habits) contribute positive amounts.</seg>
<seg id="1053"> The average contribution given was $45.</seg>
<seg id="1054"> Contributors were wealthier and more educated on average, but not by a significant amount.</seg>
<seg id="1055"> This finding stands in sharp contrast to the first two implications from the previous section.</seg>
<seg id="1056"> Second, a $10,000 increase in ˇ°exogenousˇ± public contributions to the station (that is, contributions financed by federal taxes rather than taxes on local members) reduces private contributions by $1,350 for a typical station with 9,000 members.</seg>
<seg id="1057"> This contradicts the fourth implication.</seg>
<seg id="1058"> KingmaˇŻs first finding is consistent with patterns observed in the aggregate data.</seg>
<seg id="1059"> For example, Andreoni (2006) reports that, in 1995, 68.5% of all households gave to charity, and the average gift amount was $1,081.</seg>
<seg id="1060"> Even relatively poor households gave almost 5% of their incomes, on average, to charity;</seg>
<seg id="1061"> as a fraction of income, households in upper-income brackets actually gave less.</seg>
<seg id="1062"> KingmaˇŻs second finding is also roughly consistent with other studies based on aggregate data.</seg>
<seg id="1063"> For example, Abrams and Schmitz (1978a,b) and Clotfelter (1985) find that public transfers to the ˇ®non-for-profitˇŻ sector crowd out private giving at the rate of 5-28 cents on the dollar.</seg>
<seg id="1064"> The first four implications listed in the previous section have also been tested in the laboratory.</seg>
<seg id="1065"> Isaac and Walker (1988) study the effect of group size in linear public-good experiments.</seg>
<seg id="1066"> Subjects play repeatedly with either three or nine other participants.</seg>
<seg id="1067"> In each round they receive an endowment of tokens and decide how many tokens to contribute to the public good.</seg>
<seg id="1068"> Tokens are valuable because they are exchangeable for cash at the end of the experiment.</seg>
<seg id="1069"> Each token contributed to the public good yields either 0.3 or 0.7 tokens for everyone in the group, including the contributor.</seg>
<seg id="1070"> Since each token contributed entails a net loss, the standard model predicts that it is a dominant strategy for every subject to contribute nothing.</seg>
<seg id="1071"> As in many other experiments in this literature, subjects initially contribute roughly 50% of tokens on average, but this figure falls as the experiment is repeated.</seg>
<seg id="1072"> Neither average individual contributions nor the fraction of subjects contributing a positive amount decline with group size.</seg>
<seg id="1073"> These findings contradict the second implication above.</seg>
<seg id="1074"> Andreoni (1993) studies a variant of the previous experiment in which payoffs vary nonlinearly with the number of tokens.</seg>
<seg id="1075"> This generates a Nash equilibrium with strictly positive contributions.</seg>
<seg id="1076"> He tests the neutrality of public provision (the third implication) by comparing behavior in two closely related treatments.</seg>
<seg id="1077"> In each case, subjects choose how many tokens to contribute and are given a two-dimensional table that describes how their payoffs change as a function of their contribution and the aggregate contributions of others.</seg>
<seg id="1078"> In one treatment, they are, in effect, required to contribute at least two tokens;</seg>
<seg id="1079"> in the other treatment, they are not required to contribute anything.</seg>
<seg id="1080"> AndreoniˇŻs results imply that public contributions crowd out private contributions at the rate of 71 cents on the dollar.</seg>
<seg id="1081"> While this rate of crowding- out is high in comparison with other estimates in the literature, it is still inconsistent with the third implication.</seg>
<seg id="1082"> These papers, together with a growing body of related evidence, have led many economists to reject the standard model, and to search for superior alternatives.</seg>
<seg id="1083"> The rest of this section reviews the state of the literature and summarizes its implications for public economics.</seg>
<seg id="1084"> 2.5.4
<seg id="1085"> Unfortunately, in the context of warm-glow giving and public goods, these processes are not yet well understood.</seg>
<seg id="1086"> The warm-glow model remains a ˇ°black box,ˇ± and one can interpret it as a reduced form for a variety of mechanisms with starkly differing welfare implications.</seg>
<seg id="1087"> Diamond (2006) argues that, given the limited state of knowledge concerning processes, measures of social welfare should exclude the apparent benefits from the warm glow.</seg>
