<seg id="1088"> He advocates using the warm-glow model for positive purposes (that is, to describe behavior), but favors the standard model for evaluating welfare.</seg>
<seg id="1089"> Andreoni (2006) expresses a similar view, and in addition argues that economists are unlikely to shed much light on the nature of the true preferences that give rise to warm-glow behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1090"> While we are more sanguine about the prospects for meaningful progress, we agree that economists do not yet understand warm-glow mechanisms sufficiently well to resolve important questions about positive and normative analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1091"> One concern is that apparent warm-glow behavior may sometimes reflect a divergence between choices and true preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1092"> In some instances, people may give because they derive pleasure from the act.</seg>
<seg id="1093"> For example, giving to a worthy cause may make them feel proud to have taken constructive action, or it may assuage their guilt.</seg>
<seg id="1094"> In such cases, revealed preference provides a reasonable basis for welfare evaluation (subject to the further qualifications discussed below).</seg>
<seg id="1095"> However, in other situations, exposure to an emotionally manipulative message may precipitate giving by triggering a short-lived emotional reaction such as shame,</seg>
<seg id="1096"> and people may experience remorse shortly thereafter</seg>
<seg id="1097"> Different implications for welfare follow depending on whether the individual, when in a normal state of mind, wishes to limit his ability to give upon encountering an emotional trigger.</seg>
<seg id="1098"> If behavior is dynamically consistent, it may be appropriate to adopt a state-contingent version of the warm-glow model for both positive and normative analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1099"> However, if behavior is dynamically inconsistent, it may be appropriate to discount the ˇ°revealedˇ± impact of giving on transient perceptions of well-being</seg>
<seg id="1100"> (e.g., for the reasons discussed at the end of Section 2.3).</seg>
<seg id="1101"> A second concern is that warm-glow effects appear to be context dependent.</seg>
<seg id="1102"> Experiments have shown that the amount of giving depends on framing, the identity of the group, the emotional state of the subjects, and the history of play.</seg>
<seg id="1103"> Here we mention two examples.</seg>
<seg id="1104"> First, Andreoni (1995) finds that a change in the phrasing of instructions can have a sizable effect on contributions, even when strategy sets and payoffs are unchanged.</seg>
<seg id="1105"> Second, Isaac and Walker (1988) (and dozens of subsequent studies) find that the level of contributions decreases with repetition in both small and large groups.</seg>
<seg id="1106"> Furthermore, the rate of decline depends on the behavior of others: subjects are more likely to stop acting cooperatively if others behave selfishly (see Ledyard (1995) and Camerer (2003) for surveys of the literature).</seg>
<seg id="1107"> This concern is relevant for policy analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1108"> The appropriate reduced-form representation of warm-glow giving may vary from one set of policies and institutions to another.</seg>
<seg id="1109"> The practice of forecasting the behavioral effects of a policy change based on fixed warm-glow parameters is therefore vulnerable to the Lucas critique.</seg>
<seg id="1110"> For example, if people experience less pride when making contributions in the presence of economic incentives, subsidization of contributions will stimulate less giving than anticipated, and could even reduce it.</seg>
<seg id="1111"> Alternatively, people may become less resistant to taxation if they are regularly supplied with more concrete and visual evidence of the benefits derived from public expenditures.</seg>
<seg id="1112"> Public relation campaigns that show ˇ°your tax dollars at workˇ± are, in effect, intended to foster a warm glow.</seg>
<seg id="1113"> One could, of course, modify the warm-glow model to account for context dependence by linking the taste for giving to features of the environment in just the right way.</seg>
<seg id="1114"> However, this solution is conceptually unsatisfactory.</seg>
<seg id="1115"> One cannot usefully explain a phenomenon by selecting ad hoc preferences that rationalize choices ex post.</seg>
<seg id="1116"> If every context is potentially associated with a different mode of behavior, out-of-sample prediction is impossible.</seg>
<seg id="1117"> To anticipate the positive effects of policy changes, one therefore needs a broad theory that accounts for the relevance of context.</seg>
<seg id="1118"> This requires us to open the black box.</seg>
<seg id="1119"> Since the warm-glow mechanism may differ from one context to another, a deeper understanding of context dependence is also essential for welfare analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1120"> To illustrate the problem, consider the following hypothetical example.</seg>
<seg id="1121"> How should we evaluate a policy that replaces private contributions to a public good with tax-financed contributions, without changing either the total amount obtained from any individual or the overall level of funding?</seg>
<seg id="1122"> Are people worse off because they lose the beneficial warm glow associated with giving?</seg>
<seg id="1123"> Are they better off because public funding relieves them of guilt?</seg>
<seg id="1124"> Or are they equally well-off because they experience the same warm glow from giving voluntarily and from paying their taxes?</seg>
<seg id="1125"> While it may be difficult to resolve this issue, we are optimistic about the prospects for progress through further research involving a combination of psychology, neuroscience, and experimental economics.</seg>
<seg id="1126"> Despite these concerns, the theoretical and empirical literatures concerning warm- glow giving have already contributed significantly to our understanding of public goods, and have changed the way many public economists think about related policies.</seg>
<seg id="1127"> We can now say with some confidence that people act as if they care about the levels of their own contributions.</seg>
<seg id="1128"> We know that the intensity of this effect depends on context.</seg>
<seg id="1129"> While we lack a good theory of context dependence, we have a good set of empirical regularities from which to build.</seg>
<seg id="1130"> We have good reason to believe that people feel differently about public and private contributions.</seg>
<seg id="1131"> We have both direct and indirect evidence that public contributions crowd out private contributions at a rate significantly less than dollar-for-dollar.</seg>
<seg id="1132"> Accordingly, even low levels of public contributions can significantly raise total funding.</seg>
<seg id="1133"> There is strong evidence that people give more when institutions activate psychological mechanisms, such as concerns about reciprocity and fairness, that play central roles in giving.</seg>
<seg id="1134"> From a policy perspective, this suggests that relatively inexpensive strategies involving advertising and the promotion of community leadership may deserve greater emphasis.</seg>
<seg id="1135"> 2.5.5
<seg id="1136"> and to apply them to basic questions in public finance, political economy, and mechanism design.</seg>
<seg id="1137"> We are still far from a satisfactory model of public goods that can become a new workhorse for economic applications across the board.</seg>
<seg id="1138"> However, based on the rapidly growing body of evidence concerning the psychological and neural processes at work, including research on the neural basis of empathy, punishment, and cooperation</seg>
<seg id="1139"> we are optimistic that such a framework is on the horizon.</seg>
<seg id="1140"> Given the number of likely forces at work (reciprocity, social norms, social emotions, social signaling, and so forth) it seems likely that a relatively complex and multifaceted approach is needed.</seg>
<seg id="1141"> Yet it is also likely that the discovery of new organizing principles will permit useful simplifications that render the problem more tractable.</seg>
<seg id="1142"> In focusing here on individual behavior, we have largely neglected the role of Phil-anthropic organizations.</seg>
<seg id="1143"> As Andreoni (2006) convincingly argues, it is also essential to understand the behavior of the philanthropic sector.</seg>
<seg id="1144"> A growing body of evidence shows that charities significantly stimulate giving, and that their activities respond both to government policy and to the behavior of other non-for-profit institutions.</seg>
<seg id="1145"> For example, Andreoni and Payne (2003) show that government grants to charities reduce fund-raising activities;</seg>
<seg id="1146"> Andreoni and Petrie (2004) document the role of charities in disseminating information;</seg>
<seg id="1147"> Harbaugh (1998) provides evidence that charities exploit social signaling in their fund-raising campaigns.</seg>
<seg id="1148"> For reviews of the economics of philanthropy, see Andreoni (2006) and Rose-Ackerman (1996).</seg>
<seg id="1149"> 2.6
<seg id="1150"> In our view, behavioral public economics has enormous potential, and has already demonstrated its value by making important contributions to critical policy discussions.</seg>
<seg id="1151"> We have emphasized that the behavioral perspective does not preclude coherent normative analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1152"> Indeed, in many cases, it is possible to modify and extend the tools of empirical welfare analysis without abandoning familiar methodological principles.</seg>
<seg id="1153"> We have also reviewed recent behavioral work concerning policies affecting saving, addiction, and public goods.</seg>
<seg id="1154"> Each of these literatures offers novel and important insights, as well as the potential for groundbreaking innovation.</seg>
<seg id="1155"> The goal of this final section is to briefly highlight some critical directions for future research.</seg>
<seg id="1156"> Better Models.</seg>
<seg id="1157"> While the current generation of behavioral models improves the explanatory power of economic theory, many behavioral patterns remain unexplained.</seg>
<seg id="1158"> Among other things, recent research has deemphasized considerations for which satisfactory and tractable formal models are not yet available, such as framing effects, the adoption of rules of thumb, and other responses to environmental complexity.</seg>
<seg id="1159"> To study the policy implications of these phenomena, better models are required.</seg>
<seg id="1160"> For example, we need theories that explain how people adopt rules of thumb, and how they adapt these rules to new environments.</seg>
<seg id="1161"> New Types and Sources of Data.</seg>
<seg id="1162"> With sufficiently restrictive structural assumptions, it is possible to estimate positive and normative behavioral models using data only on choices.</seg>
<seg id="1163"> The use of nonchoice data would potentially allow economists to estimate these models more reliably, and to formulate more discriminating and robust tests of competing alternatives.</seg>
<seg id="1164"> Future research should examine the possibility of measuring preferences directly, instead of inferring them from choices.</seg>
<seg id="1165"> Self-reporting is a natural source of information about tastes.</seg>
<seg id="1166"> In practice, there are several problems with self-reported preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1167"> First, when choices are not involved, questions about preferences are inherently hypothetical.</seg>
<seg id="1168"> There is some reason to believe that people do not give reliable answers to hypothetical questions</seg>
<seg id="1169"> (see, for example, List (2001) and the references therein).</seg>
<seg id="1170"> For example, unless there is something at stake, they may not take these questions seriously.</seg>
<seg id="1171"> Second, true preferences may conflict with social and moral norms, leading subjects to either rationalize or report false preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1172"> Third, people may make mistakes in assessing their own preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1173"> For example, a sizable body of literature has documented systematic errors in affective forecasting</seg>
<seg id="1174"> Fourth, context may affect an individualˇŻs ability to cognitively access his true preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1175"> Despite considerable evidence that self-reporting is susceptible to these problems for a review), there is cause for optimism.</seg>
<seg id="1176"> For the most part, the object of this body of work has been to identify experimental manipulations that lead to nonsensical self-reports.</seg>
<seg id="1177"> While this demonstrates that there are important pitfalls associated with the direct elicitation of preferences, it does not prove that this approach is worthless.</seg>
<seg id="1178"> As far as we know, there has been no systematic attempt to design elicitation protocols that are stable and resistant to manipulation.</seg>
<seg id="1179"> Many other types of data merit consideration.</seg>
<seg id="1180"> Even without eliciting complete preferences, one can potentially learn whether an individual regards a particular choice as a mistake, whether his choices correspond to his intentions, or whether he systematically fails to follow through on plans</seg>
<seg id="1181"> One can also elicit information about expectations and make comparisons with realizations</seg>
<seg id="1182"> Obviously, information along these lines raises many of the same concerns as self-reported preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1183"> Finally, economists have only just started to tap data on physical states, brain activity, and the like.</seg>
<seg id="1184"> While the value of neuroeconomic data remains largely unproven, the potential payoffs are high, and the possibilities are worth exploring.</seg>
<seg id="1185"> Difficult Issues in Welfare Economics.</seg>
<seg id="1186"> The nascent field of ˇ°behavioral welfare economicsˇ± is far from settled. Many thorny issues remain.</seg>
<seg id="1187"> The following hypothetical problem illustrates one challenging issue.</seg>
<seg id="1188"> An individual is presented with a choice between two options, A and B.</seg>
<seg id="1189"> He is indifferent between them.</seg>
<seg id="1190"> However, his preferences change as a result of his choice.</seg>
<seg id="1191"> If he chooses A, he prefers B (call this the ˇ°A selfˇ±).</seg>
<seg id="1192"> If he chooses B, he prefers A (call this the ˇ°B selfˇ±).</seg>
<seg id="1193"> Suppose he chooses A.</seg>
<seg id="1194"> Since only the initial self and the A self actually exist, it seems natural to place no weight on the preferences of the B self.</seg>
<seg id="1195"> But if we place any weight on the A self, B is the welfare optimum.</seg>
<seg id="1196"> Of course, if we enforce this choice through public policy, the A self vanishes and the B self materializes, in which case A is the welfare optimum.</seg>
<seg id="1197"> Is there a coherent way to resolve this ambiguity?</seg>
<seg id="1198"> See Bernheim and Rangel (2005a) for a more systematic treatment of welfare economics in behavioral settings.</seg>
<seg id="1199"> Nonstandard Policies.</seg>
<seg id="1200"> In the standard model, public policy affects behavior only through its effect on information and budget constraints.</seg>
<seg id="1201"> A growing body of literature, partially reviewed in the previous three sections, suggests that policy can also have powerful effects on behavior through other channels.</seg>
<seg id="1202"> For example, it can provide or suppress cues, and it can alter the way decision problems are framed.</seg>
<seg id="1203"> If economists can develop reliable formal models of these effects, it should be possible to study the optimal design of unconventional economic policies (e.g., restrictions on advertising, warning labels, and clever manipulations of framing effects as in Thaler and BenartziˇŻs (2004) Saving for Tomorrow Savings Plan) with the same rigor as traditional tax and expenditure policy.</seg>
<seg id="1204"> New Applications.</seg>
<seg id="1205"> The interesting collection of papers in this volume show that, as time passes, economists are applying behavioral economics to an increasingly wide range of economic problems.</seg>
<seg id="1206"> No doubt this trend will continue within the field of public economics.</seg>
<seg id="1207"> Many of the tools described in this chapter should prove useful in understanding issues pertaining to poverty, crime, corruption, and other important topics.</seg>
<seg id="1208"> The issue raised in the title, ˇ°Behavioral Public Economics: Welfare and Policy Analysis with Nonstandard Decision-Makers,ˇ± is a special case of a more general question.</seg>
<seg id="1209"> This more general question concerns how we should do public economics when the actions an individual or household may take do not conform with preferences which may be relevant for policy or welfare analysis, if indeed there are any preferences relevant for policy analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1210"> This more general question arises very frequently in practical public policy.</seg>
<seg id="1211"> It occurs in particularly strong forms in many areas of development economics, a subject which, in combination with public economics, has occupied me for much of my professional life.</seg>
<seg id="1212"> In both developed and developing economies, public policy faces the challenge that preferences are endogenous, in that they depend on the experience of individuals or households, and that the preferences themselves may be influenced, or should be influenced, by public action.</seg>
<seg id="1213"> In standard welfare economics, which underpins much of our theory of public policy,</seg>
<seg id="1214"> and indeed has been very fertile in shaping the way economists think about policy, individuals act consistently with their preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1215"> Further, those preferences provide the relevant basis for assessing changes in individual well-being for the purposes of constructing social preferences in a multi-individual community.</seg>
<seg id="1216"> It is clear that one can break this chain from action to social preferences at two points.</seg>
<seg id="1217"> The first break could be between individual action and individual preferences and the second could be between individual preferences and social welfare.</seg>
<seg id="1218"> The authors have focused on just one aspect here: individuals have ˇ°trueˇ± preferences but they make mistakes, so that their actions do not reflect those preferences accurately.</seg>
<seg id="1219"> The challenge is then to find ways of discovering their preferences in order to suggest policies which might improve their welfare.</seg>
<seg id="1220"> As with much research, focusing on one particular aspect of a problem is often very valuable in order to fix ideas and get clear results.</seg>
<seg id="1221"> I would encourage the authors to try to go further in the task they have defined for themselves.</seg>
<seg id="1222"> They have indeed focused on an important issue and have gone down a route where specific useful results should be available.</seg>
<seg id="1223"> However, I think it is also of great value to look at the more general set of issues.</seg>
<seg id="1224"> First, if we do this we realize just how general and pervasive the problems are, and that the economics profession has largely ignored them.</seg>
<seg id="1225"> Second, we may see ways forward that do not jump out so strongly when we look at a particular version of the problem.</seg>
<seg id="1226"> I should emphasize, as the authors indicate, that whilst this may take us into areas including philosophy, psychology, and biology, which may be unfamiliar to many economists,</seg>
<seg id="1227"> I think that there are many aspects of the economistˇŻs training, tool-kit and approach which should imply that they have a great deal to contribute to clear thinking on the subject of how to analyze policy in the context of endogenous, confused, or mistaken preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1228"> The authors emphasize a number of areas where such preferences look to be relevant, including drugs, obesity, savings, and sexual behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1229"> It should be clear that these are of great importance to policy, economic and otherwise, and not merely analytically irritating issues that occur on the margins of our subject.</seg>
<seg id="1230"> There are many more examples from development (but not only development) relating to attitudes to different ways of living, including discrimination issues involving education, gender, color, caste, ethnicity, or language.</seg>
<seg id="1231"> And we should recognize the endogeneity of preferences arising from the experiences associated with major life changes such as migration, education or employment.</seg>
<seg id="1232"> Let me illustrate with just one observation involving industrialization and urbanization.</seg>
<seg id="1233"> The economic historian Richard Tawney made the issue of endogenous preferences central in his remarkable book Land and Labor in China, written after visiting China in 1930:The movement to industrialization is a growing force.</seg>
<seg id="1234"> Where it directly affects, for better or worse, the livelihood of one, it indirectly modified the habits of ten.</seg>
<seg id="1235"> Its effect on the mind... is ultimately more important than its visible embodiment in mills and mines.</seg>
<seg id="1236"> (Tawney 1966, p. 17)Such examples suggest the notion that ˇ°trueˇ± preferences, and mistakes relative to them, may not be an appropriate way of embodying disquiet as to whether individual choices reveal preferences relevant for social choices.</seg>
<seg id="1237"> Different ways of looking at the associated policy questions include:</seg>
<seg id="1238"> (i)
<seg id="1239"> or(ii)
<seg id="1240"> or(iii)
<seg id="1241"> or(iv)
<seg id="1242"> Indeed much of the analysis of development is, or should be, about processes of change of this kind.</seg>
<seg id="1243"> To illustrate some of the relevant issues let me raise the example of a Pakistani (say) father who is unwilling to send his daughter to school.</seg>
<seg id="1244"> On what grounds should we engage with him?</seg>
<seg id="1245"> How should we (and indeed, should we) try to persuade him otherwise?</seg>
<seg id="1246"> It may be that we think he has an information problem in that he does not know how high the return will be for her, her children and the family.</seg>
<seg id="1247"> Or it may be that he does not take into account externalities for others in society.</seg>
<seg id="1248"> Both information and externalities are a standard part of the theory of economic policy and probably both are relevant to our (where ˇ°weˇ± is society in general or the policy makers) wish to engage with him on this subject.</seg>
<seg id="1249"> But, I would suggest, our discussion would and should go beyond this.</seg>
<seg id="1250"> We would want to try to influence his ideas on the meaning of a good life for his children.</seg>
<seg id="1251"> We would actively try to change his preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1252"> And many of us would suggest further that this kind of interchange and persuasion is part of the democratic process of good governance.</seg>
<seg id="1253"> If we do want to influence his preferences, then we should ask ourselves what moral authority do we have?</seg>
<seg id="1254"> There is no easy answer to this, but let us recognize that we do this all the time concerning, for example, education, exercise, savings, or discrimination.</seg>
<seg id="1255"> Part of the answer is paternalism and a judgement on the merits or demerits of his preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1256"> Part lies in a notion of rights for his daughter.</seg>
<seg id="1257"> Part also lies in an understanding of how preferences are and should be formed, i.e., in the process of formation of preferences and its link with democracy.</seg>
<seg id="1258"> There John Stuart Mill saw the issue clearlyˇŞdemocracy is about developing ideas and changing attitudes through involvement and discussion (ˇ°government by discussionˇ±).</seg>
<seg id="1259"> Jon Elster more recently argued that democracy and policy making are less about Arrow-type theories of aggregating preferences and more about forming and changing preferences through interaction.</seg>
<seg id="1260"> Thus, policy making, in this view, is about process rather than algorithm.</seg>
<seg id="1261"> But there is a very different route to understanding welfare and policy that does not go via preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1262"> If, as Amartya Sen has argued, we embrace the idea of capabilities (I would also suggest the language of empowerment here),</seg>
<seg id="1263"> then we avoid the puzzles and problems of mistaken and/or endogenous preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1264"> This capability or empowerment route will give us only partial orderings, since it may not be clear whether capabilities or empowerment have been augmented.</seg>
<seg id="1265"> But the same ambiguities will also be true of the preference-based approach in this context.</seg>
<seg id="1266"> I therefore encourage Rangel and Bernheim to pursue their line relentlessly.</seg>
<seg id="1267"> It is surely one of the key approaches and one that has a chance of being worked through successfully.</seg>
<seg id="1268"> And we do need specific examples that we can analyze directly and formally to advance or fix ideas on this difficult but very important set of issues.</seg>
<seg id="1269"> But let us also look more widely to endogenous preferences and how we should act, as a matter of policy, to influence preferences in the pursuit of a better economy and society.</seg>
<seg id="1270"> COMMENT BY EMMANUEL SAEZ</seg>
<seg id="1271"> This chapter tackles the general issue of welfare measurement and normative policy analysis in models with nonstandard decision makers.</seg>
<seg id="1272"> In recent years, there has been much interest in developing such nonstandard models, which incorporate insights from psychology.</seg>
<seg id="1273"> Most of those ˇ°behavioral economicsˇ± studies have focused on the positive analysis: uncovering empirical situations where behavior departs significantly from the classical rational model and then developing models which can account for such behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1274"> Such nonstandard models potentially offer a fertile ground for normative analysis, as there is no presumption that the free-market equilibrium will be efficient in those situations.</seg>
<seg id="1275"> However, normative analysis in behavioral models has lagged behind positive analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1276"> The main reason for such a lag is that normative analysis requires measurement of welfare and most nonstandard models raise difficult issues concerning welfare evaluation.</seg>
<seg id="1277"> The analysis of Bernheim and Rangel is, to my knowledge, the first comprehensive and systematic effort to review the difficulties that arise in measuring welfare in nonstandard models.</seg>
<seg id="1278"> The paper first proposes a general overview of the problems of measuring welfare and then turns to the detailed examination of three topics, saving, addiction, and public goods, which have attracted substantial attention of behavioral economists.</seg>
<seg id="1279"> Each of those topic-specific sections can almost be read independently and constitute interesting discussions of the welfare measurement and optimal public policy issues that have arisen in those contexts.</seg>
<seg id="1280"> Thus, the structure of the paper suggests it is difficult to make progress in a general model and that the most fruitful route is probably to advance one model at a time.</seg>
<seg id="1281"> In this discussion, I would like to make simple remarks on how some of the most basic findings in empirical behavioral economics should force applied public finance economists to rethink their interpretation of findings in empirical public economics,</seg>
<seg id="1282"> and how such newly interpreted empirical findings should be used to carry out more meaningful normative analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1283"> The empirical literature in behavioral economics has uncovered many instances in which elements that should be largely irrelevant for decision-making in the standard model actually have large effects on economic behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1284"> The most famous example is probably the large effects of default provisions on 401(k) pension contribution decisions.</seg>
<seg id="1285"> Those elements can be influenced by government policy.</seg>
<seg id="1286"> For example, in the case of 401(k) pension plans, it is conceivable to issue regulations requesting employers to enroll all employees by default with a reasonable default contribution level and investment allocation</seg>
<seg id="1287"> (see Orszag (2005) for a recent testimony to the U.S. Congress proposing reforms along those lines).</seg>
<seg id="1288"> If the goal of the policy maker is to increase savings without forcing anybody to save, it seems clear that using default rules will be less costly than using traditional economic incentives such as exempting the returns on savings from taxation.</seg>
<seg id="1289"> Behavioral economics has therefore successfully expanded the set of tools that policy makers can contemplate using to influence economic decisions.</seg>
<seg id="1290"> More subtle and more disturbing for traditional public economics, nonstandard behavioral effects probably not only affect the level of an economic decision but also the sensitivity of an economic decision with respect to traditional price incentives.</seg>
<seg id="1291"> For example, Duflo et al. (2005) show that matching incentives for IRA contributions at the time of tax preparation in the context of a field experiment have a much larger effect on take-up rates and contributions than the larger incentive effects created by the ˇ°saversˇŻ creditˇ± federal income tax program.</seg>
<seg id="1292"> The larger response for the experimental match seems to be due to its saliency and simplicity relative to the complexity of the federal program.</seg>
<seg id="1293"> In the context of charitable contributions, in a laboratory experiment, Eckel and Grossman (2003) have shown that a matching subsidy generates significantly more charitable giving than an economically equivalent rebate subsidy:</seg>
<seg id="1294"> a 100% match on voluntary contributions generates much higher total contributions (inclusive of the match) than an equivalent 50% rebate on voluntary contributions.</seg>
<seg id="1295"> It is conceivable that such framing effects can also partly explain the substantial heterogeneity that has been found across studies on labor supply elasticities with respect to taxes and transfers.</seg>
<seg id="1296"> For example, the Earned Income Tax Credit generates significant labor supply participation effects because recipients understand that positive earnings are necessary to benefit from the credit.</seg>
<seg id="1297"> The Earned Income Tax Credit does not seem to generate negative effects on hours of work conditional on working because individuals might not understand how the phasing out of the credit reduces net wage rates beyond some earnings level.</seg>
<seg id="1298"> The results from the standard normative models on optimal charitable giving subsidies or optimal taxation and redistribution depend crucially on such behavioral response parameters:</seg>
<seg id="1299"> for example, less redistribution is desirable if labor supply is very sensitive to tax rates.</seg>
<seg id="1300"> If such parameters are actually sensitive to framing effects, then two new areas of research open up.</seg>
<seg id="1301"> First, on the positive side, we need to understand how framing effects affect those behavioral parameters.</seg>
<seg id="1302"> As described above, empirical studies have started to uncover the existence of framing effects.</seg>
<seg id="1303"> The task of understanding and classifying framing effects empirically into the subtle and overlapping categories of information, attention, suggestion, persuasion, or temptation is enormous.</seg>
<seg id="1304"> Systematic laboratory or field experiments seem the most promising way to make progress on those tasks.</seg>
<seg id="1305"> Second, on the normative side, optimal framing needs to become an integral part of the analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1306"> Of course, normative analysis raises additional difficult issues.</seg>
<seg id="1307"> As Bernheim and Rangel point out in great detail, defining a concept of welfare is far from obvious and can easily fall into unsolvable paradoxes, as the simple example they present in their conclusion shows.</seg>
<seg id="1308"> Let me propose a shortcut around these issues.</seg>
<seg id="1309"> This shortcut is definitely not satisfactory conceptually, but might be acceptable in practice in many situations.</seg>
<seg id="1310"> There are a number of ˇ°normativeˇ± exercises, which could be of interest to policy makers and which do not need to be tied to properly defined welfare concepts.</seg>
<seg id="1311"> For example, in the case of savings for retirement, a government could be interested in designing policies to promote retirement savings and evaluate success in terms of the distribution of replacement rates (average net income during retirement over average net income while working).</seg>
<seg id="1312"> In the case of charitable contributions, the government's goal might be to design programs or subsidies maximizing net charitable contributions, given that an exogenous budget has been allocated for such a purpose.</seg>
<seg id="1313"> Conceptually, this is clearly a significant step back relative to the unified welfare and positive theory of the standard model.</seg>
<seg id="1314"> For practical purposes, policy debates tend to take place along such concrete normative criteria rather than along the choice of a social welfare function aggregating preferences across individuals with different tastes and abilities.</seg>
<seg id="1315"> Therefore, it is likely that policy makers will be more easily convinced about the virtues of behavioral economics lessons than traditional economists.</seg>
<seg id="1316"> Thus, I would like to conclude this discussion on a relatively optimistic note.</seg>
<seg id="1317"> In the medium term, behavioral economics should be seen as a great opportunity to try to reconcile disparate (and hence not very informative) results that have been obtained in many fields in the standard empirical public finance literature.</seg>
<seg id="1318"> Such findings could be used relatively easily for policy recommendations by focusing on concrete and nonwe lfarist objectives.</seg>
<seg id="1319"> In the long run, as Bernheim and Rangel argue, it is possible that economists will be able to define concepts of welfare in nonstandard situations that will become broadly acceptable in the profession and allow us to use again a new but unified theory of decision-making and welfare measurement.</seg>
<seg id="1320"> Many topics within economics relate to law.</seg>
<seg id="1321"> A large body of work in public economics, for instance, examines the effects of legally mandated government programs such as disability and unemployment insurance</seg>
<seg id="1322"> work on the labor market examines the effects of many types of antidiscrimination laws</seg>
<seg id="1323"> and recent corporate governance research studies the consequences of corporate and securities law on stock returns and volatility</seg>
<seg id="1324"> But, while all of these topics relate to law in some way, neither ˇ°law and economicsˇ± nor ˇ°behavioral law and economicsˇ± embraces them as genuinely central areas of inquiry.</seg>
<seg id="1325"> Thus an important threshold question for the present work involves how to characterize the domains of both ˇ°law and economicsˇ± and ˇ°behavioral law and economics.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1326"> Amid the broad span of economic topics relating to law in some way, a few distinctive features help to demarcate work that is typically regarded as within law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="1327"> One distinguishing feature is that much of this work focuses on various areas of law that were not much studied by economists prior to the advent of law and economics;</seg>
<seg id="1328"> these areas include tort law, contract law, property law, and rules governing the litigation process.</seg>
<seg id="1329"> A second feature of work within law and economics is that it often (controversially) employs the normative criterion of ˇ°wealth maximization" rather than that of social welfare maximizationŇ»</seg>
<seg id="1330"> not, for the most part, on the view that society should pursue the maximization of wealth rather than social welfare,</seg>
<seg id="1331"> but instead because law and economics generally favors addressing distributional issues that bear on social welfare solely through the tax system.</seg>
<seg id="1332"> Finally, a third distinguishing feature of much work within law and economics is its sustained interest in explaining and predicting the content, rather than just the effects, of legal rules.</seg>
<seg id="1333"> While a large body of work in economics studies the effects of law (as noted above), outside of work associated with law and economics only political economy has generally given central emphasis to analyzing the content of law, and then only from a particular perspective.</seg>
<seg id="1334"> Given this rough sketch of ˇ°law and economics,ˇ± what then is ˇ°behavioral law and economicsˇ±?</seg>
<seg id="1335"> Behavioral law and economics involves both the development and the incorporation within law and economics of behavioral insights drawn from various fields of psychology.</seg>
<seg id="1336"> As has been widely recognized since the early work by Allais and Ellsberg, some of the foundational assumptions of traditional economic analysis may reflect an unrealistic picture of human behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1337"> Not surprisingly, models based on these assumptions sometimes yield erroneous predictions.</seg>
<seg id="1338"> Behavioral law and economics attempts to improve the predictive power of law and economics by building in more realistic accounts of actorsˇŻ behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1339"> The present paper describes some of the central attributes and applications of behavioral law and economics to date;</seg>
<seg id="1340"> it also outlines an emerging focus in behavioral law and economics on prospects for ˇ°debiasingˇ± individuals through the structure of legal rules.</seg>
<seg id="1341"> Through the vehicle of ˇ°debiasing through law,ˇ± behavioral law and economics may open up a new space within law and economics between,</seg>
<seg id="1342"> on the one hand, unremitting adherence to traditional economic assumptions and,</seg>
<seg id="1343"> on the other hand, broad structuring or restructuring of legal regimes on the assumption that people are inevitably and permanently bound to deviate from traditional economic assumptions.</seg>
<seg id="1344"> The paper proceeds as follows.</seg>
<seg id="1345"> Section 4.2 traces the development and refinement of one of the central insights of behavioral economicsˇŞthat people frequently exhibit an endowment effectˇŞboth outside and within the field of behavioral law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="1346"> Section 4.3 moves to a general overview of the features of human decision making that have informed behavioral law and economics, emphasizing points of departure from work in other areas of behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="1347"> Section 4.4 describes a series of illustrative applications of behavioral law and economics analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1348"> Section 4.5 introduces the concept of debiasing through law, and Section 4.6 concludes.</seg>
<seg id="1349"> 4.2
<seg id="1350"> Early on, law and economics had a central point of contact with behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="1351"> The point of contact was the foundational debate over the Coase Theorem and the ˇ°endowment effectˇ±ˇŞthe tendency of people to refuse to give up entitlements they hold even though they would not have bought those entitlements initially.</seg>
<seg id="1352"> This early point of contact between law and economics and behavioral economics helped to lay the ground for a rich literature down the road on the endowment effect in both behavioral economics and behavioral law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="1353"> 4.2.1
<seg id="1354"> An unquestioned centerpiece of law and economics is the Coase Theorem.</seg>
<seg id="1355"> This theorem posits that allocating legal rights to one party or another will not affect outcomes if transaction costs are sufficiently low;</seg>
<seg id="1356"> thus, for instance, whether the law gives a factory the right to emit pollution next to a laundry or, instead, says the laundry has a right to be free of pollution will not matter to the ultimate outcome (pollution or no pollution) as long as transaction costs are sufficiently low.</seg>
<seg id="1357"> The reason for this result is that, with low transaction costs, the parties should be expected to bargain to the efficient outcome under either legal regime.</seg>
<seg id="1358"> The Coase Theorem is central to law and economics because of (among other things) the theoremˇŻs claim about the domain within which normative analysis of legal rulesˇŞwhether rule A is preferable to rule B or the reverseˇŞis actually relevant.</seg>
<seg id="1359"> The Coase Theorem has also played a central, albeit a rather different, role in the field of behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="1360"> More than fifteen years ago, Kahneman et al. reported the results of a set of experiments designed to provide a careful empirical assessment of the Coase Theorem.</seg>
<seg id="1361"> In one round of experiments, each subject was given an assigned value for a ˇ°tokenˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1362"> (the amount for which the subject could redeem the token for cash at the end of the experiment),</seg>
<seg id="1363"> and half of the subjects were awarded tokens.</seg>
<seg id="1364"> When subjects subsequently had the opportunity to trade tokens for money or (for those not awarded tokens) money for tokens, subjects behaved precisely in accordance with the Coase Theorem.</seg>
<seg id="1365"> Exactly half of the tokens changed hands, as theory would predict (given random assignment of the tokens in relation to the specified values).</seg>
<seg id="1366"> The initial allocation of tokens proved irrelevant.</seg>
<seg id="1367"> These findings are a striking vindication of the Coase Theorem.</seg>
<seg id="1368"> Having thus established that transaction costs in the experimental setting were sufficiently low to vindicate the Coase Theorem, Kahneman et al. went on to study subjectsˇŻ behavior when the good to be traded was not tokens but, rather, Cornell University mugs that the subjects would retain after the experiment (rather than redeeming for an assigned amount of cash).</seg>
<seg id="1369"> In direct contravention of the Coase Theorem, the initial assignment of entitlements to the mug mattered dramatically; those initially given mugs rarely sold them, while those not initially given mugs seldom bought them.</seg>
<seg id="1370"> Following Thaler, Kahneman et al. referred to this effect as the ˇ°endowment effectˇ±ˇŞˇŞthe refusal to give up an entitlement one holds initially even though one would not have been willing to pay to acquire that entitlement had one not held it initially.</seg>
<seg id="1371"> In the presence of the endowment effect, the Coase TheoremˇŻs prediction of equivalent outcomes regardless of the initial entitlement no longer holds.</seg>
<seg id="1372"> This conclusion has obvious importance for the design of legal rules.</seg>
<seg id="1373"> 4.2.2
<seg id="1374"> A central task of law and economics is to assess the desirability of actual and proposed legal rules.</seg>
<seg id="1375"> The endowment effect both preserves a larger scope for such normative economic analysisŇ»</seg>
<seg id="1376"> because the Coase Theorem and the associated claim of irrelevance of legal rules no longer holdˇŞand profoundly unsettles the bases for such analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1377"> The reason that the endowment effect so unsettles the bases for normative economic analysis of law is that in the presence of this effect the value attached to a legal entitlement will sometimes vary depending on the initial assignment of the entitlement.</seg>
<seg id="1378"> Normative analysis will then often become indeterminant, as multiple rules may maximize the desired objective (whether wealth or social welfare) depending on the starting allocation of entitlements.</seg>
<seg id="1379"> As Sunstein and Thaler recently observed, in the presence of the endowment effect a ˇ°cost-benefit study cannot be based on willingness to pay (WTP), because WTP will be a function of the default rule.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1380"> Thus, the cost-benefit study ˇ°must be a more open-ended (and inevitably somewhat subjective) assessment of the welfare consequences.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1381"> The conventional normative economic analysis feasible without the endowment effect often cannot survive in the presence of this effect.</seg>
<seg id="1382"> One possible approach to normative analysis when the value of an entitlement varies depending on the initial assignment of the entitlement is to base legal policy choices not on the joint wealth or welfare of the parties directly in questionˇŞbecause the answer to the question of which rule maximizes their joint wealth or welfare may turn on the initial rule choiceˇŞbut rather on the third-party effects of the competing rules.</seg>
<seg id="1383"> Thus, for instance, if it is unclear whether a particular workplace rule is or is not optimal for employers and employees</seg>
<seg id="1384"> (because employees will value the entitlement granted by the rule at more than its value with the rule in place but less than its value otherwise),</seg>
<seg id="1385"> but the rule will create important benefits for employeesˇŻ families, then perhaps the rule should be adopted.</seg>
<seg id="1386"> An alternative approach to normative analysis with varying entitlement values depending on the initial assignment of the entitlement is to make a judgment about which preferencesˇŞthe ones with legal rule A or the ones with legal rule BˇŞdeserve greater deference.</seg>
<seg id="1387"> Sunstein and Thaler offer some support for this view in the context of default terms in employee savings plans.</seg>
<seg id="1388"> Referring to research showing that employees are much more likely to enroll in a savings plan if enrollment is the default term and employees must affirmatively opt out to be excluded than they are if non-enrollment is the default term and employees must take affirmative steps to enroll,</seg>
<seg id="1389"> Sunstein and Thaler make the normative argument that the enrollment outcome is ˇ°highly likelyˇ± to be better under automatic enrollment than under a default term of non-enrollment because it turns out that very few employees drop out if automatically enrolled.</seg>
<seg id="1390"> They readily acknowledge that ˇ°[s]ome readers might think that our reliance on [employeesˇŻ] behavior as an indication of welfare is inconsistentˇ± with the basic point about indeterminacy of preferences,</seg>
<seg id="1391"> ˇ°[b]ut in fact, there is no inconsistencyˇ± because ˇ°it is reasonable to think that if, on reflection, workers realized that they had been ˇ®trickedˇŻ into saving too much, they might take the effort to opt out.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1392"> Sunstein and Thaler draw an analogy to rules calling for mandatory cooling-off periods before consumer purchases:</seg>
<seg id="1393"> ˇ°The premise of such rules is that people are more likely to make good choices when they have had time to think carefully and without a salesperson present.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1394"> In other words, according to Sunstein and Thaler, we have reason to think that the revealed preferences of the automatically enrolled employee, or the consumer at the end of a cooling-off period, are a more appropriate basis for normative judgment than the revealed preferences of the employee who does not choose to enroll under a default term of non-enrollment, or the consumer before the cooling-off period.</seg>
<seg id="1395"> We will see similar issues in the discussion of bounded willpower in Section 4.3.2 below.</seg>
<seg id="1396"> 4.2.3
<seg id="1397"> Particularly in light of the central relevance of the endowment effect to normative economic analysis of law, it is appropriate to emphasize the important role of context in whether this effect occurs.</seg>
<seg id="1398"> An early literature in law and economics is responsible for helping to shape understandings of when the endowment effect will and will not occur.</seg>
<seg id="1399"> Prior to the Cornell University ˇ°mugs experimentsˇ± described above, a series of law and economics articles had demonstrated a set of domains in which the Coase Theorem was in fact empirically robust.</seg>
<seg id="1400"> Hoffman and SpitzerˇŻs experiments found that in both large and small groups the predictions of the theorem were vindicated.</seg>
<seg id="1401"> Likewise, Schwab showed that the ultimate allocation of entitlements did not turn on their initial allocation in the setting he studied.</seg>
<seg id="1402"> All of these experiments, however, shared with the tokens experiment discussed above the feature that subjectsˇŻ value of each possible outcome was directly specified in dollar terms by the experimenter.</seg>
<seg id="1403"> Thus, the law and economics work from the 1980s showed that if people are told specifically what each outcome is worth to them, they will generally find their way to a value-maximizing outcome, so long as transaction costs are sufficiently low.</seg>
<seg id="1404"> However, the later ˇ°mugs experimentsˇ± demonstrated that this result tends to collapse when actors are not instructed as to the value of outcomes to them.</seg>
<seg id="1405"> Viewed in light of the later work, the law and economics work from the 1980s is best understood as showing some of the important limits on when the endowment effect will be observed and, more generally, the central role of context in influencing the occurrence or nonoccurrence of this effect.</seg>
<seg id="1406"> The work by Plott and Zeiler provides an important recent lens on the role of context in determining the existence and degree of the endowment effect.</seg>
<seg id="1407"> Within behavioral law and economics, recent research has refined the basic point about the importance of context.</seg>
<seg id="1408"> Korobkin, for instance, raised the important question of whether the endowment effect would obtain in the allocation to either prospective sellers or prospective buyers of contract law default rights,</seg>
<seg id="1409"> such as the right of sellers to withhold goods or services after unanticipated natural disasters or other similar events versus the right of buyers to demand goods or services in those circumstances.</seg>
<seg id="1410"> (For instance, if a theater owner has promised to allow its theater to be used by another party on a specific date, but the theater then burns down before that date, does the theater owner have the right not to provide the theater, or does the other party have the right to collect damages for the harm it suffered because the theater proved unavailable?)</seg>
<seg id="1411"> Such contact law entitlements do not attachˇŞand indeed are irrelevantˇŞuntil and unless a contract is ultimately agreed to, and thus Korobkin noted that it was unclear whether the initial allocation of the entitlements through contract law default rules would create the sort of sense of ownership or possession that in turn would generate an endowment effect.</seg>
<seg id="1412"> KorobkinˇŻs experiments support the operation of the endowment effect in this context.</seg>
<seg id="1413"> He finds that if contract law allocates an entitlement to party A unless party A agrees to waive it, then a contract between that party and party B is more likely to award party A that entitlement than if contract law initially allocates the entitlement to party BˇŞeven with seemingly low transaction costs.</seg>
<seg id="1414"> Thus, Korobkin concludes, the endowment effect, and not the Coase Theorem, provides the best account of the effects of contract law default rights.</seg>
<seg id="1415"> The deepening of knowledge about when the endowment effect does and does not occurˇŞacross contract settings and elsewhereˇŞwill help refine our understanding of the scope of this effect and, as a direct consequence, the validity of and limits on conventional normative economic analysis of law.</seg>
<seg id="1416"> 4.3
<seg id="1417"> Although the endowment effect has played a central role in behavioral law and economics, other features of behavioral economics are important as well.</seg>
<seg id="1418"> Following Thaler, it is useful for purposes of behavioral law and economics analysis to view human actors as departing from traditional economic assumptions in three distinct ways: human actors exhibit bounded rationality, bounded willpower, and bounded self-interest.</seg>
<seg id="1419"> All three concepts are defined in the brief discussion below.</seg>
<seg id="1420"> As described below, bounded rationality consists in part of ˇ°judgment errors,ˇ± and along with the usual types of such errors discussed in the existing literature in behavioral economics, behavioral law and economics has recently emphasized a separate form of judgment errorˇŞimplicit bias in how members of racial and other groups are perceived by individuals who consciously disavow any sort of prejudiced attitude; this form of judgment error provides the starting point for the discussion below.</seg>
<seg id="1421"> 4.3.1
<seg id="1422"> Departures from traditional economic assumptions of unbounded rationality may be divided into two main categories, judgment errors and departures from expected utility theory.</seg>
<seg id="1423"> 4.3.1.1
<seg id="1424"> Across a wide range of contexts, actual judgments show systematic differences from unbiased forecasts.</seg>
<seg id="1425"> Within this category of judgment errors, behavioral law and economics has recently emphasized errors in the form of implicit bias in peopleˇŻs perceptions of racial and other group members.</seg>
<seg id="1426"> Implicit Racial and Other Group-Based Bias.</seg>
<seg id="1427"> Perhaps the most elementary definition of the word ˇ°biasˇ± is that a person believes, either consciously or implicitly, that members of a racial or other group are somehow less worthy than other individuals.</seg>
<seg id="1428"> An enormous literature in modern social psychology explores the cognitive, motivational, and other aspects of implicit, or unconscious, forms of racial or other group-based bias.</seg>
<seg id="1429"> This literature, however, has not featured significantly in most fields of behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="1430"> But a clear contrast is behavioral law and economics, which has recently given significant emphasis to the possibility and effects of implicit racial or other group-based bias.</seg>
<seg id="1431"> The behavioral law and economics literature in this area has worked against the backdrop of a heavily Beckerian approach to discrimination.</seg>
<seg id="1432"> Seminal law andeconomics works on discrimination envision such behavior as in significant part a rational response to discriminatory ˇ°tastesˇ± that disfavor association with particular group members.</seg>
<seg id="1433"> The idea of implicit bias, by contrast, suggests that discriminatory behavior often stems not from taste-based preferences that individuals are consciously acting to satisfy, but instead from implicit attitudes afflicting individuals who seriously and sincerely disclaim all forms of prejudice, and who would regard their implicitly biased judgments as ˇ°errors.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1434"> A number of recent works in behavioral law and economics have begun to explore the implications for the analysis of discrimination law of various types of implicit bias</seg>
<seg id="1435"> While social psychologists have identified diverse means of assessing and measuring implicit bias against members of racial and other groups</seg>
<seg id="1436"> a particular measure, known as the Implicit Association Test (IAT), has had particular influence.</seg>
<seg id="1437"> In the IAT, individuals are asked to categorize words or pictures into four groups, two of which are racial or other groups (such as ˇ°blackˇ± and ˇ°whiteˇ±),</seg>
<seg id="1438"> and the other two of which are the categories ˇ°pleasantˇ± and ˇ°unpleasant.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1439"> Groups are paired, so that respondents are instructed to press one key on the computer for either ˇ°blackˇ± or ˇ°unpleasantˇ± and a different key for either ˇ°whiteˇ± or ˇ°pleasantˇ± (a stereotype-consistent pairing);</seg>
<seg id="1440"> or are instructed instead to press one key on the computer for either ˇ°blackˇ± or ˇ°pleasantˇ± and a different key for either ˇ°whiteˇ± or ˇ°unpleasantˇ± (a stereotype-inconsistent pairing).</seg>
<seg id="1441"> Implicit bias is defined as faster categorization when the ˇ°blackˇ± and ˇ°unpleasantˇ± categories are paired than when the ˇ°blackˇ± and ˇ°pleasantˇ± categories are paired.</seg>
<seg id="1442"> The IAT reveals significant evidence of implicit bias, including among those who assiduously deny any prejudice.</seg>
<seg id="1443"> An important question raised by the results on the IAT is whether implicit bias as measured by the test is correlated with individualsˇŻ actual behavior toward members of other groups.</seg>
<seg id="1444"> Several studies, including McConnell and Leibold and Dovidio et al., have found that scores on the IAT and similar tests show correlations with third partiesˇŻ ratings of the degree of general friendliness shown by individuals toward members of other groups.</seg>
<seg id="1445"> Other connections between IAT scores and actual behavior remain an active area of research.</seg>
<seg id="1446"> Although implicit racial or other group-based bias is not conventionally grouped with other forms of bounded rationality within behavioral economics, the fit may be more natural than has typically been supposed.</seg>
<seg id="1447"> Such implicit bias may often result from the way in which the characteristic of race or other group membership operates as a sort of ˇ°heuristicˇ±ˇŞa form of mental shortcut.</seg>
<seg id="1448"> (The concept of a heuristic is discussed more fully just below.)</seg>
<seg id="1449"> Indeed, recent psychology research emphasizes that heuristics often work through a process of ˇ°attribute substitution,ˇ± in which people answer a hard question by substituting an easier one.</seg>
<seg id="1450"> For instance, people might resolve a question of probability not by investigating statistics, but by asking whether a relevant incident comes easily to mind.</seg>
<seg id="1451"> The same process can operate to produce implicit bias against racial or other groups.</seg>
<seg id="1452"> Section 4.5.1, below, describes an example of how implicit bias has been analyzed within behavioral law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="1453"> The ˇ°Heuristics and Biasesˇ± Literature.</seg>
<seg id="1454"> Judgment errors may arise not only from implicit bias against racial or other group members, but also from other biases studied within the so-called ˇ°heuristics and biasesˇ± literature within behavioral eco?nomics.</seg>
<seg id="1455"> Three types of judgment errors from this literature have received particularly sustained attention within behavioral law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="1456"> One such judgment error is optimism bias, in which individuals believe that their own probability of facing a bad outcome is lower than it actually is.</seg>
<seg id="1457"> As a familiar illustration, most people think that their chances of having an auto accident are significantly lower than the average personˇŻs chances of experiencing this event, although of course these beliefs cannot all be correct; if everyone were below ˇ°average,ˇ± then the average would be lower.</seg>
<seg id="1458"> There is also evidence that people underestimate their absolute as well as relative (to other individuals) probability of negative events such as auto accidents.</seg>
<seg id="1459"> Optimism bias is probably highly adaptive as a general matter;</seg>
<seg id="1460"> by thinking that things will turn out well, people may often increase the chance that they will turn out well.</seg>
<seg id="1461"> Section 4.4.1, below, describes an application of optimism bias in the behavioral law and economics literature.</seg>
<seg id="1462"> A second judgment error prominent in behavioral law and economics is self-serving bias.</seg>
<seg id="1463"> Whenever there is room for disagreement about a matter to be decided by two or more partiesˇŞand of course there often is in litigation as well as elsewhereˇŞindividuals will tend to interpret information in a direction that serves their own interests.</seg>
<seg id="1464"> In a compelling field study, Babcock et al. find that union and school board presidents asked to identify ˇ°comparableˇ± school districts for purposes of labor negotiations identified different lists of districts depending on their respective self-interests.</seg>
<seg id="1465"> While the average teacher salary in districts viewed as comparable by union presidents was $27,633, the same average was $26,922 in districts viewed as comparable by school board presidents.</seg>
<seg id="1466"> As Babcock et al. observe, this difference was more than large enough to produce teacher strikes based on the size of past salary disagreements leading to strikes.</seg>
<seg id="1467"> Section 4.4.2, below, discusses an application of self-serving bias in the behavioral law and economics literature.</seg>
<seg id="1468"> A third judgment error extensively discussed in behavioral law and economics is the hindsight bias, in which decision makers attach excessively high probabilities to events simply because they ended up occurring.</seg>
<seg id="1469"> In one striking study, neuro?psychologists were presented with a list of patient symptoms and then asked to assess the probability that the patient had each of three conditions (alcohol withdrawal, AlzheimerˇŻs disease, and brain damage secondary to alcohol abuse).</seg>
<seg id="1470"> While the mean probabilities for physicians who were not informed of the patientˇŻs actual condition were 37%, 26%, and 37%, respectively, for the three conditions, physicians who were informed of the patientˇŻs actual condition routinely said they would have attached much higher probabilities to that condition.</seg>
<seg id="1471"> Even when, as in the study, people are specifically instructed to give the probabilities they would have assigned had they been the one making the initial diagnosis, people seem to have difficulty putting aside events they know to have occurred.</seg>
<seg id="1472"> As highlighted in Section 4.4.5, below, the hindsight bias has clear relevance to the legal system because that system is pervasively in the business of adjudicating likelihoods and foreseeability after an accident or other event has occurred.</seg>
<seg id="1473"> 4.3.1.2
<seg id="1474"> Boundedly rational individuals not only make judgment errors but also deviate from the precepts of expected utility theory.</seg>
<seg id="1475"> While this theory is a foundational aspect of traditional economic analysis, Kahneman and TverskyˇŻs ˇ°prospect theoryˇ± offers a leading alternative to expected utility theory.</seg>
<seg id="1476"> Within behavioral law and economics, the feature of prospect theory that emphasizes the distinction between gains and losses relative to an endowment point has received by far the most attention;</seg>
<seg id="1477"> the relevant work on the endowment effect was discussed in some detail in Section 4.2 above.</seg>
<seg id="1478"> 4.3.2
<seg id="1479"> We often observe individuals choosing to spend rather than save, consume desserts over salads, and go to the movies instead of the gym despite all of their best intentions.</seg>
<seg id="1480"> Why do people fail to follow through on the plans they make? Behavioral economics has emphasized the concept of bounded willpower, which has a long pedigree in economics.</seg>
<seg id="1481"> This concept has featured in behavioral law and economics as well, as illustrated in Section 4.4.4, below.</seg>
<seg id="1482"> Much work in law and economics is normatively oriented, and this feature of the work brings to the fore a set of normative questions about bounded willpowerˇŞ much in the way that normative questions have been prominent in law and economics discussions of the endowment effect.</seg>
<seg id="1483"> With respect to bounded willpower, the central normative question concerns how to view a decision to spend rather than save, to consume desserts rather than salads, or to go to the gym rather than the movies.</seg>
<seg id="1484"> Why should (if they should) the preferences of the self who wishes to save, eat salad, or go to the gym rather than the self who wishes to spend, eat dessert, or watch movies be used as the benchmark in performing normative analysis?</seg>
<seg id="1485"> One possible answer, partially reminiscent of a strand of the endowment effect discussion above, is that saving, eating salad, or going to the gym creates desirable third-party effects that are absent with spending, eating dessert, or watching movies.</seg>
<seg id="1486"> Another possible answer, also with an analogue in the earlier discussion, is that the preferences of the self who wishes to save, eat salad, or go to the gym reflect a considered judgment about the matter in questionˇŞthe rightness of which, however, it is not possible always to keep before oneˇŻs mind.</seg>
<seg id="1487"> Of course, each of these two types of judgments about the relative merits of different preferences may be contentious in at least some settings.</seg>
<seg id="1488"> 4.3.3
<seg id="1489"> In principle, traditional economic analysis is capacious with respect to the range of admissible preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1490"> Preferences that give significant weight to fairness, for instance, can be included in the analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1491"> In practice, however, much of traditional law and economics posits a relatively narrow set of ends that individuals are imagined to pursue.</seg>
<seg id="1492"> Contrary to this conventional approach, bounded self-interest within behavioral economics emphasizes that many people care about both giving and receiving fair treatment in a range of settings.</seg>
<seg id="1493"> As Thaler and Dawes observe:</seg>
<seg id="1494"> In the rural areas around Ithaca it is common for farmers to put some fresh produce on a table by the road.</seg>
<seg id="1495"> There is a cash box on the table, and customers are expected to put money in the box in return for the vegetables they take.</seg>
<seg id="1496"> The box has just a small slit, so money can only be put in, not taken out.</seg>
<seg id="1497"> Also, the box is attached to the table, so no one can (easily) make off with the money.</seg>
<seg id="1498"> We think that the farmers who use this system have just about the right model of human nature.</seg>
<seg id="1499"> They feel that enough people will volunteer to pay for the fresh corn to make it worthwhile to put it out there.</seg>
<seg id="1500"> Of course, a central question raised by bounded self-interest is what counts as ˇ°fairˇ± treatment.</seg>
<seg id="1501"> Behavioral economics suggests that people will judge outcomes as unfair if they depart substantially from the terms of a ˇ°reference transactionˇ±ˇŞa transaction that defines the benchmark for the partiesˇŻ interactions.</seg>
<seg id="1502"> In the basic version of the well-known ultimatum game, for instance, where parties divide a sum of money with no reason to think one party is particularly more deserving than the other, the ˇ°reference transactionˇ± is something like an equal split;</seg>
<seg id="1503"> substantial departures from this benchmark are viewed as unfair and, accordingly, are punished by parties who receive offers of such treatment.</seg>
<seg id="1504"> Section 4.5, below, illustrates how this conception of bounded self-interest has been applied within behavioral law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="1505"> 4.4
<seg id="1506"> The present section offers a set of illustrative applications of behavioral law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="1507"> The discussion seeks to illustrate what has become essentially ˇ°normal scienceˇ± within the literature in behavioral law and economics to date:</seg>
<seg id="1508"> identification of a departure from unbounded rationality, willpower or self-interest, followed by either an account of existing law or a proposed legal reform that takes as a fixed point the identified departure from unbounded rationality, willpower or self-interest.</seg>
<seg id="1509"> Section 4.5 shifts the focus to a new approach within behavioral law and economics, one that emphasizes the potential for responding to some bounds on human behavior not by taking peopleˇŻs natural tendencies as given and shaping law around them but, instead, by attempting to reduce or eliminate such human tendencies through the legal structureˇŞthe approach of ˇ°debiasing through lawˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="1510"> Recent surveys on behavioral law and economics by Guthrie, Korobkin, and Rachlinski have examined existing legally oriented work on bounded rationality, devoting extensive attention to both judgment errors and departures from expected utility theory.</seg>
<seg id="1511"> The present section, by contrast, focuses on a limited number of applications of bounded rationality, willpower and self-interest, attempting to give a fuller picture of some of the relevant work in these areas.</seg>
<seg id="1512"> 4.4.1
<seg id="1513"> As noted in Section 4.1, above, one distinctive feature of law and economics is its frequent focus on wealth maximizationˇŞgiving legal entitlements to those most willing to pay for them, without regard for distributional considerationsˇŞrather than social welfare maximization as the criterion for normative analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1514"> Many law and economics scholars object to ˇ°distributive legal rulesˇ±ˇŞnon-wealth-maximizing legal rules chosen for their distributive consequencesˇŞbecause they believe that distributional issues are best left solely to the tax system.</seg>
<seg id="1515"> A leading law and economics argument in favor of addressing distributional issues through the tax system rather than through nontax legal rules is the argument that any desired distributional consequence can be achieved at lower cost through the tax system than through distributive legal rules.</seg>
<seg id="1516"> Of course, pursuit of distributional objectives through the tax system is not costless; higher taxes on the wealthy will tend to distort work incentives.</seg>
<seg id="1517"> But under traditional economic assumptions precisely the same is true of distributive legal rules: ˇ°[U]sing legal rules to redistribute income distorts work incentives fully as much as the income tax systemˇŞbecause the distortion is caused by the redistribution itself...ˇ±Ł¨Kaplow and Shavell 1994, pp. 667-68).</seg>
<seg id="1518"> Thus, for example, under traditional economic analysis a 30% marginal tax rate, together with a non-wealth-maximizing legal rule that transfers an average of 1% of high earnersˇŻ income to the poor, creates the same distortion in work incentives as a 31% marginal tax rate coupled with a wealth-maximizing legal rule.</seg>
<seg id="1519"> However, the former regime also entails costs due to the non-wealth-maximizing legal rule.</seg>
<seg id="1520"> (For instance, under a distributive legal rule governing accidents, potential defendants may be excessively cautious and thus may be discouraged from engaging in socially valuable activities.)</seg>
<seg id="1521"> Thus, whatever the desired distributive consequences, under traditional economic analysis they can always be achieved at lower cost by choosing the wealth-maximizing legal rule and adjusting distributive effects through the tax system than by choosing a non-wealth-maximizing rule because of its distributive properties.</seg>
<seg id="1522"> A basic premise about human behavior underlies this analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1523"> Work incentives are assumed to be distorted by the same amount as a result of a probabilistic, nontax mode of redistribution, such as the law governing accidents, as they are as a result of a tax.</seg>
<seg id="1524"> Thus, for example, if high-income individuals face a 0.02 probability of incurring tort liability for an accident, then a distributive legal rule that imposes $500,000 extra in damages (beyond what a wealth-maximizing rule would call for) would distort work incentives by the same amount as a tax of $10,000, assuming risk-neutrality.</seg>
<seg id="1525"> Why would distributive tort liability and taxes have the same effects on work incentives?</seg>
<seg id="1526"> ˇ°[W]hen an individual... contemplates earning additional income by working harder, his total marginal expected payments [out of that income] equal the sum of his marginal tax payment and the expected marginal cost on account of accidentsˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="1527"> The expected costs of the two forms of redistribution are the same, and thus behavior is affected in the same way.</seg>
<seg id="1528"> At least that is the assumption that traditional economic analysis makes.</seg>
<seg id="1529"> Is this assumption valid?</seg>
<seg id="1530"> From a behavioral economics perspective, it is not clear that an individual would typically experience the same disincentive to work as a result of a more generous (to victims) tort-law regime as would be experienced as a result of a higher level of taxation.</seg>
<seg id="1531"> The discussion here will highlight one important reason, related to the phenomenon of optimism bias noted in Section 4.3, above, that behavioral law and economics suggests work incentives may be distorted less by distributive tort liabilityˇŞwhich operates probabilistically rather than deterministicallyˇŞthan by taxes.</seg>
<seg id="1532"> Other reasons for different effects of the two regimes, based on different contextual factors across the regimes, are discussed in Jolls(1998).</seg>
<seg id="1533"> As just noted, a salient feature of distributive tort liability is the uncertainty of its application to any given actor.</seg>
<seg id="1534"> The effect of such liability ˇ°tends to be limited to those few who become parties to lawsuitsˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="1535"> While one knows that one will have to pay taxes every year, one knows that one is quite likely not to become involved in an accident.</seg>
<seg id="1536"> To be sure, the possibility of uncertain or randomized taxation has received some discussion in the public finance literature.</seg>
<seg id="1537"> Even supporters of this approach, however, suggest that it is unrealistic from a practical perspective.</seg>
<seg id="1538"> Bounded rationality in the form of optimism biasˇŞthe tendency to think negative events are less likely to happen to oneself than they actually areˇŞsuggests that uncertain events are often processed systematically differently from certain events.</seg>
<seg id="1539"> Section 4.3.1.1 above referred to the general body of evidence suggesting the prevalence of optimism bias;</seg>
<seg id="1540"> there are also empirical studies suggesting that people offer unrealistically optimistic assessments in areas directly related to the effects of distributive tort liability.</seg>
<seg id="1541"> For instance, most people think that they are less likely than the average person to be sued.</seg>
<seg id="1542"> Likewise, people think that they are less likely than the average person to cause an auto accident.</seg>
<seg id="1543"> They also think that their own probability of being caught and penalized for drunk driving is lower than the average driverˇŻs probability of being apprehended for such behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1544"> What does optimism bias with respect to the probability of the negative event of tort liability imply for the distortionary effects of distributive tort liability as opposed to taxes?</seg>
<seg id="1545"> People will tend to underestimate the probability that they will be hit with liability under distributive tort liability;</seg>
<seg id="1546"> therefore, their perceived cost of the rule will be lower.</seg>
<seg id="1547"> As a result, their work incentives will tend to suffer a lesser degree of distortion than under a tax yielding the same amount of revenue for the government.</seg>
<seg id="1548"> For instance, in the numerical example from above, risk-neutral individuals may not attach an expected cost of$10,000 to a 0.02 (objective) probability of having to pay $500,000 extra in damages under distributive tort liability;</seg>
<seg id="1549"> they may tend to underestimate the probability that they will incur liabilityˇŞand thus they may tend to underestimate the expected cost of liabilityˇŞas a result of optimism bias.</seg>
<seg id="1550"> Of course, optimism bias is not the only phenomenon that affects how people assess the likelihood of uncertain events.</seg>
<seg id="1551"> In some cases people may tend to overestimate rather than underestimate the probability of a negative event because the risk in question is highly salient or otherwise available to themˇŞfor instance, contamination from a hazardous waste dump.</seg>
<seg id="1552"> However, the overestimation phenomenon seems relatively unlikely to affect the assessment of distributive tort liability, at least insofar as individuals rather than firms are concerned.</seg>
<seg id="1553"> Consider, for instance, the quintessential event that can expose an individual to tort liability: the auto accident.</seg>
<seg id="1554"> As noted above, people appear to underestimate the probability that they will be involved in an auto accident (relative to the actual probability);</seg>
<seg id="1555"> this presumably results from a combination of underestimation of the general probability of an accident and further underestimation of peopleˇŻs own probability relative to the average personˇŻs.</seg>
<seg id="1556"> The situation would probably be different, of course, for an event such as contamination from a hazardous waste dump, the probability of which might be overestimated due to its availability;</seg>
<seg id="1557"> but highly available events tend to involve firm, not individual, liability.</seg>
<seg id="1558"> It is difficult to come up with examples of events giving rise to individual liability the probability of which is likely to be overestimated rather than (as suggested above) underestimated.</seg>
<seg id="1559"> And with underestimation of the probability of liability, work incentives will typically be distorted less by distributive legal rules than by taxes.</seg>
