<seg id="1560"> 4.4.2
<seg id="1561"> Section 4.1, above, noted that an important aspect of work in law and economics is analysis of various areas of law that were not previously studied by economists.</seg>
<seg id="1562"> One such area concerns the rules governing the litigation process.</seg>
<seg id="1563"> When someone believes that a law has been violated, how does the legal system go about deciding the legitimacy of that claim?</seg>
<seg id="1564"> The U.S. system relies centrally upon an adversary approach, under which competing sides are represented by legal counsel who argue in favor of their respective positions.</seg>
<seg id="1565"> Of course, maximally effective advocacy for a position often requires one to obtain information under the control of oneˇŻs opponent, and thus the U.S. legal system contains a set of rules governing when and how one side in a legal dispute may obtain (ˇ°discoverˇ±) information from the other side.</seg>
<seg id="1566"> Since 1993 these rules have required opposing parties to disclose significant information even without a request by the other party.</seg>
<seg id="1567"> Under conventional economic analysis this approach should tend to increase the convergence of partiesˇŻ expectations and, thus, the rate at which they settle disputes out of court.</seg>
<seg id="1568"> The phenomenon of self-serving bias described in Section 4.3.1.1, above, however, suggests that individuals often interpret information differently depending on the direction of their own self-interest.</seg>
<seg id="1569"> Experimental work by Loewenstein et al., Babcock et al., and Loewenstein and Moore has examined self-serving bias in the specific context of litigation.</seg>
<seg id="1570"> In the first paper in the series, Loewenstein et al. found that parties assigned to the role of plaintiff or defendant interpreted the very same facts differently depending on their assigned role;</seg>
<seg id="1571"> subjects assigned to the plaintiff role offered higher estimates of the likely outcome at trial than subjects assigned to the defendant role even though they both received identical information about the case.</seg>
<seg id="1572"> Moreover, the authors found that subjects who exhibited the highest levels of self-serving bias were also least likely to succeed in negotiating out-of-court settlements.</seg>
<seg id="1573"> This work provided opening evidence of the role of self-serving bias in shaping the effect of information disclosure on the rate at which legal disputes are settled.</seg>
<seg id="1574"> The initial study just described could not rule out the possibility that the relationship between the degree of self-serving bias and the frequency of settlement was non-causal, for it is possible that an unmeasured factor influenced both the degree of self-serving bias and the frequency of settlement.</seg>
<seg id="1575"> In a follow-up study, however, Babcock et al. provided strong evidence that the relationship was in fact causal.</seg>
<seg id="1576"> They found that parties who were not informed of their roles until after reading case materials and offering their estimates of the likely outcome at trial both failed to exhibit statistically significant degrees of self-serving bias and settled at significantly greater rates than parties who were informed of their roles before reading the case materials.</seg>
<seg id="1577"> The timing of exposure to the case materials matters because ˇ°[s]elf-serving interpretations are likely to occur at the point when information about roles is assimilated,ˇ± for the simple reason that it ˇ°is easier to process information in a biased way than it is to change an unbiased estimate once it has been madeˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="1578"> The recent study by Loewenstein and Moore underlines the fact that self-serving bias will operate when there is some degree of ambiguity about the proper or best interpretation of a set of information, as will frequently be the case in litigation.</seg>
<seg id="1579"> The prospect that litigants will interpret at least some information in a self-serving fashion means that the exchange of information in litigation may cause a divergence rather than convergence of partiesˇŻ expectations.</seg>
<seg id="1580"> Relying in part on this argument, Issacharoff and Loewenstein suggest that mandatory disclosure rules in litigation may be undesirable.</seg>
<seg id="1581"> As they describe, self-serving bias undermines the conventional wisdom that ˇ°a full exchange of the information in the possession of the parties is likely to facilitate settlement by enabling each party to form a more accurate, and generally therefore a more convergent, estimate of the likely outcome of the caseˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="1582"> Consistent with Issacharoff and LoewensteinˇŻs argument, a set of amendments in 2000 significantly cut backˇŞalthough they did not completely eliminateˇŞthe mandatory disclosure rules noted above.</seg>
<seg id="1583"> 4.4.3
<seg id="1584"> The third distinctive feature of law and economics discussed in Section 4.1, above, concerned the fieldˇŻs interest in explaining and predicting the content of lawˇŞwhat the law allows and what it prohibits.</seg>
<seg id="1585"> Law and economics has emphasized the idea that laws may be efficient solutions to the problems of organizing society;</seg>
<seg id="1586"> it has also emphasizedˇŞˇŞas has the field of political economyˇŞˇŞthat laws may come about because of the rent-seeking activities of politically powerful actors.</seg>
<seg id="1587"> Behavioral law and economics has extended this conventional account of the content of legal rules in two important ways.</seg>
<seg id="1588"> The first, which is the focus of the discussion in this subsection, is that in many cases incorporation of insights about bounded rationality, willpower and self-interest is needed for a satisfactory understanding of lawˇŻs efficiency properties.</seg>
<seg id="1589"> A law may be efficient in part because of the way in which it accounts for one of the three bounds on human behavior, as the discussion below illustrates.</seg>
<seg id="1590"> The second extension of the conventional law and economics account of the content of law is the expansion of behavioral law and economics beyond the two familiar categories from the traditional accountˇŞthe category of law-as-efficiency-enhancing and the category of law-as-the-product-of- conventional-rent-seeking.</seg>
<seg id="1591"> Section 4.4.5 discusses and illustrates this second extension developed by behavioral law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="1592"> A prominent behavioral law and economics work seeking to understand and explain the efficiency of the content oflaw is Rachlinski.</seg>
<seg id="1593"> Rachlinski examines a number of areas of law, including corporate law.</seg>
<seg id="1594"> A central rule of U.S. corporate law is the ˇ°business judgmentˇ± rule, according to which corporate officers and directors who are informed about a corporationˇŻs activities and who approve or acquiesce in these activities have generally fulfilled their duties to the corporation as long as they have a rational belief that such activities are in the interests of the corporation.</seg>
<seg id="1595"> This highly deferential standard of liability makes it difficult to find legal fault for the decisions of corporate officers and directors.</seg>
<seg id="1596"> Rachlinski suggests that the business judgment rule may be corporate lawˇŻs sensible response to the problem of hindsight bias.</seg>
<seg id="1597"> As described above, hindsight bias suggests that the sorts of decisions routinely made by the legal system, adjudicating likelihoods and foreseeability after a negative event has materialized, will often be biased toward excessively high estimatesˇŞand thus in favor of holding actors responsibleˇŞsimply because the negative event materialized.</seg>
<seg id="1598"> But under the business judgment rule, officers and directors will not be held liable for decisions that turn out badlyˇŞˇ°even if these decisions seem negligent in hindsightˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="1599"> Hindsight bias suggests that things will often seem negligent in hind?sight, once a negative outcome has materialized and is known, so the business judg?ment rule insulates officers and directors from the risk of such hindsight-influenced liability determinations.</seg>
<seg id="1600"> In the absence of the business judgment rule, Rachlinski argues, officers and directors would fail to make the risk-neutral business decisions desired by investors who can limit their overall investment risk through diversification;</seg>
<seg id="1601"> ˇ°[e]nsuring that managers effectively represent this concern and do not avoid business decisions that have a high expected payoff but also carry a high degree of risk is a central problem of corporate governanceˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="1602"> In this respect, hindsight bias can help to explain the efficiency of the content of law governing corporate officers and directors.</seg>
<seg id="1603"> 4.4.4
<seg id="1604"> The behavioral law and economics applications discussed thus far have involved bounded rationality,</seg>
<seg id="1605"> but other applications have drawn on the other two bounds on human behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1606"> This subsection describes an application of the concept of bounded willpower within behavioral law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="1607"> As discussed above, an individual with bounded willpower will often have difficulty sticking to even the best-laid plans.</seg>
<seg id="1608"> With respect to decisions about consumption versus saving, for instance, individuals who earnestly plan to save a substantial amount of next yearˇŻs salary for retirement may tend, once next year arrives, to save far less than planned.</seg>
<seg id="1609"> If the failure to stick to the initial plan is understood in advance, then individuals may seek to precommit themselves to their initial plan.</seg>
<seg id="1610"> An obvious potential means of achieving such precommitment is a contract between the individual suffering from bounded willpower and a bank or other savings institution;</seg>
<seg id="1611"> but the efficacy of this approach from the standpoint of the individual at the time of contemplating such a contract depends critically on whether contracts are, or can be made, nonrenegotiable.</seg>
<seg id="1612"> Down the road it will always be in the partiesˇŻ mutual interest to renegotiate the initial contract, for at later points the individual will be better off if the individual can consume more and save less than the amount the original contract called for,</seg>
<seg id="1613"> and thus at that point there is a surplus from renegotiation to be divided between the parties.</seg>
<seg id="1614"> Only if renegotiation is impossible can the parties avoid the effects of bounded willpower and achieve commitment to the initial plan.</seg>
<seg id="1615"> The obvious question is then whether contract law allows nonrenegotiable contracts.</seg>
<seg id="1616"> Certainly the default rule of contract law is that renegotiated agreements are enforceable.</seg>
<seg id="1617"> The primary exception to enforcement of such agreements concerns renegotiated agreements coerced by one partyˇŻs threat to breach the original contract if renegotiation does not occur.</seg>
<seg id="1618"> But in the model discussed here, renegotiation is truly welfare-enhancingˇŞat the time at which it occursˇŞfor both parties relative to the original contract,</seg>
<seg id="1619"> so the coercion concern does not apply, and thus the default rule would allow enforcement of the renegotiated agreement.</seg>
<seg id="1620"> Does contract law allow the parties to supplement the default rules governing renegotiation with additional terms of their own?</seg>
<seg id="1621"> Perhaps surprisingly, the answer to this question is generally ˇ°no.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1622"> Justice CardozoˇŻs 1919 opinion in Beatty v. Guggenheim Exploration Co. provides a classic example of the rule and its underlying rationale:</seg>
<seg id="1623"> Those who make a contract, may unmake it.</seg>
<seg id="1624"> The clause which forbids a change,may be changed like any other
<seg id="1625"> You may put it out by the door, it is back through the window.</seg>
<seg id="1626"> Whenever two men contract, no limitation self-imposed can destroy their power to contract again.</seg>
<seg id="1627"> While existing contract law thus prohibits enforcement of contractual agreements not to renegotiate, a natural question is whether a contrary rule would be of any effect.</seg>
<seg id="1628"> Any clause limiting or prohibiting renegotiation will be effective only if some party to the contract has an incentive to enforce the clause.</seg>
<seg id="1629"> Jolls provides discussion of circumstances in which this will be the case.</seg>
<seg id="1630"> Consistent with the discussion here, the law has started to move away from the formalistic principle described above and in some instances now permits parties by contract to remove their future power to renegotiate their original contract orally</seg>
<seg id="1631"> (although written renegotiated agreements are still generally enforceable).</seg>
<seg id="1632"> 4.4.5
<seg id="1633"> A final illustration of behavioral law and economics reveals the way that work in this area has expanded upon the traditional law and economics notion that lawˇŻs content reflects either efficiency or conventional rent-seeking.</seg>
<seg id="1634"> The notion that laws emerge from these two considerations would probably strike most citizens as odd.</seg>
<seg id="1635"> Instead, most members of societyˇŞwhich is to say most of the people who are entitled to elect legislatorsˇŞbelieve that the primary purpose of the law is to codify ˇ°rightˇ± and ˇ°wrong.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1636"> Can this idea be formalized, drawing in part on the notion of bounded self-interest from Section 4.3.3 above?</seg>
<seg id="1637"> Consider the case of consumer protection law, which imposes bans on certain market transactions including (in many jurisdictions) ˇ°usuriousˇ± lending and some forms of price gouging</seg>
<seg id="1638"> What accounts for these laws, which impose constraints on gain-producing transactions for ordinary commodities such as television sets and lumber?</seg>
<seg id="1639"> The bans seem difficult to justify on efficiency grounds;</seg>
<seg id="1640"> rules prohibiting mutually beneficial exchanges without obvious externalities are not generally thought to have a large claim to efficiency.</seg>
<seg id="1641"> The laws also do not generally seem well explained in terms of conventional rent seeking by a politically powerful faction.</seg>
<seg id="1642"> By contrast, laws banning usurious lending and price gouging when such activities are prevalent are a straightforward prediction of the theory of bounded self-interest described above.</seg>
<seg id="1643"> (The analysis here assumes that self-interested legislators are responsive to citizensˇŻ or other actorsˇŻ fairness-based demands for the content of law. )</seg>
<seg id="1644"> In the case of such bans, the transaction in question is a significant departure from the usual terms of trade in the market for the good in questionˇŞthat is, a significant departure from the ˇ°reference transaction.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1645"> The account above of bounded self-interest suggests that if trades are occurring frequently in a given jurisdiction at terms far from those of the reference transaction, there will be strong pressure for a law banning such trades.</seg>
<seg id="1646"> Note that the prediction is not that all high prices (ones that make it difficult or impossible for some people to afford things they might want) will be banned;</seg>
<seg id="1647"> the prediction is that transactions at terms far from the terms on which those transactions generally occur in the marketplace will be banned.</seg>
<seg id="1648"> Consider this example:</seg>
<seg id="1649"> A store has been sold out of the popular Cabbage Patch dolls for a month.</seg>
<seg id="1650"> A week before Christmas a single doll is discovered in a store room.</seg>
<seg id="1651"> The managers know that many customers would like to buy the doll.</seg>
<seg id="1652"> They announce over the storeˇŻs public address system that the doll will be sold by auction to the customer who offers to pay the most.</seg>
<seg id="1653"> Kahneman et al.</seg>
<seg id="1654"> Nearly three-quarters of the respondents judged this action to be either somewhat unfair or very unfair, though, of course, an economic analysis would judge the auction the most efficient method of assuring that the doll goes to the person who values it most.</seg>
<seg id="1655"> Although the auction is efficient, it represents a departure from the ˇ°reference transaction,ˇ± under which the doll is sold at its usual price.</seg>
<seg id="1656"> As in the doll example, if money is loaned to individuals at a rate of interest significantly greater than the rate at which similarly sized loans are made to other customers, then the lenderˇŻs behavior may be viewed as unfair.</seg>
<seg id="1657"> Likewise, because lumber generally tends to sell for a particular price, sales at far higher prices in the wake of (say) a hurricane, which drives demand sky high, are thought unfair.</seg>
<seg id="1658"> How then should popular items be rationed?</seg>
<seg id="1659"> Subjects in one study asked whether a football team should allocate its few remaining tickets to a key game through an auction thought that this approach would be unfair,</seg>
<seg id="1660"> while allocation based on who waited in line longest was the preferred solution.</seg>
<seg id="1661"> Of course, waiting in line for scarce goods is precisely what happens with laws against price gouging.</seg>
<seg id="1662"> Thus, pervasive fairness norms appear to shape attitudes (and hence possibly law) on both usury and price gouging.</seg>
<seg id="1663"> While ˇ°[c]onventional economic analyses assume as a matter of course that excess demand for a good creates an opportunity for suppliers to raise pricesˇ± and that ˇ°[t]he profit-seeking adjustments that clear the market are...</seg>
<seg id="1664"> as natural as water finding its levelŇ»and as ethically neutral,ˇ± ˇ°[t]he lay public does not share this indifferenceˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="1665"> Note that the behavioral law and economics analysis does not imply that these views of fairness are necessarily rational or compelling.</seg>
<seg id="1666"> Many of those who think ˇ°usuriousˇ± lenders are ˇ°unfairˇ± might not have thought through the implications of their views</seg>
<seg id="1667"> (for example, that paying an outrageous price for a loan may be better than paying an infinite price, or that a loan to a riskier borrower is a product different in kind from a loan to a safer borrower).</seg>
<seg id="1668"> Still, if such views are widespread, they may underlie certain patterns in the content of law, such as the legal restrictions on usury and price gouging.</seg>
<seg id="1669"> The claim here is a positive one about the content of the law we observe, not a prescriptive or normative one about the shape practices or rules should take.</seg>
<seg id="1670"> As a positive matter, behavioral law and economics predicts that if trades are occurring with some frequency on terms far from those of the reference transaction, then legal rules will often ban trades on such terms.</seg>
<seg id="1671"> Of course, further inquiry would be needed to offer a definitive explanation for the full pattern of usury and price gouging laws we observe.</seg>
<seg id="1672"> Usury seems to be broadly prohibited, so one is not faced with the question of why we observe bans in some states but not others.</seg>
<seg id="1673"> The same cannot be said of price gouging, which is prohibited only in certain states. Price gouging appears to be prohibited primarily by states that have recently experienced (or whose neighbors have recently experienced) natural disasters;</seg>
<seg id="1674"> but more in-depth research would be required to determine if this pattern comprehensively bears out.</seg>
<seg id="1675"> 4.5
<seg id="1676"> The applications described in Section 4.4 illustrate the usual approach in behavioral law and economics work to date:</seg>
<seg id="1677"> the analysis identifies a departure from unbounded rationality, willpower or self-interest and then offers either a proposed legal reform or an account of existing law that takes as a fixed point the identified departure from unbounded rationality, willpower or self-interest.</seg>
<seg id="1678"> This approach might be said to focus on designing legal rules and institutions so that legal outcomes do not fall prey to problems of bounded rationality, willpower or self-interestˇŞa strategy of insulation of those outcomes from such bounds on human behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1679"> A quite different possibility, focused most heavily on the case of judgment errors by boundedly rational actors, is that legal policy may respond best to such errors not by structuring rules and institutions to protect legal outcomes from the effects of the errors (which themselves are taken as a given),</seg>
<seg id="1680"> but instead by operating directly on the errors and attempting to help people either to reduce or to eliminate them.</seg>
<seg id="1681"> Legal policy in this category may be termed ˇ°debiasing through lawˇ±;</seg>
<seg id="1682"> the law is used to reduce the degree of biased behavior actors exhibit.</seg>
<seg id="1683"> The primary emphasis is on judgment errors rather than either other aspects of bounded rationality or bounded willpower or self-interest, for the simple reason that those alternative forms of human behavior cannot uncontroversially be viewed as ˇ°biasesˇ± in need of debiasing.</seg>
<seg id="1684"> (Recall, for instance, the normative complexities discussed above in connection with both the endowment effect and bounded willpower.</seg>
<seg id="1685"> And clearly it would not generally be desirable to ˇ°debiasˇ± boundedly self-interested actors.)</seg>
<seg id="1686"> As described below, the basic promise of strategies for debiasing through law is that these strategies will often provide a middle ground between unyielding adherence to the assumptions of traditional economics, on the one hand,</seg>
<seg id="1687"> and the usual behavioral law and economics approach of accepting departures from those assumptions as a given, on the other.</seg>
<seg id="1688"> 4.5.1
<seg id="1689"> The idea of debiasing through law draws on a substantial existing psychology literature on the debiasing of individuals after a demonstration of the existence of a given judgment error</seg>
<seg id="1690"> Those who have investigated debiasing in experimental settings, however, have generally not explored the possibility of achieving debiasing through law.</seg>
<seg id="1691"> A few behavioral law and economics papers have examined the possibility of debiasing through the procedural rules governing adjudication by judges or juries;</seg>
<seg id="1692"> a well-known example builds on the studies described in Section 4.4.2, above, of self-serving bias in litigation and shows how requiring litigants to consider reasons the adjudicator might rule against them eliminates their self-serving bias.</seg>
<seg id="1693"> However, the potential promise of debiasing through law is far broader, for it is not only the procedures by which law is applied in adjudicative settings but the actual substance of law that may be employed to achieve debiasing.</seg>
<seg id="1694"> Consider an example of debiasing through law developed by Jolls (forthcoming), drawing on the work on implicit racial or other group-based bias described in Section 4.3.1.1, above.</seg>
<seg id="1695"> Might substantive rules governing employment discrimination play a role in debiasing individuals who exhibit such bias?</seg>
<seg id="1696"> Empirical studies suggest that implicit racial or other group-based bias is profoundly influenced by environmental stimuli.</seg>
<seg id="1697"> Individuals who view pictures of TigerWoods and Timothy McVeigh before submitting to testing of implicit racial bias, for example, exhibit substantially less bias than individuals not exposed to the pictures of Woods and McVeigh.</seg>
<seg id="1698"> This study and, more broadly, the large social science literature on debiasing in response to implicit racial or other group-based bias have an intriguing practical counterpart in the ongoing controversies at many universities and the U.S. Capitol over the frequent pattern of largely or exclusively white, male portraits adorning classrooms and ceremonial spaces.</seg>
<seg id="1699"> In the employment context, it may not be irrelevant to the degree of implicit racial or other group-based bias found in employment decision makers whether, for instance, the walls of the workplace feature sexually explicit depictions of womenˇŞthe source of frequent sexual harassment lawsuitsˇŞor instead feature more positive, affirming images of women.</seg>
<seg id="1700"> Employment discrimination lawˇŻs policing of what can and cannot be featured in the workplace environment, described in detail in Jolls (forthcoming), is thus an illustration of debiasing through substantive law.</seg>
<seg id="1701"> It is important to emphasize the limits of the domain of this analysis of employment discrimination law as a mechanism for achieving ˇ°debiasingˇ± in the sense in which the term is used here.</seg>
<seg id="1702"> In some cases racial or other group-based bias may reflect genuine tastes rather than, as discussed above, a divergence of implicit attitudes and behavior from non-discriminatory tastes.</seg>
<seg id="1703"> Of course, the features of employment discrimination law just referenced might still be desirable, and would certainly remain applicable, in the case of consciously discriminatory tastes,</seg>
<seg id="1704"> but they would no longer illustrate a form of debiasing through law in the sense used here because no form of judgment error would be under correction in the first place.</seg>
<seg id="1705"> 4.5.2
<seg id="1706"> The example of debiasing through employment discrimination law and the ear?lier example from Babcock, Issacharoff and LoewensteinˇŻs work of debiasing through restructuring the adjudicative process together illustrate the basic distinction between debiasing through substantive law and debiasing through procedural rules.</seg>
<seg id="1707"> Figure 4.1 generalizes the point by mapping the terrain of strategies for debiasing through law more fully. The column division marks the line between procedural rules governing the adjudicative process and substantive rules regulating actions taken outside of the adjudicative process.</seg>
<seg id="1708"> The row division marks the line between debiasing actors in their capacity as participants in the adjudicative process and debiasing actors in their capacity as decision makers outside of the adjudicative process.</seg>
<seg id="1709"> The upper-left box in this matrix represents the type of debiasing through law on which the prior work on such debiasing has focused:</seg>
<seg id="1710"> the rules in question are procedural rules governing the adjudicative process, and the actors targeted are individuals in their capacity as participants in the adjudicative process</seg>
<seg id="1711"> Moving counterclockwise, the lower-left box in the matrix is marked with an ˇ°Xˇ± because procedural rules governing the adjudicative process do not have any obvious role in debiasing actors outside of the adjudicative processŇ»</seg>
<seg id="1712"> although these rules certainly may affect such actorsˇŻ behavior in various ways by influencing what would happen in the event of future litigation.</seg>
<seg id="1713"> The lower-right box in the matrix represents the category of debiasing through law emphasized in Jolls (forthcoming) and Jolls and Sunstein:</seg>
<seg id="1714"> the rules in question are substantive rules regulating actions taken outside of the adjudicative process, and the actors targeted are decision makers outside of the adjudicative process.</seg>
<seg id="1715"> Finally, the upper-right corner of the matrix represents a hybrid category that warrants brief discussion, in part to demarcate it from the category (just discussed) of debiasing through substantive law.</seg>
<seg id="1716"> In this hybrid category, it is substantive, rather than procedural, law that is structured to achieve debiasing, but the judgment error that this debiasing effort targets is one that arises within, rather than outside of, the adjudicative process.</seg>
<seg id="1717"> For example, FarnsworthˇŻs work on self-serving bias suggests that such bias on the part of employment discrimination litigants (actors in their capacity as participants in an adjudicative process) might be reduced by restructuring employment discrimination standards (substantive rules regulating action outside of the adjudicative process) to increase the reliance of such standards on objective facts as opposed to subjective or normative judgments.</seg>
<seg id="1718"> This type of debiasing through law operates through reform of substantive law rather than procedural rules, but the actions to be debiased are those of litigants within the adjudicative process.</seg>
<seg id="1719"> In the case of debiasing through substantive law, by contrast, both the legal rules through which debiasing occurs and the capacities in which actors are targeted for debiasing are distinct from the context of the adjudicative process.</seg>
<seg id="1720"> 4.6
<seg id="1721"> In Richard ThalerˇŻs view, the ultimate sign of success for behavioral economics will be that what is now behavioral economics will become simply ˇ°economics.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1722"> The same observation applies to behavioral law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="1723"> Debiasing through law, discussed in Section 4.5, may hasten the speed at which this transition occurs by pointing to a wide range of possibilities for recognizing human limitations while at the same time avoiding the step of paternalistically removing choices from peopleˇŻs hands.</seg>
<seg id="1724"> Because debiasing through law cannot be applied in every context, however, future work in behavioral law and economics should also seek to refine and strengthen analyses concerned with structuring legal rules in light of the remaining (post-debiasing) departures from traditional economic assumptions of unbounded rationality, willpower and self-interest.</seg>
<seg id="1725"> COMMENT BY IAN AYRES</seg>
<seg id="1726"> This is an excellent paper that is jam-packed with characteristic examples of what has been going on in the field of behavioral law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="1727"> It also gives insight into JollsˇŻs own approach to this topic, which is natural because she is already such a prominent player in the area.</seg>
<seg id="1728"> For the purposes of this comment, I will adopt the role of skeptic to give the reader a taste of the criticisms that are frequently made in law school seminars whenever a behavioral economic analysis is presented.</seg>
<seg id="1729"> Although JollsˇŻs piece tries to preempt these arguments, it is nevertheless useful to make them explicit.</seg>
<seg id="1730"> Before proceeding, however, I wanted to pause to emphasize that this chapter does a very neat thing in connecting two different ˇ°biasˇ± literatures.</seg>
<seg id="1731"> This term is central to the study of bounded rationality and bounded willpower but also to the study of group subordination.</seg>
<seg id="1732"> By making the link, Professor Jolls forces us to rethink the multiple meanings of bias.</seg>
<seg id="1733"> Bias does not only mean biased tastes (as in the Beckerian concept of discriminatory preferences) and biased beliefs (as in falsifiably wrong statistical inferences);</seg>
<seg id="1734"> it can also mean biased associations which are neither tastes nor falsifiable.</seg>
<seg id="1735"> Keeping track of the type of bias is crucially important in normative analysis, because it is not clear whether education can cure biased association,</seg>
<seg id="1736"> even though there is at least the hope that it might cure falsifiably wrong beliefs.</seg>
<seg id="1737"> For example, in recent research I found that passengers in New Haven tipped black cab drivers approximately one third less than they did white drivers.</seg>
<seg id="1738"> A substantial portion of this shortfall was caused by passengersˇŻ heightened chance of leaving no tip when driving with black drivers, but another portion of the shortfall had a more subtle cause:</seg>
<seg id="1739"> passengers who rounded their total payment (fare plus tip) to an integer dollar amount were more likely to round up when driving with a white driver and to round down when driving with a black driver.</seg>
<seg id="1740"> To me, this rounding result may be a close analogue to an Implicit Association Test (IAT) moment.</seg>
<seg id="1741"> Imagine that just before your cab comes to a stop the fare clicks over to $7.</seg>
<seg id="1742"> Think fast.</seg>
<seg id="1743"> What to do you do?</seg>
<seg id="1744"> Do you leave $8 or $9?</seg>
<seg id="1745"> Called upon to make a quick decision, even people who think that they are hard-wired percentage tippers may find that unconscious factors influence this rounding decision.</seg>
<seg id="1746"> Just as people who confront the IAT sorting game often cannot help but treat blacks and whites differently, people who confront the dichotomous rounding game may have trouble purging the influence of race.</seg>
<seg id="1747"> But if this is an IAT moment, it is not clear that educating passengers about the problem of discrimination (and thereby making race more salient) is the way to reduce discrimination.</seg>
<seg id="1748"> Instead, as Professor Tim Wilson suggests, it might be preferable to prime passengers with a debiasing or reframing cue, possibly by putting a picture of Tiger Woods or Michael Jordan in the cab.</seg>
<seg id="1749"> Predicting Behavior</seg>
<seg id="1750"> One of the most frequent criticisms of behavioral law and economics is that it predicts too much.</seg>
<seg id="1751"> Instead of ˇ°impossibilityˇ± theorems, behavioral law and economics has produced tons of interesting ˇ°possibilityˇ± theorems.</seg>
<seg id="1752"> But, as with the Bible, one can find scriptural support for just about any proposition.</seg>
<seg id="1753"> Now, there are many ways for behavioralists to respond to this criticism.</seg>
<seg id="1754"> Many behavioralists respond by going on the offensive, arguing that nonbehavioral economics does not do such a good job of predicting behavior either.</seg>
<seg id="1755"> Professor Jolls does not take this tack.</seg>
<seg id="1756"> Instead she acknowledges the problem, and argues that a prime value of the behavioral approaches is in simply in destabilizing false consensus for nonbehavioral results, calling for more empirical work.</seg>
<seg id="1757"> But Professor Jolls goes further, I think, with her example of redistributive legal rules and suggests that behavioral thinking might at times shift the burden of persuasion to nonbehavioralists.</seg>
<seg id="1758"> She responds to Kaplow and ShavellˇŻs arguments that a redistributive car accident rule will have the same income-distortion effect as an analogous progressive tax, with the insight that optimism bias may keep the rich from reacting to a probabilistic redistribution as severely as to a nonprobabilistic tax</seg>
<seg id="1759"> (ˇ°I wonˇŻt get in an accidentˇ±).</seg>
<seg id="1760"> At seminars, there is a basically a 100% chance that someone would ask,</seg>
<seg id="1761"> ˇ°But are there other cognitive errors that might cut in the other direction?ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1762"> But Jolls heads them off at the pass.</seg>
<seg id="1763"> She acknowledges that an availability heuristic might cut the other way.</seg>
<seg id="1764"> But she goes on to argue, fairly persuasively, that it is not likely to be as powerful a phenomenon in this specific context.</seg>
<seg id="1765"> Predicting Content of Law</seg>
<seg id="1766"> I am less convinced by Professor JollsˇŻs discussion of the ability of behavioral law and economics to predict the content of legal rules.</seg>
<seg id="1767"> First, as a descriptive matter, I do not see very many behavioralist articles trying to predict the content of legalrules.</seg>
<seg id="1768"> Contra to Jolls, I do not see this as a central goal of the behavioral law and economics movement.</seg>
<seg id="1769"> And with good reason: Professor JollsˇŻs description of the new behavioral school project still parallels the largely discredited project of the old Chicago school.</seg>
<seg id="1770"> The meta argument is this: behavioral law and economics can better identify the things that lawmakers want (efficiency, fairness, etc.);</seg>
<seg id="1771"> the law tends toward these things;</seg>
<seg id="1772"> so behavioral law and economics will do a better job of identifying legal rules.</seg>
<seg id="1773"> But, while George Priest provided a (nonbehavioral) theory for explaining why common judging tends toward efficiency, there is no behavioral theory suggesting why either the common law or statutes would tend the things (like efficiency or fairness) that legislators want.</seg>
<seg id="1774"> The same bounded rationality and bounded willpower that distorts private decision-making should also distort the decisions of individual public decision makers.</seg>
<seg id="1775"> If behavioral law and economics is going to develop an internally consistent theory for predicting the content of legal rules, it should not be a theory of efficient law or fair law, but a theory of framing distortions and the like that predict systematic deviations from either efficiency or public choice theories of the legal production function.</seg>
<seg id="1776"> Harnessing Bias</seg>
<seg id="1777"> Professor Jolls is on a much stronger footing when discussing her own project with Cass Sunstein on ˇ°debiasing through law.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1778"> Instead of using law to disable bias, Professor Jolls has creatively imagined pragmatic ways that legal rules could reduce various cognitive biases of private decision makers.</seg>
<seg id="1779"> An example of the disabling bias approach might be the attempts to keep factoring companies from cashing out future cash flows.</seg>
<seg id="1780"> Bounded willpower problems make people too willing to sacrifice their future inference, so it may be best to simply disable their decision-making power.</seg>
<seg id="1781"> But another approach is to use law to reduce the bias itself.</seg>
<seg id="1782"> You might explain to individuals the intertemporal inconsistency of their hyperbolic preferences as a way to discourage early cash outs.</seg>
<seg id="1783"> But there is yet another meta approach with regard to bias.</seg>
<seg id="1784"> And this is to embrace existing biases and harness them for the public good.</seg>
<seg id="1785"> Indeed, Professor JollsˇŻs own example of redistributive legal rules can be thought of as an example of harness?ing bias instead of an attempt to debias.</seg>
<seg id="1786"> Redistributing wealth via probabilistic tort liability harnesses optimism bias to reduce the distortionary reactions of the wealthy.</seg>
<seg id="1787"> (Indeed, Professor JollsˇŻs insight suggests that redistribution advocates might consider other cognitively opaque devices for income distribution, including probabilistic taxes, and ex post insurance premia.)</seg>
<seg id="1788"> There are many other examples of this harnessing bias.</seg>
<seg id="1789"> The tit-for-tat strategy in finitely repeated prisonerˇŻs dilemma games can be, from a nonbehavioral perspective, strictly irrational.</seg>
<seg id="1790"> But from a behavioral perspective the strategy is a natural outgrowth of bounded self-interest.</seg>
<seg id="1791"> The law might want to assiduously avoid reducing this strategy.</seg>
<seg id="1792"> Katharine Baker and I are working on an article that might be seen as trying to economize on the preexisting bias of acquaintance rapists.</seg>
<seg id="1793"> The same misogynist attitudes that might lead a certain class of man not to pay adequate attention to whether a woman has actually consented to sex might also lead to overestimates of the probability that women will bring unsubstantiated claims of coercion.</seg>
<seg id="1794"> Baker and I try to exploit this latter bias by creating the possibility of criminal liability for first-encounter sex unless a condom is used.</seg>
<seg id="1795"> From a behavioral perspective, this law might have the biggest impact on men with the largest misogynist bias.</seg>
<seg id="1796"> But as Peter Diamond pointed out in discussion, the legal strategy of exploiting bias needs to be carefully justified on independent grounds.</seg>
<seg id="1797"> It is a form of paternalism that can be used for good or ill.</seg>
<seg id="1798"> Yale Law School has a loan forgiveness policy that can reimburse graduates in low-paying jobs for a part of their tuition fees.</seg>
<seg id="1799"> (Although the programˇŻs intent is to promote public interest law, its income-based trigger is economically equivalent to a ˇ°money-back guaranteeˇ± or ˇ°failure insurance.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="1800"> It also has the feature of transforming traditional student loans into Tobin loans where the amount paid becomes a function of future income.)</seg>
<seg id="1801"> A graduate in a low paying-job can have 100% of her loans reimbursed in as little as ten years, but the bulk of the forgiveness comes in the second half of this period: years six to ten.</seg>
<seg id="1802"> A normative justification for more strongly weighting the back end is that from a life-cycle perspective graduates who dabble in public interest law for a year or two before going to a lucrative firm job are less deserving of loan forgiveness than those who toil in the fields for several years.</seg>
<seg id="1803"> But from a behavioral perspective, the program exploits the bias of incoming students, who strongly overestimate the probability that they will dedicate their careers to public service.</seg>
<seg id="1804"> (This is an optimism bias where they are overly optimistic about earning a lower salary!)</seg>
<seg id="1805"> Professor Diamond notes that exploiting this bias lets Yale Law School engage in more extreme forms of price discrimination that fly below the studentsˇŻ cognitive radar screens.</seg>
<seg id="1806"> It may also distort student choice between schools.</seg>
<seg id="1807"> Ultimately, the strategy may still be justified.</seg>
<seg id="1808"> But the justification for any strategy of exploiting bias will ultimately have to rest on an independent normative analysis of the probable consequences.</seg>
<seg id="1809"> Comment by Christoph Engel Introduction</seg>
<seg id="1810"> The evolution of disciplines is path dependent.</seg>
<seg id="1811"> Had there not been scholars like Guido Calabresi, Richard Posner, or Steven Shavell, there would be no such thing as law and economics</seg>
<seg id="1812"> Had there not been scholars like Cass Sunstein, Christine Jolls or Don Langevoort, there would be no such thing as behavioral law and economics</seg>
<seg id="1813"> Behavioral law and economics is a response to some of the most severe limitations of the law and economics approach.</seg>
<seg id="1814"> Actually, it is mostly an adaptation of behavioral economics to law.</seg>
<seg id="1815"> In its core, the approach is thus piggybacking on an unease felt by a growing number of economists with the rigor of the basic assumptions in the rational-choice model.</seg>
<seg id="1816"> As it turns out, many of the experimental findings generated by behavioral economists are highly relevant for the law as well.</seg>
<seg id="1817"> Likewise, the models developed by behavioral economists can often be put to good use by lawyers.</seg>
<seg id="1818"> Behavioral law and economics thus is indeed a valuable enterprise.</seg>
<seg id="1819"> But it is not the only way in which the law can open itself up to the scientific study of behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1820"> The alternative approach is best labeled ˇ°law and psychologyˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="1821"> On first hearing, this may sound old-fashioned.</seg>
<seg id="1822"> For more than a century, lawyers have been interested in psychological findings, but topics have been different.</seg>
<seg id="1823"> Classic questions have been the study of free will, criminal behavior, or the credibility of witness testimony.</seg>
<seg id="1824"> However, the impact of psychological findings and concepts to understanding the law as a governance tool is, optimistically speaking, underdeveloped.</seg>
<seg id="1825"> This comment contrasts the two approaches.</seg>
<seg id="1826"> Behavioral Law and Economics</seg>
<seg id="1827"> In the interest of making both approaches comparable, behavioral law and economics may be characterized by the following stylized facts: a descriptive methodology, a normative benchmark, a type of prescriptive advice to policymakers, and an analytical handle on the political process.</seg>
<seg id="1828"> Descriptively, behavioral law and economics is an extension of classic law and economics, and of neoclassical economics more generally.</seg>
<seg id="1829"> Along with these two approaches, it subscribes to methodological individualism.</seg>
<seg id="1830"> Behavior is the independent variable.</seg>
<seg id="1831"> The research challenge is to explain outcomes, given behavioral dispositions.</seg>
<seg id="1832"> Usually, some set of restrictions is also taken as given, be that budget constraints, information asymmetries, or institutional intervention.</seg>
<seg id="1833"> Given these restrictions, actors are assumed to maximize individual utility.</seg>
<seg id="1834"> Classic law and economics applies this methodology to explaining the effect of legal institutions on outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="1835"> Behavioral law and economics does the same, but replaces classic Homo oeconomicus assumptions with experimental findings.</seg>
<seg id="1836"> Its standard research objects are systematic deviations from standard rationality, called biases.</seg>
<seg id="1837"> Characteristically, behavioral law and economics is thus interested in optimization under behavioral constraints.</seg>
<seg id="1838"> Classic law and economics has taken much of its appeal from its normative strand.</seg>
<seg id="1839"> Scholars in this tradition have thus not only borrowed from the descriptive side of economics, but also from welfare economics.</seg>
<seg id="1840"> Their aim was to demonstrate the hidden efficiency of legal institutions.</seg>
<seg id="1841"> With an interesting twist, behavioral law and economics follows suit.</seg>
<seg id="1842"> Here, the (often implicit) benchmark is not social, but individual.</seg>
<seg id="1843"> Any behavior that falls short of the Homo oeconomicus assumptions is seen as below standard.</seg>
<seg id="1844"> This has led to ˇ°anti-antipaternalismˇ±, and to ˇ°debiasingˇ± (see above).</seg>
<seg id="1845"> Prescriptively, quite some consumer protection legislation can be justified this way</seg>
<seg id="1846"> Not so rarely, individual bias is also seen as a social problem, calling for institutional intervention</seg>
<seg id="1847"> An additional attractive feature of the rational-choice model is its applicability to political process</seg>
<seg id="1848"> Legally speaking, rule generation and rule application can therefore be explained within the same model</seg>
<seg id="1849"> A logical extension is behavioral public choice and the behavioral analysis of law making.</seg>
<seg id="1850"> A classic example of the latter is the interpretation of politicians as ˇ°availability entrepreneurs,ˇ± surfing on a good scandal to push anissue.</seg>
<seg id="1851"> There are good epistemic reasons for behavioral law and economics.</seg>
<seg id="1852"> Two philosophers can be cited in support.</seg>
<seg id="1853"> Hans Albert demonstrates that ignoring any facts is a necessary precondition for seeing and understanding.</seg>
<seg id="1854"> He who tries to see everything will see nothing.</seg>
<seg id="1855"> Modeling is inescapable.</seg>
<seg id="1856"> It should therefore not be held against behavioral law and economics that it deliberately ignores many psychological insights about behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1857"> Kuhn shows that science develops by revolutions and explains why.</seg>
<seg id="1858"> A paradigm is not given up because it has been falsified, or has become implausible, at the margin.</seg>
<seg id="1859"> Developing a paradigm is a costly social endeavor.</seg>
<seg id="1860"> A discipline would be foolish to embark on building a new paradigm from scratch as long as the old one still basically worked.</seg>
<seg id="1861"> Scientists should thus not compare the reality of the current paradigm with the utopia of a fully developed alternative paradigm.</seg>
<seg id="1862"> Consequently, one should not blame behavioral law and economics for staying too close to neoclassical economics as long as alternative approaches are not viable.</seg>
<seg id="1863"> Law and Psychology</seg>
<seg id="1864"> Against this backdrop, the alternative approach of law and psychology becomes discernible, as do the significant challenges inherent in it.</seg>
<seg id="1865"> The presentation follows the same four steps from description to norm, and from prescription to political process.</seg>
<seg id="1866"> Not only are economics and psychology different disciplines, but they also differ in the predominant way they define themselves.</seg>
<seg id="1867"> In its neoclassical mainstream, economics is no longer defined by the economy as an issue area.</seg>
<seg id="1868"> Rather, the field is defined by its method: methodological individualism.</seg>
<seg id="1869"> Largely, the self-definition is even narrower.</seg>
<seg id="1870"> An economist is a person who uses the rational-choice model.</seg>
<seg id="1871"> At least this is the part of economics on which law and economics relies.</seg>
<seg id="1872"> Psychology, however, defines itself by human behavior as its field of observation .</seg>
<seg id="1873"> Currently, this difference goes along with deemphasizing theoretical work, and predominantly focusing on experimental evidence</seg>
<seg id="1874"> This state of affairs makes it difficult to contrast the economic and the psychological paradigms.</seg>
<seg id="1875"> Instead, the following observations illustrate the differences by one prominent example: habit-driven decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="1876"> Most of the time, most people do not engage in careful reasoning before they take a decision.</seg>
<seg id="1877"> Rather, they rely on ready-made tools, which are often called heuristics.</seg>
<seg id="1878"> They thus do not calculate whether they should sell their cars and use public transport for getting to work.</seg>
<seg id="1879"> They simply take their cars because they always have.</seg>
<seg id="1880"> This is not to say that they would never switch to public transport.</seg>
<seg id="1881"> But a mere change in the opportunity structure, say cheaper tickets for the train, will not in and of itself take people out of their mobility habits, for habits are made to use as little information as possible.</seg>
<seg id="1882"> Thereby, the human mind is freed from superfluous cognitive effort.</seg>
<seg id="1883"> Even more radically, habit has no judgmental component at all. There is no weighing of advantages and disadvantages.</seg>
<seg id="1884"> Actually, there is not even a motivational component, apart from the general drive to respond to a cue.</seg>
<seg id="1885"> All that happens is matching.</seg>
<seg id="1886"> The mind checks whether the situation at hand comes close enough to the mental model of a situation for which the habit has been acquired.</seg>
<seg id="1887"> As with behavioral law and economics, defining a normative benchmark should start at the individual rather than the social level.</seg>
<seg id="1888"> In principle, there are two options.</seg>
<seg id="1889"> The first option stays fairly close to rational choice.</seg>
<seg id="1890"> It takes the cost of generating, transmitting, and processing information into account.</seg>
<seg id="1891"> This leads to a transaction cost approach, and to a variant of optimization under constraints.</seg>
<seg id="1892"> Along the same lines, the scarcity of mental resources could be taken into account.</seg>
<seg id="1893"> The second option is more radical.</seg>
<seg id="1894"> It has been dubbed ˇ°ecological rationalityˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="1895"> It starts from a different definition of reality.</seg>
<seg id="1896"> Rational choice is most powerful in a certain environment.</seg>
<seg id="1897"> It still works well if both the problem space and the probabilities of possible events are known ex ante.</seg>
<seg id="1898"> At the limit, it can be extended to situations where probabilities are unknown.</seg>
<seg id="1899"> But rational choice is inappropriate in situations of ignorance, i.e., with open problem space.</seg>
<seg id="1900"> Such problems are categorically ill-defined.</seg>
<seg id="1901"> Moreover, rational choice is not the right norm if the problem is well-defined, but overly complex, like a game of chess.17 In such contexts, aiming for perfection is pointless.</seg>
<seg id="1902"> Being good enough is what matters.</seg>
<seg id="1903"> Consequently, the benchmark cannot be defined in the abstract.</seg>
<seg id="1904"> It must be taken from the context itself.</seg>
<seg id="1905"> Specifically, the actor should aim at productively exploiting those features of the context that fit best his personal abilities.</seg>
<seg id="1906"> Adaptation to a context is hence not ad hoc, but a process.</seg>
<seg id="1907"> Individual learning history matters.</seg>
<seg id="1908"> Turning this into a social norm, i.e., a welfare criterion, is more demanding.</seg>
<seg id="1909"> The classic Pareto criterion is open to individuals who do not know the environment.</seg>
<seg id="1910"> But it assumes that they would know what they want, were they to know the environment.</seg>
<seg id="1911"> However, in a fundamentally uncertain environment, or in one characterized by prohibitive complexity, desires must be adaptive too.</seg>
<seg id="1912"> Two things are crucial.</seg>
<seg id="1913"> If socially relevant information is present in part of the population, it should travel.</seg>
<seg id="1914"> Conversely, socially detrimental reactions to the environment should stay as local as possible.</seg>
<seg id="1915"> If the predominant reaction is a heuristic, socially beneficial heuristics should thus spread, and socially detrimental heuristics should be contained.</seg>
<seg id="1916"> Consequently, communication and learning become decisive for welfare.</seg>
<seg id="1917"> Moreover, in uncertain or overly complex environments, society permanently faces a trade-off between maintaining viability and remaining open to surprise.</seg>
<seg id="1918"> In the short run, viability is the main concern.</seg>
<seg id="1919"> If too many members of society start from different assumptions about reality, the scope for socially beneficial cooperation shrinks dramatically.</seg>
<seg id="1920"> However, in the long run, society must be able to replace previously predominant assumptions about reality in the light of patent counter evidence</seg>
<seg id="1921"> If addressees are likely to decide by a heuristic argument, this must also have implications for institutional design.</seg>
<seg id="1922"> Again, one example must suffice.</seg>
<seg id="1923"> Heuristics exploit very little information.</seg>
<seg id="1924"> Rule change is a rare event.</seg>
<seg id="1925"> Typically, in decision-making by heuristics, the stability of the underlying normative expectations will therefore be taken for granted.</seg>
<seg id="1926"> Consequently, it is not evident that legal reform changes behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1927"> The legislator is well advised to see the law is properly learned.</seg>
<seg id="1928"> This means a number of things.</seg>
<seg id="1929"> Addressees must be induced to pay attention.</seg>
<seg id="1930"> Since usually they are not professional lawyers, the technical doctrinal change must be translated into normative expectations a standard addressee is able to understand.</seg>
<seg id="1931"> Usually, the legislator is interested in long-term behavioral change.</seg>
<seg id="1932"> The importance of heuristics for making new law in Parliament or in the courts is still very much a virgin territory.</seg>
<seg id="1933"> Two dimensions seem worth exploring.</seg>
<seg id="1934"> The first dimension is heuristics in the rule-making process itself.</seg>
<seg id="1935"> In principle, one should expect that heuristics play a remarkable role here, for the degree of perceived complexity and uncertainty could hardly be more pronounced than in policy-making.</seg>
<seg id="1936"> However, both parliamentary legislation and judicial legal policy-making are embedded in a dense institutional setting.</seg>
<seg id="1937"> To date, knowledge about the interaction between heuristics and institutions is still fairly limited.</seg>
<seg id="1938"> The second dimension is heuristics in addressees as anticipated in rule-making.</seg>
<seg id="1939"> At first sight, this leads to a rationalistic nightmare. Not all people use heuristics all the time.</seg>
<seg id="1940"> They keep their ability of rational reasoning.</seg>
<seg id="1941"> Moreover, heuristics are individual reactions to perceived environment.</seg>
<seg id="1942"> For both reasons, addressees are likely to exhibit heterogeneity.</seg>
<seg id="1943"> Seemingly, the complexity of the rule-making task thus explodes.</seg>
<seg id="1944"> However, there are two benevolent qualifications.</seg>
<seg id="1945"> Not so rarely, the legislator or the courts can make educated guesses about behavioral dispositions.</seg>
<seg id="1946"> This is particularly promising if they are interested only in average responses, not in reaching each and every single addressee.</seg>
<seg id="1947"> For people do not normally build their heuristics from scratch: they learn them by observing their peers.</seg>
<seg id="1948"> Arguably, one can even push the idea one step further.</seg>
<seg id="1949"> Since rule-making is an activity under high uncertainty, rule-makers themselves should develop pertinent heuristics for successfully navigating in this environment.</seg>
<seg id="1950"> Conclusion</seg>
<seg id="1951"> Stylized facts are bound to overly accentuate differences.</seg>
<seg id="1952"> If one looks at individual contributions in the literature, it will often not be easy to classify them as either behavioral law and economics or law and psychology.</seg>
<seg id="1953"> The point of this comment, therefore, is as follows:</seg>
<seg id="1954"> the catchy label ˇ°behavioral law and economicsˇ± should not lure lawyers into looking exclusively at behavioral economics for insights.</seg>
<seg id="1955"> Psychology proper is as attractive for the enterprise of laying behavioral foundations for law as governance.</seg>
<seg id="1956"> 5.1
<seg id="1957"> Most empirical tests of the many competing theories of wage rigidity use publicly available data on pay rates and employment that reveal little about the institutions and motivations that explain wage behavior.</seg>
<seg id="1958"> In order to learn more, some economists have analyzed unusual sources of data or have conducted surveys and experiments.</seg>
<seg id="1959"> Management scientists and organizational psychologists have for years been collecting data relevant to wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="1960"> I here report on what I know of these sources of information about the origins of wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="1961"> 5.2
<seg id="1962"> It is sensible to check whether wages really are downwardly rigid before considering why this is the case.</seg>
<seg id="1963"> This question is surprisingly hard to answer, because appropriate data are lacking.</seg>
<seg id="1964"> It is not even clear what the appropriate definition of the wage should be.</seg>
<seg id="1965"> A firmˇŻs marginal costs depend on the average hourly nominal labor cost per job.</seg>
<seg id="1966"> Employee welfare depends on total nominal compensation per worker.</seg>
<seg id="1967"> A third possibility is nominal compensation for an employee with a given job tenure and continuing in the same position with the same employer under fixed working conditions.</seg>
<seg id="1968"> If the employee is paid by the hour, it is the hourly rate plus the fringe benefits that count.</seg>
<seg id="1969"> Total compensation is the relevant pay rate for salaried employees, where total compensation is the salary plus fringe benefits and bonuses.</seg>
<seg id="1970"> This third definition is the one most closely associated with employeesˇŻ and managersˇŻ notions of fairness and hence is most pertinent to the managerial concerns that explain downward wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="1971"> In order to adhere even more closely to the sense of fairness prevailing in business, it might be advisable to include only base pay and exclude variable components, such as bonuses.</seg>
<seg id="1972"> The three pay rates can change independently.</seg>
<seg id="1973"> For instance, the average hourly labor costs of a job can increase with no change in any workerˇŻs pay, if the seniority of workers assigned to the job increases.</seg>
<seg id="1974"> Similarly, changes in hours worked or in job assignments can change an individualˇŻs total pay without changing hourly pay rates or labor costs for any job.</seg>
<seg id="1975"> There are conceptual ambiguities associated with benefits.</seg>
<seg id="1976"> For instance, if an increase in the cost of a given medical insurance policy were shared between a firm and its workforce, the firmˇŻs nominal labor cost per job would increase,</seg>
<seg id="1977"> but workers would probably feel that the total value of their medical benefits had decreased.</seg>
<seg id="1978"> A wage cut should be defined as a reduction in the wage of the third definition above, the pay of an employee continuing to work under unchanged conditions.</seg>
<seg id="1979"> Unfortunately, this pay rate is the most difficult to measure, because it requires knowledge of much more than just total pay.</seg>
<seg id="1980"> Lebow et al. (2003) is the only study I know of that measures the first definition of wage, the firmˇŻs average labor costs.</seg>
<seg id="1981"> The authors use U.S. Bureau of Labor Statistics data and find that wage costs are somewhat rigid in the downward direction, though there is a considerable amount of wage reduction.</seg>
<seg id="1982"> There is a large literature that uses surveys of the income of individual workers to study variation in the third kind of wage.</seg>
<seg id="1983"> The sources of data are household panel surveys and social security data.</seg>
<seg id="1984"> Some of these authors struggled with possible errors in the reporting of earnings.</seg>
<seg id="1985"> All of the studies suffer from ignorance of changes in hours worked, job assignments, bonuses, or working conditions, so that it is not clear that the data reveal the wage of the third definition.</seg>
<seg id="1986"> All the studies report large amounts of wage reduction.</seg>
<seg id="1987"> Goux is able to attribute about 60% of the pay reductions to better working conditions as from a change from night to day shift, a change in occupation, or a change in the annual bonus.</seg>
<seg id="1988"> She has no information on changes in hours worked.</seg>
<seg id="1989"> None of the other authors have had such detailed information.</seg>
<seg id="1990"> The difficulties of the surveys cited are discussed in Kramarz (2001) and Howitt (2002).</seg>
<seg id="1991"> Surveys of firms reach conflicting conclusions on wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="1992"> Kaufman (1984), Blinder and Choi (1990), Agell and Lundborg (1999, 2003), Agell and Bennmarker (2002, 2003), and Bewley (1999) simply asked employers whether they had reduced pay.</seg>
<seg id="1993"> The responses probably apply to the third definition of wage, but one cannot be sure.</seg>
<seg id="1994"> None of the firms in KaufmanˇŻs sample of twenty-six British firms had considered nominal wage cuts during the recession occurring at the time of his study.</seg>
<seg id="1995"> Blinder and Choi found a high incidence of pay reduction, in five of the nineteen American firms they studied.</seg>
<seg id="1996"> Agell and Lundborg, on the other hand, found almost no wage cutting;</seg>
<seg id="1997"> two out of 153 responding Swedish firms had experienced nominal wage cuts during the previous seven years, a period of high unemployment and low inflation.</seg>
<seg id="1998"> Agell and Bennmarker found that only 1.1% of workers received a pay cut during the deep recession in Sweden in the 1990s.</seg>
<seg id="1999"> The wage cuts that did occur were for just a few employees.</seg>
<seg id="2000"> Swedish laws that make it difficult to reduce pay may explain the near absence of wage cutting in the studies by Agell and his coauthors.</seg>
<seg id="2001"> Although I conducted my survey during a recession and actively sought out firms that had cut pay, I found a low incidence of pay cuts;</seg>
<seg id="2002"> of 235 businesses studied, 24 had reduced the base pay of some or all employees during the recession of the early 1990s.</seg>
<seg id="2003"> Similarly conflicting results appear in surveys of union wage agreements.</seg>
<seg id="2004"> In Current Wage Developments and the Monthly Labor Review, the Bureau of Labor Statistics reports on general wage changes for both union and nonunion manufacturing production workers for the years 1959 through 1978.</seg>
<seg id="2005"> These data show a negligible number of wage reductions: cuts for less than 0.5% of the workers in every year.</seg>
<seg id="2006"> (The corresponding percentage for my sample was 0.14 %.)</seg>
<seg id="2007"> Conflicting evidence has been found by Mitchell (1985), who uses Bureau of Labor Statistics data to calculate that 13% of all workers covered by major new contracts suffered wage cuts in 1983.</seg>
<seg id="2008"> Similarly, Fortin (1996) finds that 6% of 1,149 large non-COLA union wage settlements in Canada from 1992 to 1994 involved wage cuts.</seg>
<seg id="2009"> Much less ambiguous evidence of downward rigidity in the third kind of wage is contained in the few studies that use company records to learn the histories of job assignments, hours worked, and pay of individual employees.</seg>
<seg id="2010"> The studies include Baker et al. (1994), Wilson (1997), Altonji and Devereux (2000), Dohmen (2003), and Fehr and Gotte (2005).</seg>
<seg id="2011"> Unfortunately, these authors study only six firms.</seg>
<seg id="2012"> Baker et al. study one firm.</seg>
<seg id="2013"> Wilson studies two, one of which is the firm studied by Baker et al.</seg>
<seg id="2014"> Altonji and Devereux study a third.</seg>
<seg id="2015"> Dohmen studies a bankrupt Dutch firm, and Fehr and Gotte study two Swiss firms.</seg>
<seg id="2016"> Altonji and Devereux, Dohmen, and Fehr and Gotte report data on both hourly and salaried workers.</seg>
<seg id="2017"> The other two studies have information only on salaried employees.</seg>
<seg id="2018"> All five studies find a negligible number of pay reductions.</seg>
<seg id="2019"> Altonji and Devereux find that 2.5% of hourly workers experienced wage cuts, but almost all of these were ˇ°associated with changes between full- and part-time status, or with changes in whether performance incentives are part of compensation.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2020"> These findings are reinforced by a telephone survey that Akerlof et al. (1996) made of 596 people in the Washington, DC, area.</seg>
<seg id="2021"> The key question was: ˇ°Excluding overtime, commissions, and bonuses, has your base rate of pay changed since a year ago today?ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2022"> A negligible number reported pay reductions.</seg>
<seg id="2023"> Given the form of the question, this evidence probably pertains to the third definition of wages.</seg>
<seg id="2024"> Contradicting this evidence are two similar surveys conducted in New Zealand in 1992 and 1993, where 8% and 5%, respectively, of the respondents reported hourly wage reductions.</seg>
<seg id="2025"> More work should be done: no one has yet conducted a large survey that accurately measures the incidence of cuts in pay according to the third definition.</seg>
<seg id="2026"> 5.3
<seg id="2027"> There are eight surveys by economists of business managers responsible for compensation policy.</seg>
<seg id="2028"> The goal of seven of these was to learn the reasons for downward wage rigidity, the studies of Kaufman (1984), Blinder and Choi (1990), two surveys by Agell and Lundborg (1995, 1999, 2003), and single studies by Campbell and Kamlani (1997), Bewley (1999), and by Agell and Bennmarker (2002, 2003).</seg>
<seg id="2029"> The eighth study, that of Levine (1993), also contains relevant information.</seg>
<seg id="2030"> Although the findings of the studies differ to some extent, they give a consistent picture of the sources of wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="2031"> I also discuss a paper by Smith (2002), who analyzes a survey of British workers.</seg>
<seg id="2032"> I first summarize my own findings, based on interviews with 246 company managers and nineteen labor leaders in the Northeast of the United States during the early 1990s, when unemployment was high because of a recession.</seg>
<seg id="2033"> I present my findings as reflecting the views of managers, though labor leaders had almost exactly the same opinions on the matters discussed.</seg>
<seg id="2034"> The primary resistance to wage reduction comes from upper management, not from employees.</seg>
<seg id="2035"> The main reason for avoiding pay cuts is that they damage morale.</seg>
<seg id="2036"> Morale has three components.</seg>
<seg id="2037"> One is identification with the firm and an internalization of its objectives.</seg>
<seg id="2038"> Another is trust in an implicit exchange with the firm and with other employees;</seg>
<seg id="2039"> employees know that aid given to the firm or to coworkers will eventually be reciprocated, even if it goes unnoticed.</seg>
<seg id="2040"> The third component is a mood that is conducive to good work.</seg>
<seg id="2041"> The mood need not be a happy one, though happiness is important for the performance of some jobs, such as those that involve dealing with customers.</seg>
<seg id="2042"> The mood could be dislike of an unpleasant job combined with grim focus on achievement or pride in accomplishment.</seg>
<seg id="2043"> Good morale is not equivalent to happiness or job satisfaction.</seg>
<seg id="2044"> Workers may be content, simply because they do nothing.</seg>
<seg id="2045"> Good morale has to do with a willingness voluntarily to make sacrifices for the company and for coworkers.</seg>
<seg id="2046"> A general sense of fairness is conducive to good morale; it contributes to an atmosphere of mutual trust.</seg>
<seg id="2047"> The sense of fairness is created by having supervisors treat workers decently, by having impartial rules for settling disputes and determining promotions and job assignments, and by using reasonable standards for setting the relative pay of different employees.</seg>
<seg id="2048"> These standards are often elaborate systems and are termed internal pay structures.</seg>
<seg id="2049"> They fix pay differentials on the basis of such factors as training, experience, tenure at the firm, and productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2050"> The structures are extremely important, because perceived pay inequity within a firm can cause indignation and disrupt work.</seg>
<seg id="2051"> The standards of internal equity are somewhat arbitrary and can depend strongly on company tradition.</seg>
<seg id="2052"> The pay standards often do not specify that pay be proportional to productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2053"> Many employers believe that productivity of the workforce as a whole is maximized if pay does not fully reflect productivity, though some individuals might produce more if given stronger financial incentives.</seg>
<seg id="2054"> There is a division of opinion within business about how sensitive pay should be to productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2055"> Big income differentials due to differences in productivity can cause resentment, especially if productivity is difficult to measure, which it often is.</seg>
<seg id="2056"> Many firms, nevertheless, use piece rates when productivity can be measured unambiguously, and, even when piece rates are impractical, ordinary notions of equity require that differences in peopleˇŻs contributions be rewarded financially to some extent.</seg>
<seg id="2057"> The sensitivity of pay to productivity may be blunted by the influence on pay of other factors, such as longevity with the firm.</seg>
<seg id="2058"> No matter how sensitive the pay of individuals is to their productivity, firms automatically keep the average pay of broad categories of workers roughly equal to the average value of their marginal product by adjusting the number of workers in each category to the profit-maximizing level.</seg>
<seg id="2059"> Managers are concerned about morale because of its impact on labor turnover, on recruitment of new employees, and on productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2060"> Disgruntled employees are likely to quit as soon as they find another job.</seg>
<seg id="2061"> A companyˇŻs best recruiters are its employees, so that it is important not to have them complain about their company.</seg>
<seg id="2062"> Morale has little impact on productivity in the sense of speed in carrying out routine tasks.</seg>
<seg id="2063"> Habit and working conditions largely determine this sort of productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2064"> Managers have in mind the impact of morale on workersˇŻ willingness to do the extra thing, to encourage and help each other, to make suggestions, and to work well even when not supervised.</seg>
<seg id="2065"> Also, workers with bad morale waste time complaining to each other.</seg>
<seg id="2066"> In considering the impact of morale on productivity, it is important to realize that supervision is so expensive that many employees are not closely supervised and have a significant amount of freedom on the job.</seg>
<seg id="2067"> Except in some low-level jobs, employers rely on workersˇŻ voluntary cooperation and do not simply give orders.</seg>
<seg id="2068"> When considering why wage cuts hurt morale, it is necessary to distinguish new from existing employees.</seg>
<seg id="2069"> The morale of existing employees is hurt by pay cuts, because of an insult effect and a standard-of-living effect.</seg>
<seg id="2070"> Workers are used to receiving regular pay increases as a reward for good work and loyalty, and so interpret a pay cut as an affront and a breach of implicit reciprocity, even if the pay of all employees is reduced.</seg>
<seg id="2071"> Individual workers may take a pay cut less personally if everyoneˇŻs pay falls, but when everyone in a company suffers they stimulate each otherˇŻs discontent by griping.</seg>
<seg id="2072"> The standard-of-living effect is the resentment caused by the fall in income;</seg>
<seg id="2073"> workers blame their employer when they find their lifestyles curtailed.</seg>
<seg id="2074"> This effect is closely related to what experimental economists call loss aversion.</seg>
<seg id="2075"> The arguments just given do not apply to newly hired workers.</seg>
<seg id="2076"> They probably would hardly care if their firm had a general pay cut just before they were hired.</seg>
<seg id="2077"> It is possible, however, to reduce the pay of newly hired workers while continuing to give normal pay increases to existing employees;</seg>
<seg id="2078"> Firms have experimented with such two-tier pay structures,</seg>
<seg id="2079"> and managers say that new workers hired in the lower tier may be glad at first to have their jobs, but that later their attitude changes after they learn that their pay violates the traditional internal pay structure.</seg>
<seg id="2080"> They believe they were being treated unfairly, their resentment hurts their morale, and their discontent can spread to the senior workers on the old pay scale.</seg>
<seg id="2081"> Resistance to wage reduction and the need for internal pay equity both stem from ideas of fairness that usually refer to some reference wage.</seg>
<seg id="2082"> The reference wage for pay cuts is the past wage.</seg>
<seg id="2083"> The reference wage for internal equity is that of other workers at the work place with similar qualifications and a similar job.</seg>
<seg id="2084"> The fairness of wages has little to do with profits or productivity, though both workers and managers find it appropriate that employees share in the success of their company.</seg>
<seg id="2085"> Although managers attempt to use reasonable criteria when establishing an internal pay structure, once a structure is established, tradition by itself makes it a standard of fairness.</seg>
<seg id="2086"> The explanation of downward pay rigidity just given is closely related to the morale theory proposed by Solow (1979), Akerlof (1982), and Akerlof and Yellen (1988,1990).</seg>
<seg id="2087"> They assert that morale and hence productivity increase with the wage and that the trade-off between labor costs and productivity determines a wage that is independent of the unemployment rate.</seg>
<seg id="2088"> Akerlof (1982) uses his gift-exchange model to explain the link between the wage and morale.</seg>
<seg id="2089"> According to this model, workers offer more effort than is demanded by the employer in exchange for pay rates in excess of market clearing levels, so that effort increases with the wage level.</seg>
<seg id="2090"> I do not believe that this theory is fully accurate, because employers say they do not see much connection between effort or morale and wage levels;</seg>
<seg id="2091"> productivity and morale do not increase when pay levels rise, though they can be hurt by pay reductions or disappointingly small raises.</seg>
<seg id="2092"> Even generous pay raises do not increase morale or productivity, because workers quickly get used to increases and grow to believe they have a right to them.</seg>
<seg id="2093"> They soon lose track of any idea that they should offer extra effort in exchange for higher pay.</seg>
<seg id="2094"> Employers do not think about a trade-off between labor costs and the productivity of existing employees when setting pay, though managers do consider the trade-off between labor costs and the quality of labor that a firm can attract and retain.</seg>
<seg id="2095"> In the theory of Akerlof, Solow, and Yellen, morale depends on the level of the wage, whereas in the explanationI have described, wages affect morale only when reduced.</seg>
