<seg id="2096"> What is accurate in the Akerlof-Solow-Yellen theory is the idea that employers avoid cutting pay because doing so would hurt morale.</seg>
<seg id="2097"> What the theory misses is that employees usually have little notion of a fair or market value for their services and quickly come to believe they are entitled to their existing pay, no matter how high it may be.</seg>
<seg id="2098"> Workers usually do not use pay rates at other firms as reference wages, for they know too little about them.</seg>
<seg id="2099"> Although pay cuts are unusual, they do occur and those that occur usually do not have the harmful effects described by managers when arguing that pay should not be cut.</seg>
<seg id="2100"> The explanation for this inconsistency is that pay cuts are accepted by the workforce if they prevent a firm from closing or if they save a large number of jobs.</seg>
<seg id="2101"> Managers were confident they could convince the workforce that a pay cut was necessary, if it were in fact so, and they do not cut pay unless they view the reduction as necessary.</seg>
<seg id="2102"> One of the puzzles discussed in the literature on wage rigidity has been why firms lay off workers rather than reduce their pay.</seg>
<seg id="2103"> I found that most managers believe that the elasticity of their companyˇŻs demand for labor is so low that pay cuts would not reduce an excess supply of labor within the firm.</seg>
<seg id="2104"> The elasticity is small, because direct labor is a small fraction of marginal costs and the price elasticity of product demand is far from infinite.</seg>
<seg id="2105"> Only in firms with a high elasticity of product demand, such as construction companies, is it believed that pay cuts would significantly increase the demand for labor.</seg>
<seg id="2106"> Many of the pay cuts that occurred were made in such firms or in those that were in danger of closing.</seg>
<seg id="2107"> Other firms where pay reduction was an alternative to layoff were those that laid off workers simply to save money, not to get rid of excess labor, and there were many such companies.</seg>
<seg id="2108"> The main argument for preferring layoffs to pay cuts is that layoffs do less damage to morale.</seg>
<seg id="2109"> Laid off workers suffer, but they are no longer in the firm.</seg>
<seg id="2110"> In the words of one manager, ˇ°Layoffs get the misery out the door.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2111"> Good management practice is to save up potential layoffs, make a large number all at once, and then to assure workers who remain that there will be no more for some time.</seg>
<seg id="2112"> Any damage to morale from layoffs is temporary, whereas that from a pay reduction is long-term.</seg>
<seg id="2113"> Other arguments are that layoffs increase productivity, whereas pay cuts hurt it, and layoffs give management some control over who leaves, whereas the best workers are likely to quit when pay is reduced.</seg>
<seg id="2114"> The tendency for the best to quit is a concern in many firms, because the leveling effects of internal equity on pay mean that pay for workers within a job category increases less than their contribution to profits.</seg>
<seg id="2115"> Another consideration is that feasible layoffs often save much more money than feasible pay cuts, which usually cannot be more than about 20% of base pay.</seg>
<seg id="2116"> Layoffs save the fixed costs of employment, which are often substantial, whereas cuts in pay reduce only its variable part.</seg>
<seg id="2117"> Another puzzle appearing in the economics literature is why unemployed workers do not try to take jobs away from employed people by offering to replace them at lower pay.</seg>
<seg id="2118"> Solow (1990) has proposed that the unemployed do not engage in such undercutting because of a social convention against it.</seg>
<seg id="2119"> I found that explicit undercutting is impossible for most people, because they do not know exactly what job they are applying for or what its pay is.</seg>
<seg id="2120"> However, it is not uncommon during periods of high unemployment for job applicants to offer to work for extremely low pay.</seg>
<seg id="2121"> These offers are not frowned upon, but are almost never accepted, because accepting the offers would violate the internal pay structure and could demoralize the new hire.</seg>
<seg id="2122"> Low offers may, however, lead to reduced pay during the initial probationary period of employment.</seg>
<seg id="2123"> A similar puzzle is why firms do not take advantage of recessions to replace employees with cheaper unemployed ones.</seg>
<seg id="2124"> Lindbeck and Snower (1988) proposed, with their insider-outsider theory, that firms seldom replace workers because the employees who had not been replaced would harass and refuse to cooperate with and train the new ones, thereby reducing their productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2125"> I found that the main reasons employers do not replace employees are that the new ones would lack the skills of the existing ones and replacement would demoralize the workforce.</seg>
<seg id="2126"> The skills would be lost in part because many of them are specific to the firm.</seg>
<seg id="2127"> Managers agreed that, after replacement, workers who had not been replaced might boycott the new ones, but asserted that other factors took precedence over this possibility as an explanation of why employees were not replaced.</seg>
<seg id="2128"> Keynes (1936) proposed that downward wage rigidity is explained by employeesˇŻ preoccupation with pay differentials with respect to workers in similar jobs at other firms.</seg>
<seg id="2129"> I found, however, that such external pay differentials are not an issue, except in highly unionized industries.</seg>
<seg id="2130"> In most companies, employees know so little about pay rates at other firms that they do not know whether they are underpaid.</seg>
<seg id="2131"> Although labor unions do try to keep their members informed of pay rates at other companies, unions are weak in the United States.</seg>
<seg id="2132"> A popular explanation of wage rigidity is the ˇ°no shirkingˇ± theory of Shapiro and Stiglitz (1984).</seg>
<seg id="2133"> According to their model, managers induce workers to perform well by firing them if their productivity falls below a prescribed level.</seg>
<seg id="2134"> Job loss is more costly to the worker the higher is his or her wage, so that higher wages make it possible to insist on greater productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2135"> According to the theory, managers set the wage so as to optimize the trade-off between wage costs and productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2136"> This theory does not really explain downward wage rigidity, because it implies that wages should decline when unemployment increases.</seg>
<seg id="2137"> As unemployment rises, it becomes harder to find a new job, so that firing is more costly to the worker and the theory implies that firms can then obtain the same productivity at lower wages.</seg>
<seg id="2138"> Despite this drawback, the theory is so popular among economists that I frequently asked managers and labor leaders about it and was almost always told that it did not apply.</seg>
<seg id="2139"> As was explained in connection with AkerlofˇŻs gift-exchange model, employers do not believe there is much connection between pay and morale, except when pay is reduced.</seg>
<seg id="2140"> Nor do employers obtain cooperation by threatening to fire shirkers.</seg>
<seg id="2141"> To do so would create a negative atmosphere that could damage morale and encourage rebeliousness.</seg>
<seg id="2142"> Workers may malinger on the job, but are seldom dismissed for doing so, except during the short probationary period after hiring.</seg>
<seg id="2143"> Shirking is usually dealt with through discussions and reprimands, and workers are normally fired only because of a pattern of egregious behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2144"> Managers elicit effort by explaining clearly what is expected, facing employees with their shortcomings in a constructive manner, pointing out the importance of the tasks performed, showing interest and appreciation, and making workers feel they are valued members of the organization.</seg>
<seg id="2145"> Most employees like to work and to cooperate and want to please their boss.</seg>
<seg id="2146"> Despite the inapplicability of the ˇ°no shirkingˇ± theory, the incentive mechanism posited in it can be effective.</seg>
<seg id="2147"> For instance, employees do work harder during economic slowdowns, when new jobs are difficult to find and layoffs are imminent, especially if layoffs are made on the basis of performance, that is, if the least productive workers in a job category are laid off first.</seg>
<seg id="2148"> The increase in effort occurs both because job loss becomes more dangerous during a slowdown and because workers try to avoid layoff by being cooperative and productive.</seg>
<seg id="2149"> Because layoffs stem from circumstances not controlled by management, they do not generate the hostility that might be generated by systematically firing slackers.</seg>
<seg id="2150"> Although firing is not used to incite work effort, financial incentives are thought to be very effective in doing so and are believed not to impair morale.</seg>
<seg id="2151"> Incentives can even improve morale, because workers find it fair that they be rewarded for their contributions to the company.</seg>
<seg id="2152"> When incentives are not exaggerated, they contribute to internal equity.</seg>
<seg id="2153"> Discipline and even firing can do so as well, because workers who make the effort to do their job well and obey company rules can be outraged if they see others get away with flagrant misbehavior.</seg>
<seg id="2154"> The main purposes of firing are to protect the company from malefactors and incompetents and to maintain internal equity.</seg>
<seg id="2155"> Dismissals that are managed correctly earn managers respect.</seg>
<seg id="2156"> What is to be avoided is an atmosphere of retribution that menaces everyone.</seg>
<seg id="2157"> This assertion appears not to apply, however, to low-level jobs.</seg>
<seg id="2158"> There was evidence that employers do sometimes use coercion to motivate workers in low-paid jobs that require little training and where employees are easily supervised.</seg>
<seg id="2159"> Another popular explanation of wage rigidity is the adverse selection model of Weiss (1980, 1990).</seg>
<seg id="2160"> There are two versions, having to do with quits and hiring, respectively.</seg>
<seg id="2161"> In the quits version, managers prefer layoffs to pay cuts, because the best workers leave if pay is reduced, whereas if managers lay off workers they can select those who leave.</seg>
<seg id="2162"> According to the hiring version, managers believe that the higher the level of pay that a job applicant is willing to accept, the higher is his or her unobservable quality, and pay offers to new hires are determined by the trade-off between worker quality and pay.</seg>
<seg id="2163"> Weiss asserts that the relation between pay and job candidate quality is determined by alternative employment in the secondary sector, where quality is perfectly observable.</seg>
<seg id="2164"> The secondary sector is home production or jobs that have high turnover and are usually part-time.</seg>
<seg id="2165"> The hiring version of WeissˇŻs adverse selection theory applies to the primary sector, where jobs are long-term and usually full-time.</seg>
<seg id="2166"> He assumes that real wages in the secondary sector are downwardly rigid because of constant returns to labor in production there.</seg>
<seg id="2167"> According to the theory, this downward rigidity is then transferred to the primary sector through the impact of adverse selection on hiring pay.</seg>
<seg id="2168"> I found strong support for WeissˇŻs theory as it applies to quits, but none as it applies to hiring.</seg>
<seg id="2169"> Although managers do believe that a pay cut would cause their best employees to quit, I found no evidence that recruiters use pay aspirations as an indicator of job candidate quality.</seg>
<seg id="2170"> Job recruiters treated the trade-off between pay and worker quality as a basic fact of life, but they did not infer candidate quality from pay demands.</seg>
<seg id="2171"> Recruiters used the trade-off as a reason for not reducing pay only for skills that were in short supply despite the economic slowdown.</seg>
<seg id="2172"> For most skills, they believed they could hire all the workers they needed during the recession at lower rates of pay.</seg>
<seg id="2173"> The secondary sector does not sustain candidatesˇŻ reservation wages.</seg>
<seg id="2174"> Hiring pay is more flexible in the secondary than the primary sector: the opposite of the situation predicted by WeissˇŻs theory.</seg>
<seg id="2175"> Two-tier or multiple-tier wage structures are commonplace in the secondary sector, because the part-time and casual nature of the jobs keeps workers from getting to know each other well and so reduces the need to maintain internal pay equity.</seg>
<seg id="2176"> KaufmanˇŻs (1984) results support my main findings.</seg>
<seg id="2177"> He conducted interviews in twenty-six British firms in 1982 during a period of high unemployment.</seg>
<seg id="2178"> He too found that employers ˇ°believed they could find qualified workers at lower wages.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2179"> He found that employers avoid replacing workers with cheaper ones because of the value of skills and of long-term employment relationships.</seg>
<seg id="2180"> Employers avoid pay cuts because of concern about productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2181"> Because supervision is costly, employers rely ˇ°heavily on the goodwill of their employees.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2182"> Workers view wages as ˇ°a reward for performing competentlyˇ± and would regard a wage cut as an ˇ°affront.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2183"> Employers avoid hiring new employees at lower pay rates than existing ones because doing so would create ˇ°intolerable frictions,ˇ± especially with ˇ°the newer workers who would eventually become disgruntled about the two-tier wage structure.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2184"> Managers feel they can cut nominal pay if ˇ°severe cutbacks or closure will be necessary unless the nominal wage cuts are enacted.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2185"> Blinder and Choi (1990) interviewed managers in nineteen firms, and their findings largely agree with my own.</seg>
<seg id="2186"> They found little evidence to support WeissˇŻs idea that job candidatesˇŻ wage demands are useful indicators of productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2187"> Few of Blinder and ChoiˇŻs nineteen respondents thought that a higher wage would induce greater work effort, though a majority thought that a wage cut would diminish effort.</seg>
<seg id="2188"> The majority said effort would decrease because of reduced morale.</seg>
<seg id="2189"> None mentioned the decreased penalty for being fired.</seg>
<seg id="2190"> A majority of the respondents believed that higher unemployment would bring greater work effort.</seg>
<seg id="2191"> All respondents answering the question felt that a wage cut would increase labor turnover, though only one of the five firms that had recently reduced pay had experienced a significant increase in quits.</seg>
<seg id="2192"> ˇ°The reason for the wage cut seemed to matter</seg>
<seg id="2193"> Generally, wage reductions made to save the firm from failure or to align wages with those of competitors are viewed as justifiable and fair while those made just to raise profits are not.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2194"> Managers felt strongly that having a wage policy viewed as unfair ˇ°would affect workeffort, quits, and the quality of future applicants</seg>
<seg id="2195"> Attitudes like this must be strong deterrents to implementing an ˇ®unfairˇŻ wage policy though.. .that does not necessarily rule out wage reductions under the right circumstancesˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="2196"> Blinder and Choi found strong support for the idea that worker concern about relative wages is a reason for downward wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="2197"> The question asked, however, did not distinguish between internal and external pay comparisons, so that the support given to KeynesˇŻs relative wage theory is ambiguous.</seg>
<seg id="2198"> Campbell and Kamlani (1997) surveyed 184 firms, sending questionnaires to managers, who were asked to rate the importance of various statements on a scale from one to four.</seg>
<seg id="2199"> Most of their findings agree with my own and those of the other surveys.</seg>
<seg id="2200"> Their respondents attached the greatest importance to the idea that wage cuts would induce the best workers to quit, which is WeissˇŻs adverse selection idea as it applies to quits.</seg>
<seg id="2201"> Campbell and Kamlani found that the best workers are valued because pay does not increase as rapidly as productivity, and employeesˇŻ skills are often firm specific.</seg>
<seg id="2202"> Other important management concerns were that a wage cut would increase turnover and hence hiring and training costs and would generate bad feeling that would lead to less work effort.</seg>
<seg id="2203"> Campbell and Kamlani found less support for the idea that pay cuts would make recruitment more difficult and found no support for the ˇ°no shirkingˇ± model.</seg>
<seg id="2204"> Managers did not agree that cutting pay would decrease effort because of a reduced fear of job loss, but did agree that effort would decline because of decreased gratitude and loyalty.</seg>
<seg id="2205"> Furthermore, good management-worker relations were thought to have a much greater impact on effort than high wages, close supervision, and high unemployment.</seg>
<seg id="2206"> There was also no support for the insider-outsider theory.</seg>
<seg id="2207"> Most managers did not believe that if the firm discharged some of its current workers and replaced them with new ones at a lower wage, the old workers who remained would harass and refuse to cooperate with the newly hired ones.</seg>
<seg id="2208"> The reasons for a pay cut matter;</seg>
<seg id="2209"> its negative impact on effort would be greater if the firm were profitable than if it were losing money.</seg>
<seg id="2210"> There is an asymmetry between the impact of wage increases and decreases;</seg>
<seg id="2211"> the deleterious effect on effort of a decrease would greatly exceed the positive effect of an increase.</seg>
<seg id="2212"> Similarly, a wage decrease would have a worse impact on effort and morale than having paid the lower wage for a long time.</seg>
<seg id="2213"> Agell and Lundborg (1995,1999,2003) carried out questionnaire surveys of managers in Swedish manufacturing firms, obtaining responses from 179 firms in 1991 and from 157 of those firms in a follow-up survey in 1998.</seg>
<seg id="2214"> A large majority of the respondents felt that a nominal wage cut would be strongly resisted by employees and that at least 50% of the firmˇŻs jobs would have to be threatened to make a cut acceptable.</seg>
<seg id="2215"> The respondents gave strong support to KeynesˇŻs theory that the desire to preserve external wage relativities explains downward wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="2216"> The inconsistency between this finding and my own is probably explained by the much greater importance of labor unions in Sweden than in the United States.</seg>
<seg id="2217"> This explanation is supported by the finding of Agell and Bennmarker (2002,2003) that the importance attributed to external pay comparisons as an explanation of wage rigidity increases with a firmˇŻs degree of unionization.</seg>
<seg id="2218"> Agell and Lundborg found little or no support for the ˇ°no shirkingˇ± model.</seg>
<seg id="2219"> Managers did not regard shirking as very common, and ˇ°employees who were repeatedly caught shirking were punished by a simple verbal rebukeˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="2220"> Like Campbell and Kamlani, they found that good management-worker relations were much more important to work effort than high wages, supervision, or unemployment.</seg>
<seg id="2221"> When managers were asked to list the factors most important to worker motivation, ˇ°they answered that their employees ought to be given stimulating work assignments, and to feel involved in decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="2222"> Some stressed that it was important that all employees felt noticed and trusted, and were provided with continuous feedback and appreciationˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2223"> As the authors note, these answers were very similar those I heard from U.S. managers.</seg>
<seg id="2224"> Managers reported that higher unemployment increased worker effort, and workers seemed to be providing more effort in 1998, when there was high unemployment, than in 1991, when there was little unemployment.</seg>
<seg id="2225"> These findings on the effect of unemployment confirm those of myself and of Kaufman.</seg>
<seg id="2226"> Like Blinder and Choi, Agell and Lundborg found little support for WeissˇŻs idea that job candidatesˇŻ reservation wages are a useful signal of productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2227"> Agell and Lundborg also found little support for SolowˇŻs theory about undercutting.</seg>
<seg id="2228"> They found, as did I, that offers to work for little pay were not uncommon, though fewer such offers occurred in 1998 than in 1991, perhaps because the much higher unemployment rate in 1998 discouraged job search.</seg>
<seg id="2229"> Managers usually rejected low offers, because accepting them would create pay inequities within the firm and low bidders were thought to have poor skills.</seg>
<seg id="2230"> In my survey, I often heard the first explanation, but seldom the second.</seg>
<seg id="2231"> The findings of Agell and Bennmarker (2002, 2003) are largely consistent with those of Agell and Lundborg.</seg>
<seg id="2232"> Agell and Bennmarker also obtained information on the influence of firm size and employee gender on the explanation of wage rigidityˇŞ matters that are not germane to this chapter.</seg>
<seg id="2233"> Levine (1993) obtained responses to questionnaires on pay policy from 139 compensation managers of large U.S. corporations.</seg>
<seg id="2234"> The questions focused on the determinants of wages and salaries rather than on the reasons for downward wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="2235"> Nevertheless, he found that the unemployment rate and other measures of excess demand for labor had almost no impact on pay.</seg>
<seg id="2236"> Also, internal equity considerations take precedence over changes in market pay rates in the determination of relative pay rates for closely related jobs and skills.</seg>
<seg id="2237"> In summary, the eight surveys of managers are largely consistent and point to an explanation of wage rigidity based on morale rather than on the work incentives that play a role in the ˇ°no shirkingˇ± model.</seg>
<seg id="2238"> I now turn to the analysis by Smith (2002) of data from the British Household Panel Study of 6,000 employed workers from 1991 to 1999.</seg>
<seg id="2239"> She uses data on the 70% of workers who did not change employer or job grade during the nine years.</seg>
<seg id="2240"> The data include monthly income and responses to questions about satisfaction with pay and job.</seg>
<seg id="2241"> She finds that, in a typical month, about 28% of workers suffered nominal reductions in their monthly income and the pay of about 6% was frozen (in that their monthly nominal income did not change).</seg>
<seg id="2242"> She studies the association between changes in satisfaction and monthly income and finds that workers who suffered cuts were on average less satisfied than those who enjoyed pay increases, though the difference in satisfaction is not striking.</seg>
<seg id="2243"> She also finds that those whose pay was frozen were just as satisfied as those whose income declined.</seg>
<seg id="2244"> She interprets this last finding as evidence against the morale theory of wage rigidity outlined above, because according to that theory pay cuts should cause greater unhappiness than do pay freezes.</seg>
<seg id="2245"> The theory, of course, may be wrong, but it is not clear what conclusions should be drawn from her analysis, because she probably does not have data on pay cuts and freezes in the sense of the third definition given in the previous section, and this is the definition that is relevant to downward wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="2246"> Monthly incomes can fluctuate for a great many reasons, such as changes in overtime, shifts, job assignments, bonuses, or hours, and she has information on none of these except hours, and she is not sure the data on hours are accurate.</seg>
<seg id="2247"> Pay raises, freezes, and cuts have to do with the rules by which pay is calculated.</seg>
<seg id="2248"> A great deal more information is required than total monthly income in order to detect changes in these rules.</seg>
<seg id="2249"> I find it extremely unlikely that on average 28% of the workforce suffered pay cuts from one month to the next according to the proper definition of pay cut.</seg>
<seg id="2250"> Another issue is that actual pay cuts often turn out to do little harm to morale, because they are done for a good reason and are accepted by workers as fair.</seg>
<seg id="2251"> When managers say that pay cuts would hurt morale, they refer to unjustified cuts.</seg>
<seg id="2252"> Also, job and pay satisfaction are probably not good measures of morale.</seg>
<seg id="2253"> A question of interest to macroeconomists is whether downward wage rigidity is real or nominal.</seg>
<seg id="2254"> In other words, do workers suffer from money illusion?</seg>
<seg id="2255"> Would they be as offended by a 10% reduction in nominal wages when the consumer price level had declined by 5% as they would be by a wage freeze when inflation was 5%?</seg>
<seg id="2256"> It is hard to address this issue using surveys without asking hypothetical and hence confusing questions of informants.</seg>
<seg id="2257"> Most surveys have been done during periods of low inflation, when inflation does not seem relevant.</seg>
<seg id="2258"> The answers that managers gave Agell and Bennmarker (2002, 2003) to a question similar to the one posed above indicate that managers believe that their workers suffer from money illusion.</seg>
<seg id="2259"> In my own work, I obtained similar responses on the few times I asked such questions.</seg>
<seg id="2260"> It is hard to know how to evaluate such findings because of the speculative nature of the questions.</seg>
<seg id="2261"> Although there is ample evidence that many people do suffer from money illusion</seg>
<seg id="2262"> (Kahneman et al. 1986; Shafir et al. 1997; Fehr and Tyran 2001),</seg>
<seg id="2263"> it is not clear that money illusion is a significant factor in downward wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="2264"> 5.4
<seg id="2265"> Experimental evidence is accumulating that, for the most part, agrees with what managers say about their own choices and about worker motivation.</seg>
<seg id="2266"> The most important finding is the prevalence of reciprocity.</seg>
<seg id="2267"> Many people, when placed experimentally in the role of worker or employer, offer extra effort when offered extra pay or offer extra pay after receiving extra effort, even when no quid pro quo is required.</seg>
<seg id="2268"> People also reciprocate bad for bad.</seg>
<seg id="2269"> In experiments, subjects incur a cost in order to harm others who have hurt them.</seg>
<seg id="2270"> The general willingness to reciprocate good for good is the essence of good morale.</seg>
<seg id="2271"> Negative reciprocity is what underlies the insult effect of pay cuts, which is resentment caused by the firmˇŻs perceived breach of positive reciprocity;</seg>
<seg id="2272"> workers expect employers to offer pay increases, not cuts, in exchange for loyalty and effort.</seg>
<seg id="2273"> The pervasiveness of negative reciprocity probably explains managersˇŻ belief that the systematic use of firing would not motivate employees to work well.</seg>
<seg id="2274"> Another finding is that financial incentives do inspire effort, provided they are framed in a way that avoids an impression of menace.</seg>
<seg id="2275"> Surveys of the experimental literature are given in Fehr and Gachter (1998b, 2000), Fehr and Falk (2002), and Fehr and Fischbacher (2005).</seg>
<seg id="2276"> A series of laboratory experiments demonstrate the importance of reciprocity in mock employment relationships.</seg>
<seg id="2277"> In these experiments, there are two types of subjects (employers and workers) and two stages of interaction.</seg>
<seg id="2278"> At the first stage, each employer makes a wage offer, which is either accepted or rejected by some worker.</seg>
<seg id="2279"> Acceptance leads to employment and to the second stage, where either the worker or the experimenter chooses an effort level.</seg>
<seg id="2280"> An employer can employ only one worker, and a worker can work for only one employer.</seg>
<seg id="2281"> An employed workerˇŻs payoff is the wage minus a cost, which is increasing in the effort level.</seg>
<seg id="2282"> The employerˇŻs payoff increases in the effort level and, of course, decreases with the wage.</seg>
<seg id="2283"> Notice that the employer has no way to enforce the workerˇŻs effort choice.</seg>
<seg id="2284"> The two stages are repeated, usually ten to fifteen times.</seg>
<seg id="2285"> In some experiments, one worker and one employer are paired for all the repetitions.</seg>
<seg id="2286"> In others, the experimenter changes the pairings after every repetition.</seg>
<seg id="2287"> In still another version, the pairings are established at each repetition by competitive bidding for workers and jobs.</seg>
<seg id="2288"> In such market interactions there are more workers than employers, so that market-clearing wages should be little more than the workersˇŻ reservation level, which is their cost of effort.</seg>
<seg id="2289"> Experimenters consistently find that if workers choose the effort level, then the average wage is considerably higher than the reservation level, even when competitive bidding should force wages down to it.</seg>
<seg id="2290"> Furthermore, the workerˇŻs average effort is higher than the minimum allowed and increases with the wage offered.</seg>
<seg id="2291"> In addition, the wage equals little more than the reservation level, if the experimenter chooses effort and there is competitive bidding with an excess supply of labor.</seg>
<seg id="2292"> These results hold, even if the employer and worker interact only once.</seg>
<seg id="2293"> That is, workers offer extra effort in exchange for a higher than minimal wage, even though wages are agreed on before workers choose effort levels and employers never have another opportunity to reward or punish workers.</seg>
<seg id="2294"> Employers anticipate and exploit workersˇŻ reciprocity by offering generous wages.</seg>
<seg id="2295"> The experiments show that only some people reciprocate.</seg>
<seg id="2296"> Others do not do so and behave selfishly.</seg>
<seg id="2297"> Selfish workers offer the minimum amount of effort.</seg>
<seg id="2298"> Some employers who would probably otherwise behave selfishly are induced to offer generous wages by the expectation that some workers will react to them by offering liberal amounts of effort.</seg>
<seg id="2299"> Because wages fall to minimal levels when the experimenter fixes the effort level, we may tentatively conclude that employersˇŻ behavior is driven mainly by the expectation of reciprocation, not by a sense of fairness,</seg>
<seg id="2300"> that is, by a desire to divide evenly the economic surplus generated by the worker-employer interaction.</seg>
<seg id="2301"> The tendency to reciprocate may be built into the human psyche.</seg>
<seg id="2302"> Rilling et al. (2002) used magnetic resonance imaging to study the reactions of the brain to repeated playing of the prisonerˇŻs dilemma game and found that experiencing cooperative responses and deciding to cooperate were both accompanied by patterns of brain activity normally associated with pleasure.</seg>
<seg id="2303"> All these findings support the explanation of wage rigidity proposed by Akerlof (1982) in his gift-exchange model.</seg>
<seg id="2304"> I pointed out earlier that this theory does not seem to apply in business contexts because workers quickly grow to believe that they deserve whatever pay they receive.</seg>
<seg id="2305"> Experiments do not continue for long enough to capture this habituation effect.</seg>
<seg id="2306"> What is important about the experiments is that they reveal that a significant fraction of the population reciprocates.</seg>
<seg id="2307"> In addition, the experimental findings do reflect some of the practices that managers describe.</seg>
<seg id="2308"> When setting the pay of new hires, recruiters sometimes offer a little more than applicants expect in order to get the relationship with them started off on the right footing and to create excitement about the new job.</seg>
<seg id="2309"> One of the many reasons recruiters dislike hiring overqualified applicants is that they are likely to be disgruntled because their pay disappoints their expectations.</seg>
<seg id="2310"> Fehr and Falk (1999) perform interesting modifications of the experiments of Fehr et al. (1993) and others described above.</seg>
<seg id="2311"> Fehr and Falk make the bidding for jobs and workers two-sided rather than one-sided in the situation with competitive bidding and an excess supply of workers.</seg>
<seg id="2312"> That is, workers as well as employers can make wage offers.</seg>
<seg id="2313"> The authors find that, when the experimenter determines effort, the employers accept only the lowest offers and wages are forced down almost to the reservation level.</seg>
<seg id="2314"> When the workers choose the effort level, however, the wage is higher, just as in experiments where only employers make offers.</seg>
<seg id="2315"> Workers make many low offers to try to obtain a job, but these are refused, apparently because the employers hope to incite high effort by paying good wages.</seg>
<seg id="2316"> The experimental employersˇŻ behavior corresponds to that of actual firms, who do usually refuse workersˇŻ offers to work for very little.</seg>
<seg id="2317"> Burda et al. (1998) have performed experiments involving wage cuts.</seg>
<seg id="2318"> In their work, an employer and worker are matched for two periods, and the employer makes a wage offer in each of them, which the worker may accept or reject.</seg>
<seg id="2319"> If the worker rejects the offer, the employer may, after paying a fixed training cost, hire a fictitious worker at a market wage, which the actual worker also receives, as if hired by some other fictitious firm.</seg>
<seg id="2320"> The market wage is predetermined by the experimenters and declines from the first to the second period.</seg>
<seg id="2321"> In the experiments, there is little wage rigidity;</seg>
<seg id="2322"> the wages that employers and employees agree on tend to decline along with the market wage.</seg>
<seg id="2323"> The employer and worker in effect play two successive ultimatum games, the bargaining position of the worker weakens from the first to the second game, and as a result the wage declines.</seg>
<seg id="2324"> There is no reciprocation of effort for income that could give rise to an insult effect, and the standard-of- living effect does not apply, since the workers do not live off their earnings.</seg>
<seg id="2325"> The experiments, therefore, provide evidence that without these two effects wages would be downwardly flexible.</seg>
<seg id="2326"> Experimental evidence supports the view of businesspeople that financial incentives are effective, even when negative, provided they are not presented in a hostile manner.</seg>
<seg id="2327"> For instance, Nagin et al. (2002) report on a field experiment performed by a telemarketing firm.</seg>
<seg id="2328"> In this firm, the telemarketersˇŻ pay increased with the number of successful solicitations they claimed, and the company monitored these claims by calling back a fraction of the people declared to be successes.</seg>
<seg id="2329"> The company secretly varied the fraction of bad calls reported to employees while increasing the true callback rate.</seg>
<seg id="2330"> By analyzing the companyˇŻs data, the authors found that cheating increased as the fraction of bad calls reported declined, so that workers did respond to variation in the negative incentive.</seg>
<seg id="2331"> Laboratory experimental work by Fehr and Gachter (1998a) and Brown et al. (2004a) shows that the possibility of negative rewards does not keep reciprocation from being a powerful incentive.</seg>
<seg id="2332"> Fehr and Gachter (1998a) performed the two- stage experiments of Fehr et al. (1993) with the modification that at stage one the employer requested an effort level.</seg>
<seg id="2333"> The authors compared the results with experiments where in a third stage the employer could reward or punish the worker.</seg>
<seg id="2334"> The amount of the reward or punishment was chosen by the employer and was not announced in advance.</seg>
<seg id="2335"> The employer incurred a cost that increased with the absolute magnitude of the reward or punishment.</seg>
<seg id="2336"> Despite the cost, many employers did reward high effort and punish low effort, and workers on average offered more effort and earned lower wages in the three-stage than in the two-stage experiments.</seg>
<seg id="2337"> Brown et al. (2004a) repeated the two-stage experiments of Fehr et al. (1993) fifteen times under two conditions.</seg>
<seg id="2338"> Under one, employers and workers could identify each other by a number, and employers could make offers to a particular worker.</seg>
<seg id="2339"> This arrangement made it possible for an employer and worker to form a long-term relationship.</seg>
<seg id="2340"> In the other condition, the identifying numbers were reassigned in every period, so that long-term relationships were impossible.</seg>
<seg id="2341"> When identity numbers remained stable, individual workers and employers did form relationships that were valuable to both, because they established a pattern of exchanging high effort for high wages.</seg>
<seg id="2342"> Employers could and many did punish workers for low effort by dismissing them, that is, by ceasing to make them offers.</seg>
<seg id="2343"> Average wages and effort were considerably higher when identity numbers were stable than when they were reassigned, so workers were not discouraged from reciprocating by the threat of dismissal that was made possible by stable identity numbers.</seg>
<seg id="2344"> When Brown et al. (2004b) repeated the experiments just described while allowing there to be more firms than workers, long-term relationships formed even though workers could quickly obtain work with another firm if they were let go.</seg>
<seg id="2345"> The fact that the negative incentives were not made explicit in these experiments may have diminished any bad impression they made.</seg>
<seg id="2346"> Another explanation for the effectiveness of the negative incentives may have to do with the presence of both selfish and reciprocating workers.</seg>
<seg id="2347"> Although the reciprocating workers might have been offended by the possibility of punishment, selfish ones might have been induced to offer more effort by the prospect of reward and risk of punishment.</seg>
<seg id="2348"> Other experiments that imitate the ˇ°no shirkingˇ± model provide additional evidence that punishments do not necessarily crush reciprocation and discourage effort.</seg>
<seg id="2349"> These experiments are described in Fehr et al. (1996, 1997, 2001), and Fehr and Gaachter (2002).</seg>
<seg id="2350"> The experiments have the form of the two-stage experiments described in Fehr et al. (1993), except that the employer requests a certain effort level and a worker is fined with a fixed probability if the effort level offered falls short of that demanded by the employer, that is, if the worker shirks.</seg>
<seg id="2351"> In its offer, the employer specifies a wage, the fine, and the effort level demanded.</seg>
<seg id="2352"> The ˇ°no shirkingˇ± model of Shapiro and Stiglitz (1984) also has a probability of being caught shirking, and the fine in the experiment corresponds to being fired.</seg>
<seg id="2353"> One finding is that the threat of being fined elicits more than the minimum possible level of effort.</seg>
<seg id="2354"> Also, some reciprocation exists, in that employers obtain effort above the level they demand when they offer generous wages.</seg>
<seg id="2355"> Probably because employers hope for reciprocation, they often request effort levels that are too high to be enforced by the fine.</seg>
<seg id="2356"> The average level of actual effort is reduced by a considerable amount of shirking that may reflect reciprocation of the hostility perceived in the possibility of being fined.</seg>
<seg id="2357"> The evidence is mixed on the degree to which the specification of fines discourages reciprocity.</seg>
<seg id="2358"> Fehret al. (2001) and Fehr and Gachter (2002) compare experimental labor relations models imitating the ˇ°no shirkingˇ± model, as in Fehr et al. (1996), with labor relations models that depend for success solely on reciprocity or trust, as in Fehr et al. (1993).</seg>
<seg id="2359"> In the trust model, the employer offers a wage and makes a nonbinding effort request, and the worker then offers an effort level.</seg>
<seg id="2360"> The ˇ°no shirkingˇ± model is as described in the previous paragraph.</seg>
<seg id="2361"> The two papers report opposite results.</seg>
<seg id="2362"> In Fehr and Gaachter (2002), the trust model achieves higher actual effort than the ˇ°no shirkingˇ± model.</seg>
<seg id="2363"> In Fehr et al. (2001), the ˇ°no shirkingˇ± model achieves higher effort.</seg>
<seg id="2364"> I see no way of explaining the discrepancy, as the payoffs are nearly the same in the two experiments and the differences between them do not seem relevant.</seg>
<seg id="2365"> Fehr and Gachter (2002) go on to make another comparison that shows that the fine may vex workers to some extent.</seg>
<seg id="2366"> They compare the ˇ°no shirkingˇ± model with a mathematically equivalent bonus model, in which the punishment is not a fine but the deprivation of a bonus.</seg>
<seg id="2367"> The bonus model gives rise to greater effort than the ˇ°no shirkingˇ± model, but less than the trust model.</seg>
<seg id="2368"> Further experimental evidence of the harmful effects of negative incentives is given in Fehr and Rockenbach (2003).</seg>
<seg id="2369"> In their experiments, subjects play a game, in which an investor chooses a quantity of money to give to a respondent and specifies the amount he or she would like the respondent to return.</seg>
<seg id="2370"> The amount given is tripled by the experimenter, so if the investor gives x, the respondent receives 3x.</seg>
<seg id="2371"> The respondent then chooses how much to return to the investor.</seg>
<seg id="2372"> In another version of the game, the investor, when making the gift to the respondent, may commit to imposing a fine on the respondent if he or she returns less than the amount requested by the investor.</seg>
<seg id="2373"> On average, respondents were least generous when the fine was imposed, next most generous when there was no possibility of a fine, and most generous when the investor could impose a fine but chose not to do so.</seg>
<seg id="2374"> Experiments by Falk and Kosfeld (2004) show that efforts to control people can also diminish their willingness to cooperate.</seg>
<seg id="2375"> They had two people play a game in which one is an agent and the other a principal.</seg>
<seg id="2376"> The agent is given 120 units of money and gives x units of it to the principal.</seg>
<seg id="2377"> The experimenter doubles this quantity, so that the principal receives 2x.</seg>
<seg id="2378"> The principal can either oblige the agent to give at least 10 units or do nothing.</seg>
<seg id="2379"> A majority of agents give less if the principal restricts them in this way,</seg>
<seg id="2380"> and on average agents give less when so constrained.</seg>
<seg id="2381"> Gneezy and Rustichini (2000) provide other evidence on the destructive effects of negative incentives.</seg>
<seg id="2382"> They made an experiment with a day-care center in which they imposed for the first time a monetary fine on parents for picking up their children late.</seg>
<seg id="2383"> The introduction of the fine increased parentsˇŻ lateness, and the lateness did not diminish after the fine was removed.</seg>
<seg id="2384"> It seems likely that the increase in lateness occurred because parents interpreted the fine as a fee charged for keeping their children longer.</seg>
<seg id="2385"> Two papers by Falk et al. (2000, 2003) provide experimental evidence that perceived intentions as well as the desire for a fair division affect reciprocation.</seg>
<seg id="2386"> Falk et al. (2000) report on experiments with a variant of the game, described above, of Fehr and Rockenbach (2003).</seg>
<seg id="2387"> On the first move, the investor may take money away from or give money to the respondent, and the respondent may then in turn give or take money away from the investor.</seg>
<seg id="2388"> In another version of the game, the experimenter determines the investorˇŻs move according to a random distribution.</seg>
<seg id="2389"> In both versions, respondents on average react by taking money back if it is taken from them and give money back when it has been given to them.</seg>
<seg id="2390"> Their responses are, however, of a larger magnitude when the first move is chosen by the investor rather than by the experimenter.</seg>
<seg id="2391"> This behavior shows that the respondentsˇŻ behavior was driven to some extent by a desire to even the winnings from the game, but above all by an urge to reciprocate the good or bad intentions of the investor.</seg>
<seg id="2392"> Falk et al. (2003) reach similar conclusions from experiments with various ultimatum games.</seg>
<seg id="2393"> Player A can propose one of two possible splits of ten monetary units to a respondent.</seg>
<seg id="2394"> One possibility is always an (8, 2) split: eight for the proposer and two for the respondent.</seg>
<seg id="2395"> Alternatives are (5, 5), an even split, or (2, 8), (10, 0), or even (8, 2), which means that there is really no alternative.</seg>
<seg id="2396"> Respondents reject the (8, 2) split more frequently, the less fair it seems in comparison with the alternative.</seg>
<seg id="2397"> For instance, (8, 2) is rejected most often if (5, 5) is the alternative, and least often if (10, 0) is the alternative.</seg>
<seg id="2398"> These results provide some (but not strong) support for managersˇŻ assertions that using firing systematically to stimulate effort would dampen morale and depress productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2399"> I suspect that the effects managers refer to are difficult to capture experimentally, because firing is a much more severe punishment than can be imposed in the laboratory and it is hard to reproduce in a laboratory the menacing atmosphere that could be created in a work place by frequent firings and by the explicit threat of firing.</seg>
<seg id="2400"> 5.5
<seg id="2401"> Although early investigations by managerial scientists and organizational psychologists of the relations between pay, morale,</seg>
<seg id="2402"> and productivity contradicted some of what managers say about these matters, the subject has since evolved and now much of what they say has been corroborated.</seg>
<seg id="2403"> Recall that managers assert that pay levels have little impact on motivation or performance, but that financial incentives linked to performance can increase productivity considerably.</seg>
<seg id="2404"> These conclusions have been supported by a large amount of research by management scientists and psychologists, which I do not describe.</seg>
<seg id="2405"> The relevant literature is reviewed in Vroom (1964, p. 252) and Lawler (1971, p. 133).</seg>
<seg id="2406"> The management intuitions that did not receive much support in early research had to do with the link between morale and productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2407"> Morale was measured from questionnaire evidence on job satisfaction and organizational commitment or loyalty, and performance was measured through direct observation or by supervisorsˇŻ evaluations.</seg>
<seg id="2408"> There is a huge literature on this subject that has been reviewed by many</seg>
<seg id="2409"> The general conclusion is that the correlations between the measures of morale and performance are positive, but small.</seg>
<seg id="2410"> The measures of performance include those of both individuals and groups.</seg>
<seg id="2411"> In a way, these findings confirm what managers say, because most of them assert that good morale is not the same as happiness.</seg>
<seg id="2412"> There is a considerable amount of evidence that job satisfaction is negatively related to quitting and absences.</seg>
<seg id="2413"> There was interesting research in the 1950s which did support management feelings about the importance of morale.</seg>
<seg id="2414"> The investigators made experimental changes in management practices to determine the relation between attitudes and performance of work groups</seg>
<seg id="2415"> One of the main conclusions was that performance is positively associated with pride in the work group or firm, but is not related to other attitudes.</seg>
<seg id="2416"> In response to the failure to find a significant relation between job satisfaction and performance, researchers studied the link between job attitudes and workersˇŻ doing things for employers that are outside normal duties.</seg>
<seg id="2417"> Contact with business may have led scholars to look for such a connection, because managers claim that the impact of good morale on productivity is felt mainly through employeesˇŻ willingness to do more than the minimum required of them.</seg>
<seg id="2418"> Investigators have given ˇ°doing more than the minimumˇ± various names, such as spontaneous behavior, prosocial behavior, extra-role behavior, and, most commonly, organizational citizenship behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2419"> These concepts differ to some extent.</seg>
<seg id="2420"> Organ (1988) defines five categories of organization citizenship behaviors,</seg>
<seg id="2421"> altruism (helping other workers),</seg>
<seg id="2422"> conscientiousness (obeying company rules, being punctual, and showing up for work regularly),</seg>
<seg id="2423"> sportsmanship (good-humored toleration of inconveniences),</seg>
<seg id="2424"> courtesy (considerate treatment of fellow workers),</seg>
<seg id="2425"> and civic virtue (participation in the internal political life of the organization).</seg>
<seg id="2426"> An initial question is whether good morale increases organizational citizenship behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2427"> Organizational psychologists have done most of the research on this topic.</seg>
<seg id="2428"> They typically start with a number of loosely defined concepts, such as job satisfaction, perceptions of fairness in the work place, and organizational citizenship behavior, and then try to determine how these are related by analyzing responses of a sample of several hundred people to questionnaires.</seg>
<seg id="2429"> Each concept is usually broken into several components, such as OrganˇŻs five categories of organizational citizenship behavior, and a list of questions is associated with each component.</seg>
<seg id="2430"> Employees answer questions on job satisfaction and perceptions of fairness.</seg>
<seg id="2431"> Employees or their supervisors answer questions on organizational citizenship behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2432"> Factor analysis is used to check whether responses to the questions are such that those corresponding to one conceptual component are highly correlated with each other and have less correlation with responses to other questions.</seg>
<seg id="2433"> The relations among the concepts and their components are then estimated using regression analysis.</seg>
<seg id="2434"> The advantage of such surveys over laboratory experiments is that they can investigate real-life situations in which there are long-term associations between workers and employers.</seg>
<seg id="2435"> The subjects in laboratory experiments are usually college students and do not know each other well.</seg>
<seg id="2436"> A disadvantage is that causation is much harder to establish in surveys than it is in experiments.</seg>
<seg id="2437"> The findings of organizational psychologists do not all agree, but their work supports the conclusion that typical measures of morale, such as job satisfaction and organizational commitment, do have a positive relation with organizational citizenship behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2438"> What is more important is that a perception of fairness within a business organization has a positive relation with both job satisfaction and organizational citizenship behavior and may be the dominant factor affecting both.</seg>
<seg id="2439"> Furthermore, procedural justice, and especially the interactional aspect of procedural justice, is more closely related to job satisfaction and organizational citizenship behavior than is distributive justice.</seg>
<seg id="2440"> Distributive justice has to do with the allocation of rewards to employees, whereas procedural justice has to do with the system used to arrive at the allocation.</seg>
<seg id="2441"> Interactional justice has to do with the consideration, politeness, and respect with which superiors treat subordinates.</seg>
<seg id="2442"> Another conclusion is that organizational citizenship behavior depends less on the employeesˇŻ mood than on their conscious perceptions about their jobs.</seg>
<seg id="2443"> Moorman (1991) discusses the relative impacts of the various forms of justice.</seg>
<seg id="2444"> The impact of mood is discussed in Organ and Konovsky (1989), George (1991), and Moorman (1993).</seg>
<seg id="2445"> The relative impacts of mood and cognitive job satisfaction are discussed in Organ and Konovsky (1989) and Moorman (1993).</seg>
<seg id="2446"> The impact of job satisfaction and commitment on organizational citizenship behavior is discussed in OˇŻReilly and Chatman (1986), Puffer (1987), Farh et al. (1990), Moorman (1991), Organ and Lingl (1995), Organ and Ryan (1995), Konovsky and Organ (1996), Netemeyer et al. (1997), and MacKenzie et al. (1998).</seg>
<seg id="2447"> Good reviews of the impact of fairness on organizational citizenship behavior are Organ (1988, 1990), Schnake (1991), Greenberg (1993), and Organ and Moorman (1993).</seg>
<seg id="2448"> Another connection between morale and organizational citizenship behavior is made through studies of the impact of leadership style on subordinatesˇŻ organizational citizenship behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2449"> A distinction is made between transactional and transformational leadership.</seg>
<seg id="2450"> The transactional style asserts itself by means of praise and admonishment, whereas the transformational style inspires people to go beyond their personal interests and think of those of the company or task.</seg>
<seg id="2451"> The transformational style persuades people to identify with the company, and the transactional style focuses on peopleˇŻs self-interest.</seg>
<seg id="2452"> The transformational style is intended to create good morale in the sense that businesspeople seem to have in mind.</seg>
<seg id="2453"> Investigators have found that transformational leadership has a strong positive impact on both in role job performance and organizational citizenship behavior, that its impact exceeds that of transactional leadership, and that the impact of transformational leadership is due in part to increased trust in the leadership.</seg>
<seg id="2454"> The relevant studies are by Podsakoff et al. (1990, 1996) and MacKenzie et al. (2001).</seg>
<seg id="2455"> An obvious question is whether organizational citizenship behavior increases a companyˇŻs profitability.</seg>
<seg id="2456"> Managers apparently think that it does, because there is evidence that supervisorsˇŻ performance evaluations of subordinates are strongly and positively influenced by organizational citizenship behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2457"> Papers that establish this connection are MacKenzie et al. (1991, 1993) and Podsakoff et al. (1993).</seg>
<seg id="2458"> A few studies have measured the impact of organizational citizenship behavior on the performance of work groups in various settings and have found the effects to be positive.</seg>
<seg id="2459"> These studies include George and Bettenhausen (1990), Podsakoff and MacKenzie (1994, 1997), Walz and Niehoff (1996), and Podsakoff et al. (1997).</seg>
<seg id="2460"> The observed correlations may be spurious, however, because there is evidence from laboratory experiments that the high performance of a work group may have a positive influence on perceptions within the group of organizational citizenship behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2461"> The subject is reviewed in Podsakoff et al. (2000).</seg>
<seg id="2462"> Some interesting recent work has explored the connection between identification with an organization on the one hand, and quits and performance, especially extra role performance, on the other hand.</seg>
<seg id="2463"> Tyler has participated in much of this work.</seg>
<seg id="2464"> He thinks of identification with a company as internalization of its goals and asserts that identification occurs as a result of judgments about organizational status, which he calls pride, and about status within the organization, which he calls respect.</seg>
<seg id="2465"> Pride has to do with a favorable view of the organization as a whole, and respect has to do with being treated well within it.</seg>
<seg id="2466"> Status judgments can be comparative or autonomous, where a comparative judgment relates an organization or person to others and an autonomous judgment is an absolute one.</seg>
<seg id="2467"> Tyler believes that if people identify with an organization, they will want it to succeed, because its success will strengthen their own self-image.</seg>
<seg id="2468"> Identification with an organization is, in my opinion, a much better interpretation of what managers mean by good morale than are job satisfaction and even organizational commitment.</seg>
<seg id="2469"> Tyler and his coauthors find that identification is a dominant explanation of voluntary cooperation with organizations.</seg>
<seg id="2470"> In the context of business organizations, identification with the company is a much more important explanatory factor than financial rewards received from it.</seg>
<seg id="2471"> These investigators find that the greatest impact of identification is on organizational citizenship, extra role, or discretionary behavior as opposed to in-role or mandatory behavior, that is, behavior required by a job description.</seg>
<seg id="2472"> The primary impact of pride is on rule following or conscientiousness, whereas the primary impact of respect is on helping behavior, that is, assisting coworkers.</seg>
<seg id="2473"> Autonomous judgments of status have a much bigger effect than comparative ones.</seg>
<seg id="2474"> Tyler and his coauthors assert that perceptions of fairness and especially procedural justice have an important impact on judgments about the status of an organization and hence on willingness to identify with it.</seg>
<seg id="2475"> Recall that management scientists cited earlier also found a connection between pride in an organization and performance.</seg>
<seg id="2476"> The work of Tyler and his colleagues is reported in Tyler (1999) and Tyler and Blader (2000, 2001).</seg>
<seg id="2477"> Abrams et al. (1998) observe a close association between identification with an organization and intentions to quit.</seg>
<seg id="2478"> Much of the work of Tyler and his coauthors on identification and cooperation with organizations has been done in the context of political, social, and educational institutions, but the recent work just cited has to do with businesses.</seg>
<seg id="2479"> This interesting work raises the question of why people identify with organizations.</seg>
<seg id="2480"> Status is an incomplete explanation, since the term status has little independent content and includes all possible reasons for liking an organization.</seg>
<seg id="2481"> It is interesting that fairness has a strong influence on status and that people are proud of organizations that treat them and others fairly, but researchers have given no explanation of why this is so.</seg>
<seg id="2482"> An obvious question is: ˇ°What evidence has been collected on the impact of actual pay cuts or pay freezes on morale?ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2483"> The only works I have found on the subject are by Greenberg (1989, 1990) and Schaubroeck et al. (1994).</seg>
<seg id="2484"> In the first paper, Greenberg finds from a survey that workers did feel underpaid after a 6% pay cut, but job satisfaction did not decline and employees instead paid more attention to the nonfinancial advantages of their jobs.</seg>
<seg id="2485"> In the second paper, Greenberg reports that, at another business, theft of company property increased after a 15% pay cut.</seg>
<seg id="2486"> In this chapter, he conducted an experiment in which he gave employees a good explanation of the pay cut in one plant where the pay cut occurred but not in another.</seg>
<seg id="2487"> In the plant where the explanation was made, feelings of pay inequity and pilferage were fewer than in the other plant.</seg>
<seg id="2488"> This evidence supports the assertions managers make that employees tolerate pay cuts more easily if they feel they are justified and that it is possible to persuade workers that cuts are necessary.</seg>
<seg id="2489"> These conclusions are further reinforced by the work of Schaubroeck et al. (1994), who studied the reactions of salaried employees to a pay freeze.</seg>
<seg id="2490"> These investigators also conducted an experiment, giving a good explanation to some of the employees and not to others.</seg>
<seg id="2491"> The explanations of the freeze diminished resentment.</seg>
<seg id="2492"> For those who did not receive the explanation, job dissatisfaction increased with self-reported economic hardship resulting from the freeze, and there was no such relation for those who did receive the explanation.</seg>
<seg id="2493"> 5.6
<seg id="2494"> Perhaps the outstanding conclusion to be drawn from the works discussed is the importance of fairness to labor performance.</seg>
<seg id="2495"> It is not easy to judge what fairness means.</seg>
<seg id="2496"> Fairness certainly does not mean an equal distribution of the benefits from a companyˇŻs operations, for pay levels within firms are far from egalitarian.</seg>
<seg id="2497"> Even workers doing the same job may receive very different pay because of many factors, such as longevity with the company, skills acquired, and productivity.</seg>
<seg id="2498"> Fairness is recognized in business as being inherently ambiguous.</seg>
<seg id="2499"> For instance, judgments about the fairness of internal pay structures are said to depend strongly on company tradition.</seg>
<seg id="2500"> Another indication that fairness does not mean equality of gains is the evidence from organizational psychology that procedural and interactive justice are more important to an impression of fairness than is distributive justice.</seg>
<seg id="2501"> A very significant finding, I believe, is that of Tyler and Blader (2000, 2001): perceptions of procedural justice contribute to pride in an organization.</seg>
<seg id="2502"> We do not know why people so urgently desire fairness.</seg>
<seg id="2503"> Is it because it contributes to an atmosphere of positive reciprocation and people like to exchange favors?</seg>
<seg id="2504"> Does fairness make people feel more secure?</seg>
<seg id="2505"> Do people feel that fairness is right and want their surroundings to accord with their moral principles?</seg>
<seg id="2506"> Do people simply want to have a level playing field on which to compete?</seg>
<seg id="2507"> It is to be hoped that further empirical work will give more insight into these questions.</seg>
<seg id="2508"> A sense of fairness is probably the most important determinant of good company morale.</seg>
<seg id="2509"> Other important factors are close ties among coworkers and the significance attached to the firmˇŻs output.</seg>
<seg id="2510"> One reason pay cuts can be resented is that they can dissolve the sense of fairness.</seg>
<seg id="2511"> Workers accept a pay cut that they feel is fair and they see it as fair when it saves a great many jobs.</seg>
<seg id="2512"> Another important conclusion is that firms try to gain the cooperation of employees by getting them to identify with the company and to internalize its objectives.</seg>
<seg id="2513"> As Tyler and Blader (2000, 2001) have emphasized, an atmosphere of fairness makes workers more willing to do these things.</seg>
<seg id="2514"> For an understanding of wage rigidity and of how organizations obtain cooperation, it would be useful to know why people value fairness, why it promotes identification with a company, and why people identify with organizations at all. That they do so is clear.</seg>
<seg id="2515"> Comment by Seppo Honkapohja Introduction</seg>
<seg id="2516"> Bewley presents a very interesting survey of the recent empirical microresearch on wage formation and on reasons for wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="2517"> Much of this work is behavioral in its underpinnings and the results show how notions of fairness and reciprocity play an important role in wage determination.</seg>
<seg id="2518"> An important aspect of the reviewed empirical work is that these studies are based not just on published statistics but rather on a variety of approaches including questionnaires, field studies, surveys, and experiments.</seg>
<seg id="2519"> His own work of course stands out as a particularly meticulous example of this brand of empirical economics.</seg>
<seg id="2520"> I am a consumer and not a producer of this kind of research.</seg>
<seg id="2521"> I am, however, an interested consumer since questions of wage formation and wage rigidity are central to macroeconomics.</seg>
<seg id="2522"> My comments focus on what macroeconomics can learn from the microresearch discussed in this chapter.</seg>
<seg id="2523"> I will also take up an important open issue in the surveyed literature.</seg>
<seg id="2524"> Lessons from the Empirical Microresearch</seg>
<seg id="2525"> The first lesson from this chapter is that the notion of (downward) wage rigidity is not as simple as it sounds.</seg>
<seg id="2526"> Different concepts of the wage have been used.</seg>
<seg id="2527"> It could in principle mean a firmˇŻs marginal labor costs or total compensation per worker, or total compensation for employees with given job and work conditions.</seg>
<seg id="2528"> The empirical work has been ambiguous with respect to the different notions.</seg>
<seg id="2529"> While the results are not fully clear-cut, there seems to be a fair amount of evidence that basic compensation of employees, excluding bonuses, overtime and the like is subject to considerable downward rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="2530"> This is not a surprising answer.</seg>
<seg id="2531"> In some form it has been used as casual evidence for a long time in much of macroeconomics.</seg>
<seg id="2532"> Numerous models and reasons for wage rigidity have been developed and discussed in macroeconomics over nearly seven decades since the publication of KeynesˇŻs General Theory and the birth of Keynesian economics.</seg>
<seg id="2533"> Explicit analysis of the motivations behind wage rigidity is a new and interesting aspect of the research discussed by Bewley.</seg>
<seg id="2534"> The reasons for rigidity given in this behavioral literature can be seen as tests on the different economic theories of wage rigidity.</seg>
<seg id="2535"> I personally found this aspect of the paper quite interesting as it provides a novel perspective to the different models of wage rigidity used in modern macroeconomics.</seg>
<seg id="2536"> An important observation from the microresearch is that pay cuts are resisted because they damage morale, and it is the management who seems to think this way.</seg>
<seg id="2537"> There is a second important observation that morale is damaged when wages are cut, while wage levels have less to do with morale.</seg>
<seg id="2538"> Bewley suggests that ˇ°employees usually have little notion of a fair or market value for their services,ˇ± which leads to an important question about the determinants of the reference point for judging fairness.</seg>
<seg id="2539"> The related literature has some ideas about this and I will return to this point later.</seg>
<seg id="2540"> The observation about wage changes and morale runs counter to some earlier models of labor markets.</seg>
<seg id="2541"> These were largely based on levels of wages and productivity or morale.</seg>
<seg id="2542"> Correspondingly, KeynesˇŻs notion of workersˇŻ concern about relative wages in similar jobs in different firms does not seem to get much support from the work of Bewley and others.</seg>
<seg id="2543"> Interestingly, the model of insiders and outsiders fares somewhat better, as harassment and refusal to cooperate can perhaps be interpreted as consequences of the loss of morale.</seg>
<seg id="2544"> BewleyˇŻs basic observation about pay changes and morale does not provide any support for the model of shirking and wages.</seg>
<seg id="2545"> Apparently, cooperation between managers and firms is not obtained by threats of firing of shirkers (or workers with low productivity).</seg>
<seg id="2546"> This is not to say that incentives are not important.</seg>
<seg id="2547"> Workers do work harder in times of recession but not because management threatens them.</seg>
<seg id="2548"> The incentives come directly from a tougher external environment.</seg>
<seg id="2549"> Another fairly popular model emphasizing information problems and incentives is the adverse selection model of Weiss (1990).</seg>
<seg id="2550"> Here the micro evidence is interestingly in agreement with that theory when it concerns quits.</seg>
<seg id="2551"> Managers prefer layoffs to pay cuts, since using the latter would lead to departure of the best workers.</seg>
<seg id="2552"> However, the other half of WeissˇŻs model, the connection between high wages and hiring of high-productivity workers, is not supported by the micro studies.</seg>
<seg id="2553"> These descriptions are just a few examples of the ideas from BewleyˇŻs comprehensive survey.</seg>
<seg id="2554"> Many macroeconomists have for a long time thought that labor markets and wage determination/rigidity involve large degrees of social interactions and that they are not just relatively anonymous markets of commodity exchange.</seg>
<seg id="2555"> This recent micro work provides support and makes these notions more precise.</seg>
<seg id="2556"> There are important methodological questions in the micro evidence that Bewley reviews.</seg>
<seg id="2557"> In particular, the idea of interviewing not only about wage determination but also about the respondentsˇŻ reasoning can be problematic.</seg>
<seg id="2558"> Observations are somehow more reliable about ˇ°factsˇ± than about responses to questions about motivation.</seg>
<seg id="2559"> Do we know enough about the respondentsˇŻ incentives during the interviews?</seg>
<seg id="2560"> Yet, it must be acknowledged that these answers are often fascinating.</seg>
<seg id="2561"> Fairness Norms and Multiple Equilibria</seg>
<seg id="2562"> I now move away from the micro studies and take up a particular aspect of these models, relating to norms or reference points, which are a central part of the new applied models of fairness in wage setting.</seg>
<seg id="2563"> Let me conduct my discussion by means of a recent interesting work by Driscoll and Holden (2002, 2004) about fairness and inflation persistence.</seg>
<seg id="2564"> Driscoll and Holden suggest that inclusion of fairness considerations in a standard model of wage bargaining leads to a range of equilibria and this feature can in turn be useful for explaining inflation persistence in the data.</seg>
<seg id="2565"> The Driscoll-Holden model depicts a symmetric economy with many monopolistically competitive firms and worker insiders bargaining over wages.</seg>
<seg id="2566"> The workers are concerned about fair treatment and in particular they resent being worse off than identical workers elsewhere.</seg>
<seg id="2567"> Moreover, the dissatisfaction from being paid less is stronger than the satisfaction from being paid more.</seg>
<seg id="2568"> There is thus a kink in the utility function of workers at the wage level of other workers in their reference group.</seg>
<seg id="2569"> This is a form of loss aversion, which is often employed in the literature on wage norms.</seg>
<seg id="2570"> Finally, there are several symmetrical groups of workers in the economy.</seg>
<seg id="2571"> In this kind of model, Nash wage bargaining is characterized by the existence of two thresholds.</seg>
<seg id="2572"> There is a unique outcome, which is equal to what is perceived to happen in the reference group when the reference group wage is within the thresholds.</seg>
<seg id="2573"> If the reference group wage is either above the upper threshold or below the lower threshold, then the bargaining outcome is equal to the corresponding threshold.</seg>
<seg id="2574"> The thresholds depend on the reference group wage, the economy-wide average wage, and other macro variables.</seg>
<seg id="2575"> Assume for simplicity that there are only two groups in the economy, and that the aggregate demand function specifies demand as being equal to real balances.</seg>
<seg id="2576"> Changes in the money supply will move the graph of the best response map.</seg>
<seg id="2577"> Yet, inside the range (A, B), the REE contract wage may well be unresponsive to money supply variations.</seg>
<seg id="2578"> This depends on the perceptions of the workers in wage bargaining.</seg>
<seg id="2579"> Driscoll and Holden assume that workers are just concerned about the wages elsewhere and use past data as a way of generating their perceptions.</seg>
<seg id="2580"> In this case the whole segment is indeed REE and an interior equilibrium is pinned down by history and it will not respond to current policy variations.</seg>
<seg id="2581"> On the other hand, the perceptions of workers might be more subtle.</seg>
<seg id="2582"> In particular, the perceptions could well incorporate money supply variations, especially if systematic patterns in money supply movements are apparent.</seg>
<seg id="2583"> If this is the case, then monetary policy variations will not leave the contracted wages unaffected, as workers will incorporate such systematic patterns into their wage formation.</seg>
<seg id="2584"> I will not pursue the analysis any further;</seg>
<seg id="2585"> I just wanted to make a basic point: we need to know much more about the determination of fairness reference points and how the environment in which agents operate (in particular, macro variables) affects their reference points.</seg>
<seg id="2586"> Conclusion</seg>
<seg id="2587"> The recent research on wages and fairness surveyed by Bewley is highly interesting and thought provoking.</seg>
<seg id="2588"> It has already provided useful evidence on what might be empirically the more interesting models of labor markets as part of the macro economy .</seg>
<seg id="2589"> There is clearly potential for further theoretical and empirical work concerning the determination of reference points in different economic environments and how these considerations affect range of potential equilibria and the effects of economic policy.</seg>
<seg id="2590"> 6.1 INTRODUCTION AND BACKGROUND</seg>
<seg id="2591"> If one examines the salient economic institutions of the health sector, one might expect that sector to be a breeding ground for applied behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="2592"> Consider a set of economic activities where addictions figure prominently;</seg>
<seg id="2593"> where consumers have limited information that they must use to make choices in the context of fear, urgency, and trust in an expert;</seg>
<seg id="2594"> and where the services used are often credence goods  whose applications are frequently governed by professional norms and habit.</seg>
<seg id="2595"> In such an economic environment, the methods of behavioral economics might be expected to be prevalent in modifying traditional models to take account of those features that appear to conflict with simple notions of rationality in economic behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2596"> Yet the application of behavioral economics to issues in health economics has been largely confined to understanding addictive behavior around cigarettes, drugs, and alcohol</seg>
<seg id="2597"> 6.1.1
<seg id="2598"> Research on addictions has its origins in research on household production and the demand for good health .</seg>
<seg id="2599"> Decisions about substance abuse involve a set of harmful health choices that appear to represent extreme tradeoffs between todayˇŻs pleasures and tomorrowˇŻs health and well-being.</seg>
<seg id="2600"> A substantial amount of progress has been made in both theoretical analysis and empirical testing of models in this area, and a lively program of research that encompasses behavioral economic concepts is underway.</seg>
<seg id="2601"> In this chapter I draw on a different research tradition, which emanates from the seminal paper by Arrow (1963).</seg>
<seg id="2602"> The efficiency of health insurance markets, doctor- patient relationships, and the role of nonmarket institutions in promoting efficiency were raised by Arrow and continue to be central issues in the health economics research program.</seg>
<seg id="2603"> This side of health economics has received far less attention from scholars armed with the tools of behavioral economics than the addictions area.</seg>
<seg id="2604"> I focus on decision-making about treatment of illness, rationing of health services, and markets for health insurance.</seg>
<seg id="2605"> I argue that behavioral economics may be particularly important for modifying neoclassical approaches because, as in many areas of the economy, people appear to make choices about health care that are situation- or context-specific, resulting in cognitive errors and failures to optimize.</seg>
<seg id="2606"> The health sector is also characterized by institutions and decision-making circumstances that, in addition to making cognitive errors more likely, create friction that impedes market adjustments that may lead to correction of errors over time</seg>
