<seg id="2607"> These include the stress of decision-making about oneˇŻs health, professionalism, and a lack of information about medical care.</seg>
<seg id="2608"> The paper is organized into four sections.</seg>
<seg id="2609"> This introductory section sets the intellectual context for the review.</seg>
<seg id="2610"> The second section focuses on physician behavior, which represents a large class of problems in the health sector, where I believe behavioral economics has much to contribute.</seg>
<seg id="2611"> The third section addresses some puzzles in health economics related to demand behavior and the behavior of health insurance markets.</seg>
<seg id="2612"> The final section offers some concluding observations and comments on normative issues in health economics.</seg>
<seg id="2613"> 6.1.2
<seg id="2614"> ArrowˇŻs (1963) paper seeks to explain the health sectorˇŻs institutions as response to the special features of health care and health insurance.</seg>
<seg id="2615"> Among the special characteristics are information asymmetry and the significance of medical care in human affairs.</seg>
<seg id="2616"> The research program stemming from ArrowˇŻs work has produced great understanding of health-care delivery.</seg>
<seg id="2617"> Among the issues at the heart of the research program have been: market failure in health insurance due to moral hazard and adverse selection; the significance of the nonprofit organizational form in health care;</seg>
<seg id="2618"> and the role of institutions such as professional norms, trust, and morality in medical markets.</seg>
<seg id="2619"> Applying traditional economic models that rely on rational utility-maximizing consumers and physicians and efficient market equilibriums has been highly productive both theoretically and empirically, and has contributed to the design of public policy in health care .</seg>
<seg id="2620"> For example, research on hospital payment methods has advanced notably and has been very influential in public policy debates .</seg>
<seg id="2621"> Yet the application of traditional models has also repeatedly faced puzzles that have served as intellectual impasses.</seg>
<seg id="2622"> It is in attending to such puzzles that the ideas of behavioral economics may be particularly useful in advancing the analysis of health-care markets and institutions.</seg>
<seg id="2623"> In the next section I focus on markets for physician services and some puzzles found in those markets.</seg>
<seg id="2624"> I believe that these issues are representative of broader issues in the economics of the health sector.</seg>
<seg id="2625"> 6.2 Models of Physician Behavior</seg>
<seg id="2626"> Early in his essay, Arrow (1963, p. 949) remarked:It is clear from everyday observation that the behavior of sellers of medical care is different from that of businessmen in general.</seg>
<seg id="2627"> No area in health economics relies on a vocabulary that is more closely linked to the work of behavioral economics than research on physician behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2628"> As Pauly (2001) notes, Arrow used words such as ˇ°trustˇ± and ˇ°moralsˇ± to discuss physician behavior. These are words that tend to make economists uncomfortable.</seg>
<seg id="2629"> Some of the first econometric studies of health-care markets took aim at the market for physiciansˇŻ services .</seg>
<seg id="2630"> These studies took the competitive model as their point of departure.</seg>
<seg id="2631"> Each obtained results showing a positive partial correlation between the physician stock in a market and physician prices: a finding inconsistent with the competitive model.</seg>
<seg id="2632"> These papers set off a line of research on models of physician behavior that continues to this day.</seg>
<seg id="2633"> The first reactions to the surprising results were to suspect monopoly in the physiciansˇŻ market .</seg>
<seg id="2634"> Further analysis found the evidence to be inconsistent with both monopoly and competitive models.</seg>
<seg id="2635"> Health economists rapidly developed a set of ad hoc explanations grounded in motivations of physicians that departed from profit maximization.</seg>
<seg id="2636"> Yet, as McGuire (2000) notes, ˇ°there is no agreeable alternativeˇ± model to that of profit maximization.</seg>
<seg id="2637"> Feldstein (1970) sought to explain the apparent departure from simple profit maximization.</seg>
<seg id="2638"> He argued that physician markets experience persistent excess demand.</seg>
<seg id="2639"> He proposed that the reason for this is that physicians deliberately set prices below market clearing levels to enable them to choose the most ˇ°interesting casesˇ± from among the patient pool.</seg>
<seg id="2640"> This explanation was not grounded in any evidence about physician motives or the details of medical practice.</seg>
<seg id="2641"> A second explanation attempted to import an early behavioral economics idea, namely the satisficing model .</seg>
<seg id="2642"> Newhouse (1970) and Evans (1974) suggested that physicians might set prices so as to achieve a ˇ°target incomeˇ± in part because maximizing behavior may be seen as socially undesirable.</seg>
<seg id="2643"> Arrow (1963) first raised this point by observing approvingly that sociologists had argued for a ˇ°collectivity orientationˇ± of physicians that would lead them away from profit-maximizing behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2644"> Evans (1974) proposed that the target income level was set relative to the local income distribution.</seg>
<seg id="2645"> Careful analysis of the target income model reveals a variety of shortcomings .</seg>
<seg id="2646"> The basis for establishing the ˇ°targetˇ± was quite fuzzy.</seg>
<seg id="2647"> In addition, the ˇ°targetˇ± is merely an objective.</seg>
<seg id="2648"> There are numerous ways of hitting the target; the theory does not explain which approach will be taken.</seg>
<seg id="2649"> A complete model also requires a rule of choice for pursuing the objective.</seg>
<seg id="2650"> The target income model is typically specified in the context of a monopolistically competitive market structure, where physicians choose a price that will achieve a target income and meets demand.</seg>
<seg id="2651"> This model setup yields multiple equilibria that are unstable, resulting in dramatic swings in prices in response to small changes in physician supply.</seg>
<seg id="2652"> In sum the target income model might be viewed as a ˇ°false startˇ± in linking behavioral economics and health economics.</seg>
<seg id="2653"> A different modeling approach introduced the notion of ˇ°physician-induced demandˇ± in the context of a model of utility maximization.</seg>
<seg id="2654"> Evans (1974) posited that physicians create demand for their own services by exploiting their role as an agent for the incompletely informed patient.</seg>
<seg id="2655"> That is, physicians are imperfect agents in that they will recommend treatment beyond the ˇ°patientˇŻs optimum levelˇ± in order to gain income.</seg>
<seg id="2656"> Medical uncertainty and asymmetric information all serve to reinforce this conception of physician behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2657"> This model treats demand inducement in a manner akin to how advertising is introduced into market models.</seg>
<seg id="2658"> That is, demand inducement is a shifter in the demand function facing the doctor.</seg>
<seg id="2659"> The ability to induce demand is also specified as an argument in the physiciansˇŻ objective function, so that exploiting the agency relation for self-interest reduces physician utility.</seg>
<seg id="2660"> This device serves to constrain an otherwise unbounded ability to shift demand.</seg>
<seg id="2661"> While intuitively appealing, given the importance of the agency relation between physician and patient, little research has been done to understand the forces underlying the ability to induce demand as proposed in these models .</seg>
<seg id="2662"> Most discussions of demand inducement appeal intuitively to notions of medical ethics and trust between doctors and patients;</seg>
<seg id="2663"> poor information, fear, and anxiety on the part of patients;</seg>
<seg id="2664"> and uncertainty in medical choices.</seg>
<seg id="2665"> The behavioral economics literature has explored how some of these circumstances and others affect decision-making and economic behavior .</seg>
<seg id="2666"> Demand inducement may well result from a number of psychological features of doctors and patients operating in health-care markets.</seg>
<seg id="2667"> There are a number of additional constructs in behavioral economics, such as self-serving behavior, wishful thinking, and the impact of the desire by patients to avoid regret, that might contribute to what appears as demand inducement .</seg>
<seg id="2668"> The application of these concepts leads one to consider in greater detail the nature of doctor-patient interactions in the context of models of physician decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="2669"> 6.2.1
<seg id="2670"> Physicians are highly educated and skilled professionals who are motivated to serve their patients in order to improve their health and well-being.</seg>
<seg id="2671"> They are also frequently in positions in which they face great clinical uncertainty and must persuade patients to pursue one clinical course over others.</seg>
<seg id="2672"> Physicians have been shown to be creatures of habit in making medical choices, and are slow to adopt new practices and technologies that would improve the quality of care and, in turn, their patientsˇŻ health .</seg>
<seg id="2673"> Specifically, a series of studies have shown that, in treating conditions like otitis media, diabetes, depression, and asthma, physicians regularly depart from evidence-based practice .</seg>
<seg id="2674"> This runs counter to what one might expect from market models that assume either profit maximization or maximization of a physician objective function that might include patient health benefits and income as arguments.</seg>
<seg id="2675"> For example, physicians in the United States do not gain financially based on the specific drugs they choose to prescribe for their patients.</seg>
<seg id="2676"> Therefore one might expect physicians to choose the most cost-effective drugs on behalf of their patients, thereby serving as a ˇ°perfect agent.</seg>
<seg id="2677"> ˇ°Yet physicians have been observed to be habitual in their choice of pharmaceutical agents, relying on drugs they became familiar with in medical training or learned about through pharmaceutical promotions.</seg>
<seg id="2678"> This has meant a reluctance to use more effective new drugs or lower cost versions of older drugs (generics) .</seg>
<seg id="2679"> Also, physicians have been slow to prescribe drugs that have been widely shown to improve health outcomes, such as beta-blockers for patients following a heart attack .</seg>
<seg id="2680"> Health economists attempting to address such puzzles have frequently appealed to the costs of learning about new drugs and procedures.</seg>
<seg id="2681"> Empirical research on the simplest substitution choices in prescription drugs, that between branded and generic versions of the same drug, show a great heterogeneity in willingness to substitute that is unexplained by factors clearly associated with experience or learning costs .</seg>
<seg id="2682"> Thus, simple extensions of the profit or utility-maximizing models have not produced satisfactory explanations of basic clinical choices.</seg>
<seg id="2683"> 6.2.2
<seg id="2684"> A hallmark of medical decision-making is choice under uncertainty.</seg>
<seg id="2685"> ArrowˇŻs (1963) paper begins with this premise.</seg>
<seg id="2686"> Microeconomic models of physician decision-making that follow in the tradition of Arrow recognize that there is uncertainty about the effectiveness of any medical technology and there is heterogeneity in patient responses to treatment and preferences about care and how the process of care will unfold.</seg>
<seg id="2687"> In this section, a simple model of physician behavior is set out that will enable us to examine some potential contributions of behavioral economics to modifying the model.</seg>
<seg id="2688"> I adopt the model of physician behavior and notation of McGuire (2000) in his review of Physician Agency.</seg>
<seg id="2689"> Markets for medical care are characterized not only by uncertainty but also by asymmetric information between physician and patient.</seg>
<seg id="2690"> Most approaches to paying doctors in medical markets implicitly assume that outcomes are ˇ°noncontractible,ˇ± that is, it is impractical to pay doctors on outcome .</seg>
<seg id="2691"> Thus, most health-care markets make use of payment arrangements in which doctors are rewarded for performance through retention of patients or by attracting new patients.</seg>
<seg id="2692"> The vast majority of physician services are paid for either via capitation contracts (per person per year payment) or under fee for service arrangements.</seg>
<seg id="2693"> In either case, the physician is rewarded for having a higher volume of patients seeking his or her services .</seg>
<seg id="2694"> Thus, if patients choose doctors or decide to stay with doctors based on performance, then physicians will have an incentive to undertake unobserved actions to improve quality and performance.</seg>
<seg id="2695"> 6.2.3.1
<seg id="2696"> Consider how people make choices about physicians and the sources of information used to make judgments about the quality of a physician.</seg>
<seg id="2697"> The methods by which patients obtain information about doctors prior to an initial contact and referrals are obtained play central roles in the formation of priors about doctor quality and willingness to exert effort.</seg>
<seg id="2698"> Most patients are close to fully insured, carrying cost sharing of 20% or less for physician services.</seg>
<seg id="2699"> The health sector in recent years has been characterized by expanded public availability of information on provider performance.</seg>
<seg id="2700"> State governments have published performance data on surgeons and hospitals.</seg>
<seg id="2701"> Local magazines and newspapers regularly report lists of top-ranking physicians.</seg>
<seg id="2702"> The Internet has increased the availability of information on hospitals, physicians and their practices. This expansion of information is part of a deliberate strategy to boost consumerism, improve market efficiency and presumably quality in health care .</seg>
<seg id="2703"> Yet recent surveys of consumers show that 70% of people have not seen any information on the quality of providers .</seg>
<seg id="2704"> These same surveys reveal that 70% of people report that they rely on family and friends and 65% on their usual physician as their most reliable sources of information on other providers of health care and their quality.</seg>
<seg id="2705"> In addition, patients report that they prefer providers with whom they are familiar.</seg>
<seg id="2706"> For example, 76% of survey respondents say they would choose a surgeon that they are familiar with over one that was more highly rated by experts such as state government or accrediting organizations.</seg>
<seg id="2707"> The implications of these data are that the basis upon which priors about quality and performance are formed is the recounted experience of friends and relatives and to a lesser extent a regular doctor.</seg>
<seg id="2708"> Patients also put great weight on familiarity with a physician, presumably based on a relatively small number of positive interactions.</seg>
<seg id="2709"> In the case of choosing a primary care physician the reliance on friends and family is likely to be more important, since the choice suggests that there may be no regular doctor or that the relationship with the regular physician may not be satisfactory.</seg>
<seg id="2710"> This decision-making context is ripe for the types of situational influences on economic choices that are the focus of behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="2711"> Tversky and Kahneman (1973) refer to an availability heuristic in order to describe the reliance on more vivid or memorable evidence to construct a prior about the probability that a physician is high quality or will exert adequate effort.</seg>
<seg id="2712"> Akerlof (1991) calls the phenomenon ˇ°salience.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2713"> Given the data cited above, it is likely that personal reports will be relied upon over examination of systematic information collected on health-care qualifications or performance.</seg>
<seg id="2714"> Personal testimonials on the multidimensional service provided by physicians will be far more available than any systematic data-reporting system.</seg>
<seg id="2715"> Reliance on reports from family and friends may create distortions in perceived quality because of what is most memorable in personal reports.</seg>
<seg id="2716"> Consumers of health care have been shown to be good reporters of certain attributes of care and weakly aware of others.</seg>
<seg id="2717"> For example, patients have been shown to accurately report information on whether a physician was respectful, attentive, clear in explaining clinical issues, and had operated a clean and efficient office.</seg>
<seg id="2718"> These same patients were found to be inaccurate in reporting of the technical quality of care.</seg>
<seg id="2719"> That is, they were not accurate in judging whether a physician supplied appropriate evidence-based treatment .</seg>
<seg id="2720"> It has also been shown that the dimensions of care that consumers understand and can accurately report are not highly correlated with so-called technical aspects of quality care .</seg>
<seg id="2721"> The implication here is that patients will commonly develop a prior about a primary care physician by relying on reports from family and friends based on observations about some dimensions of medical care and perhaps not on the dimensions that most directly affect their health outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="2722"> Applying notions of an availability heuristic that focuses on one dimension of physician performance in the formulation of priors on quality expands the conception of demand and demand response in markets for physician services.</seg>
<seg id="2723"> This offers one promising avenue for better linking of market models to the observed behavior in health care.</seg>
<seg id="2724"> The decision-making environment in health care also offers a context where the formation of priors about providers of medical care may be subject to the ˇ°law of small numbersˇ± bias .</seg>
<seg id="2725"> Rabin (2002) explains that when economic agents believe in the ˇ°law of small numbersˇ± they over interpret the extent to which information from observing a small number of events reflects the experience of the ˇ°populationˇ± from which those events are drawn.</seg>
<seg id="2726"> In the case of medical markets, priors about a physician may be formed based on reports from friends, family, and physicians.</seg>
<seg id="2727"> The reports from friends and family will typically be based on a relatively small number of encounters with a medical professional.</seg>
<seg id="2728"> Referrals from a physician may also be based on a relatively small number of experiences.</seg>
<seg id="2729"> In fact, health-care consumers regularly report that they have greater confidence in sources of care that are likely to rely on small numbers than they do in sources that rely on large representative samples such as accreditation agencies and health plan sponsors.</seg>
<seg id="2730"> National survey data also suggest a high degree of confidence by consumers that they have sufficient information to make the ˇ°rightˇ± choices about medical procedures and providers.</seg>
<seg id="2731"> Eighty-one percent of consumers stated they had enough information to make the right choice about a treatment option and 79% said they had enough information to make the right choice of doctor</seg>
<seg id="2732"> A key implication of a model that views consumers as believing in the law of small numbers is that priors will reflect too much confidence in an opinion formed about the quality or performance of a health-care provider</seg>
<seg id="2733"> 6.2.3.2
<seg id="2734"> The Bayesian consumer is assumed to assess new evidence on the performance of a health-care provider independently of the established priors on the quality of a provider.</seg>
<seg id="2735"> This feature of the model applies to physician encounters that are repeated, such as those with a primary care physician or a specialist treating a chronic illness.</seg>
<seg id="2736"> The contrasting case is a specialty surgery, where the physician choice would not involve an updating process.</seg>
<seg id="2737"> Health-care markets are characterized by a set of institutions and circumstances that might lead consumers to filter information so as to conflict with the Bayesian updating assumption.</seg>
<seg id="2738"> Arrow (1963) notes that trust is a central feature of the doctor-patient relationship.</seg>
<seg id="2739"> Giving trust this important role stems from the uncertainty and vulnerability of the patient.</seg>
<seg id="2740"> Trust is offered up as a nonmarket institution that increases efficiency by reducing the disutility and inefficiency from the uncertainty and the anxiety of illness, as well as the agency problems associated with the asymmetry of information between doctor and patient.</seg>
<seg id="2741"> The health sector is replete with complementary institutions that aim to increase trust between medical providers and patients.</seg>
<seg id="2742"> These include a code of medical ethics, medical education, professionalism, licensure, and, in the case of hospitals, nonprofit status.</seg>
<seg id="2743"> Trust is also recognized as an ingredient of treatment affecting the well-being of a patient.</seg>
<seg id="2744"> Core concepts in behavioral economics seem to fit well with institutions that govern the flow of information and evidence on the manner in which patients use information to make choices.</seg>
<seg id="2745"> The psychology of forming priors and Bayesian updating, discussed above, points to hypotheses about the nature of demand response to quality signals in markets for medical care.</seg>
<seg id="2746"> One hypothesis that emerges from applying behavioral concepts to the formation of priors is that the quality signal based on extant market information, or even improved public reporting of quality, may not yield strong demand response to quality signals, as has generally been assumed.</seg>
<seg id="2747"> The normative implication is that improved information may not result in an efficient quality equilibrium in the market unless some other institution is introduced that overrides the type of patient decision-making we have described</seg>
<seg id="2748"> (e.g., employers guiding provider choice based on systematic data).</seg>
<seg id="2749"> Integrating behavioral economics into models of physician behavior would result in different specifications of econometric models of physician services.</seg>
<seg id="2750"> For example, the degree of demand response might be expected to depend on how patients reach a physician.</seg>
<seg id="2751"> Those who were referred by family or a physician would be expected to respond differently to quality signals than those referred by a health plan or the local medical society.</seg>
<seg id="2752"> 6.2.4
<seg id="2753"> Medicine is, I have found, a strange and in many ways disturbing business.</seg>
<seg id="2754"> Thething that still startles me is how fundamentally human an endeavor it is.</seg>
<seg id="2755"> Embedded in the model of physician behavior set out above is a process by which a physician chooses among a complex set of possibilities to treat a particular patient or type of patient.</seg>
<seg id="2756"> A physicianˇŻs choices (assuming profit maximization) depend on her view of the production function, which has also been characterized as a Bayesian learning process .</seg>
<seg id="2757"> These models recognize the institutions through which physicians learn after completion of their training.</seg>
<seg id="2758"> Key sources of learning are peers with whom a physician practices (partners, fellow employees, peers on a hospital staff), continuing medical education courses, pharmaceutical promotion personnel, and medical journals.</seg>
<seg id="2759"> The application of traditional Bayesian updating models has emphasized local sources of information because they are asserted to be low-cost sources of learning .</seg>
<seg id="2760"> These models have been used to explain the persistent variation across geographic areas in the use of medical procedures that cannot be explained by patient illness characteristics, demographics, or indicators of preferences.</seg>
<seg id="2761"> For example, in 1999 Medicare spending was $9,941 per enrollee in Miami, FL, and $4,886 in Minneapolis, MN.</seg>
<seg id="2762"> The difference cannot be accounted for by health or demographics factors such as age, gender, and race, or differences in medical care prices.</seg>
<seg id="2763"> Instead, the spending variation is mostly due to more frequent physician visits, diagnostic tests, and hospitalizations and procedures .</seg>
<seg id="2764"> Consistent with this, Phelps (2000) reports coefficients of variation in hospitalization rates across New York State.</seg>
<seg id="2765"> He shows that coefficients of variation for conditions such as pediatric otitis media, depressive neurosis, and chronic obstructive lung disease were 0.49,0.42, and 0.35, respectively.</seg>
<seg id="2766"> These contrast with services where little discretion can be exercised, such as heart attacks, where the coefficient of variation was 0.12.</seg>
<seg id="2767"> In the Bayesian updating model of physician choice of treatment technology, the prior about the appropriate use of a particular procedure to treat a specific illness (e.g., cesarean section for deliveries) is formed in medical training (medical school and residency) according to the observed share of total cases of a given illness treated with the procedure.</seg>
<seg id="2768"> The physician is then assumed to combine new local observations with prior information, giving each case equal weight in order to form the posterior distribution.</seg>
<seg id="2769"> The result is a weighted average of accumulated observations from medical training and subsequent practice.</seg>
<seg id="2770"> This model can be used to explain how a ˇ°local styleˇ± of medical practice can persist and why adoption of new treatments may be slow.</seg>
<seg id="2771"> In this model it is assumed that the Bayesian physician overweights information from local sources or that data from medical journals or medical conferences get ignored or are given little weight when assessing the use of medical technologies.</seg>
<seg id="2772"> Phelps and Mooney (1993) argue that low-cost local sources of information are emphasized.</seg>
<seg id="2773"> They also note the importance of personal advice from colleagues about how to apply specific medical techniques.</seg>
<seg id="2774"> This latter point is consistent with the availability heuristic, where people tend to overemphasize more vivid personal descriptions of successes and failures than perhaps more representative reports of data from the literature.</seg>
<seg id="2775"> Another behavioral construct that is potentially relevant for understanding why local opinions should receive such high weight is the notion of regret .</seg>
<seg id="2776"> Physicians commonly must choose from among many competing approaches to treating a particular condition and trusting patients rely centrally on the recommendations of the physician.</seg>
<seg id="2777"> This makes the physician largely responsible for the consequences of the complex choice.</seg>
<seg id="2778"> Moreover, these choices are commonly made under considerable time pressure.</seg>
<seg id="2779"> The typical physician visit is about fifteen minutes.</seg>
<seg id="2780"> Thus, the potential for regret is high and the physician has incentives to attempt to lower the responsibility costs of medical decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="2781"> One institution that has long been recognized as an important influence on physician behavior is the professional norm .</seg>
<seg id="2782"> In this case the norm might be viewed as the local approach to treatment that a physician can point to as guiding her choices.</seg>
<seg id="2783"> In the event of a poor outcome, the physician that practices according to local norms can note that they followed standard operating practices, whereas they could more easily be individually tied to a choice if they departed from local practice .</seg>
<seg id="2784"> Giving great weight to local data therefore reduces responsibility costs and might therefore help explain the particular way that Bayesian updating works in medical decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="2785"> These behavioral influences on decision-making are reinforced by local health plan attempts to affect practice patterns through local profiling of physicians.</seg>
<seg id="2786"> This Bayesian physician decision model has been coupled with ideas about how innovations take hold in order to also explain the establishment and persistence of geographic differences in patterns of treatment.</seg>
<seg id="2787"> The existence of this variation that is unexplained by patient characteristics or outcome implies substantial differences in economic welfare across markets that are not competed away.</seg>
<seg id="2788"> While not formally acknowledged, ideas from behavioral economics are at least casually invoked in some of this literature.</seg>
<seg id="2789"> In one example, Phelps (2000) speculates about why different markets might differentially perceive the value of a new medical technique.</seg>
<seg id="2790"> He proposes that a physician innovator might be exposed to a new technique through the medical literature or by attending a conference.</seg>
<seg id="2791"> She tries out the new technique on twenty cases and then makes a judgment about whether the new technique is ˇ°betterˇ± than existing treatment methods.</seg>
<seg id="2792"> Phelps (2000) notes in passing that the physician may not be entirely unbiased because they are invested in the new method. This scenario highlights the potential relevance of the law of small numbers in creating very different views of a particular medical procedure across geographic areas based on a tendency to over interpret the results from a small trial.</seg>
<seg id="2793"> It also suggests the potential role for confirmatory bias or filtering of data among physician innovators and adherents of older methods.</seg>
<seg id="2794"> These ideas can be applied to a Bayesian model of decision-making to help explain the high degree of variation in patterns of treatment across geographic areas and the slow adoption of many new and effective treatment technologies.</seg>
<seg id="2795"> The empirical implications of this line of thinking lead to the view that it would be important to include data reflecting a physicianˇŻs own experiences and those of the immediate community in econometric analyses of physician treatment patterns.</seg>
<seg id="2796"> These factors may also be important in understanding diffusion of new technologies and reduced use of dominated technologies (sometimes referred to as ˇ°exnovationˇ±).</seg>
<seg id="2797"> Such measures have typically not been included in econometric models of physician behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2798"> 6.2.5
<seg id="2799"> A recent natural experiment highlights the potential contribution of the ideas set out above.</seg>
<seg id="2800"> In 1989 the New York State Department of Health initiated a program that would use market forces to improve the quality of care for Coronary Artery Bypass Graft (CABG) surgery.</seg>
<seg id="2801"> The idea was that, by improving the standardization, reliability, and availability of information on physician- and hospital-specific outcomes, consumers who are largely insured would be better equipped to make choices based on performance.</seg>
<seg id="2802"> That is, demand response to outcome signals would discipline poorly performing providers of care and would improve quality of care.</seg>
<seg id="2803"> The State Department of Health developed a very detailed approach to ˇ°risk adjustmentˇ± of mortality outcomes for both physicians and hospitals, so that outcomes would be standardized for systematic patient mortality risk.</seg>
<seg id="2804"> Beginning in November 1992, mortality data were released publicly on surgeons performing 200 or more operations per year at one hospital.</seg>
<seg id="2805"> The Department of Health took great pains to inform the media on issues of quality improvement and interpretation of risk-adjusted mortality rates.</seg>
<seg id="2806"> The surgeon-specific mortality rates were published in major newspapers, including the New York Times and Newsday.</seg>
<seg id="2807"> Two initial observations were made that pose a puzzle for health economists.</seg>
<seg id="2808"> The first is that risk-adjusted mortality rates associated with CABG declined from 4.17% in 1989 to 2.45% in 1992 .</seg>
<seg id="2809"> The second is that no demand response was observed.</seg>
<seg id="2810"> There were no significant shifts in patient volume from surgeons and hospitals with relatively high mortality rates to those with low rates .</seg>
<seg id="2811"> This was initially thought to be a result of the low awareness of the mortality reports among the public.</seg>
<seg id="2812"> Subsequent analyses that reexamined patient volume changes at points later in time, when public awareness of the mortality data was higher, show no evidence of significant demand response to the new information.</seg>
<seg id="2813"> Little attention from health economists has been accorded the demand response result that represents a challenge to basic models of quality competition in health like the one set out above and like that of Dranove and Satterthwaite (1992).</seg>
<seg id="2814"> Some facts about doctor-patient roles in decision-making suggest some direction for applying behavioral economics ideas.</seg>
<seg id="2815"> Data reported above and elsewhere suggest that patients place the greatest weight on recommendations of friends, family, and primary care doctors.</seg>
<seg id="2816"> The patient appears to become even more reliant on the known professional when making choices about treatments for severe medical conditions.</seg>
<seg id="2817"> Research on the division of labor in medical decision-making shows that, for medical conditions such as breast cancer and colorectal cancer, the majority of patients prefer the physician to make the treatment decision (78% for colorectal cancer and 52% for breast cancer).</seg>
<seg id="2818"> In addition, only 20% of the breast cancer patients wanted to be actively involved as opposed to sharing decision-making .</seg>
<seg id="2819"> This research also shows that, when faced with major procedures such as surgery, patients often prefer to be informed but want the physician to make key treatment decisions.</seg>
<seg id="2820"> In fact, a substantial number of patients report being pushed into more active roles in decision-making than preferred.</seg>
<seg id="2821"> Such evidence is consistent with the importance of trust in forming priors and the idea of regret as a limiting factor in people playing an active ˇ°consumerˇ± role.</seg>
<seg id="2822"> Patients frequently appear not to want to carry the consequences of having made a choice if outcomes are poor.</seg>
<seg id="2823"> Economics research has instead focused on selection incentives on the supply side and how the reduction in mortality observed should be interpreted .</seg>
<seg id="2824"> The arguments advanced have been that physicians are risk averse, presumably to a loss of reputation, and as a result will avoid more risky patients.</seg>
<seg id="2825"> This is possible because of imperfections in the risk-adjustment mechanism.</seg>
<seg id="2826"> As a result, physicians are less willing to perform surgery on the most severely ill patients.</seg>
<seg id="2827"> The result is a misleading inference that mortality declined due to improved quality based on the simple morality trends.</seg>
<seg id="2828"> The empirical analysis performed by Dranove and coworkers offers evidence consistent with the hypothesized selection behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2829"> Controlling for selection, they show a reduction in severity of patient illness accompanied by a spending increase.</seg>
<seg id="2830"> (It is worth noting that other analyses of the NewYork experience show that nearly one in five doctors with low performance ratings (bottom quartile) left the practice of cardiac surgery in New York State, which implies an alternative explanation of the observed data .)</seg>
<seg id="2831"> Dranove et al. (2002) interpret the evidence as pointing to a loss in welfare.</seg>
<seg id="2832"> To make this interpretation they presume that, prior to the reporting initiative, the sickest patients were being appropriately treated:</seg>
<seg id="2833"> an assumption not clearly supported by evidence.</seg>
<seg id="2834"> The analysis therefore asserts a type of reputation-maximizing behavior that dominates concerns about the well-being of the sickest patients.</seg>
<seg id="2835"> Another interpretation might be that the public reporting of data led physicians to reweight how they use information that comes from sources other than their own or the immediate local experience.</seg>
<seg id="2836"> This could well generate observations consistent with selection and the observed compression of variance, but might have a less depressing normative conclusion.</seg>
<seg id="2837"> This might be the case if at baseline physicians were offering CABG to ˇ°too manyˇ± very sick patients.</seg>
<seg id="2838"> 6.2.6
<seg id="2839"> One prominent theme in health policy that has only begun to receive careful attention from economists is the issue of disparities in treatment and health-care utilization according to race and ethnicity .</seg>
<seg id="2840"> Balsa and McGuire (2003) suggest a behavioral approach to modification of the type of model of doctor- patient interactions discussed above.</seg>
<seg id="2841"> They propose a central role for a stereotyping heuristic that is used by physicians who face important time constraints and must quickly make assessments of complex clinical circumstances.</seg>
<seg id="2842"> Such a heuristic can offer an efficient method of making uncertain judgments with limited information.</seg>
<seg id="2843"> It can also result in important errors.</seg>
<seg id="2844"> Because personal attributes and health habits are typically important for deciding upon a course of treatment, the use of stereotypes might particularly distort clinical choices.</seg>
<seg id="2845"> For example, physicians have been shown to hold beliefs that African-American patients are more likely to abuse drugs and less likely to comply with recommended treatments than are otherwise similar (with respect to socioeconomic characteristics) white patients .</seg>
<seg id="2846"> The fact that physicians hold such beliefs potentially affects the nature of the efforts that physicians take in administering a particular treatment, because judgments about patient compliance, for example, influence expected outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="2847"> A second area of application involves the complexity of the medical practice.</seg>
<seg id="2848"> American doctors practice in an environment where they treat patients associated with a wide range of insurance arrangements, meaning that the patients face different out-of-pocket prices for differing services and doctors are compensated in a variety of ways.</seg>
<seg id="2849"> For example, a physician will typically encounter fifteen or more different formulary arrangements that define which prescription drugs are paid for and how.</seg>
<seg id="2850"> In addition, they will face similar numbers of compensation schemes, bonus arrangements, quality reporting methods, and patient cost-sharing rules.</seg>
<seg id="2851"> A recent analysis by Glied and Zivin (2002) shows that physician behavior is not consistent with simple maximization models.</seg>
<seg id="2852"> They examine physician choices about intensity of treatment as measured by duration of visits, test orders, and medications prescribed.</seg>
<seg id="2853"> Their results show that physicians respond to incentives but not necessarily to the incentives of the marginal patient.</seg>
<seg id="2854"> They report that the intensity of treatment received by a given patient has as much to do with the payment incentives associated with other patients in the practice as with their own.</seg>
<seg id="2855"> Similar patients should get similar treatment.</seg>
<seg id="2856"> If physicians have a collectivity orientation (possibly reinforced by professional norms) as proposed by Arrow (1963), then treating patients similarly may have intrinsic value and may constrain how a physician can respond to heterogeneous incentives in payments.</seg>
<seg id="2857"> Kahneman et al. (1986) explore how notions of fairness constrain profit-maximizing choices.</seg>
<seg id="2858"> The notion that there are established standards or entitlements that are internalized by physicians and that these influence the supply responses to heterogeneous incentive arrangements constitutes an extension to the ideas of how fairness constrains profit-seeking.</seg>
<seg id="2859"> Another tack that may complement the idea of incorporating fairness into models of physician behavior is that of applying a heuristic in the face of a complex environment in which it is costly to optimize.</seg>
<seg id="2860"> Fairness in this manner would contribute to the choice of rules of thumb in responding to the daunting array of payment arrangements commonly faced by medical practices .</seg>
<seg id="2861"> 6.3
<seg id="2862"> This chapter has focused primarily on markets for physician services, in part because they serve as an excellent model for a large class of prevalent health sector issues related to the supply and demand for treatment under conditions of asymmetric information and uncertainty.</seg>
<seg id="2863"> There are also other issues in health economics where traditional models of economic behavior have not resolved some important puzzles and these may lend themselves productively to the application of behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="2864"> I raise several puzzles that relate to demand and health insurance to illustrate the possibilities.</seg>
<seg id="2865"> 6.3.1
<seg id="2866"> In the discussion of physician behavior, considerable attention was devoted to consumer demand for individual physician services.</seg>
<seg id="2867"> Here we briefly identify several other issues in the demand for health care where behavioral economics may have a constructive role in aiding the explanation of persistent puzzles.</seg>
<seg id="2868"> It is commonly observed by health policy analysts concerned with efficiency and quality that there is persistent overuse, underuse and inappropriate use of care 
<seg id="2869"> The discussion of physician behavior offered some explanations for what has been termed overuse and inappropriate use of services.</seg>
<seg id="2870"> The demand-side sources of underuse that are commonly used in the health sector rely primarily on factors such as externalities, as is the case with the demand for vaccines or lack of insurance.</seg>
<seg id="2871"> The existence of external effects results in inefficiently low levels of demand even when vaccines are made available at a price of zero.</seg>
<seg id="2872"> The policy response is to install some coercive measures such as requiring childhood immunizations to be documented as a condition of entry into school.</seg>
<seg id="2873"> Another major source of underuse is the existence of financial obstacles.</seg>
<seg id="2874"> However, underuse exists in insured populations.</seg>
<seg id="2875"> In the absence of externalities, understanding the demand side of underuse in the context of a nearly fully insured population is more difficult.</seg>
<seg id="2876"> Existing literature on demand for health care offers several directions for studying the issue of underuse of medical care.</seg>
<seg id="2877"> One line of research incorporates ideas of stigma into the preference function in a fashion that assigns stigma the role of a ˇ°price.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2878"> Research on the demand for mental health services and treatment for HIV- related disorders are cases in point .</seg>
<seg id="2879"> In these models getting treatment serves to reveal a stigmatizing condition, thereby imposing an additional cost on the patient if they get care.</seg>
<seg id="2880"> Stigma has been used to explain the low rates of treatment among people with mental disorders</seg>
<seg id="2881"> (e.g., 27% of people with depression and about 50% of people with schizophrenia).</seg>
<seg id="2882"> A new approach to understanding demand behavior draws on research in behavioral economics by Caplin and Leahy (2001).</seg>
<seg id="2883"> Koszegi (2003) makes use of Caplin and LeahyˇŻs psychological expected utility model, where mental states that affect utility are influenced by both beliefs and observed outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="2884"> Information affects beliefs and in turn the mental states that are related to utility.</seg>
<seg id="2885"> Koszegi uses this framework to show how anxiety might affect the demand for specific information, tests, and treatment.</seg>
<seg id="2886"> He identifies a trade-off between seeking treatment and obtaining information.</seg>
<seg id="2887"> Information, for some patients, can create lost utility from anxiety if the news is bad, but having more complete information enables a patient and their doctor to more optimally choose a treatment strategy.</seg>
<seg id="2888"> Depending on the net contribution of the information to utility, a patient will demand contact with providers and information about their condition.</seg>
<seg id="2889"> Not having all the available information can cause anxiety in other patients.</seg>
<seg id="2890"> Applying models of consumer behavior that incorporate anticipatory feelings into quasi-Bayesian updating models about how medical information and associated physician contacts are used and in turn affect demand for services has the potential to address a range of ˇ°underuseˇ± problems such as noncompliance with treatment regimens .</seg>
<seg id="2891"> A third direction for studying demand is suggested by observations made by decision scientists who have attempted to study health utility states.</seg>
<seg id="2892"> They have noted a rather dramatic ability of people to adapt to changing health states.</seg>
<seg id="2893"> That is, people who develop significant chronic conditions (e.g., diabetes, strokes, limited mobility) tend to rate their well-being at levels similar to that of the general population.</seg>
<seg id="2894"> The usual specifications of preferences propose inter-temporal and inter-state stability in preferences.</seg>
<seg id="2895"> These observations suggest a somewhat different process than that studied in hyperbolic discounting .</seg>
<seg id="2896"> Modeling adjustment to health states may contribute to explaining behavior with respect to hospice care, end-of- life care, and the use of advance medical directives.</seg>
<seg id="2897"> Unrelated to issues of ˇ°under treatmentˇ± is a demand-side puzzle that has come to light in recent studies of demand response to different drug formulary designs</seg>
<seg id="2898"> Those studies estimated demand response to changes in the relative prices, created by implementation of a formulary design change, for different drugs within a therapeutic class (e.g., proton pump inhibitors).</seg>
<seg id="2899"> Several therapeutic classes were studied.</seg>
<seg id="2900"> Some classes contained drugs that were found in clinical studies to be highly substitutable in production (they produce comparable clinical benefits and have similar side effect profiles), while others are considered more heterogeneous and less substitutable.</seg>
<seg id="2901"> Estimated demand responses across classes were generally quite modest and of similar magnitude across therapeutic classes.</seg>
<seg id="2902"> In particular, the demand response for proton pump inhibitors (a treatment for gastritis) was small, even though studies of the clinical properties of these drugs indicate that they are highly substitutable in production .</seg>
<seg id="2903"> Moreover, physicians appear to view these drugs as clinically similar.</seg>
<seg id="2904"> The implication is that consumers are reluctant to change drugs.</seg>
<seg id="2905"> Why should consumers be unresponsive to substantial relative demand price differences (100%) given the evidence on the high degree of substitution in production?</seg>
<seg id="2906"> The parallels between the observations made in the preceding paragraphs and the theoretical and experimental research on reference levels and status quo bias is striking .</seg>
<seg id="2907"> It appears that the initial choice of products affects preferences even in the context of low transaction costs and high degrees of similarity in product attributes.</seg>
<seg id="2908"> Models of demand for prescription drugs might therefore profitably incorporate information on the history of individual product use.</seg>
<seg id="2909"> The normative implications are potentially significant in that evidence of such preference structures would potentially constitute a rationale for the design of formularies that override consumer preferences.</seg>
<seg id="2910"> Closed formularies, which will only pay for drugs on the list of preferred products, represent one mechanism that would override consumer preferences and cause a shift to a new reference or status quo product.</seg>
<seg id="2911"> 6.3.2
<seg id="2912"> One puzzle from the health insurance markets that has received some treatment from behavioral economics is that of why insurance plans with close to first-dollar coverage dominate health insurance markets.</seg>
<seg id="2913"> Economists have long been concerned with the nature of the trade-off between moral hazard and risk spreading .</seg>
<seg id="2914"> The fact that health insurance markets persistently have equilibrium policies with levels of cost sharing that approximate first-dollar coverage is puzzling in that there appears to be a surprisingly high willingness to pay for the least valuable forms of insurance protection.</seg>
<seg id="2915"> Thaler (1980) addressed this issue by appealing to the idea of regret.</seg>
<seg id="2916"> He argued that insurance features such as deductibles that force consumers to trade-off medical care use for money impose important psychic costs because of the potential for regret.</seg>
<seg id="2917"> In order to avoid making trade-offs that they might later regret, consumers are posited to demand health insurance designs that allow them to remove themselves from making such choices.</seg>
<seg id="2918"> The result is an increased willingness of people to pay premiums to obtain first-dollar coverage.</seg>
<seg id="2919"> Finally, an issue related to the use of markets to purchase health insurance.</seg>
<seg id="2920"> A longstanding debate in health policy and health economics involves the trade-off between the efficiency gains from competitive insurance markets and the losses that stem from adverse selection .</seg>
<seg id="2921"> The empirical health economics literature offers evidence of both substantial price reductions when competition is intense and large losses from adverse selection .</seg>
<seg id="2922"> There is a presumption in much of health economics that more choice is better.</seg>
<seg id="2923"> In fact, the de facto model of health-care delivery in the United States and other nations is that of ˇ°managed competitionˇ± .</seg>
<seg id="2924"> The assumption is that consumers find the right health plans and that overall the net gains of wider choice are positive.</seg>
<seg id="2925"> At the same time consumers appear to know little about health plans in their choice sets.</seg>
<seg id="2926"> Recent consumer surveys suggest that only about 36% of consumers believe they understand differences between health plans offered to them .</seg>
<seg id="2927"> For example, less than 50% of surveyed consumers knew that health maintenance organizations (HMOs) were plans that place greater emphasis on preventive care.</seg>
<seg id="2928"> In addition, employers that organize markets for health insurance frequently limit the range of consumer choice of health insurance.</seg>
<seg id="2929"> Nevertheless, U.S. public policy has in recent years encouraged the broadest choices possible in the design of health insurance schemes such as Medicare and the Federal Employees Health Benefit program.</seg>
<seg id="2930"> Little research has been conducted on the effectiveness of consumer choice in health insurance markets.</seg>
<seg id="2931"> General lessons from the behavioral economics literature may lead one to conclude that the analysis of the trade-off from expanding choice among competing health plans may be quite incomplete.</seg>
<seg id="2932"> Research in behavioral economics shows that, as the number of choices among complicated products expands, consumers appear to consider a decreasing number of the available options,</seg>
<seg id="2933"> or they attempt to avoid choices altogether by putting off decisions or reverting to a default option .</seg>
<seg id="2934"> It has also been shown that, as decisions become more complex, people adopt increasingly simple rules of thumb to make their choices .</seg>
<seg id="2935"> Loewenstein (2000) applies these ideas to the problem of U.S. Social Security reform.</seg>
<seg id="2936"> He argues that, in that case, the costs of more choice may exceed the benefits.</seg>
<seg id="2937"> The health insurance market is filled with natural experiments in complex, seemingly high-stakes choices where the choice set varies dramatically.</seg>
<seg id="2938"> Empirical analysis motivated by the behavioral economics concepts noted here may offer valuable information for better understanding of the net benefits of expanding choice in health insurance.</seg>
<seg id="2939"> For example, the U.S. Medicare program offers a rich laboratory for such investigations.</seg>
<seg id="2940"> There is a great heterogeneity in the number of managed care plan options available to Medicare beneficiaries across the United States.</seg>
<seg id="2941"> Yet in all markets there is a common default plan, the traditional fee-for-service Medicare plan.</seg>
<seg id="2942"> Policy makers and researchers have been repeatedly surprised by the low rates of enrollment in Medicare managed care plans given the richer assortment of insurance coverage they frequently offer (e.g., prescription drugs at no additional premium cost).</seg>
<seg id="2943"> Understanding how the structure of the choice set affects the willingness to depart from the default option would be important for understanding trade-offs in program design choices.</seg>
<seg id="2944"> Specifically, are Medicare beneficiaries who face larger numbers of choices more likely to choose traditional Medicare over managed care plans?</seg>
<seg id="2945"> This would be important information for a U.S. Congress that has shown such a strong interest in encouraging enrollment in managed care arrangements for Medicare beneficiaries.</seg>
<seg id="2946"> 6.4
<seg id="2947"> The health sector has long offered a challenging venue for applied economists.</seg>
<seg id="2948"> Arrow recognized this in the early 1960s.</seg>
<seg id="2949"> Following the tradition of Arrow, I have focused the discussion here on markets for health services and health insurance.</seg>
<seg id="2950"> Health economists have become habituated to dealing with institutions and facts that raise questions about the usefulness of applying the neoclassical model to health-care markets.</seg>
<seg id="2951"> The nature of the doctor-patient interaction in the presence of insurance is at the heart of many of the most challenging problems in health policy and health economics.</seg>
<seg id="2952"> The first empirical studies in health economics came face to face with the issue of characterizing the doctor-patient interaction, and it remains a central problem in health economics.</seg>
<seg id="2953"> The application of standard principal-agent models has produced an increasingly sophisticated ability to describe doctor-patient interactions and to develop refutable hypotheses about parameters that affect price, quantity, and quality outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="2954"> Nevertheless, observed demand and supply responses in markets for physician services are incompletely understood.</seg>
<seg id="2955"> The case of public reporting of physician performance in CABG surgery offers a vivid example of the limited ability of the standard model to explain market behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2956"> Behavioral economics offers some concepts and analytical tools that fit well with the institutions of the health sector.</seg>
<seg id="2957"> The doctor-patient decision-making context of limited information, a noisy health production function, fear, anxiety, insurance coverage, and trust is well suited to the concerns of behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="2958"> I have attempted to highlight how relatively simple models of the doctor-patient interactions can be extended using ideas from behavioral economics to enrich the ability to understand and develop empirical models of health-care markets.</seg>
<seg id="2959"> The issues identified go to the core concerns of health economics.</seg>
<seg id="2960"> These include: how information affects equilibrium quality in health-care markets; how physician competition might affect prices and quantities;</seg>
<seg id="2961"> what produces persistent variations in treatment patterns across markets and the associated welfare losses;</seg>
<seg id="2962"> and what explains racial and ethnic disparities in health-care use.</seg>
<seg id="2963"> I have emphasized the role of behavioral economics in extending positive models of health-care markets.</seg>
<seg id="2964"> Behavioral economics offers direction for addressing long-standing impasses in positive health economics.</seg>
<seg id="2965"> There are also important normative implications.</seg>
<seg id="2966"> Clearly, if doctors and patients make the types of context-specific choices and cognitive errors described here, demand functions can no longer be given the normative interpretations that are common even in health economics .</seg>
<seg id="2967"> Critics of the use of standard demand models in health care have clearly and persuasively highlighted the limits to using these for normative model analysis.</seg>
<seg id="2968"> They have also failed to offer new approaches to understanding such behavior.</seg>
<seg id="2969"> One direction pointed to by Thaler and Sunstein (2003) is to use cost-benefit analysis of alternate polices that aim to account for the type of cognitive errors described here.</seg>
<seg id="2970"> Cost-effectiveness is widely applied in health care to make efficiency judgments about competing medical practices and policies.</seg>
<seg id="2971"> There are, however, a variety of difficulties in applying these tools in the context of trying to account for consumer preferences for making welfare judgments .</seg>
<seg id="2972"> Another interesting approach is known as case-based decision theory .</seg>
<seg id="2973"> In this case the normative analysis is conducted by assessing whether a policy action results in a reduction in the type of decision-making ˇ°errorsˇ± that serve to undermine the types of expected utility models frequently used in health economics.</seg>
<seg id="2974"> Thus, policies that reduce distortions in decision-making would be viewed as ˇ°welfare-improving.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="2975"> These are new ideas and to date there is no clear path toward a new normative approach that might be applied in fields such as health economics.</seg>
<seg id="2976"> The policy implications stemming from the application of behavioral economics in analyzing health-care markets are potentially profound.</seg>
<seg id="2977"> Two cornerstones of recent U.S. health policy are the presumption that increasing the availability of information to consumers will result in improved quality of care, and that more choice of health plans and providers will inevitably make consumers better off.</seg>
<seg id="2978"> Examining the basic laboratory and psychological research used in behavioral economics immediately raises challenges to these policy fundamentals.</seg>
<seg id="2979"> I have attempted to identify some specific areas where these ideas might have particular importance for ongoing policy debates.</seg>
<seg id="2980"> I expect that a sustained program of research involving the ideas of behavioral economics applied to health-care markets will likely result in at least some intelligent modification of these articles of faith.</seg>
<seg id="2981"> COMMENT BY JACOB GLAZER</seg>
<seg id="2982"> 6.4.1
<seg id="2983"> There are few, if any, sectors in the economy where the growing paradigm generally termed ˇ°behavioral economicsˇ± seems to be as relevant and important as the market for health services.</seg>
<seg id="2984"> Starting with Arrow (1963), many economists have recognized the fact that, in the health services and health insurance market, consumers and providers cannot (on the basis of their behavior) reasonably be described as rational agents acting to maximize their expected utility or profit.</seg>
<seg id="2985"> Furthermore, motives and considerations such as altruism, trust, and norms, often outside the economistsˇŻ usual playground, appear to play an important role in the agentsˇŻ decision-making process.</seg>
<seg id="2986"> Most of these researchers, however, have implemented their own, often ad hoc, assumptions about how agents behave in these markets and very few have actually relied on models developed and results obtained in related behavioral fields such as psychology, sociology, and, especially, the more recently emerging field of behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="2987"> It is perhaps for this reason that, in spite of the voluminous research addressing this issue and the tremendous effort it has involved, many economists feel that we have not yet come up with a good model (or models) for understanding one of the most important activities in health care: the doctor-patient interaction</seg>
<seg id="2988"> There is no doubt in my mind that bridging between these two almost parallel lines of research behavioral economics and heath economicsˇŞwill take us in the right direction if we wish to get a better understanding of the relationship between doctors and patients.</seg>
<seg id="2989"> FrankˇŻs work is an excellent start in laying down the foundations for that bridge.</seg>
<seg id="2990"> Frank identifies the main domains in the health-care market in which agents do not seem to behave according to the ˇ°cognitive paradigmˇ± and, more importantly in my opinion, he points out some ˇ°regularitiesˇ± in the, so to speak, ˇ°boundedly rationalˇ± behavior of these agents.</seg>
<seg id="2991"> Frank goes on to raise many interesting ideas of how models and ideas developed by behavioral economists and other researchers might be applied to obtain a better understanding of how patients and doctors act and interact,</seg>
<seg id="2992"> and he even suggests possible explanations for some of the puzzling phenomena that have been observed in this market.</seg>
<seg id="2993"> In what follows I briefly present my understanding of some the main aspects of the doctor-patient interaction where behavioral economics seems very relevant and potentially very useful.</seg>
<seg id="2994"> This discussion is based mainly on FrankˇŻs presentation (see above) as well as some other readings.</seg>
<seg id="2995"> In the first part of this essay, I present the main dimensions on which providers and patients decide and act in the market for health care.</seg>
<seg id="2996"> In the second part I highlight some of the main observations regarding how agents behave in this market.</seg>
<seg id="2997"> In the third part I bring the two groups of agents, doctors and patients, together and briefly discuss the phenomenon that many economists view as the main symptom of the market failure in health care: small area variations.</seg>
<seg id="2998"> The last part is devoted to some thoughts regarding the implications of behavioral health economics for the efficiency of markets and welfare in general.</seg>
<seg id="2999"> 6.4.3
<seg id="3000"> Health care is an extremely complicated product.</seg>
<seg id="3001"> Each case is different and may involve multiple decisions with multiple uncertainties.</seg>
<seg id="3002"> New data and evidence about the effectiveness of different procedures keep arising alongside new technologies.</seg>
<seg id="3003"> It is no surprise, therefore, that both consumers and producers find decision-making in such an environment very difficult and frustrating and that they often find themselves following a decision-making procedure that might seem suboptimal to an outside observer</seg>
<seg id="3004"> (a recommended reading for most of the issues discussed here is McCall (1996)).</seg>
<seg id="3005"> Casual observation and much empirical evidence have demonstrated that patients often form their beliefs and make their decision on the basis of</seg>
<seg id="3006"> ?
<seg id="3007"> (e.g., a young woman decides to have a mammography just because a friend has been diagnosed with breast cancer),</seg>
<seg id="3008"> ?
<seg id="3009"> (e.g., people who suffer from back pain choose to have acupuncture because many people have taken this treatment before and seem to be doing better),</seg>
<seg id="3010"> ?
<seg id="3011"> (e.g., a pregnant woman decides to have amniocentesis after a neighbor has had her baby diagnosed with DownˇŻs Syndrome),</seg>
<seg id="3012"> ?
<seg id="3013"> (the patient simply follows the doctorˇŻs advice, often saying: ˇ°you are the doctorˇ±),</seg>
<seg id="3014"> ?
<seg id="3015"> (patients do not seek a second opinion because they do not want to upset their doctor),</seg>
<seg id="3016"> ?
<seg id="3017"> (ˇ°this medication really works for meˇ±).</seg>
<seg id="3018"> Similarly, providers of health care often form their beliefs and take actions on the basis of</seg>
<seg id="3019"> (e.g., the doctor stops prescribing a particular medication just because it did not seem to help one or a few of her patients),</seg>
<seg id="3020"> (e.g., a doctor might prefer one treatment over another just because more of her own or her colleaguesˇŻ patients seem to be doing better on it),</seg>
<seg id="3021"> ?
<seg id="3022"> (e.g., the doctor believes that certain groups of patients tend not comply with a physicianˇŻs recommendations),</seg>
<seg id="3023"> ?
<seg id="3024"> (the doctor does not actually ˇ°make a cognitive decisionˇ± for each new case she treats, but rather follows some ˇ°standard operating procedureˇ± common among her peers or colleagues or even simply something that she has adopted and became familiar with),</seg>
<seg id="3025"> ?
<seg id="3026"> (sometimes, even when there is really no solution to the patientˇŻs problem, the doctor feels that she cannot just send the patient home without a recommendation or prescription),</seg>
<seg id="3027"> ?
<seg id="3028"> (e.g., sometimes doctors prescribe antibiotics just because this is what the patient has asked for, not because it is supposed to work in his case).</seg>
<seg id="3029"> Furthermore, even though deciding on the optimal treatment almost always involves solving a ˇ°decision tree,ˇ± agents (i.e., doctors and patients) often prefer to think about it ˇ°one step at a timeˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3030"> (i.e., they do not apply backward induction).</seg>
<seg id="3031"> Overall, it seems that agents in the health-care market adopt different decision-making procedures and modes of behavior, depending on the circumstances.</seg>
<seg id="3032"> I think that health economists have done far too little in trying to understand how exactlythe agentsˇŻ decision-making procedure is determined in the different situations and how it can be affected.</seg>
<seg id="3033"> This is precisely where ideas from behavioral economics might be most helpful.</seg>
<seg id="3034"> There are many different hypotheses that might be useful in understanding doctorsˇŻ and patientsˇŻ behavior.</seg>
<seg id="3035"> Among them are</seg>
<seg id="3036"> ?
<seg id="3037"> ?
<seg id="3038"> ?
<seg id="3039"> ?
<seg id="3040"> ?
<seg id="3041"> ?
<seg id="3042"> ?
<seg id="3043"> ?
<seg id="3044"> ?
<seg id="3045"> Though these hypotheses ought to be analyzed and tested, they at least provide a starting point for a theory that will better explain the doctor-patient interaction.</seg>
<seg id="3046"> 6.4.5
<seg id="3047"> I would like to end this discussion by raising two thoughts that I find somewhat intriguing.</seg>
<seg id="3048"> 6.4.5.1
<seg id="3049"> Until recently the market for health care was built mainly on trust.</seg>
<seg id="3050"> Patients did not search for the best available provider whenever they had a medical problem, but rather used the one with which they had become familiar.</seg>
<seg id="3051"> Patients usually did not know much, and in most cases did not even try to learn much, about their problem and what the available treatments were.</seg>
<seg id="3052"> And, finally, when their doctor made a recommendation, the patients hardly ever questioned her judgment.</seg>
<seg id="3053"> Things are very different today.</seg>
<seg id="3054"> More and more information about providersˇŻ performance is becoming public and may assist patients in choosing their provider.</seg>
<seg id="3055"> Medical information about various diseases and the most recent procedures with which to treat them is easily accessible (e.g., via the Internet) and patients often demand a certain treatment from their doctor.</seg>
<seg id="3056"> Moreover, if they are unsatisfied with the doctorˇŻs treatment, patients are much less reluctant to ask for a second opinion or seek another doctor.</seg>
<seg id="3057"> The market for health care is becoming more and more like any other market.</seg>
<seg id="3058"> The advantages of this new market organization are clear: patients can make more educated and overall much better decisions as they have more information to rely on and doctors have much stronger incentives to provide high-quality care, since their quality is being more tightly ˇ°monitoredˇ± by the consumers and payers.</seg>
<seg id="3059"> One should remember, however, that, given the complexity and dynamics of the healthcare product, market competition and incentive schemes, efficient as they may be, will probably not be sufficient to achieve the highest feasible quality of care</seg>
<seg id="3060"> and in the end it will still be the doctorˇŻs preferences and sense of responsibility that play a major role in the treatmentˇŻs outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="3061"> Even under this new market organization, a significant part of the information regarding the suitable and relevant procedures to treat a patient will remain with the doctor, among other things,</seg>
<seg id="3062"> because of her professional education and experience, and the patient will still have to rely a lot on the doctorˇŻs effort and good intentions.</seg>
<seg id="3063"> An interesting question, I think, is whether the shift of the doctor-patient relationship, from one that is mainly built on trust and where it is common knowledge that the patient expects the doctor to care for him to a relationship that looks more like any other economic transaction, may not diminish the doctorˇŻs sense of responsibility for the patient and, hence, her (unobservable) effort and, at the end, the quality of care she provides.</seg>
<seg id="3064"> Is it not possible that the doctorˇŻs preferences will change with the change in the market environment?</seg>
<seg id="3065"> The second question that I would like to raise has to do more with the normative aspects of behavioral health economics.</seg>
<seg id="3066"> COMMENT BY BOTOND KOSZEGI</seg>
<seg id="3067"> As with any speaker at the conference, Frank had his work cut out for him, having to summarize the state of research in behavioral health economics.</seg>
<seg id="3068"> He responded with a very nice paper, a tour de force of problems in health economics, with some issues we know a lot about and many puzzles behavioral economics might help resolve.</seg>
<seg id="3069"> There is theory in here, mostly an outline of the classical model of physician decision-making and a little on patient decision-making. There are many empirical facts on both physician and patient behavior, and thoughtful discussions tying theory and empirical data together.</seg>
<seg id="3070"> There is speculation about the psychological forces underlying the different types of behavior, and suggestions for future research.</seg>
<seg id="3071"> I learned a lot from the paper.</seg>
<seg id="3072"> In this discussion, I will briefly describe the context of medical decision-making and the paperˇŻs topics, and then point out a few directions in which future research will hopefully go.</seg>
<seg id="3073"> I confine the discussion to treatment of illness and health services, and ignore the quite different health insurance market.</seg>
<seg id="3074"> One of the main lessons we have taken to heart from psychology is that behavior is sensitive to the context in which choices are made.</seg>
<seg id="3075"> Thus, it is worth identifying some general aspects of the context in which medical decision-making occurs.</seg>
<seg id="3076"> Decisions about health are typically very important for welfare; they involve extreme trade-offsˇŞboth the costs and the benefits are often very highˇŞand occur under uncertainty and asymmetric information and knowledge.</seg>
<seg id="3077"> The consumers of health care are not in a position to make decisions by themselves,</seg>
<seg id="3078"> but there are experts (doctors) who know much more about the available treatment options and the patientˇŻs condition than the patient does, and who offer their advice and services in a market with lots of options and (at least nowadays) lots of information about providers.</seg>
<seg id="3079"> Neoclassical economic theory predicts that in these situations, outcomes are going to be pretty close to optimal (under the constraints of medical knowledge and resources).</seg>
<seg id="3080"> Because the decision is important and the potential costs and benefits are high, patients seek out a lot of information from a variety of sources, exert a lot of effort in their rehabilitation and treatment programs and to maintain their health, carefully follow their drug regimens, and so forth.</seg>
<seg id="3081"> And because the business is so important and therefore lucrative, doctors work hard to keep their knowledge up to date, and provide the best care to maintain their reputations and patients.</seg>
<seg id="3082"> In stark contrast to this economic theory view that health care is an environment that will induce people to make very good choices, psychologists would say that it is in some ways rather conducive to making the worst mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="3083"> This makes health care an illustrative and important application of behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="3084"> In medical decision-making, a lot of information must be acquired and processed quickly and distilled into major decisions, andˇŞlike most of usˇŞboth doctors and patients make many mistakes in doing this.</seg>
<seg id="3085"> Because of the importance of the decisions and the large stakes, and because of uncertainty, patientsˇŻ decisions must be made under extreme emotions and stress.</seg>
<seg id="3086"> And patients must make many painful investments that will show returns only far in the future.</seg>
<seg id="3087"> These are exactly where psychologists predict behavior will deviate greatly from neoclassical economic theory.</seg>
<seg id="3088"> With this as a general motivation, one way to categorize the themes in the paper is the following.</seg>
<seg id="3089"> First, a few key aspects of the health care context fit right in with standard economic concepts and, because of this, classical methods have been extremely helpful in understanding health-care markets.</seg>
<seg id="3090"> But that is not what the focus is on here, so I proceed to what the bulk of the paper is about.</seg>
<seg id="3091"> The paper devotes considerable attention to physician motivations and decision-making mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="3092"> Physicians appear not to be driven purely by profit-maximization incentives.</seg>
<seg id="3093"> A number of alternatives have been proposedˇŞmany of which RichardˇŻs paper has discussedˇŞbut none of them very strongly grounded in evidence or anywhere close to satisfactory.</seg>
<seg id="3094"> Doctors seem to respond to incentives, but more to average, not marginal ones.</seg>
<seg id="3095"> And doctors are slow to put into practice even widely available information on the effectiveness of treatments and drugs.</seg>
<seg id="3096"> The paper then deals with mistakes on the other side of the market, the patient side.</seg>
<seg id="3097"> Patients often underuse medical care, and are subject to default effects in drug use and insurance provider choices.</seg>
<seg id="3098"> Like doctors, patients also use information quite poorly, forming their opinion of a physician based on limited information from family and friends, trusting a lot of their treatment and advice to a single doctor they know well, and tending to forgive bad outcomes as inevitable and outside the doctorˇŻs control.</seg>
<seg id="3099"> This is in part understandable, since patients have difficulty judging the technical expertise of a doctor,</seg>
<seg id="3100"> but it is in part puzzling, because information on doctors and hospitals is easily available.</seg>
<seg id="3101"> These points are mostly about individual decision-making on the two sides of the market.</seg>
<seg id="3102"> What economists are most interested in is how these patterns of behavior feed through the market to produce health outcomes and costs.</seg>
<seg id="3103"> It seems obvious that the first-order effect of many of the mistakes discussed is to make health outcomes worse.</seg>
<seg id="3104"> But doctors and patients interact in the market, so psychological effects on their behavior will interact and influence each other.</seg>
<seg id="3105"> This is a place where economists, with their understanding of equilibrium feedback, can add a lot to the debate, and where a lot of the interesting and complex questions are going to be.</seg>
