<seg id="3106"> With individual decision-making being complicated enough, there is very little research on this so far.</seg>
<seg id="3107"> But since I expect and hope that there will be much more in the near future, I devote some space to speculation.</seg>
<seg id="3108"> One natural question to ask is how patient behavior affects the incentives of doctors to provide good care.</seg>
<seg id="3109"> To the extent that FrankˇŻs paper deals with this question, it comes across as quite pessimistic.</seg>
<seg id="3110"> Since patients use very limited information and tend to trust and stay with their regular physicians, the argument goes, demand is unresponsive to performance, so doctors have little or no incentives to perform well.</seg>
<seg id="3111"> I think this is a more complicated issue, and I am not sure whether I agree with the paperˇŻs conclusion.</seg>
<seg id="3112"> True, the fact that patients ask their friends for advice about doctors, and do not look at more reliable publicly available data, implies that most of the time they do not respond to doctor performance.</seg>
<seg id="3113"> This of course benefits a doctor with mediocre performance.</seg>
<seg id="3114"> But for the same reason, demand can be quite volatile.</seg>
<seg id="3115"> If a critical mass of people decides that a doctor is not good enough, this doctor might lose a lot of patients very quickly, as rumors about her performance spread.</seg>
<seg id="3116"> Once this happens, the same forces that benefited a mediocre doctor before now work against her: it will be very difficult for her to win the patients back.</seg>
<seg id="3117"> This threat might discipline doctors as much as or more than a competitive market.</seg>
<seg id="3118"> Of course, at the same time it might also give them incentives to provide good care in the wrong dimensions</seg>
<seg id="3119"> (e.g., to take steps so that people like them, not necessarily to choose the technically best options).</seg>
<seg id="3120"> To model these demand responses, we will certainly need very different market models than those that exist today.</seg>
<seg id="3121"> The paper does not deal at all with the flip side of this issue: how doctorsˇŻ motivations and decision-making mistakes affect the behavior of patients.</seg>
<seg id="3122"> This is an equally rich area for exploration.</seg>
<seg id="3123"> To begin with, it would be interesting to know to what extent patients are aware that doctors regularly make mistakes, and if they are not, what creates their ˇ°faithˇ± in the medical profession.</seg>
<seg id="3124"> If they are awareŇ»which is likely to be the case to some extentŇ»further fascinating issues arise.</seg>
<seg id="3125"> From a classical point of view, it is then even more puzzling why patients often rely on the advice of just one doctor and do not seek out a lot of medical information.</seg>
<seg id="3126"> But realizing that doctors are imperfect might also make medical decision-making more uncertain and stressful, exacerbating the psychological mechanisms that hinder proper health behavior.</seg>
<seg id="3127"> There are three things I felt were missing from the paper.</seg>
<seg id="3128"> First, in terms of the overarching topics emphasized in the paper, there is very little about the psychological aspects of the doctor-patient relationship.</seg>
<seg id="3129"> The two sides of the market are treated more or less in isolation, and the interaction that occurs (demand response to doctor performance) is very classical.</seg>
<seg id="3130"> Yet doctors serve an important role in communicating diagnoses and treatment options to patients, in motivating them to follow through in their treatment, in making sure that they practice adequate preventive care, that they perform checkups and have the right lifestyle, and so on.</seg>
<seg id="3131"> These are all psychologically rich issues.</seg>
<seg id="3132"> They also cause a lot of frustration for doctors and are very important for patients.</seg>
<seg id="3133"> In surveys, patients tend to express satisfaction with their doctorˇŻs technical expertise, but complain about interaction-type issues such as respect, speaking in understandable, nontechnical terms, and communicating in a sensitive manner.</seg>
<seg id="3134"> As an illustration that understanding the doctor-patient relationship requires more than understanding the two sides of the market separately, consider Table 6.4, taken from Christakis (1999).</seg>
<seg id="3135"> Doctors were given short descriptions of hypothetical medical scenarios, and were asked about how they would behave in each case.</seg>
<seg id="3136"> Strikingly, doctors are much more likely to communicate optimism about a given case when dealing with the patient than when talking to another doctor.</seg>
<seg id="3137"> This discrepancy is clearly not about how doctors form their own opinions, and is unlikely to be about demand inducement or other classical concerns.</seg>
<seg id="3138"> Rather, it is probably about the human side of the doctor-patient relationship.</seg>
<seg id="3139"> SecondˇŞand this is a general concern regarding behavioral economicsˇŞonce we start to import insights from psychology, a lot of observed economic behavior will seem grossly overdetermined.</seg>
<seg id="3140"> That is, there will be multiple reasonable explanations for the same phenomenon.</seg>
<seg id="3141"> Yet to think about the right policies, having some explanation for a phenomenon is not sufficient;</seg>
<seg id="3142"> since different models will often have very different policy implications, it is crucial to have the right explanation.</seg>
<seg id="3143"> For example, FrankˇŻs paper identifies three possible sources of patient underuse of medical care, i.e., that patients do not take advantage of health resources when they should: externalities, stigma, and emotions.</seg>
<seg id="3144"> If underuse of care is due to externalities (that part of the reason people should get care is to benefit others), the right policy is to subsidize or regulate use (e.g., by requiring immunizations).</seg>
<seg id="3145"> Subsidies will probably work in the other cases as well, but they will be very expensive and crude policies for the purpose.</seg>
<seg id="3146"> If underuse is due to stigma (that being known to have certain conditions is stigmatizing) policies should probably aim to ensure complete privacy and assure patients that nobody needs to know about their condition,</seg>
<seg id="3147"> and to make it really easy for them to get treatment, so that they do not have to deal with the stigmatizing activity for very long.</seg>
<seg id="3148"> If underuse is due to emotions such as fear of bad news, the best policies will manage the information patients receive and how they receive them.</seg>
<seg id="3149"> In this and other examples, it will be important to identify possible sources of information (either existing sources or potential ways of collecting the information) that might be used to decide which explanation is the right one to what extent.</seg>
<seg id="3150"> It is likely that we will need very different sources of information than those traditionally used in economics, even health economics.</seg>
<seg id="3151"> One way to keep models and ideas grounded in reality is to make sure that they are well founded on psychological and economic evidenceˇŞand I think the paper does a good job in doing this.</seg>
<seg id="3152"> But some of the work in health economics that is reviewed is much less convincing on this front, treating doctors as people whose motivations and norms of conduct are fundamentally different from everyone elseˇŻs.</seg>
<seg id="3153"> To a first approximation, doctors are like other humans.</seg>
<seg id="3154"> Investigating how the psychological tendencies that are present in all of us play out in the context of a doctorˇŻs work is a disciplined way to enrich the modeling of their behavior.</seg>
<seg id="3155"> Third, related to all this, a lot of FrankˇŻs paper is positive in nature, describing patterns of behavior and their possible causes.</seg>
<seg id="3156"> It would be interesting to develop some of the normative implications of the analysis:</seg>
<seg id="3157"> how we can use these insights to improve medical outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="3158"> 7.1 INTRODUCTION</seg>
<seg id="3159"> This essay analyzes how behavioral economics can be applied to organizations, and how it can be enriched by thinking about the economic questions associated with economic organization.</seg>
<seg id="3160"> Behavioral economics modifies the standard economic model to account for psychophysical properties of preference and judgment, which create limits on rational calculation, willpower, and greed</seg>
<seg id="3161"> The modified economics theory aims at providing parsimonious and psychologically sound explanations for empirical findings that the standard model has a tough time explaining.</seg>
<seg id="3162"> From a methodological perspective, behavioral economics is simply a humble approach to economics, which respects the comparative empirical advantages of neighboring social sciences and sees neighboring sciences as trading partners.</seg>
<seg id="3163"> The empirical regularity and constructs carefully explored by those neighboring fields are presumed to be an important input which should often trump the seduction of mathematically elegant economic theories that are empirically unmotivated.</seg>
<seg id="3164"> Thinking about organizations naturally extends the definition of behavioral economics to include how socialization, networks, and identity shape individual behavior in organizations</seg>
<seg id="3165"> As such, the behavioral economics of organizations involve both a special challenge and opportunity.</seg>
<seg id="3166"> Consider how behavioral finance has developed as an academic discipline, and how behavioral organizational economics could develop.</seg>
<seg id="3167"> Until 1990 or so, finance was arguably the area of economics most hostile to the idea that psychological limits matter for the focus of the fieldˇŻs attentionˇŞnamely, stock price movements.</seg>
<seg id="3168"> Since then, there has been a dramatic shift in the amount of careful attention paid to behavioral ideas.</seg>
<seg id="3169"> This is surprising because it has been argued that large stock markets are the ultimate domain in which highly rational traders should limit the influence of those who make mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="3170"> So why did academic asset pricers start to ˇ°misbehaveˇ± so fast?</seg>
<seg id="3171"> One reason was good data, which made it easy to test new behavioral theories against the rational incumbents.</seg>
<seg id="3172"> Another reason was the availability of a clear benchmark model (market efficiency) to argue with.</seg>
<seg id="3173"> Good data and a sharp benchmark enabled researchers to create a set of clear anomalies in asset pricing.</seg>
<seg id="3174"> The situation was reversed in corporate finance.</seg>
<seg id="3175"> Rational arbitrageurs cannot easily limit suboptimal corporate-finance decisions, since it is hard to short- sell a CEO or CFO.</seg>
<seg id="3176"> Quite the opposite occurs: top executives may be entrenched and hard to get rid of.</seg>
<seg id="3177"> Biases and mistakes in decision-making are thus much more likely to have a persistent and large effect in corporate finance than on asset pricing.</seg>
<seg id="3178"> Nevertheless, behavioral research in corporate finance took off considerably later than that in asset pricing, possibly due to the lack of good data and a clear theoretical benchmark.</seg>
<seg id="3179"> Obtaining executive-level or even firm-level data on corporate finance decision-making has always been more intricate than obtaining stock price data.</seg>
<seg id="3180"> At the same time, numerous, conflicting theories have been proposed for virtually all types of corporate finance decisions, whether on mergers, stock issuance, or dividends.</seg>
<seg id="3181"> The situation in organizational economics closely resembles that of corporate finance.</seg>
<seg id="3182"> The lack of rich within-firm panel data sets and cleanly identified exogenous variation is a persistent stumbling block, hampering progress in our understanding of the economics of organizations.</seg>
<seg id="3183"> On the other hand, if systematic biases are identifiable within the firm, they may have a large and persistent impact on organizational decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="3184"> If workers misallocate their human capital, nobody can take advantage of their mistakes by ˇ°short-sellingˇ± their capital.</seg>
<seg id="3185"> Adjustment to mistakes must come from some other source than simply trading against a mistake.</seg>
<seg id="3186"> Moreover, biases in organizations give rise to the interesting challenges of how organizations should be designed to repair these mistakes or to exploit them, or how firms organize around them if they represent genuine regret-free preferences rather than errors.</seg>
<seg id="3187"> Moving away from mistakes due to heuristics, a lot of psychology is involved when workers team up in an organizationˇŞsocial comparison, changes in identity, camaraderie, attribution and diffusion of credit and blame, and so forth.</seg>
<seg id="3188"> This kind of psychology has played a small role in behavioral economics in recent years, but looms large when thinking about organizations.</seg>
<seg id="3189"> Our paper is divided into four parts. Each part poses a broad question and suggests some ideas.</seg>
<seg id="3190"> Little systematic knowledge has been cumulated on many of these topics.</seg>
<seg id="3191"> For those topics, the paper should be read as a research agenda rather than a review of what has been learned.</seg>
<seg id="3192"> Section 7.2 lays out the basic single-activity risk-incentive conflict model and points out psychological considerations which complicate the model.</seg>
<seg id="3193"> Section 7.3 notes that the simplest risk-incentive model does not particularly account for the fact that people work together in organizations and discusses the importance of group loyalty, peer effects, and the coordinating role and cognitive economics of culture.</seg>
<seg id="3194"> Section 7.4 is about top management and governance and special considerations that arise like CEO overconfidence.</seg>
<seg id="3195"> Section 7.5 asks how patterns in individual judgment and choice aggregate into organizational outcomes when organizations can repair or exploit them.</seg>
<seg id="3196"> Section 7.6 concludes the chapter.</seg>
<seg id="3197"> 7.2
<seg id="3198"> A good place to start is to discuss how psychology complicates the simple risk- incentive model of principal-agent relations that is a workhorse in organizational economics.</seg>
<seg id="3199"> First we will lay out a simple agency model with one type of activity.</seg>
<seg id="3200"> In the standard labor economics model, workers face a prevailing wage and decide how much labor to supply at that wage (and consume the remaining hours as leisure).</seg>
<seg id="3201"> We assume that people like money and, by definition, dislike work and like leisure.</seg>
<seg id="3202"> A useful way to critique the standard principal-agent model is to ask when its basic assumptions are violated.</seg>
<seg id="3203"> The goal is not to heckle the modelˇŻs shortcomings (which are an inevitable byproduct of simplicity), but to offer empirical facts and build up intuition about how it could be extended in useful ways.</seg>
<seg id="3204"> 7.2.1
<seg id="3205"> Labor economics extends a standard assumption from consumer theoryˇŞˇŞnamely, that people have complete and consistent preferences across bundles of goodsŇ»to the case of labor and leisure.</seg>
<seg id="3206"> But young people may have only a vague idea of what work they would like to do when deciding on their first jobs, or on college majors (or even colleges), which partly determine their career paths due to irreversibilities and path-dependence.</seg>
<seg id="3207"> A way to investigate the stability of labor-market preferences is to measure how much expressed preferences can be influenced by the way that work is described, or the procedure by which preferences are elicited (e.g., bidding wages or choosing at a fixed wage).</seg>
<seg id="3208"> For example, Ariely et al. (2004a) asked some subjects whether they would pay $2 to attend a fifteen-minute poetry reading, and asked other subjects whether they would attend if they were paid $2.</seg>
<seg id="3209"> Later, a third of those who were anchored on paying said they would attend for free, compared to only 8% attendance by those who were anchored on being paid.</seg>
<seg id="3210"> Of course, students may not have developed clear preferences for whether listening to poetry is labor or leisure.</seg>
<seg id="3211"> But if a random anchor can even influence the sign of c(e), then it is likely that stronger influences affect at least some labor market entry decisions about relative values of c(e).</seg>
<seg id="3212"> If employers know about anchoring and marketing influences on c(e), then they will try to generate positive anchors, convincing prospective employees that working in their firm is fun.</seg>
<seg id="3213"> Little is known about the long-run influence or robustness of constructed-preference effects.</seg>
<seg id="3214"> Anchors might wear off: after experiencing an actual poetry reading, for example, subjects may quickly develop a consistent hedonic preference which is no longer affected by the initial anchor or subsequent ones, and resembles the complete preference assumed in economic theory.</seg>
<seg id="3215"> Furthermore, in competitive labor markets, anchors will not affect wages, because wages are determined by the marginal revenue product of workers.</seg>
<seg id="3216"> But anchoring could affect the quantity of labor supplied, even if it does not affect the ˇ°priceˇ± (i.e., the wage) for a given amount of labor supply.</seg>
<seg id="3217"> In the poetry example, think of the anchor fee/wage as creating a perception about the cost of effort of listening to poetry.</seg>
<seg id="3218"> Fixing the wage of poetry (through firm competition), more people will listen to poetry if they came to perceive the cost as low.</seg>
<seg id="3219"> So anchors can affect quantities even if they do not affect prices.</seg>
<seg id="3220"> Another kind of reference dependence is two-tier wage deals, which occur when firms are struggling financially.</seg>
<seg id="3221"> Senior workers doing the same jobs as entry-level workers are sometimes paid a larger wage, to avoid cutting their wages, while entry- level workers are paid less to save on the wage bill.</seg>
<seg id="3222"> Social comparison models predict that new workers will be unhappy at being paid less than senior workers beyond the disutility from the lower wage itself.</seg>
<seg id="3223"> An important feature of reference dependence is that reference points may reflect failure to adjust for purchasing power.</seg>
<seg id="3224"> The best-studied example is money illusion.</seg>
<seg id="3225"> Firms act as if workers care about nominal wages rather than inflation-adjusted real wages, in making intertemporal comparisons</seg>
<seg id="3226"> Baker et al. (1994) found there were hardly any nominal year-to-year wage cuts in a financial services firm, but many real wage cuts in inflationary years.</seg>
<seg id="3227"> The psychological principle behind money illusion may extend to illusions in comparing purchasing power across cities (leading people to prefer higher-salary jobs in the more expensive cities to lower-paying ones in cheaper cities) and in adjusting annual salaries for work hours</seg>
<seg id="3228"> (leading to taking high-salary jobs with the highest hours),</seg>
<seg id="3229"> but we do not know of any formal studies of these illusions.</seg>
<seg id="3230"> 7.2.3
<seg id="3231"> A broader point is that the process by which wages and other organizational outcomes are determined (particularly terminations) may affect how people value the consequences.</seg>
<seg id="3232"> In organizational studies this phenomenon is called ˇ°procedural justice,ˇ± and it is argued that it is important empirically.</seg>
<seg id="3233"> One component of procedural justice is the desire to have a voice or participate in important decisions that affect you.</seg>
<seg id="3234"> Another component is consistency of procedures.</seg>
<seg id="3235"> People dislike not knowing the rules that are being applied to judge them.</seg>
<seg id="3236"> The taste for clear rules motivates bright-line rules like seniority-based firing policies, and many policies in employment law.</seg>
<seg id="3237"> Of course, a taste for procedural justice is often hard to distinguish from standard preferences.</seg>
<seg id="3238"> For example, the desire to ˇ°have a voiceˇ± could just reflect the attempt to achieve individually favorable rules.</seg>
<seg id="3239"> Clear rules may simply help workers to optimize their effort allocation and reduce influence costs.</seg>
<seg id="3240"> Moreover, we may worry that procedural concerns, even if we could identify them, are second-order compared with wages.</seg>
<seg id="3241"> For example, while people may complain that a coworker was unfairly fired, will they actually quit their own job in protest, or accept a wage cut to get the coworker reinstated?</seg>
<seg id="3242"> Experiments suggest they might.</seg>
<seg id="3243"> But we would need more empirical research, tied to economic models, to calibrate the taste for procedural justice by pitting it against money in experiments or field analyses.</seg>
<seg id="3244"> 7.2.4
<seg id="3245"> The basic risk-incentive model divides the workerˇŻs world into efforts they dislike, and rewards they like.</seg>
<seg id="3246"> It is convenient to talk about wages as rewards because they are easily measured, and do not satiate.</seg>
<seg id="3247"> But people are motivated by many others types of nonpecuniary ˇ°psychic incomeˇ± as well.</seg>
<seg id="3248"> For example, Stern (2004) uses multiple offers for postdoc biologists to estimate that those who accept offers at more science- oriented firms earn 25% lower wages in order to be able to publish their research and engage in the scientific community.</seg>
<seg id="3249"> This is consistent with a ˇ°compensating differential,ˇ± in which workers trade off money wages for other aspects of the job they like (or dislike).</seg>
<seg id="3250"> The existence of compensating differentials is not controversial.</seg>
<seg id="3251"> Behavioral economics suggests, however, that the source of these differentials might include emotion, social comparison, and other forces not included in typical analyses which focus on safety and education.</seg>
<seg id="3252"> Ariely et al. (2004b) use a simple experimental paradigm to calibrate psychic income for what they call the ˇ°meaningˇ± of work.</seg>
<seg id="3253"> Students proofread pages for a declining wage schedule (the wage for the marginal page fell) and handed finished pages to an experimenter.</seg>
<seg id="3254"> When their work was ˇ°ignoredˇ± (simply placed on a stack) or immediately shredded, they finished only about six pages, compared with nine pages when their pages were signed and filed away.</seg>
<seg id="3255"> Thus, the subjects traded off marginal wages for ˇ°meaningfulˇ± work which was kept rather than shredded.</seg>
<seg id="3256"> In discussing our paper at the conference, Holmstrom suggested that what workers fundamentally care about is that their efforts are appreciated.</seg>
<seg id="3257"> For example, entrepreneurs often talk about their desire to get rich as a byproduct of making a good product that many people want to buy.</seg>
<seg id="3258"> They see riches as an expression of appreciation.</seg>
<seg id="3259"> The fact that tentative evidence suggests income is more (neurally) valuable if it is earned by oneˇŻs own effort fits with this idea too.</seg>
<seg id="3260"> Of course, agents may strive for appreciation of their work for more standard reasons.</seg>
<seg id="3261"> In a study of ˇ°superstarˇ± CEOs, Malmendier and Tate (2005a) found that CEOs who had won awards (such as ˇ°CEO of the Yearˇ± or ˇ°Top Managerˇ±) got a big relative pay boost.</seg>
<seg id="3262"> This effect goes in the opposite direction of a trade-off between appreciation and payˇŞthe award is appreciation and a raise in wages.</seg>
<seg id="3263"> Their findings may indicate a signaling role of awards, or the ability of CEOs to use awards to justify pay increases in the face of weak governance.</seg>
<seg id="3264"> Moreover, any theory rooted in the desire for effort appreciation needs to take into account the fact that paying wages is one way of showing appreciation.</seg>
<seg id="3265"> But appreciation may also depend on relative wages, which creates a role for social comparison in signaling appreciation.</seg>
<seg id="3266"> Appreciation might also be cheaply generated by organizational activities such as awards, titles, executive visits, ˇ°employee of the monthˇ± plaques, etc.</seg>
<seg id="3267"> The desire for feeling appreciated might also be the underpinning of the desire for procedural justiceˇŞfor being listened to, holding outcomes constantˇŞ that was discussed above.</seg>
<seg id="3268"> Note that, if employees value appreciation, organizations who figure out how to generate effort-appreciation most cheaply will save on the wage bill and will have a competitive advantage over organizations who express appreciation only through expensive wages.</seg>
<seg id="3269"> 7.2.5
<seg id="3270"> PsychologistsˇŻ synonym for psychic income is ˇ°intrinsic motivationˇ±ˇŞthe satisfac?tion a worker gets from work for its own sake.</seg>
<seg id="3271"> An interesting phenomenon documented in psychology is the possibility that extrinsic incentives like money can ˇ°crowd outˇ± or extinguish intrinsic motivation.</seg>
<seg id="3272"> For example, Lepper et al. (1973) had children coloring pictures (with no extrinsic incentive).</seg>
<seg id="3273"> Then they paid the children a small sum of money for each picture colored.</seg>
<seg id="3274"> When the wage was later removed, the children colored fewer pictures than they had in the initial phase with no payments.</seg>
<seg id="3275"> Their interpretation, called ˇ°overjustification,ˇ± is that the children infer something about their own intrinsic motivation from the wages they are paid;</seg>
<seg id="3276"> when they are paid for coloring, they infer that they must not like to color very much, so when the wage is removed they quit coloring pictures.</seg>
<seg id="3277"> Kreps (1997) and Benabou and Tirole (2003) consider crowding-out from an economic point of view.</seg>
<seg id="3278"> Since incentives often do have positive effects, the challenge is to create a theory which permits both positive effects and negative effects</seg>
<seg id="3279"> (i.e., ˇ°hidden costsˇ± or ˇ°crowding outˇ±).</seg>
<seg id="3280"> In Benabou and TiroleˇŻs theory, crowding out occurs when an agent thinks the principal knows more about job difficulty or the agentˇŻs abilities than she herself does.</seg>
<seg id="3281"> Then the agent takes the provision of high- powered incentives as a bad sign.</seg>
<seg id="3282"> Their theory is much like the original idea in social psychology but more carefully specified and rich with empirical implications</seg>
<seg id="3283"> (chiefly, that hidden costs should only occur when workers think incentives signal bad news).</seg>
<seg id="3284"> Benabou and Tirole (2004) discuss the case where incentives interact with prosocial behavior.</seg>
<seg id="3285"> Leaving aside crowding out, monetary incentives could also affect performance negatively if high incentives create arousal that inhibits automatic responses or cre?ates distraction (e.g., ˇ°choking under pressureˇ±).</seg>
<seg id="3286"> According to the ˇ°Yerkes-Dodson Lawˇ±, arousal increases performance up to a point at which further arousal degrades performance.</seg>
<seg id="3287"> Ariely et al. (2003) demonstrate this effect with experiments conducted in India.</seg>
<seg id="3288"> They allowed their subjects to earn up to six months of wages in various simple games.</seg>
<seg id="3289"> Surprisingly, the subjects with the highest payment perform worse than those with the moderate and the low pay?ment in almost all games.</seg>
<seg id="3290"> It is, of course, unclear how relevant this effect is for the economics of organizations, given actual wage-setting and reward policies.</seg>
<seg id="3291"> Even if the effect were prevalent, wage-setters may account for it and adjust wages appropriately.</seg>
<seg id="3292"> Moreover, if experience with high-stakes situations decreases arousal, the effect may only apply to inexperienced agents.</seg>
<seg id="3293"> 7.2.6
<seg id="3294"> In the standard agency model laid out above, agents exert effort which is productive, but principals can only observe agentsˇŻ output.</seg>
<seg id="3295"> Observed output depends on unobserved effort and unobserved ˇ°luck.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3296"> The usual assumption is that principals know the relative importance of effort and error in determining output, and also anticipate the agentsˇŻ reaction to knowing their effort is not clearly observed.</seg>
<seg id="3297"> This simple assumption buries a lot of behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="3298"> Separating skill from luck is difficult.</seg>
<seg id="3299"> As a result, it is quite possible that psychological forces bias principalsˇŻ judgments of whether output is due to skill or luck.</seg>
<seg id="3300"> Three important psychological forces suggest that separating hard work from luck will often be biased in predictable ways: hindsight bias;</seg>
<seg id="3301"> overattributing cause to personal traits rather than situational influences;</seg>
<seg id="3302"> and overconfidence.</seg>
<seg id="3303"> 7.2.6.1
<seg id="3304"> The brainˇŻs tremendous ability to sense patterns is manifested by our tendency to rapidly rewrite our memories of the past to fit what we have learned.</seg>
<seg id="3305"> The problem is that revising our beliefs after an event naturally leads to misremembering how little we knew before the event.</seg>
<seg id="3306"> Rapid rewriting creates ˇ°hindsight biasˇ±Ň»the ex post recollection of the ex ante probability of an event will be biased in the direction of the eventˇŻs realization.</seg>
<seg id="3307"> Hindsight bias is on display every day in sports commentary and news coverage.</seg>
<seg id="3308"> It is surely an important force in organizational life but has not been studied at all.</seg>
<seg id="3309"> This sketch of a model becomes more interesting if agents anticipate that principals will be hindsight-biased.</seg>
<seg id="3310"> Agents who anticipate being second-guessed will record as much ex ante evidence as they can</seg>
<seg id="3311"> (also known as ˇ°covering your assˇ±).</seg>
<seg id="3312"> Agents may also herd into the same decisions other agents make or seek other ways to avoid anticipated hindsight bias.</seg>
<seg id="3313"> It would be very useful to have field evidence distinguishing the effects of hindsight bias from other distortions.</seg>
<seg id="3314"> One method is to compare behavior of owner- managed firms with similar firms where shareholders and boards can second-guess the managers.</seg>
<seg id="3315"> Hindsight bias should be lower and risk-taking higher among owner- managed firms.</seg>
<seg id="3316"> 7.2.6.2
<seg id="3317"> In the context of the firm, attribution is likely to be especially important in evaluating top executives, who make far-reaching decisions with noisy and lagged feedback.</seg>
<seg id="3318"> They are likely to be both credited and blamed too much for forces beyond their control.</seg>
<seg id="3319"> Applied to a personˇŻs own judgement of his or her efforts, however, attri?butions tend to be self-serving.</seg>
<seg id="3320"> For example, Bettman and Weitz (1983) find that, when companies do well, corporate annual reports attribute the success to internal factors and skill.</seg>
<seg id="3321"> When companies do badly, the reports attribute blame to external factors (regulation, demand shocks, and so forth).</seg>
<seg id="3322"> Those findings lack, of course, the ultimate proof of distortion.</seg>
<seg id="3323"> Moreover, even evidently distorted information provision may simply be the rational response to investorsˇŻ lack of information or to investor credulity.</seg>
<seg id="3324"> Bertrand and Mullainathan (2001) show that exogenous variation in profits is reflected in executivesˇŻcompensation.</seg>
<seg id="3325"> They find that compensation of oil company executives rises with worldwide oil prices, even though oil prices are set by global demand and OPEC output and are not influenced much by one executiveˇŻs action.</seg>
<seg id="3326"> At the same time, executives are largely shielded from blame (in the form of lower compensation) when oil prices fall.</seg>
<seg id="3327"> Indeed, the phenomenon of ˇ°negative shieldingˇ±ˇŞrelative insensitivity of compensation to poor performance, compared to stronger sensitivity to good performanceˇŞis widely documented in executive compensation (Garvey and Milbourn, forthcoming).</seg>
<seg id="3328"> It remains unclear from these studies which agentsˇŞif anyˇŞare misattributing positive outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="3329"> One possibil?ity is that boards mistakenly give credit for positive outcomes and CEOs rationally exploit or even induce the misattribution of board members.</seg>
<seg id="3330"> Alternatively, execu?tives themselves attribute positive outcomes to their own skills or effort and bad outcomes to bad luck.</seg>
<seg id="3331"> If corporate governance is weak, such CEOs may then extract disproportionately higher compensation after exogenously higher profits.</seg>
<seg id="3332"> Finally, it is possible that no party inside the corporation exhibits misattribution error.</seg>
<seg id="3333"> CEOs in badly governed firms may overadjust their compensation to good outcomes and underadjust for bad outcomes, exploiting investorsˇŻ naivety, lack of information, or misattribution of executive credit to skill, and blame to outside forces.</seg>
<seg id="3334"> 7.2.6.3
<seg id="3335"> Many studies show that people are often overconfident.</seg>
<seg id="3336"> We define overconfidence as the tendency to overestimate oneˇŻs own (relative) abilities and resulting outcomes and overoptimism as overestimation of general prospects.</seg>
<seg id="3337"> Here we briefly mention overconfidence in the basic agency model.</seg>
<seg id="3338"> Section 7.4, below, has more discussion of overconfidence of top managers.</seg>
<seg id="3339"> The opposite of overconfidence might be important in worker motivation as well.</seg>
<seg id="3340"> Psychiatrists discuss an ˇ°impostor syndromeˇ± in which workers discount their own success, or attribute it to luck, and feel that others have been fooled into thinking they are talented</seg>
<seg id="3341"> Self-perceived ˇ°impostorsˇ± may work harder than others if they think that they have talent but need to work even harder to achieve true success.</seg>
<seg id="3342"> More generally, there are important caveats to applying the evidence of widespread overconfidence to organizations.</seg>
<seg id="3343"> Rather than assuming that agents uniformly overestimate their abilities, researchers need to account for heterogeneity and attempt to evaluate ex ante indicators of overconfidence.</seg>
<seg id="3344"> For example, some studies show that women are less overconfident than men, which might be important for personnel selection.</seg>
<seg id="3345"> Seventy- to eighty-year-olds are also less overconfident than younger college students on general knowledge questions, which suggests that age teaches people what they know.</seg>
<seg id="3346"> And depressed people are typically not overconfident (ˇ°wiser but sadderˇ±), which suggests overconfidence is part of mental health, and probably evolutionarily adaptive</seg>
<seg id="3347"> Overconfidence depends also on the type of task and its framing.</seg>
<seg id="3348"> For example, overconfidence about a particular estimate shrivels up when a portfolio of estimates are considered on the single-case ˇ°inside viewˇ± compared to the portfolio ˇ°outside viewˇ±).</seg>
<seg id="3349"> Consider the 90%-confidence interval task, where subjects are instructed to give a wide inter?val for each numerical quantity which is only wrong 10% of the time;</seg>
<seg id="3350"> typically, five out of ten intervals are too narrow. Subjects typically give too narrow intervals.</seg>
<seg id="3351"> But when subjects are asked how many out of ten questions they got right, they typically do not say ˇ°one out of ten,ˇ± they say ˇ°five out of tenˇ±- which is empirically accurate.</seg>
<seg id="3352"> Overconfidence also shrivels up when the question is posed more narrowly, breaking an outcome into components.</seg>
<seg id="3353"> While drivers are overconfident about overall driving ability, they are less overconfident about specific components like driving in snow, avoiding fender-bender accidents in heavy traffic, and so on.</seg>
<seg id="3354"> This fact suggests that if the criteria used to judge managerial success become more vague as people rise in a firmˇŻs hierarchy, overestimation of future success (and career outcomes) will rise too.</seg>
<seg id="3355"> 7.3
<seg id="3356"> The basic model above focuses on a single principal-agent relation.</seg>
<seg id="3357"> It makes little use of the special properties of large organizations with many principals, agents, and hierarchies.</seg>
<seg id="3358"> Scaling the models up to large firms has a variety of implications for social comparison, group and peer effects, emotional and cognitive firm boundaries, and the coordinating role of culture and leadership in large groups.</seg>
<seg id="3359"> 7.3.1
<seg id="3360"> In the worker expected utility in (7.1), only the workerˇŻs own effort and wage enter into her utility.</seg>
<seg id="3361"> But in real organizations, people make friends and enemies, and compare themselves to others.</seg>
<seg id="3362"> Workers may thus sacrifice their own earnings to help their friends and harm their enemies, or to create better social comparisons.</seg>
<seg id="3363"> This topic, and its importance for contracting and labor economics, is discussed in some detail in Fehr and Gachter (2000).</seg>
<seg id="3364"> The influence of social comparison can lead to conflict when different agents choose different comparison sets.</seg>
<seg id="3365"> In a study of actual teacher strikes in Pennsyl?vania, it was found that teachersˇŻ unions tend to say that nearby school systems with high wages are most comparable to their own, and school boards say that nearby systems with low wages are most compa?rable.</seg>
<seg id="3366"> The gap between the two sidesˇŻ comparisons is correlated with strike incidence.</seg>
<seg id="3367"> Experiments also show that the gap between perceptions of fairness predicts strike incidence.</seg>
<seg id="3368"> Importantly, these perceptions are not merely strategic posturing.</seg>
<seg id="3369"> The gap disappears if subjects are assigned to a bargaining role after reading facts about the case.</seg>
<seg id="3370"> This implies that the selfserving perceptions of fairness arise from differences in encoding what facts are important.</seg>
<seg id="3371"> One might think that social comparison is an inferior goodˇŞas people are paid more and more, concern for social comparison becomes relatively less impor?tant.</seg>
<seg id="3372"> But social comparison also appears to enter organizational economics even in the very high compensation of executives.</seg>
<seg id="3373"> Hall and Murphy (2003) note the effect of the U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC) regulatory changes in 1992 on CEO pay which required ˇ°company compensation committees to describe company pay practices and justify in detail how they determined the pay of the chief executive officer</seg>
<seg id="3374"> ˇ± Presumably the SEC thought that by requir?ing clearer disclosure of what CEOs earned, and how earnings were linked to past performance, embarrassment would rein in high payouts unjustified by per?formance, and might link compensation more closely to performance.</seg>
<seg id="3375"> But in a world where executives hate being paid less than a peer they think is less deserving, and their perceptions are self-servingly biased, disclosing wages leads to a ratchet effect:</seg>
<seg id="3376"> information which tells CEOs they are being paid less than others can drive all wages up.</seg>
<seg id="3377"> Similarly, Belliveau et al. (1996) found that CEO pay was more closely correlated with earnings of members of the boardˇŻs compensation committee than to pay of other board members.</seg>
<seg id="3378"> One explanation is that compensation committee members compare the CEOˇŻs pay with their own.</seg>
<seg id="3379"> Social comparison may also affect the boundaries of the firm.</seg>
<seg id="3380"> If people natu?rally compare their own wages to those who work in the same firm, then one of the economic effects of a merger is a shift in the comparison sets of workers in the newly merged company.</seg>
<seg id="3381"> Some workers will now be underpaid relative to their new colleagues.</seg>
<seg id="3382"> This creates turnover or influence costs, and those costs should be accounted for as part of the merger cost.</seg>
<seg id="3383"> For example, when General Electric (GE) bought the television network NBC, the high salaries of NBC employees angered many successful engineers, who suddenly had an immediate reason to compare their wages with the highly paid ˇ°new neighbors.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3384"> Kole and Lehn (2000) make the same point about the takeover of Piedmont Aviation by U.S. Airways.</seg>
<seg id="3385"> The choice of the boundary of the firm, therefore, is also a choice of social comparison sets, which has economic consequences of its own.</seg>
<seg id="3386"> 7.3.2
<seg id="3387"> One place in which social preferences have entered labor economics is in the concept of ˇ°peer pressure.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3388"> Peer pressure is often invoked to explain why group-based incentives are surprisingly successful despite the incentive to free ride.</seg>
<seg id="3389"> For example, Knez and Simester (2001) note that a $65/month bonus for all Continental Airlines employees, for improved on-time-arrival rates, actually worked, even though any one employeeˇŻs performance has a minuscule effect on the firmˇŻs overall on-time performance.</seg>
<seg id="3390"> They note the important influence of immediate pressure by peers to improve performance.</seg>
<seg id="3391"> The experimental literature on public goods games with sanctions also shows the power of peer pressure.</seg>
<seg id="3392"> When subjects can punish others at a cost to themselves, they often do so, which drives contributions up.</seg>
<seg id="3393"> Note that not every subject punishes, but enough do so to create higher contributions.</seg>
<seg id="3394"> Falk and Ichino (2003) measured peer effects on output in an experiment, exploiting the ability to assign workers to pairs randomly, which avoids the problem of endogenous peer selection that is typical in most (though not all) field data sets.</seg>
<seg id="3395"> They recruited subjects to stuff letters into envelopes for four hours for a fixed fee (with no performance bonus).</seg>
<seg id="3396"> Some subjects did the activity by themselves; others did it in the presence of a randomly assigned peer.</seg>
<seg id="3397"> Random pairing led to closely matched output.</seg>
<seg id="3398"> The absolute deviation of output within each actual peer group averaged 20.6ˇŞmuch smaller than 99% of simulated absolute deviations within artificial pairs composed of solo subjects.</seg>
<seg id="3399"> Pairing workers also raised output on average (about 10%).</seg>
<seg id="3400"> Pairs were more likely to escalate each othersˇŻ output than to tacitly collude by goofing off.</seg>
<seg id="3401"> The estimated effect of a one-unit increase in output on the peerˇŻs output was 0.14 units, close to the 0.14-0.18 estimated from field data in an Italian firm.</seg>
<seg id="3402"> 7.3.3
<seg id="3403"> There is a substantial psychological literature on group identification which could be useful for organizational economics, on the economics of identity).</seg>
<seg id="3404"> Psychological evidence from ˇ°minimal groupˇ± experiments suggests that people form group affiliations rapidly, e.g., based on which room they were placed into, or according to their preference for Klee or Kandinsky paintings.</seg>
<seg id="3405"> Furthermore, these affiliations influence allocations in dictator and public-good-type gamesˇŞthey give more to in-group members and less to out-group members.</seg>
<seg id="3406"> Extrapolated to organizations, these findings suggest that workers will help members of their own groupˇŞas they perceive their group and its membership.</seg>
<seg id="3407"> In this view, a group is a club of people who all agree to help each other.</seg>
<seg id="3408"> This introduces an interesting role for management of group boundaries.</seg>
<seg id="3409"> To fix ideas, suppose there is a component of effort which is productive for the firm but does not increase an employeeˇŻs own measured output.</seg>
<seg id="3410"> Call this ˇ°helpingˇ± and think of it as a contribution to the firm-wide public good.</seg>
<seg id="3411"> In order to focus attention on group identification rather than multitasking, assume that choosing to help does not cut too deeply into a workerˇŻs other productive activities.</seg>
<seg id="3412"> In the context of the organization, note first that the firm would most like its own workers to help each other, but to not help competitorsˇŻ workers.</seg>
<seg id="3413"> But group affiliations that determine helping probably do not map neatly onto legal boundaries.</seg>
<seg id="3414"> Friendship, ethnicity and language, geography and architecture (which workers live or work nearby), marital status (who hang out after work together at happy hour, or during their childrenˇŻs play dates),</seg>
<seg id="3415"> and other distinctions create an archipelago of group affiliations within a firm, and across firms.</seg>
<seg id="3416"> To some extent, firms can manage group creation through sorting (selecting ˇ°our kind of personˇ± in hiring) and other organizational practices.</seg>
<seg id="3417"> For example, if helping customers too much is bad for the firm, it may use job rotation to keep its workers from becoming too helpful with favored customers.</seg>
<seg id="3418"> The sociology literature, however, provides numerous examples of firms misestimating group effects, such as RoyˇŻs (1952) famous study of a small-parts machine shop in Chicago.</seg>
<seg id="3419"> In this shop, the management designed task-specific piece rates such that for all the different jobs a certain monetary target could be reached with the same ˇ°effort.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3420"> RoyˇŻs study demonstrates that workers were able to mislead management and induce piece rates, for which meeting the target was very easy.</seg>
<seg id="3421"> The workers imposed strong sanctions on anybody attempting to deviate and to exert more effort.</seg>
<seg id="3422"> Evolutionary psychology provides an interesting way to speculate about groups and firms.</seg>
<seg id="3423"> Psychologists think of some kinds of groups as ˇ°essential,ˇ± which means that group members share a common essence which is immutable.</seg>
<seg id="3424"> Gil-White (2001, p. 519), for example, argues that ethnic groups are perceived as a species. They have</seg>
<seg id="3425"> (1) a distinctive morphology. . .</seg>
<seg id="3426"> (2) many intercorrelated hidden properties. . .</seg>
<seg id="3427"> (3)within-category mating and descent-based membership, and</seg>
<seg id="3428"> (4) saliently labeled categories in cultural discourse.</seg>
<seg id="3429"> If a firm can create a sense among workers that they are like a speciesŇ»with immutable special properties that are inheritableˇŞit might be able to hijack neural circuitry which is highly evolved to distinguish friend from foe on the basis of species and species-like ethnic characteristics,</seg>
<seg id="3430"> to create a deep sense of group membership and helping.</seg>
<seg id="3431"> For example, a body feature which cannot be changed marks a species, so one way to create a species illusion is with tattoos.</seg>
<seg id="3432"> In fact, Nike founder Phil Knight and many of his employees have tattoos of the Nike ˇ°swooshˇ± logo on their left calves as a sign of camaraderie.</seg>
<seg id="3433"> Group loyalty can persist even when workers leave the firm.</seg>
<seg id="3434"> For example, it is typical for lawyers and consultants to eventually leave their firms and work for one of the firmsˇŻ clients (whom they worked with originally).</seg>
<seg id="3435"> While there is a clear difference in the employeeˇŻs decision rights after switching firms, there may be little difference in emotional attachment to their old group (the law firm) and their new group (the client firm).</seg>
<seg id="3436"> Many consulting firms keep active lists of ˇ°alumniˇ± who have left the firm to work for their clients, and presumably use the residue of group loyalty to keep those clients and to resolve disputes.</seg>
<seg id="3437"> 7.3.4
<seg id="3438"> Organizational relationships are typically repeated games.</seg>
<seg id="3439"> As Gibbons (2004a) notes, the folk theorem gives us an embarrassment of riches about how repeated games might be playedŇ»there are too many equilibria.</seg>
<seg id="3440"> As Schelling (1960) wrote, predicting what players will do in games with many equilibria by pure mathematics is like trying to prove that a joke is funny without telling it.</seg>
<seg id="3441"> When there are many equilibria, social considerations like historical traditions and norms, and credibility of leaders who make announcements intended to focus attention on good equilibria, will make a difference.</seg>
<seg id="3442"> These issues are highlighted by putting incentive conflicts aside and focusing on organizations as ˇ°teams.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3443"> A team has a common interest, but has to coordinate activity and information to make a commonly beneficial choice.</seg>
<seg id="3444"> Coordination is often neglected in organizational economics, perhaps because of a belief that it is easy to achieve.</seg>
<seg id="3445"> Indeed, in small groups a quick e-mail may coordinate activity.</seg>
<seg id="3446"> But when organizations face time pressure, have rapid turnover, and scale up in size, coordination is challenging.</seg>
<seg id="3447"> Experimental studies of coordination games have shown that even in very simple settings, with modest group sizes (2-16) and very clear games, coordination failure is common.</seg>
<seg id="3448"> Several studies have used a ˇ°weak-linkˇ± game : players simultaneously choose effort levels (integers from 1-7) and earn an amount which increases in the minimum effort, as well as a penalty if their effort is above the minimum.</seg>
<seg id="3449"> The game is tricky because there is one incentive to choose high numbers, to raise the minimum, and another incentive to choose low numbers, to avoid the penalty from wasted effort.</seg>
<seg id="3450"> Every effort level is a different equilibrium, but choosing the highest effort is Pareto-dominant.</seg>
<seg id="3451"> Many studies show that coordination failure in groups playing simultaneously without communication: they generally fail to reach the Pareto-dominant equilibrium</seg>
<seg id="3452"> (see, for example, Van Huyck et al. 1990; Camerer 2003, Chapter 7).</seg>
<seg id="3453"> Efficiency can be achieved when two people play together repeatedly, when groups ˇ°growˇ± slowly, starting with two and adding one player at a time,</seg>
<seg id="3454"> or when a small bonus is publicly announced if everyone reaches the efficient choices.</seg>
<seg id="3455"> Surprisingly, limited communication does not help much.</seg>
<seg id="3456"> When one ˇ°leaderˇ± is randomly chosen to talk briefly to the group (usually exhorting everyone to choose ˇ°7ˇ±)Ł¬large groups still do not reach efficiency and subjects misattribute credit and blame to the leader.</seg>
<seg id="3457"> These studies illustrate how coordination games could be used to study facets such as growth of firms, the attention-getting effect of bonuses, and leader credibility in very simple experimental organizations.</seg>
<seg id="3458"> They also suggest that efficient coordination is usually not easy, even in simple games with clear strategies and payoffs, so that coordination is likely to be important for success of large organizations, and perhaps underestimated in importance.</seg>
<seg id="3459"> For example, Heath and Staudenmayer (2000) had experimental subjects divide labor to create parts of a ˇ°manˇ± from LEGO blocks; they were scored on how good their composite man looked when assembled from the parts.</seg>
<seg id="3460"> Appointing a coordinator who made sure the parts being created fit together improved output, but few of the teams spontaneously did so.</seg>
<seg id="3461"> 7.3.4.1
<seg id="3462"> Kreps (1990) introduced the idea of an organizationˇŻs culture as a correlating device.</seg>
<seg id="3463"> In this view, cultural rules are meta?principles which tell workers what to do in the face of unforeseen contingencies, in the spirit of SchellingˇŻs (1960) focal points in matching games, but extended to meta-principles which prescribe focal points in an ensemble of games.</seg>
<seg id="3464"> The idea is illustrated by Figure 7.1, an advertisement from a Southwest Airlines in-flight magazine. This shows a hypothetical quiz given to potential employees about how to react to a customerˇŻs problem.</seg>
<seg id="3465"> A companyˇŻs culture can be thought of as a set of general recipes which help workers and managers coordinate on the ˇ°rightˇ± answer in quizzes like this, which correspond to workplace incidents.</seg>
<seg id="3466"> At Southwest Airlines, the correct answer is get some baby formula for the delayed passenger and pick up a coloring book as well.</seg>
<seg id="3467"> The example gains force from the fact that it is based on a true story.</seg>
<seg id="3468"> The point of the ad is that Southwest Airlines is deeply customer-oriented (note that the term ˇ°Customerˇ± is capitalized).</seg>
<seg id="3469"> The idea that culture is a set of principles for generating focal points in specific microgames played by workers and managers is not so different from the way organization theories describe culture.</seg>
<seg id="3470"> For example, Schein (1983) defines culture as ˇ°the basic assumptions and beliefs that are shared by members of an organization.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3471"> He later notes that these ˇ°assumptions and beliefs are learned responses to a groupˇŻs problems of survival in its external environment and its problems of internal integration.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3472"> Shared understanding reduces conflict and, if the culture is visible to outsiders, also encourages efficient assortative matching between workersˇŻ attitudes and the attitudes a firm requires.</seg>
<seg id="3473"> As important as culture appears to be, it has not been studied very carefully empirically.</seg>
<seg id="3474"> The biggest obstacle is the lack of empirical measures and exogenous variation.</seg>
<seg id="3475"> One stream of literature uses psychometric scales to map organizations onto some two-dimensional or multidimensional space.</seg>
<seg id="3476"> These studies are not statistically sophisticated and typically treat the whole corporation as one culture, ignoring subcultures.</seg>
<seg id="3477"> Other studies focus on a dimension of culture, like ˇ°strength,ˇ± and explore its relation to profitability or profit variation.</seg>
<seg id="3478"> Still other studies establish the existence of distinct workplace culture (called ˇ°climateˇ± by organizational researchers) and study its consequences for phenomena like turnover.</seg>
<seg id="3479"> The lack of a sharp formal framework and clean empirical identification has certainly held back organizational economists interested in culture but progress is being made.</seg>
<seg id="3480"> There is a small emerging literature on ˇ°codesˇ±ˇŞthe language workers use to describe events.</seg>
<seg id="3481"> Codes have efficiency prop?erties, depend on shared understanding, and can be difficult to change and imitate, like other facets of culture (e.g., corporate values).</seg>
<seg id="3482"> Cremer et al. (2003) assume that codes are coarse partitions of a large space of events.</seg>
<seg id="3483"> Coarse partitions are adequate because workers need not communicate exactly which event has occurred.</seg>
<seg id="3484"> Wernerfelt (2004) offers a similar model.</seg>
<seg id="3485"> Codes have been studied experimentally by using a picture-naming paradigm.</seg>
<seg id="3486"> In these experiments, agents see pictures on a screen.</seg>
<seg id="3487"> A ˇ°managerˇ± must figure out a code to identify predetermined target pictures to workers.</seg>
<seg id="3488"> Payoffs depend on how rapidly the target pictures can be named without errors.</seg>
<seg id="3489"> Codes develop rapidly: subjects typically use about 120 characters to describe four picture at first (that is the length of this sentence so far), but use only about five characters per picture after 20-40 practice trials.</seg>
<seg id="3490"> One study shows that it is better to have one manager creating code than to rotate code-creation among the subjects.</seg>
<seg id="3491"> This suggests that having a single ˇ°founderˇ± creating code is betterŇ»specialization is better than taking the best code ideas from different workers.</seg>
<seg id="3492"> However, the single-manager groups have a harder time integrating new employees;</seg>
<seg id="3493"> the managers are more likely to give up on teaching the new employee, who then makes a lot of costly picture-naming errors.</seg>
<seg id="3494"> Two other studies explore how conflict is created when groups ˇ°merge.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3495"> Weber and Camerer (2003) found that when two groups naming the same pictures merged (like a horizontal merger of similar businesses), subjects were too optimistic about how rapidly code could be created in the merged group.</seg>
<seg id="3496"> Feiler and Camerer (2006) replicated this result with different groups of pictures (like a diversifying merger) and where subjects were endogenously sorted into the merger group through bids for wage premia to join the merged firm.</seg>
<seg id="3497"> SubjectsˇŻ guesses about the speed of the merger group and bids inˇŞtheoretically incentive-compatibleŇ»second-price auctions were too optimistic relative to actual earnings differentials of merged and unmerged groups.</seg>
<seg id="3498"> It is easy to think of other applications of this sort of code-creation paradigm, as well as expanding its scope to include development of corporate values like reciprocity and camaraderie, expressed by cooperativeness in public goods games.</seg>
<seg id="3499"> 7.3.4.2
<seg id="3500"> Organizations are complicated.</seg>
<seg id="3501"> Knowledge about how to behave in complicated systemsŇ»like the world, or an organizationŇ»is often encoded in a form that is very different than the state-space partitions and Bayesian probability that dominate economic theory, and is much simpler and evolutionarily advanced.</seg>
<seg id="3502"> This suggests that understanding cognitive economyŇ»what kinds of information fits the brainˇŻs natural structures for storing, remembering, and reliably repeating informationŇ»is important for understanding organizations.</seg>
<seg id="3503"> Organization theorists have argued that a narrative or story is a common way of encoding information about what to do in an organization.</seg>
<seg id="3504"> If the punch line of the story is success or failure, a story is basically an instruction about how to behave.</seg>
<seg id="3505"> Narratives may be a superior encoding form from the point of view of human memoryŇ»if they are better remembered, they will not degrade as much as other kinds of information when passed down from old workers to new ones.</seg>
<seg id="3506"> If it is fun to tell storiesŇ»they often involve humorŇ»they may spread more rapidly and reliably through a firm than boring instructions.</seg>
<seg id="3507"> Another element of cognitive economy in organizations is ˇ°personification.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3508"> Much of human perception and judgment is organized around what we know about people and how we summarize our knowledge in the form of traits and thumbnail sketches of people.</seg>
<seg id="3509"> It is cognitively natural to identify an organization with a charismatic founder or iconic leader, such as Walt Disney, Bill Gates, and Herb Kelleher from Southwest Airlines.</seg>
<seg id="3510"> If a leaderˇŻs behavior and values are thought to exemplify those of the culture, then it may be easier to answer the question,</seg>
<seg id="3511"> ˇ°What does my implicit employment contract require me to do?ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3512"> by answering the substitute question, ˇ°What would Walt do?ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3513"> (as employees at Disney often did, long after WaltˇŻs death).</seg>
<seg id="3514"> The personality of a corporate icon then becomes an asset that economizes on transaction costs by telling people what to do, in a way their brains can remember and understand more easily than a boring list of rules.</seg>
<seg id="3515"> 7.3.4.3
<seg id="3516"> Thinking about coordination and cognitive economy may help explain the nature of leadership and language in companies.</seg>
<seg id="3517"> A striking feature of corporate talk is that it is highly simplified, but catchy.</seg>
<seg id="3518"> ˇ°Maximize profitˇ± is not an inspiring mission statement.</seg>
<seg id="3519"> Instead, companies have slogans like ˇ°Quality is job 1ˇ± (Ford), ˇ°Just do itˇ± (Nike) ˇ°Do no evilˇ± (Google), ˇ°Copy EXACTLY!ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3520"> (Intel, to maximize transfer of wafer-production processes from old plants to new ones)</seg>
<seg id="3521"> and ˇ°Flatter the novice; reward the expert driverˇ± (Mustang cars).</seg>
<seg id="3522"> The Ritz-Carlton hotel chain supposedly has a rule that up to $2,000 can be spent to solve a guestˇŻs problem.</seg>
<seg id="3523"> The figure $2,000 is useful for two reasons: it is a round number thatˇŻs memorable; and it is a surprisingly large number, a ˇ°put your money where your mouth isˇ± signal of the RitzˇŻs commitment to luxury hotel quality.</seg>
<seg id="3524"> Why are these stated goals so simple?</seg>
<seg id="3525"> One explanation is that meta-principles which help workers coordinate on equilibrium workplace norms have to be applied to a broad range of different microgames.</seg>
<seg id="3526"> The principles have to be made commonly known, and repeated over many years and between many workers without linguistic degradation.</seg>
<seg id="3527"> Heath and Seidel (2004) suggest some good properties of ˇ°language memesˇ±: they should be simple, memorable, broadly applicable, and evoke com?plex emotions.</seg>
<seg id="3528"> Their hypotheses could easily be tested in experiments on how well various linguistic meta-principles coordinate actions in the face of garbling due to labor turnover and the passage of time.</seg>
<seg id="3529"> Van Den Steen (2005) tells a story about leadership which is closer to the meta?principle theory of culture.</seg>
<seg id="3530"> In his model, managers with ˇ°visionˇ± have a clear pref?erence for project types.</seg>
<seg id="3531"> A strong vision is good because it enables efficient labor- market sorting of other managers who agree with the vision into the firm, and saves time that could be wasted by proposing projects that do not fit.</seg>
<seg id="3532"> 7.4
<seg id="3533"> Behavioral considerations contribute to our understanding of corporate finance from two perspectives:</seg>
<seg id="3534"> first, recognizing that markets make mistakes which managers exploit;</seg>
<seg id="3535"> and, second, that managers make mistakes which markets do not fully correct.</seg>
<seg id="3536"> In this section we assume the perspective most relevant to the economics of organizations and consider the possibility that top managers make mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="3537"> Managers are most likely to be affected in their judgment when they face decisions in which three conditions hold:</seg>
<seg id="3538"> (i)
<seg id="3539"> (ii)
<seg id="3540"> (iii)
<seg id="3541"> As for (i), the higher the level of a manager in the hierarchy, the less likely decisions are to be repeated enough to learn from feedback.</seg>
<seg id="3542"> Large-scale investments, merger agreements, or capital restructuring are relatively rare decisions, and the organizational framework of a company may not be optimized to facilitate them.</seg>
<seg id="3543"> It is also hard to evaluate outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="3544"> For example, there is no agreed-upon methodology on how to evaluate the long-run returns to mergers, even in the academic literature.</seg>
<seg id="3545"> Regarding (ii), the level of specialization is likely to decrease the higher up in the hierarchy decisions are made.</seg>
<seg id="3546"> As Lazear (2004) formulates in the context of entrepreneurship, the top decision maker in a firm has to be (and typically is) a jack-of-all-trades rather than a specialist.</seg>
<seg id="3547"> And regarding (iii), we know from many studies of corporate governance and entrenchment that CEOs often manage to protect themselves from market pressure.</seg>
<seg id="3548"> Sheltered from the discipline of the outside market, they may make significantly less efficient corporate decisions, as Bertrand and Mullainathan (2003) show, exploiting variations in anti-takeover laws.</seg>
<seg id="3549"> To push the argument for biased decision-making in corporations even further, the market not only may be unable to ˇ°short-sell biased CEOsˇ± and to thereby remedy distorted corporate decision-making, but may actually value managerial overconfidence.</seg>
<seg id="3550"> As Goel and Thakor (2000) and Gervais et al. (2003) point out, diversified shareholders, who would like managers to make risk-neutral choices and to maximize the expected value of their firms, may prefer managers to be overconfident if it balances managersˇŻ risk aversion.</seg>
<seg id="3551"> If, for all of those reasons, certain managerial biases are unlikely to be eliminated by market pressures or to be remedied by the organizational design, the question is: are top managers likely to exhibit biases in the first place?</seg>
<seg id="3552"> ShouldnˇŻt people successful and smart enough to become the leader of a companyŇ»in particular of large companies in the S&amp;P 500ˇŞbe the very embodiment of the hyper-rational Homo oeconomicus?</seg>
<seg id="3553"> For example, would executive selection processes not ensure that more rational and socially neutral agents rise to the top?</seg>
<seg id="3554"> Not necessarily.</seg>
<seg id="3555"> As long as a specific bias is not negatively correlated with the ability to have a success?ful corporate career, CEOs are not necessarily less likely to deviate from rational decision-making than the average individual.</seg>
<seg id="3556"> Goel and Thakor (2000) also point out that managers may even be more likely to be promoted to CEOs in a tournament setting if they underestimate project risk, which leads them to take very risky projects with the largest probabilities of very large returns.</seg>
<seg id="3557"> In the implicit rank-order tourna?ment among potential successors of a CEO, the biased person is thus more likely to have the highest outcome, conditional on success, and is therefore unconditionally more likely to have the highest rank.</seg>
<seg id="3558"> Moreover, if superior management skills are correlated with limits on rationality and self-interest then it is conceivable that top managers are even more judgmentally biased and social-comparison-minded than average workers.</seg>
<seg id="3559"> Confidence may be correlated with other kinds of managerial valueˇŞlike inspiring workers or mes?merizing Wall Street analystsŇ»so sorting may actually select for overconfidence rather than filter it out.</seg>
<seg id="3560"> Mistakes in one type of judgment may be a price one pays for other kinds of managerial skill.</seg>
<seg id="3561"> Whether this is true is an empirical matter, which cannot be resolved purely by theory.</seg>
<seg id="3562"> 7.4.1
<seg id="3563"> Overconfidence and overoptimism, which we may define as overestimation of own skills relative to others, and overestimation of oneˇŻs own prospects or future outcomes are two patterns that have been widely studied in the academic literature and also discussed often in the business press.</seg>
<seg id="3564"> One well-established stylized fact is the ˇ°better than averageˇ± effect.</seg>
<seg id="3565"> Svenson (1981) first demonstrated that the vast majority of subjects, if asked to assess their driving skills compared to the median person in the group, rate themselves overwhelmingly as ˇ°above average.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3566"> Sven- sonˇŻs finding has been replicated numerous times and with respect to various IQ- or skill-related outcomes other than driving.</seg>
<seg id="3567"> For example, when asking a sample of entrepreneurs about their chances of success, Cooper et al. (1988) found that 33% attached zero probability to failure, 81% of them between 0 and 30%.</seg>
<seg id="3568"> Several additional findings suggest that high-rank executives are particularly likely to overestimate outcomes related to their own actions or skills.</seg>
<seg id="3569"> First, overcon?fidence tends to be observed especially in decision makers who have a position of controlˇŞor at least have the illusion of control.</seg>
<seg id="3570"> And who, if not the person on top of the corporate (or divisional) ladder, is the person believed to be the ultimate holder of control?</seg>
<seg id="3571"> A CEO has the ultimate say about the big strategic decisions and is the one who decides whether or not a large-scale investment goes ahead.</seg>
<seg id="3572"> Such a position may induce the CEO to believe that he or she can also control the outcomeˇŞand thus underestimate the likelihood of a bad outcome.</seg>
<seg id="3573"> Second, personal commitment increases the occurrence of overconfidence.</seg>
<seg id="3574"> As a quick search in ExecuComp for the option and stock packages of the top four to nine executives of publicly traded companies reveals, a huge portion of their compensationˇŞand presumably of the wealth of many of themˇŞdepends on how well the company is doing.</seg>
<seg id="3575"> Moreover, the value of their human capital is tightly related company returns.</seg>
<seg id="3576"> If the company is underperforming, this is bad news for their prospects of keeping their job and reduces their outside options.</seg>
<seg id="3577"> Thus, for compensation and career reasons alone, we would expect top executives to be highly committed to the outcome of their investment, merger and other corporate decisions.</seg>
<seg id="3578"> And, finally, a third factor contributing to the occurrence of overconfidence within the top management team is the lack of clear measures or benchmarks for good and bad decisions.</seg>
<seg id="3579"> Overconfidence has been found to be strongest when it is difficult to compare performance across individuals and when the reference point is abstract.</seg>
<seg id="3580"> Given the amount of noise in stock prices, and the long horizon over which good and bad investments pay off or fail, there is little clear feedback that would shake a CEOˇŻs overconfidence.</seg>
<seg id="3581"> It is thus not surprising that experimental studies have found that executives are particularly prone to display overconfidence, both in terms of the better-than-average effect and in terms of underestimating risk</seg>
<seg id="3582"> (Larwood and Whittaker 1977; Moore 1977).</seg>
<seg id="3583"> Overconfidence, in the form of the better-than-average effect, also affects the attribution of causality.</seg>
<seg id="3584"> Because individuals expect their behavior to produce success, they are more likely to attribute good outcomes to their actions, but bad outcomes to (bad) luck.</seg>
<seg id="3585"> But how important are such biases in real-world corporate decision-making?</seg>
<seg id="3586"> Roll (1986) was one of the first to link overconfidence to mergers.</seg>
<seg id="3587"> He proposed a ˇ°hubris hypothesisˇ±: in corporate takeovers the overconfidence of individual decision makers often results in bidding firms paying too much for their targets.</seg>
<seg id="3588"> What he had in mind is a type of winnerˇŻs curse in merger fights.</seg>
<seg id="3589"> RollˇŻs hubris hypothesis may be best summarized by a famous quote from the 1981 Berkshire Hathaway Annual Report:</seg>
<seg id="3590"> Many managements apparently were overexposed in impressionable childhood years to the story in which the imprisoned handsome prince is released from a toadˇŻs body by a kiss from a beautiful princess.</seg>
<seg id="3591"> Consequently, they are certaintheir managerial kiss will do wonders for the profitability of Company T[arget]</seg>
<seg id="3592"> WeˇŻve observed many kisses but very few miracles.</seg>
<seg id="3593"> Nevertheless, many managerial princesses remain serenely confident about the future potency of their kissesˇŞeven after their corporate backyards are knee-deep in unresponsive toads.</seg>
<seg id="3594"> (Taken from Weston et al. 1998)</seg>
<seg id="3595"> Confirming this hypothesis, Malmendier and Tate (2003) find that a firm is indeed significantly more likely to acquire another firm if the CEO is overconfident.</seg>
<seg id="3596"> In a sample of CEOs from Forbes 500 companies in the 1980s and the first half of the 1990s, Malmendier and Tate find that overconfident CEOs are about 1.7 times as likely to conduct a merger in any given year as their rational counterpart.</seg>
<seg id="3597"> Here, the measurement of overconfidence is not based on responses to questions, but on actual personal portfolio decisions.</seg>
<seg id="3598"> The authors point to the high degree of under?diversification, which high-ranking executives face due to their stock-based compensation and the investment of their human capital in their firm, and argue that rational CEOs of Forbes 500 companies should therefore try to diversify their personal portfolios whenever possible.</seg>
<seg id="3599"> For example, CEOs should exercise their stock options after the vesting period, if the stock price went up by enough, and should sell company stock following a regular (precommitted) schedule.</seg>
<seg id="3600"> But some CEOs do exactly the opposite, holding options that are well in the money (far above their exercise price) and buying, rather than selling, company stock (half the CEOs who do this lose money).</seg>
<seg id="3601"> In fact, during recent corporate scandals, while exorbitant pay, forgiven loans, and accounting manipulations grabbed public attention, it was less noticeable that many of the apparently guilty executives truly believed in the potential of their firms and their industry.</seg>
<seg id="3602"> The former CEO of WorldCom, Bernard Ebbers, lost millions of dollars because his private portfolio was narrowly invested in telecommunication stocks, while doing one merger after another.</seg>
<seg id="3603"> Managerial overconfidence thus has the potential to explain the big puzzle implied by $2 trillion spent on acquisitions by U.S. firms between 1998 and 2001, of which $250 billion was lost by shareholders of the acquiring companies.</seg>
<seg id="3604"> The same holds for internal investment projects.</seg>
<seg id="3605"> While it is hard to measure whether internal investments are generally bad bets (such data are rarely available), it is easy to find examples.</seg>
<seg id="3606"> Roger Smith, the former CEO of General Motors, became convinced that fully robotized plants, with no human presence, are the future of automobile production.</seg>
<seg id="3607"> His vision led to massive firings at GM and triggered negative responses from business analysts and engineers alike because they did not believe the technology was advanced enough.</seg>
<seg id="3608"> Such concerns did not stop a visionary like Smith.</seg>
<seg id="3609"> The result was plants in which the robots often began dismembering each other, smashing cars, spraying paint everywhere or even fitting the wrong equipment.</seg>
<seg id="3610"> (The Economist, August 10, 1991)</seg>
<seg id="3611"> The implications of overconfidence are, however, more subtle than simply ˇ°more mergersˇ± and ˇ°more investment,ˇ± as the winnerˇŻs curse view of Roll would suggest.</seg>
<seg id="3612"> Managerial overconfidence implies a difference in opinion about the value of the firm between the (overconfident) corporate insider and the outside investor.</seg>
<seg id="3613"> An overconfident CEO will persistently feel undervalued by the capital market and thus be reluctant to issue equity to finance investment projects or mergers.</seg>
<seg id="3614"> It is just the flip side of traditional Myers-Majluf-type models with asymmetric information.</seg>
<seg id="3615"> There, the manager has inside information, and good types are reluctant to issue equity since they will be taken for bad types.</seg>
<seg id="3616"> Here, the overconfident manager thinks he has (positive) inside information, with the same effect on equity issuance.</seg>
<seg id="3617"> Overconfidence amounts to ˇ°perceived asymmetric information.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3618"> As a result, overconfidence may actually lead to less investment or fewer mergers whenever the overconfident CEO needs to tap the external capital market.</seg>
<seg id="3619"> A CEO is expected to overinvest only if a firm has abundant internal resources or extra debt capacity.</seg>
<seg id="3620"> In other words, the investment decision of an overconfident CEO should be sensitive to the availability of internal cash flow, and such investment-cash-flow sensitivity should be strongest in firms with little internal resources.</seg>
<seg id="3621"> At the same time, the inclination to undertake bad acquisitions should have highest impact in firms with abundant internal resources.</seg>
<seg id="3622"> The data confirm all of the predictions above, including some type of pecking- order financing among overconfident CEOs.</seg>
<seg id="3623"> Similar evi?dence has been found for a sample of French start-up firms.</seg>
<seg id="3624"> This increasingly robust evidence on managerial overconfidence gives rise to the question of how corporate governance should respond to CEO overconfidence.</seg>
