<seg id="3625"> A large literature in corporate finance analyzes how options and stock grants help to align the interest of the CEO (or other corporate executive) with the interest of shareholders.</seg>
<seg id="3626"> The possibility of executive overconfidence alters basic conclusions about these incentive mechanisms.</seg>
<seg id="3627"> If we worry that a CEO just may have too positive a view on the value he or she can generate, then options and stocks are not helpful.</seg>
<seg id="3628"> Overconfident CEOs do not need incentives to maximize the market value of the firmˇŻs equityˇŞthat is what they perceive to be doing already.</seg>
<seg id="3629"> In fact, options and stocks may then push them to act as if they were risk-loving and choose investment which are riskier (and lower net present value) than shareholders would prefer.</seg>
<seg id="3630"> This concern is of particular relevance given that the CEO overestimates the expected value of those gambles to begin with.</seg>
<seg id="3631"> If CEOs are overconfident, then other incentive devices may work better than stocks and options.</seg>
<seg id="3632"> One example is debt.</seg>
<seg id="3633"> An overconfident CEO in a cash-rich firm tends to undertake too many investment projects, including acquisitions.</seg>
<seg id="3634"> If cash is tied up for debt repayments and the capacity for senior debt is exhausted, the CEO would need to issue more risky debt or equity to finance projects.</seg>
<seg id="3635"> Given that overconfident CEOs perceive their firms to be undervalued by the equity market and, consequently, expected returns to their projects will be underestimated by providers of risky debt, the preexisting debt will curtail the tendency to overinvest.</seg>
<seg id="3636"> More generally, a mechanism that requires the CEO to get an outsiderˇŻs approval on an investment project would counterbalance CEO overconfidence.</seg>
<seg id="3637"> That ˇ°outsiderˇ± could be a vigilant board.</seg>
<seg id="3638"> Companies typically specify in their charter that the board has to approve any investment beyond a certain dollar amount.</seg>
<seg id="3639"> Setting the threshold for board approval low enough seems a sensible strategyŇ»if we believe that the board fulfills its monitoring function.</seg>
<seg id="3640"> The concern about upward biases in a managerˇŻs perception thus gives additional importance to group decision-making, even in the absence of informational constraints.</seg>
<seg id="3641"> Of course, while CEO overconfidence can create agency costs, overconfidence may be correlated with valuable charisma, the ability to convince investors of the value of a firm, or the ability to motivate employees.</seg>
<seg id="3642"> Firms may want to attract overconfident leaders but also restrain their actions in some way.</seg>
<seg id="3643"> One method is to unbundle the leadership and resource-allocation roles of top managers, so that a confident leader creates inspired workers and optimistic investors, but does not overinvest resources.</seg>
<seg id="3644"> 7.4.2
<seg id="3645"> The last section focused on biases in top managers and whether organizational structures can constrain them.</seg>
<seg id="3646"> Biases in capital budgeting which occur below the CEOˇŻs sphere of influence are also important and might crop up in three ways: a tendency to divide capital equally among divisions; escalation in commitment to bad projects; and empire building (and capital suppression).</seg>
<seg id="3647"> 7.4.2.1</seg>
<seg id="3648"> Corporate Socialism and Partition Dependence</seg>
<seg id="3649"> Scharfstein and Stein (2000) show empirically that capital is allocated relatively evenly across divisions of different sizes.</seg>
<seg id="3650"> They call this pattern ˇ°corporate social?ism,ˇ± as if equal allocations are a deliberate attempt to subsidize smaller divisions at the expense of larger ones.</seg>
<seg id="3651"> Lamont (1997) shows the flip side of such within- company subsidization.</seg>
<seg id="3652"> After the 1986 oil price shock, oil companies significantly reduced their capital expenditure in non-oil subsidiaries even though the quality of non-oil investment projects did not decrease.</seg>
<seg id="3653"> Equality-biased allocations might also reflect a cognitive pattern called ˇ°partition dependenceˇ± or the ˇ°1 /n heuristicˇ±Ň»the tendency for allocations to be biased toward even allocations, given a particular partition of categories.</seg>
<seg id="3654"> For example, Fox and Rottenstreich (2003) asked subjects to guess the probabilities that the hottest day in the next week would be Sunday or another day of the week.</seg>
<seg id="3655"> The latter beliefs are influenced by the arbitrary partition of the week into two subsets, into ˇ°Sundayˇ± and ˇ°non-Sunday.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3656"> A similar effect is found in both laboratory and field data of investment in 401(k) plans.</seg>
<seg id="3657"> When asked to choose between investing in a stock or bond fund, many subjects choose to invest 50% in each.</seg>
<seg id="3658"> Empirical tests of partition dependence in capital budgeting could study what happens when divisions are combined or split apart, for reasons that are exogenous to changes in the demand for capital across business units (e.g., regulation or tax considerations).</seg>
<seg id="3659"> Anticipating Section 7.6.2 (on organizational ˇ°repairsˇ±) organizations that recognize the tendency to allocate capital equally across divisions could deliberately structure divisions to mitigate the bias.</seg>
<seg id="3660"> A fast-growing product line that is starving for capital might be given its own small division to be sure it gets enough capital.</seg>
<seg id="3661"> 7.4.2.2
<seg id="3662"> A common theme in behavioral judgment research is that people and organizations escalate their commitments to losing projects.</seg>
<seg id="3663"> Most of this literature is based on experiments in which people make ex ante choices, receive good or bad feedback, and then decide whether to continue a project.</seg>
<seg id="3664"> Experiments suggest that even when feedback is bad, people continue to invest, throwing ˇ°good moneyˇ± after ˇ°bad.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3665"> Despite the clear experimental evidence of escalation to commitment to bad projects, there is little convincing field evidence of this pattern.</seg>
<seg id="3666"> Case studies of projects which failed in retrospect have been related to escalation of commitment.</seg>
<seg id="3667"> But these may simply be examples of hindsight bias about especially bad projects.</seg>
<seg id="3668"> Other field studies analyze whether new managers who take over projects pull the plug on them.</seg>
<seg id="3669"> But the tendency to do so might just reflect differences in prior beliefs rather than escalation by the managers who initiated the bad projects</seg>
<seg id="3670"> (see, for example, Barsade et al. 1997; Weber and Camerer 1999).</seg>
<seg id="3671"> The ideal situation for an empirical analysis would thus be</seg>
<seg id="3672"> (i)
<seg id="3673"> (ii)
<seg id="3674"> (like the ˇ°sudden deathsˇ± of CEOs in Johnson et al. 1985), and</seg>
<seg id="3675"> (iii)
<seg id="3676"> The constraint to (i) is the poor quality of data on decision-making inside the firm and on the success of individual projects.</seg>
<seg id="3677"> As for (ii), changes in managerial position that are exogenously based on schedule or promotion (rather than promotions which endogenously follow project failure) are most promising.</seg>
<seg id="3678"> And condition (iii) could be remedied by analyzing decisions with objective criteria which leave little room for differing priors.</seg>
<seg id="3679"> Even if data satisfying criteria (i)-(iii) were available, we would need to go one step further and account for endogenous organizational response.</seg>
<seg id="3680"> Organizational design may limit bad escalation.</seg>
<seg id="3681"> Budget constraints and milestones are in place precisely to avoid escalation.</seg>
<seg id="3682"> The bigger the risk of bad escalation, the more extensive should be the implementation of such mechanisms.</seg>
<seg id="3683"> In fact, they may sometimes produce an opposite bad effect: good projects are terminated prematurely.</seg>
<seg id="3684"> Heath (1995) points out that sometimes people de-escalate too quickly, when a projectˇŻs losses hit a budget constraint.</seg>
<seg id="3685"> A promising place to study escalation and organizational responses are firms with large portfolios of small-scale investments, as in corporate R&amp;D, or consumer product companies which expect a high rate of product failure.</seg>
<seg id="3686"> These firms need to routinely pull the plug on bad projects.</seg>
<seg id="3687"> Understanding how they cope with high rates of failure could be useful in understanding how to prevent escalation.</seg>
<seg id="3688"> 7.4.2.3
<seg id="3689"> The tendency of top managers to ˇ°build empiresˇ±- to create divisions which are too large in an economic sense, benefiting managers at the expense of shareholdersŇ»is often discussed as an agency cost within the standard, rational framework.</seg>
<seg id="3690"> Behavioral economics offers a reinterpretation: when managers grow their firms at the detriment of shareholders, they may do so not (only) because of their desire to reign a larger empire and in full awareness of the resulting value destruction.</seg>
<seg id="3691"> Rather, they may (also) overestimate how much value will be created under their leadership, as discussed in Section 7.2.6.3 on overconfidence.</seg>
<seg id="3692"> Or they may feel competitive towards top managers of other firms, without full awareness of the resulting value destruction.</seg>
<seg id="3693"> The same psychology, then, could help a better understanding of the reverse tendencyŇ»suppression of capitalŇ»in business units that are speculative but grow?ing rapidly and have huge potential.</seg>
<seg id="3694"> As an example of capital suppression, Holmstrom and Kaplan (2001) note that it is hard to imagine how internal capital allocation could have created the kind of fast-growing technology-based firms that grew rapidly through venture capital financing and initial public offerings (IPOs) in the late 1990s.</seg>
<seg id="3695"> They suggest that ˇ°if Netscape, eBay or Amazon had been invented inside a big company, their potential value would probably have been overlookedˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3696"> (Holmstrom and Kaplan 2001, pp. 138-39).</seg>
<seg id="3697"> They further suggest the barriers to ˇ°intrapreneurshipˇ± are due to weak internal incentives:</seg>
<seg id="3698"> ˇ°Even if some degree of value had been seen, it would have been difficult or impossible to give management a strong incentive to maximize the value inherent in these ideas.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3699"> But what prevents large firms like IBM, Xerox, or Microsoft from simply repro?ducing the capital allocation mechanisms used by venture capitalists and then selling off its successes to IPO investors (or growing them internally) just as venture capitalists do?</seg>
<seg id="3700"> Given the interest of larger firms in replicating high-powered incentives for intrapreneurship, it is natural to ask whether there are other barriers that prevent them from allowing very rapid growth of promising business.</seg>
<seg id="3701"> One barrier might be the psychology of managers of conventional businesses who compete with the rapidly growing new business units.</seg>
<seg id="3702"> Loss-aversion and social comparison suggest that managers in traditional businesses will be uncomfortable about seeing lots of cash poured into businesses which could outgrow their own.</seg>
<seg id="3703"> The managerial psychology which creates empire building probably also encourages suppression of growing empires within the firm.</seg>
<seg id="3704"> 7.5
<seg id="3705"> Our discussion so far considered biases on the side of the managers.</seg>
<seg id="3706"> We touched upon adjustments in organizational design and corporate governance mechanisms to deal with them.</seg>
<seg id="3707"> Obviously, limits on rationality and expression of social preferences affect only managers but also the ˇ°corporate governorsˇ± e.g., board members.</seg>
<seg id="3708"> The influence of governance forces examination of the conjectures made in the last section.</seg>
<seg id="3709"> While overconfidence about the firmˇŻs projects may be less likely in external board members than in CEOs, partition dependence or escalation of commitment easily affects board decisions as well.</seg>
<seg id="3710"> Another very important issue is the selection of board members.</seg>
<seg id="3711"> Do CEOs look for the most able and knowledgeable people?</seg>
<seg id="3712"> Or do they install yes-men?</seg>
<seg id="3713"> The fundamental problem in evaluating board quality is that the literature has yet to come up with a reliable measure of high-quality boards.</seg>
<seg id="3714"> The analysis of governance rules such as corporate bylaws in Gompers et al. (2003) is certainly a big step forward.</seg>
<seg id="3715"> But sample limitations (there are still few panel data sets with a long enough time series to analyze changes in corporate governance within firms) and endogenous firm response limit the broader use and long-term reliability.</seg>
<seg id="3716"> The behavioral perspective may help the quest for better measures of ˇ°who speaks up in the boardroom.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3717"> Monetary incentives may not be all that matters.</seg>
<seg id="3718"> The ability to speak the language of the CEO and/or the other board members, or even feelings of moral obligation or social image concerns, may be far better determinants of directors monitoring quality.</seg>
<seg id="3719"> 7.6
<seg id="3720"> A special contribution of organizational economics to behavioral economics will come from highlighting the role of selection and endogenous firm response to nonstandard preferences.</seg>
<seg id="3721"> Given a pool of workers with various skills, cognitive limits and social preferences, organizational output will depend on how workers are sorted, how organizations ˇ°repairˇ± mistakes, and whether worker mistakes can be exploited for the good of the firm.</seg>
<seg id="3722"> 7.6.1
<seg id="3723"> The starting point is heterogeneity.</seg>
<seg id="3724"> People are different in skills and tastes.</seg>
<seg id="3725"> From a behavioral economics point of view, heterogeneity means people might differ in their limits on rational calculation, willpower, and greed, and their familiarity with labor-leisure trade-offs.</seg>
<seg id="3726"> The impact of these behavioral biases will depend on which kind of people sort into which organizational environment.</seg>
<seg id="3727"> Lazear et al. (2005) demonstrate how sorting can reverse the impact of behavioral preferences in a simple experimental setting.</seg>
<seg id="3728"> In a standard dictator game, where subjects decide how much to share with an anonymously matched person, about 75% of people share some positive amount and 25% keep everything for themselves.</seg>
<seg id="3729"> These proportions reverse if subjects are allowed to sort and decide whether to play the dictator game or to ˇ°opt outˇ± and keep the full amount for themselves (without the receiver finding out about the dictator game).</seg>
<seg id="3730"> Only 30% end up sharing.</seg>
<seg id="3731"> Interestingly, making the dictator game financially more attractive attracts first and foremost the ˇ°sharks,ˇ± who keep all or most of the amount available to dictators for themselves.</seg>
<seg id="3732"> The flip side of this result is the selection of agents with ˇ°desiredˇ± behavioral traits.</seg>
<seg id="3733"> Firms may design hiring practices to identify ˇ°team playersˇ± with a sense of fairness.</seg>
<seg id="3734"> Promotion criteria may select workers who are subject to strong in-groups effects, inducing effort beyond monetary incentives.</seg>
<seg id="3735"> Similar reasoning applies to cognitive limits.</seg>
<seg id="3736"> An organization will be more pro?ductive if it matches people who make mistakes with jobs in which the types of mistakes they make are not costly.</seg>
<seg id="3737"> This means that the organizationˇŻs overall performance could be partly or largely immunized against mistakes by assortative matching.</seg>
<seg id="3738"> Put differently, the output of organizational calculation could be more rational than the averaged decisions of its individuals (or even than the smartest individual).</seg>
<seg id="3739"> Not much is known, however, about which types of cognitive mistakes are actually eliminated by organizational sorting and aggregation.</seg>
<seg id="3740"> 7.6.2
<seg id="3741"> Beyond the selection process, organizations can attempt to ˇ°repairˇ± mistakes of individuals and to overcomes biases with organizational practices.</seg>
<seg id="3742"> One example is from Microsoft.</seg>
<seg id="3743"> Software engi?neers did not believe the many complaints coming from 1-800 customer service phone lines about the difficulty of using software, or disparaged the frustrated users as stupid.</seg>
<seg id="3744"> To make customer complaints vivid, Microsoft used a room with a one?way mirror.</seg>
<seg id="3745"> Through the mirror, engineers could watch customersŇ»who looked like normal, reasonably intelligent folks, not Luddite moronsŇ»struggling with software.</seg>
<seg id="3746"> The room replaced statistical information about phone line complaints with a smaller sample of more vivid information, exploiting the way in which the brain automatically privileges firsthand visual information over abstract symbols.</seg>
<seg id="3747"> The sheepish engineers went back to the drawing boards to try to make their software easier to use for the people on the other side of the mirror.</seg>
<seg id="3748"> Another example was conveyed to us by Chris Mayer.</seg>
<seg id="3749"> In their study of loss- aversion in housing, Genosove and Mayer (2001) found that the nominal purchase price condominium owners in Boston, MA, had paid for their houses influenced their listing prices and the prices at which they sold their houses.</seg>
<seg id="3750"> In talking about his findings before some investment bankers, one person in the audience said he knew about this effect, and that his firm had taken steps to limit it.</seg>
<seg id="3751"> His firm combats loss-aversion by forcing a trader to periodically switch his ˇ°positionˇ± (the portfolio of assets the trader bought and is blamed or credited for) with the position of another trader in the firm.</seg>
<seg id="3752"> Switching ensures that traders do not make bad trades because of loss-aversion and emotional attachment to their past actions without affecting the firmˇŻs net trading position.</seg>
<seg id="3753"> If attachment to oneˇŻs trading position is bad, the switch creates a purely behavioral scope economy, which permits firms with many traders to outcompete firms with fewer traders.</seg>
<seg id="3754"> Deadlines are another kind of organizational repair.</seg>
<seg id="3755"> When people procrastinate, methods for enforcing deadlines will help them get work done (OˇŻDonoghue and Rabin 2001).</seg>
<seg id="3756"> If firms are able to enforce deadlines better than individuals can, the firm has a cost advantage over individual workers who are self-employed.</seg>
<seg id="3757"> Deadline enforcement can come in the hard form of firing threats, or in the soft form of feeling bad about letting coworkers down, or the in-between form of risking coworkersˇŻ wrath if a missed deadline on a joint project leads to mistaken blame.</seg>
<seg id="3758"> While we are lacking convincing field data on organizational repairs, these examples suggest the following recipe for studying them.</seg>
<seg id="3759"> Find a mistake most people make in individual decisions; ask what organizational practice would limit its effect; then investigate whether the practice works.</seg>
<seg id="3760"> (Ideally, convince a firm to randomly vary the organizational design across similar departments!) Or reverse engineer the recipe: think of a curious organizational practice; ask what economic or behavioral problem in individual choice it might be a solution to.</seg>
<seg id="3761"> Repairs can also come from the market rather than from an organizational practice.</seg>
<seg id="3762"> Commenting on the slow speed of industry consolidation, Jensen wrote:</seg>
<seg id="3763"> In industry after industry with excess capacity, managers fail to recognize that they themselves must downsize;</seg>
<seg id="3764"> instead they leave the exit to others while they continue to invest.</seg>
<seg id="3765"> The reluctance to downsize might stem from emotional reluctance of executives to fire people</seg>
<seg id="3766"> (though self-interest surely plays a role ).</seg>
<seg id="3767"> An aversion to firings and layoffs is an organizational manifestation of loss-aversion.</seg>
<seg id="3768"> For most managers this is a kind of effort in the simple agency model sketched aboveŇ»an activity which they dislike doing but which is good for shareholders.</seg>
<seg id="3769"> One solution to the problem of necessary downsizing is sortingŇ»hire managers who do not find firing people effortful (like ˇ°Chainsaw Alˇ± Dunlop and ˇ°Neutron Jackˇ± Welch).</seg>
<seg id="3770"> Jensen suggested that the emergence of the leveraged buyout (LBO) organizational form has been a useful mechanism for downsizing (and other changes in governance would help too).</seg>
<seg id="3771"> An important feature of the LBO is that control shifts to new investors (the LBO partnership), who typically have no emotional attachment to the firmˇŻs workers.</seg>
<seg id="3772"> This element of psychological transfer, and willingness to accept a ˇ°lossˇ± from the firmˇŻs previous policy, may be central to the success of this kind of restructuring.</seg>
<seg id="3773"> Of course, the LBO form also offers huge financial incentives to restructure, and to do so rapidly (because of the large debt service burdens).</seg>
<seg id="3774"> But maybe these large rewards just reflect how emotionally painful downsizing is for most managers.</seg>
<seg id="3775"> 7.6.3
<seg id="3776"> A growing literature on the ˇ°behavioral economics of industrial organizationsˇ± deals with the interaction of individuals and firms</seg>
<seg id="3777"> (DellaVigna and Malmendier 2004; Eliaz and Spiegler 2004; Gabaix and Laibson 2005; Heidhues and Koszegi 2005).</seg>
<seg id="3778"> The baseline assumption in this literature is that consumers make systematic mistakes and firms are rational actors.</seg>
<seg id="3779"> This is reasonable because experience, specialization, larger resources, sorting, and market competition all work in the direction of enabling organizations to train and choose managers who are expert at exploiting mistakes by consumersˇŞand by their own workers.</seg>
<seg id="3780"> Rational firms can exploit the biases of individual consumers, for instance by charging high flat rates for services or goods consumers will consume less than they plan toˇŞthink of your gym attendance!</seg>
<seg id="3781"> ˇŞby designing contracts with automatic renewal if consumers tend to procrastinate cancelation, or by issuing stock when investor sentiment is high.</seg>
<seg id="3782"> Similarly, the individual employee in an organization may display biases from which the organization can extract rents.</seg>
<seg id="3783"> For example, Oyer and Schaefer (2005) analyze why firms give options to medium- or lower-level employees.</seg>
<seg id="3784"> Given the relatively small size of these grants and the risk premia associated with them, options are unlikely to be an efficient way to provide incentives for these employees.</seg>
<seg id="3785"> Option grants may, however, allow firms to attract and retain employees.</seg>
<seg id="3786"> The analysis of Oyer and Schaefer suggests that the benefits of option compensation are particularly high if employees are overly optimistic about the future prospects of the company.</seg>
<seg id="3787"> Making options part of the compensation package allows firms to identify those (potential) employees who haveparticularly strong beliefs about the future prospects of the company and to reap the rents from compensating them with overvalued options rather than cash.</seg>
<seg id="3788"> Such a compensation strategy is profitable if these employees are also more productive, for instance, due to their enthusiasm about the30company.</seg>
<seg id="3789"> Other elements of organizational design have similar properties.</seg>
<seg id="3790"> Lazear and Rosen (1981) have shown conditions under which tournaments dominate other forms of labor contracts, especially with sorting among high-ability and low-ability workers.</seg>
<seg id="3791"> Their argument is strengthened (and more robust) if we introduce heterogeneity in self-confidence among workers.</seg>
<seg id="3792"> If some workers are overly convinced of their own abilities (and their overconfidence is not negatively correlated with ability), tournaments become a particularly cheap form of compensation.</seg>
<seg id="3793"> Overconfident individuals will overestimate the expected value of entering the tournament outcome and will be willing to accept lower current compensation.</seg>
<seg id="3794"> Option-based compensation and rank-order based promotion are two examples of how firms can reduce their compensation costs at the expense of employees who are too optimistic about the firmˇŻs prospects.</seg>
<seg id="3795"> These examples assume implicitly that the organization itselfŇ»or the top management teams of an organizationŇ»is not affected by biases observed in individuals further down the hierarchy or outside the organization, but may remedy or even exploit them.</seg>
<seg id="3796"> 7.7
<seg id="3797"> This chapter is about both applying behavioral economics to organizations, and enriching behavioral economics by asking questions that are specific to the roles of workers and managers in organizations.</seg>
<seg id="3798"> This chapter is less of a review of what is known than it is a research agenda, with more questions than answers.</seg>
<seg id="3799"> This conclusion recaps some of the common themes.</seg>
<seg id="3800"> The first question is how to complicate the basic agency model.</seg>
<seg id="3801"> One complication is that agentsˇŻ preferences (i.e., effort disutility and reservation wages) may be sensitive to how jobs are described or how wage offers and job choices are elicited.</seg>
<seg id="3802"> Limits on greed and self-interest imply that people often like to help their friends and harm their enemies, and dislike unequal treatment.</seg>
<seg id="3803"> Precise models of social preference that have been carefully honed on experimental data from simple games would need to be plugged into organizational economics applications.</seg>
<seg id="3804"> In the laboratory, preferences are often sensitive to reference points, such as previous wages (due perhaps to habit formation) and wages of other workers.</seg>
<seg id="3805"> There is evidence of such effects from labor practices (e.g., two-tier wage deals in financially distressed firms) and executive compensation.</seg>
<seg id="3806"> Workers also seem to care about fair or just procedures for determining outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="3807"> The strength of these preferences in field data and organizational design responses has, however, not been studied thoroughly.</seg>
<seg id="3808"> Psychological influences on judgments of causality (in agency-theory terms, inferring agent effort from output) are also important.</seg>
<seg id="3809"> Hindsight bias, overattribution of cause to workers rather than luck, asymmetric attribution (taking credit and exporting blame),</seg>
<seg id="3810"> diffusion of blame across a large group, and overconfidence about skill may all play important roles, and these have not been studied much in organizational contexts.</seg>
<seg id="3811"> Section 7.2 moves from the individual to the organizational level.</seg>
<seg id="3812"> Organizing requires informal rules for coordinating action.</seg>
<seg id="3813"> Following Kreps (1990), we take the view that corporate culture is a set of shared meta-focal principles for resolving coordination problems in the face of the cognitive inability to fully specify an employment contract.</seg>
<seg id="3814"> Having a sharp economic concept like this to work with could provide a basis for useful empirical work on culture.</seg>
<seg id="3815"> The way the brain organizes information (ˇ°cognitive economyˇ±) may also explain why culture is often transmit?ted in the form of stories, personification, and simple slogans
<seg id="3816"> The value of having cultural principles that can be commonly understood, and are immune to errors in language translation, gives a role for leadership and managerial statements that are simple</seg>
<seg id="3817"> (like corporate mission statements).</seg>
<seg id="3818"> In large business organizations (especially U.S. firms), the judgment of top managers (e.g., CEOs) is especially influential.</seg>
<seg id="3819"> Section 7.3 discusses the fact that top managers are particularly prone to specific biases, such as overconfidence, given their past successes and the noisy feedback on their large-scale decisions.</seg>
<seg id="3820"> Then they may make mistakes, especially if weak corporate governance does not discipline mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="3821"> Overconfident managers, for example, will rely too much on internal cash flow when (over)investing, and enter into value-reducing mergers.</seg>
<seg id="3822"> If organizations are aware of the mistakes workers and managers make, they can sort around them (via hiring practices and internal matching of workers into jobs where their mistakes are least costly),</seg>
<seg id="3823"> they can design ˇ°organizational repairs,ˇ± or they can exploit mistakes by workers</seg>
<seg id="3824"> (e.g., offering backloaded incentives to optimistic workers who are sure they will succeed).</seg>
<seg id="3825"> Thinking about the optimal design problem in the face of worker error, as suggested in Section 4.4, is both positive economics and potentially normatively useful for managers.</seg>
<seg id="3826"> We end the book with an important caveat.</seg>
<seg id="3827"> Psychology and economics share a basic methodological individualism: psychologists are interested in people, and economists are interested in how economic outcomes arise from the interaction of people (and institutions).</seg>
<seg id="3828"> Many organizational theorists, however, work at a middle level sometimes called ˇ°meso,ˇ± in which the organization is the unit of analysis.</seg>
<seg id="3829"> Meso concepts like organizational routines and learning are not rooted in individual behavioral concepts.</seg>
<seg id="3830"> It is possible that organizational economics could be tied to meso-organizational (or macro-organizational) constructs, but this is not an active area of research.</seg>
<seg id="3831"> Comment by Michael D. Cohen</seg>
<seg id="3832"> Beyond Bounded Rationality</seg>
<seg id="3833"> This chapter views organizational phenomena through the lens of behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="3834"> It generates many productive insights.</seg>
<seg id="3835"> In the course of doing so it shifts between two different conceptual frameworks in a way that reflects an important contest of perspectives playing out at the moment across the social sciences.</seg>
<seg id="3836"> The careful reader will notice that two quite different organizing perspectives are employed at different points in the article, each reflecting one side of the contention that is in progress.</seg>
<seg id="3837"> The first perspective employed might be called ˇ°deviations from baseline rationality,ˇ± while the second organizing principle might be labeled ˇ°the economic implications of how the brain works.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3838"> One sees examples of the former in their discussions of topics such as hindsight bias and partition dependence.</seg>
<seg id="3839"> Examples of the latter occur in their discussions of the ˇ°cognitive economiesˇ± achieved by narratives and personification and of the set-point dependencies that may shape the valuation of wages.</seg>
<seg id="3840"> In my view, this oscillation among frameworks embodied in the essay accurately mirrors the state of the field.</seg>
<seg id="3841"> Across the social sciences, the working assumptions about the human mind that have relied on metaphors of computation are contending with metaphors of biological process.</seg>
<seg id="3842"> The ˇ°deviationsˇ± perspective exemplifies the older approach, with roots stretching back to Herbert SimonˇŻs bounded rationality and his models of heuristic human problem solving that rest in turn on his symbol processing hypotheses.</seg>
<seg id="3843"> Although Simon and his followers engaged in some bitter contests with orthodox economists, both camps worked with a view centered on a strongly cognitive approach to human decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="3844"> Both presumed that much of what was important about choice and problem solving was accessible for research via introspection and ˇ°thinking aloudˇ± protocols.</seg>
<seg id="3845"> Both cleanly factored emotional aspects of action, allowing them to enter models of choice or decision only through concepts such as value, utility, goal, or aspiration level.</seg>
<seg id="3846"> In this approach, models of economic action based on empirical observation are framed as conforming with, or systematically deviating from, behavior expected under conventional assumptions of rationality.</seg>
<seg id="3847"> The perspective that might be called ˇ°economic implications of how the brain worksˇ± is newer.</seg>
<seg id="3848"> It is being propelled into the foreground by a number of cultural and technical forces, but most notably by the very rapid gains in psychological mea?surement capability that are discussed below.</seg>
<seg id="3849"> In this approach the starting point is models of mental processing as understood in psychological research.</seg>
<seg id="3850"> Economic action is taken to be generated by individuals who are characterized by the memory processes, habitual dispositions, and emotional response patterns found in psychological studies.</seg>
<seg id="3851"> Economic analysis then tries to elucidate the consequences of such actions.</seg>
<seg id="3852"> The defining feature of the ˇ°deviations from baseline rationalityˇ± approach is that it is organized around observed patterns of economic action that fall short of some plausible standard of efficiency.</seg>
<seg id="3853"> People are found to exhibit a consistent tendency to act in some way that ˇ°leaves money (or utility) on the table.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3854"> For example, they underestimate the probabilities of events that did not occur (examples of this type are collected as ˇ°hindsight biasˇ±).</seg>
<seg id="3855"> Indeed, from this viewpoint, observed deviations are often characterized as ˇ°mistakes.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3856"> The term implies that psychological processes are being assessed for their adequacy in meeting the demands of economic rationality.</seg>
<seg id="3857"> In the second approach, it is the problem of economic research to accurately model action generated by normal psychological processes.</seg>
<seg id="3858"> Though psychological accuracy would seem to be a valuable goal, the risk of this approach is that psychologically rich models may lose contact with the powerful theoretical machinery developed over the last century that allows economists to infer outcomes for groups from assumptions about individuals.</seg>
<seg id="3859"> A research survey such as the Camerer-Malmendier essay tries to look both backward over what is known, and forward toward what should be researched.</seg>
<seg id="3860"> Since so much of what is known was accumulated in the ˇ°deviationsˇ± perspective, it is appropriate that it play a large role.</seg>
<seg id="3861"> However, since the gains are developing so rapidly in contemporary psychology and the new tools becoming available are so powerful, it is natural that much of the forward thinking about what research is promising should bring in the perspective of ˇ°how the brain works.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3862"> My remarks are therefore divided into two clusters.</seg>
<seg id="3863"> The first consists of observations on the strengths and limitations of the deviations framework.</seg>
<seg id="3864"> The second discusses examples that may help to bring out the potentialŇ»and the challengesŇ» for economics of taking ˇ°how the brain worksˇ± as a starting point for analysis of organizational economics.</seg>
<seg id="3865"> The ˇ°Deviationsˇ±Perspective</seg>
<seg id="3866"> There are some important advantages to the deviations framework as a way of organizing results from behavioral economics for application to the economics of organization.</seg>
<seg id="3867"> First and foremost, the underlying behavioral economics literature has often used this framework, and so transfer to application in the economics of organization is most direct when it is maintained.</seg>
<seg id="3868"> But additional advantages are also obtained.</seg>
<seg id="3869"> For example, the deviations approach often suggests extensions of existing formal apparatus.</seg>
<seg id="3870"> Camerer and Malmendier provide a nice example of this with their addition of a skill term to a more stan?dard equation for effort level in an agency model.</seg>
<seg id="3871"> Though it is a complication, the extension is not unmanageable, and the authors show that the term may allow theory to accommodate a range of observed deviations from simpler representations.</seg>
<seg id="3872"> The deviation approach is often able to suggest promising modification of existing formalisms.</seg>
<seg id="3873"> Indeed the whole apparatus of risk aversion, now taken for granted as standard theory, can be viewed as embodying just such a modification.</seg>
<seg id="3874"> The deviations approach likewise suggests areas of direct potential benefit to management or policy.</seg>
<seg id="3875"> Because people are known from field and laboratory observation to have systematic tendencies to miss potential gains, pointing out a general pattern like hindsight bias can be directly useful to practitioners in avoiding costly errors.</seg>
<seg id="3876"> Moreover, the naming conventions of the deviations approach result in quite memorable labeling of the principles, with the implication that students can be fairly effectively taught to watch out for problems like ˇ°the fundamental attribution error.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3877"> And finally, the deviations approach suggests to those who study the economics of organization research questions that are likely to be productive.</seg>
<seg id="3878"> Camerer and Mal- mendier illustrate this advantage nicely with their account of known individual devi?ations that may motivate organizations to develop corrective devices.</seg>
<seg id="3879"> For example, they suggest that budget constraints and project milestones may be organizational devices that function to limit individual tendencies to escalate bad commitments.</seg>
<seg id="3880"> There are, of course, some costs to the deviations framework, along with the benefits.</seg>
<seg id="3881"> Earlier I suggested the advantage that a deviations approach facilitates adjusting current theory to observed patterns of ˇ°mistakeˇ±.</seg>
<seg id="3882"> But the quite distorted psychology this can build into theory may itself become a rhetorical albatross.</seg>
<seg id="3883"> As the richer model of mind now developing in psychology gains credibility in the general intellectual community, that shift may markedly constrain the freedom of economics to operate credibly in the eyes of policy makers and managers while retaining the strongly stylized representations of economic action that might be the most convenient for formalization.</seg>
<seg id="3884"> Consumers of economic advice may begin to ask where human habits, skills, identifications, and emotions enter into the analysis.</seg>
<seg id="3885"> And within economics itself the strategy of modifying existing theory to accomodate behaviorally observed deviations may gradually undermine the elegant simplicity that has been one of its hallmark attractions.</seg>
<seg id="3886"> The danger is that theory can become encumbered with a mass of Ptolemaic epicycles and the discipline can be left behind after an eventual Copernican simplification.</seg>
<seg id="3887"> Still, we have seen that there are considerable advantages to the deviations approach and therefore good reasons to continue using it, despite some problems.</seg>
<seg id="3888"> Now we can turn to some examples suggestive of the potentialŇ»and challengesŇ»of the alternative.</seg>
<seg id="3889"> I offer my views on the second perspective, the approach that starts from ˇ°how the brain works,ˇ± not as an economist, and also not as a psychologist, but as an organizational theorist who tries to pay close attention to psychology and economics.</seg>
<seg id="3890"> At bottom, the relationship to psychology has been a major feature distinguishing organization theory from organizational economics.</seg>
<seg id="3891"> Roughly, one might say that many organizational theorists have been closer to the second approach described here, feeling obliged to take psychological (and social psychological) results on their own terms,</seg>
<seg id="3892"> and build up organization theory with those results as starting points,</seg>
<seg id="3893"> while economists have askedŇ»and rightly soˇŞhow psychological results may be assimilated to the needs and theories of economics.</seg>
<seg id="3894"> In consequence, organization theory has far less formalized theoretical development than does organizational economics, though it addresses a broader array of significant issues.</seg>
<seg id="3895"> The Perspective Starting from "How the Brain Works"</seg>
<seg id="3896"> My method for illustrating the possibilities now emerging for the second approach will be to consider a few examples of striking developments in psychology and their possible significance for economistsˇŻ views of organization.</seg>
<seg id="3897"> I have focused inparticular on examples involving emotions, since these seem to present some of the deepest challenges.</seg>
<seg id="3898"> The first thing to say is that psychology itself is being transformedˇŞas so often happens to a scienceˇŞin the wake of stunning new possibilities of measurement.</seg>
<seg id="3899"> The most dramatic and widely noted are various forms of brain imagingˇŞsuch as positron emission tomography (PET) and functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI)ˇŞbut there are others as well,</seg>
<seg id="3900"> including improved precision of lesion studies in humans and in animal models;</seg>
<seg id="3901"> improved chemical detection methods</seg>
<seg id="3902"> (a few dollars for a simple, reliable saliva assay of important chemicals such as testosterone, cortisol, or progesterone);</seg>
<seg id="3903"> knockout gene studies in animal models;</seg>
<seg id="3904"> improved placement of, and recording from, micro-electrodes, now reaching the single brain-cell level; and many more.</seg>
<seg id="3905"> The consequences of this measurement gain in psychology are many.</seg>
<seg id="3906"> Among them is an important increase in our ability to exhibit well-measured counterparts of the previously ˇ°softˇ± observations of clinicians.</seg>
<seg id="3907"> Thus ˇ°hardened,ˇ± they become more difficult to set aside as we study decision and choice.</seg>
<seg id="3908"> A case that dramatizes this change is that of Phineas Gage, a young man of excellent reputation and work habits who was the victim of a construction accident in 1848 that shot a metal rod cleanly through his head.</seg>
<seg id="3909"> Gage made a full physical recovery from the accident.</seg>
<seg id="3910"> His intelligence was unaffected.</seg>
<seg id="3911"> But his physician docu?mented dramatic changes in his character in the dozen years he lived thereafter.</seg>
<seg id="3912"> Gage lost his ability to conform to social conventions or to honor his own commitments.</seg>
<seg id="3913"> He became an unemployable and foul-mouthed wanderer.</seg>
<seg id="3914"> The physician archived GageˇŻs skull after he died, and 155 years later researchers were able to correlate the areas between the entry and exit holes with what is now known from many other studies about lesions affecting the same areas of the brain, the right ventro-medial prefrontal cortex.</seg>
<seg id="3915"> Some images from a paper reporting the re-analysis of GageˇŻs skull are shown in Figure 7.2.</seg>
<seg id="3916"> And here is the authorsˇŻ interpretation, linking their analysis to the cases of many modern patients with similar behavioral disturbances:</seg>
<seg id="3917"> For patients with damage comparable to GageˇŻs, their ability to make rational decisions in personal and social matters is invariably compromised and so is their processing of emotion.</seg>
<seg id="3918"> On the contrary, their ability to tackle the logic of an abstract problem, to perform calculations, and to call up appropriate knowledge and attend to it remains intact.</seg>
<seg id="3919"> The establishment of such a pattern has led to the hypothesis that emotion and its underlying neural machinery participate in decision-making within the social domain and has raised the possibility that the participation depends on the ventromedial frontal region.</seg>
<seg id="3920"> This region is reciprocally connected with the subcortical nuclei that control basic biological regulation, emotional processing, and social cognition and behavior, for instance, in amygdala and hypothalamus.</seg>
<seg id="3921"> (Damasio et al. 1994)</seg>
<seg id="3922"> The case serves to demonstrate two points:</seg>
<seg id="3923"> (i)
<seg id="3924"> (iii)
<seg id="3925"> (in fact some control subjects never got the theory but steadily played the C and D decks);</seg>
<seg id="3926"> (iv)
<seg id="3927"> Normal subjects are responding emotionally to projected actions even before they can voice a hunch, while patients only have baseline responses, never express hunches, and do not execute the theory even if they do state it correctly.</seg>
<seg id="3928"> The data thus provide another indication that in the brain a complex process is required to relate evaluations of prior experiences to future actions.</seg>
<seg id="3929"> That process may affect action before it affects cognition or even without affecting cognition.</seg>
<seg id="3930"> Conversely, the results for the patients show that a cognitive grasp of the situation may be attained without affecting action.</seg>
<seg id="3931"> The examples provided are intended to illustrate how much difference it will make to economic analysis of organizationŇ»and other topics in economicsŇ»if the shift continues from ˇ°deviationsˇ± to ˇ°how the brain works.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3932"> The magnitude of the difference can be seen in the concluding remark of the Camerer-Malmendier review:</seg>
<seg id="3933"> Meso concepts like organizational routines and learning are not rooted in individual behavioral concepts.</seg>
<seg id="3934"> It is possible that organizational economics could be tied to meso-organizational (or macro-organizational) constructs, but this is not an active area of research.</seg>
<seg id="3935"> From the deviations perspective on ˇ°individual behavioral conceptsˇ± this blunt summary is correct.</seg>
<seg id="3936"> Yet to an organizational theorist it looks odd, since the evolutionary economics community has produced hundreds of papers about firms stemming from the treatment of routine in Nelson and Winter (1982).</seg>
<seg id="3937"> Moreover, the performance properties of routine action have been linked to ˇ°how the brain worksˇ±ˇŞ</seg>
<seg id="3938"> in this case to the distinction between procedural and declarative memory.</seg>
<seg id="3939"> This stark difference suggests that the visibility of research on routine action in organizations, and of many other important aspects of the day-to-day performance of firms, will depend crucially on which of the two contending perspectives future economic researchers have in mind.</seg>
<seg id="3940"> THE PROBLEMATIC CONTENT AND CONTEXT OF DECISIONS: COMMENT BY ELDAR SHAFIR</seg>
<seg id="3941"> Good theories are often based on the few things that really matter, and ignore large amounts of detail.</seg>
<seg id="3942"> What makes a theory successful, however, is our ability to use it to distinguish between detail that can be ignored at low cost and detail that is needed for a successful characterization of the relevant facts.</seg>
<seg id="3943"> A theory can run into trouble when it dismisses as irrelevant factors that are at the heart of important outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="3944"> With regard to modern economic theory as a descriptive enterprise, we have increasingly good evidence for, and some insight into, a variety of behavioral factors that appear to be highly consequential for economic outcomes.</seg>
<seg id="3945"> Many of these factors, however, are dismissed by the classical theory, explicitly or implicitly, as unimportant detail.</seg>
<seg id="3946"> The list is long.</seg>
<seg id="3947"> It includes a variety of cognitive, perceptual, social, cultural, and other factors that appear to play a central role in peopleˇŻs decisions, in ways that are profoundly different from what was envisioned by the progenitors of rational agents.</seg>
<seg id="3948"> A number of these factors have been discussed in this book, and some are further considered in TiroleˇŻs commentary below.</seg>
<seg id="3949"> Now, on the descriptive front, it is true that what we have so far consists largely of ˇ°a listˇ± of factors, rather than a characterization that is more general or theoretical in nature.</seg>
<seg id="3950"> And this lack of generalization makes any attempt to incorporate the new behavioral insights into the models quite daunting.</seg>
<seg id="3951"> It is also at the heart of TiroleˇŻs admonition that we resist the temptation to incorporate ˇ°stylized factsˇ± into economistsˇŻ relatively unified, ˇ°portableˇ± theory.</seg>
<seg id="3952"> As a modeling strategy, that may be exactly right.</seg>
<seg id="3953"> But for those of us who want an account that is descriptively faithful, some fairly radical revisions to the portable theory seem unavoidable.</seg>
<seg id="3954"> The problem is deeper than merely choosing which of the stylized facts are in and which are out.</seg>
<seg id="3955"> The problem we have, if you will, is one not just of content, but also of structure.</seg>
<seg id="3956"> The dialogue between economics and psychology presents a classic divergence in disciplinary sensitivities.</seg>
<seg id="3957"> Whereas the psychologist is happy to focus on the fascinating events happening behind the eyes and between the ears, the economist wants to know what specific behaviors are generated thereby.</seg>
<seg id="3958"> This tension has had a substantial impact on psychologists who are engaged in the dialogue with economists: their work has veered in the direction of a greater focus on actual outcomes, in real circumstances, with real incentives, and so on.</seg>
<seg id="3959"> At the same time, economists interested in a behaviorally more sophisticated theory need to take into account some fundamental facts about our mental lives.</seg>
<seg id="3960"> And by that I do not mean any specific brain processes underlying any particular activity but, rather, a much more general, somewhat mundane, fact about psychology that is critical to decision-making.</seg>
<seg id="3961"> Whereas the notion of ˇ°construalˇ± is second nature to psychologists, it is far less obvious to the untrained observer and even less so to economists.</seg>
<seg id="3962"> Economic thinking about optimization concerns itself with things in the world, with alternatives as they really are</seg>
<seg id="3963"> (or, as they are best understood by a rational actor).</seg>
<seg id="3964"> The axioms that underlie the normative theory are stated in terms of extensional outcomes, where each option, representing some state of the world, is clearly and uniquely designated, independently of how precisely it may be described or perceived.</seg>
<seg id="3965"> The problem with this perspective is a terribly trivial but profoundly consequential fact about human nature: people do not actually choose between items in the worldŇ» they choose between those items as they are represented in the mind.</seg>
<seg id="3966"> Decisions are not about objective (extensional) states of the world, but about the mental (intensional) representations of those states .</seg>
<seg id="3967"> And the mental representation of states of the world is subject to a rich psychology involving perception, embellishment, interpretation, and distortion.</seg>
<seg id="3968"> Construal processes are central to many areas of psychology, ranging from auditory and visual perception, memory, language, and self-perception, to judgments, decisions, opinion surveys, and social interaction.</seg>
<seg id="3969"> In the case of decision-making, as we all know well, nuanced differences in otherwise logically equivalent representations of options can generate significantly different perceptions, trigger different reactions, and yield systematically different choices.</seg>
<seg id="3970"> We thus are left with multiple sources of tension.</seg>
<seg id="3971"> First, there are the ˇ°contentsˇ± of decision: what attributes enter into peopleˇŻs calculations.</seg>
<seg id="3972"> These include things such as prosocial behavior, a concern with fairness, or interpersonal comparisons, which turn out to have a more substantial then normatively anticipated impact.</seg>
<seg id="3973"> Then there is the structure, or context, of decision.</seg>
<seg id="3974"> This includes unavoidable contextual nuances, ranging from how the options are described (even when providing otherwise identical information), to the process used to elicit preference, the presence or absence of other (ˇ°irrelevantˇ±) alternatives, the point of reference, and so on.</seg>
<seg id="3975"> As a matter of fact, there are additional factors that are somewhere in between context and content.</seg>
<seg id="3976"> There are features thatˇŞunlike fairness, sayˇŞpeople do not profess to care about, and may even be unaware of, but which, once introduced into the context of a decision, nonetheless can have a substantial impact on choice.</seg>
<seg id="3977"> For example, people gently touched on the shoulder by a waitress at a restaurant tip higher than those who were not touched.</seg>
<seg id="3978"> And when asked later, all attribute their tipping to the quality of service, with no awareness of the influence of the touch .</seg>
<seg id="3979"> We have recently conducted a field experiment in South Africa to assess the relative importance of price and of various subtle psychological features in the decision to take-up a loan offer from a local lender .</seg>
<seg id="3980"> Some 57,000 incumbent clients of the lender were sent letters offering large short-term loans at randomly chosen interest rates.</seg>
<seg id="3981"> Consistent with standard economics, those offered higher rates were less likely to take up a loan then those with access to lower rates.</seg>
<seg id="3982"> In addition, various psychological features on the offer letter, which did not affect offer terms or economic content, were also independently randomized.</seg>
<seg id="3983"> One of the features consisted of the number of sample loans displayed: the offer letter displayed either one example of a loan size and term, along with the respective monthly repayment, or it displayed multiple (four) examples of loan sizes and terms.</seg>
<seg id="3984"> In contrast with standard economic prediction, we found higher take-up under the one-example description than under the multiple-example version.</seg>
<seg id="3985"> The magnitude of this effect was large: the simpler description of the offer had the same positive effect on take-up as dropping the monthly interest on these loans by two percentage points.</seg>
<seg id="3986"> (In a similar vein, Huberman et al. (2004) found that employeesˇŻ participation in 401(k) plans drops as the number of fund options proposed by their employer increases.)</seg>
<seg id="3987"> We also randomized the presence or absence of a smiling womanˇŻs picture in the bottom corner of the offer letters.</seg>
<seg id="3988"> For the men in the sample, the presence of that picture had the same positive effect on take-up as dropping the monthly interest on the loans by 4.5 percentage points!</seg>
<seg id="3989"> On average, any one psychological manipulation we tried had the same effect as a one-half percentage point change in the monthly interest rate.</seg>
<seg id="3990"> Unlike a concern with fairness or even an aversion to losses, nobody would acknowledge or, for that matter, be willing to entertain the possibility that a touch on the shoulder or a picture in the corner of a letter predisposes them to make a substantially different economic decision.</seg>
<seg id="3991"> Choice behavior is the outcome of a variety of cognitive and affective processes.</seg>
<seg id="3992"> Some of these we consciously endorse and even insist on;</seg>
<seg id="3993"> others we have no introspective access to, nor control over .</seg>
<seg id="3994"> And many of both kinds of processes depart in significant ways from economistsˇŻ unified, ˇ°portableˇ± theory.</seg>
<seg id="3995"> Tirole correctly observes that (see p. 298, this volume)</seg>
<seg id="3996"> When parents report that they ˇ°valueˇ± education, when Americans ˇ°believeˇ± they should be saving more, or when people ˇ°believeˇ± that they perform better than average in a number of dimensions,</seg>
<seg id="3997"> they may indeed be making mistakes, but they may also be engaging in cheap (although therefore not very effective) reinforcement of their identity or self-image.</seg>
<seg id="3998"> Similar remarks may apply to economistsˇŻ ˇ°beliefˇ± that their model is ˇ°doing just fine, thank you.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="3999"> Perhaps they are not actually making an error, just reaffirming their identity.</seg>
<seg id="4000"> And meetings such as this may help alleviate the pressure and allow for a more permissive self-image.</seg>
<seg id="4001"> It is, in fact, quite respectable to be in possession of a theory that is in many ways beautiful, but that nonetheless requires substantial restructuring before it is able to predict our quirky behaviors.</seg>
<seg id="4002"> 8.2 COMMENT BY JEAN TIROLE</seg>
<seg id="4003"> 8.2.1 A Familiar Trade-Off</seg>
<seg id="4004"> Churchill once famously stated that ˇ°democracy is the worst form of government except for all those others that have been triedˇ±;</seg>
<seg id="4005"> economists have long regarded their neoclassical model in a similar light: clearly unsatisfactory, yet superior to alternatives.</seg>
<seg id="4006"> This unhappy state is of course what makes research and volumes like this so exciting.</seg>
<seg id="4007"> The economistˇŻs self-view is currently being challenged.</seg>
<seg id="4008"> Building in particular on work in psychology, the standard model of maximization of present discounted expected utility has been recently amended to allow for</seg>
<seg id="4009"> ? a broader description of preferences with the introduction of anticipatory feelings and memories, concerns about self-image, social values and even emotions, and the use of reference points and path dependency,</seg>
<seg id="4010"> ?  a broader approach to discounting, as with hyperbolic preferences,</seg>
<seg id="4011"> ? a broader take on belief formation, including errors in Bayesian updating, imperfect memory, and biases in hedonic forecasts, and</seg>
<seg id="4012"> ? a challenge to the optimization hypothesisˇŞrules of thumb, mental representations, analogies, and categorization strategies, with an ongoing debate as to whether limited optimization is rational given memory, mental search and time constraints.</seg>
<seg id="4013"> As has already been noted in this volume, while other social scientists often do not aim at an overarching theory of decision-making, economists often tend to prefer a relatively unified, ˇ°portableˇ± theory that helps explain a wide range of behaviors with a small number of core assumptions.</seg>
<seg id="4014"> They use a relatively parsimonious framework that has predictive power and normative implications across a broad range of situations.</seg>
<seg id="4015"> Thaler once rightly remarked that behavioral economics will be successful once it stops being ˇ°behavioralˇ± and it becomes just economics.</seg>
<seg id="4016"> To this purpose, and in my opinion, we sometimes need to resist the temptation to incorporate ˇ°stylized factsˇ± about human behavior into reduced forms for economic modeling and should rather build more structural models, ˇ°dig deeper,ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="4017"> and run experiments to discriminate finely among alternatives in order to perform normative analysis.</seg>
<seg id="4018"> Our profession has sometimes learned this lesson the hard way, as when, in reaction to the (correct) observation that firms do not maximize profits, the literature of the 1950s and 1960s added objectives into a firmˇŻs reduced form payoff function</seg>
<seg id="4019"> (this literature was abandoned when agency theory built an alternative approach based on the unifying principle of asymmetric information).</seg>
<seg id="4020"> Understand me well: warning against the use of reduced forms has its limits;</seg>
<seg id="4021"> after all, we all to some degree always use reduced forms in order to limit complexity, and even reduced forms that are by any measure very sloppy can prove stimulating and ultimately useful (witness IS-LM theory).</seg>
<seg id="4022"> 8.2.2 Some Illustrations</seg>
<seg id="4023"> 8.2.2.1 Prosocial Behavior</seg>
<seg id="4024"> My first illustration may be tainted by recent work with Roland Benabou (Benabou and Tirole, forthcoming),</seg>
<seg id="4025"> but I will be discussing the challenge to modeling, whichI feel rather confident about, more than the way we address it, for which I am a poor judge.</seg>
<seg id="4026"> This illustration refers to how economics can take on board prosocial behavior.</seg>
<seg id="4027"> People frequently engage in activities that are costly to them and mostly benefit others: they vote, volunteer time, help strangers, give to political or charitable organizations, donate blood, join rescue squads, and sometimes even risk or sacrifice their life.</seg>
<seg id="4028"> One tractable approach for economists would consist in positing a reduced form in which individualsŇ»or at least a fraction of individualsŇ»have a taste for prosocial behavior in the same way macroeconomists use warm-glow preferences in order to build a tractable model of bequest behavior.</seg>
<seg id="4029"> One could perhaps also add some situational parameters to account for the wide variations in prosocial contributions across individuals, circumstances, and causes.</seg>
<seg id="4030"> Even so enriched, the ˇ°taste-for-prosocial- behavior-in-the-utility-functionˇ± approach may encounter some difficulties.</seg>
<seg id="4031"> First, it would be difficult to explain, except in an ad hoc manner, why incentives sometimes crowd out prosocial behavior.</seg>
<seg id="4032"> In this reduced form, incentives would just add to the natural proclivity of individuals to engage in prosocial behavior.</seg>
<seg id="4033"> Second, reduced forms would not offer clues as to why prosocial behaviors are sometimes socially determined.</seg>
<seg id="4034"> Third, and quite importantly, the ˇ°prosocialityˇ± of prosocial behaviors often needs to be questioned.</seg>
<seg id="4035"> Consider the following.</seg>
<seg id="4036"> ?We tip taxi drivers in rich countries and fail to give money to starving children in Africa.</seg>
<seg id="4037"> ? In studies reported in Glazer and Konrad (1996), less than 1% of donations are anonymous.</seg>
<seg id="4038"> ? People often react with disapproval when someone reveals how generous and disinterested he is.</seg>
<seg id="4039"> ? In a transportation-related survey of about 1,300 individuals, Johansson- Stenman and Svedsater (2003) find that people who are asked which attributes are most important to them in a car systematically put environmental performance near the top and social status near the bottom;</seg>
<seg id="4040"> but when asked about the true preferences of their neighbors or average compatriots, they give dramatically reversed rankings.</seg>
<seg id="4041"> ?A clever experiment by Dana et al. (2003) reveals that when people know how their choices will affect other people, they often display substantial altruistic behavior;</seg>
<seg id="4042"> when they are given the opportunity to remain ignorant of how their choices affect others, or of their precise role in the outcome, many choose not to know and revert to selfish choices.</seg>
<seg id="4043"> They exploit the ˇ°moral wriggle room.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="4044"> To illustrate this, suppose that an individual plays a ˇ°dictator game,ˇ± i.e., a game in which he chooses between (say) two actions affecting his welfare and that of another, passive individual.</seg>
<seg id="4045"> A ˇ°selfish action,ˇ± A, gives the highest material payoff, 5, to the dictator.</seg>
<seg id="4046"> The other action, B, gives him only 4.</seg>
<seg id="4047"> Suppose that the selfish action yields 0 and action B yields 8 to the passive player.</seg>
<seg id="4048"> As is well-known, a substantial fraction of dictators in those circumstances would choose action B, sacrificing a bit of material payoff for the psychic payoff associated with a good deed.</seg>
<seg id="4049"> Suppose, next, that there are two states of nature, and that the dictatorˇŻs payoff is as above regardless of the state of nature.</seg>
<seg id="4050"> By contrast, the passive playerˇŻs preferences across actions depend on the state of nature.</seg>
<seg id="4051"> In the first state, they are as above.</seg>
<seg id="4052"> In the second state, action B yields 4 to the passive individual while action A yields her 5.</seg>
<seg id="4053"> Finally, the dictator is asked whether he wants to know which state of nature prevails.</seg>
<seg id="4054"> It would seem that the dictator would want to know which state prevails and, provided that she enjoys the psychic payoff from raising the passive individualˇŻs payoff from 0 to 8, choose action B in the former case, and action A in the latter case.</seg>
<seg id="4055"> A substantial fraction of dictators, though, choose not to know and select the selfish action, A, with the ˇ°excuseˇ± (in their own eyes at least) that action A may actually benefit the other individual.</seg>
<seg id="4056"> ? Identification with a group and the concomitant altruistic or even heroic acts are often associated with a hostile or even cruel attitude toward the out-group.</seg>
<seg id="4057"> These six observations, and others, suggest that the economistsˇŻ rendering of prosocial behavior should combine heterogeneity in the individualsˇŻ degree of altruism and greed with concerns for social reputation and self-respect.</seg>
<seg id="4058"> This is in line with (many) psychologistsˇŻ approach to these issues in terms of the ˇ°over-justification effect.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="4059"> For instance, a prominent explanation of the ˇ°crowding out effectsˇ± of prosocial behavior by incentives alluded to earlier is that the presence of rewards or punishments changes the meaning that people attach to actions (by others or their own) in a detrimental way.</seg>
<seg id="4060"> My own work with Benabou on prosocial behavior combines heterogeneous social preferences with cognition.</seg>
<seg id="4061"> That is, prosocial actions are undertaken both because a fraction of individuals are genuinely other-regarding, and because individuals either are in a quest for social esteem or strive to maintain a certain self-view of ˇ°what kind of personˇ± they are.</seg>
<seg id="4062"> In such an environment, incentives then give rise, in economics parlance, to a signal extraction problem, which gives a formal content to the over-justification effect.</seg>
<seg id="4063"> (This cognitive approach is then used to study various issues such as the form and confidentiality of prosocial behavior, the emergence of multiple social norms enforced by the interplay of honor and shame, and the competition among the recipients of altruism.)</seg>
<seg id="4064"> A digression here: when discussing survey evidence on attitudes and beliefs quite generally, we should take social signaling and especially self-signaling more seriously than we currently do.</seg>
<seg id="4065"> This was pretty clear in the transportation survey just discussed.</seg>
<seg id="4066"> Similarly, when parents report that they ˇ°valueˇ± education, when Americans ˇ°believeˇ± they should be saving more, or when people ˇ°believeˇ± that they perform better than average in a number of dimensions they may indeed be making mistakes,</seg>
<seg id="4067"> but they may also be engaging in cheap (although therefore not very effective) reinforcement of their identity or self-image.</seg>
<seg id="4068"> With prosocial behavior as with any other theme of behavioral economics, social scientists may have to dig deeper in order to find the primitives and to perform a welfare analysis.</seg>
<seg id="4069"> Other illustrations include three concepts that many at the meeting, including myself, think economists should take on board: fairness, endowment effects, and bounded willpower.</seg>
<seg id="4070"> 8.2.3 Fairness</seg>
<seg id="4071"> Why do people turn down unfair offers in ultimatum games;</seg>
<seg id="4072"> or, as Bewley documented (see Chapter 5, this volume), have their morale reduced when their wage is cut?</seg>
<seg id="4073"> Is it because of intention-based preferences (an intrinsic preference for reciprocity), social preferences (a preference for certain forms of income distributionŇ»</seg>
<seg id="4074"> perhaps one that refers to a moral ideal of equal pay for equal work, a rule highly sensitive to adverse selection, or equal pay for equal output, a rule highly sensitive to moral hazard),</seg>
<seg id="4075"> a preoccupation with self-image (for example, the management of oneˇŻs self-reputation as someone who does not turn the other cheek), a taste for procedural fairness (also perhaps stemming from a moral ideal associated with organizational efficiency),</seg>
<seg id="4076"> or a more standard economic explanation, suggested by Holmstrom at this meeting, in which the victim learns about othersˇŻ views of himself and project the implications of the signal for the future of the relationship?</seg>
<seg id="4077"> I do not know, but two points are worth making: first, different hypotheses have different positive and policy implications.</seg>
<seg id="4078"> Second, and relating to the first four hypotheses, it would be helpful to understand the origin of the preferences.</seg>
<seg id="4079"> Ernst FehrˇŻs research agenda should prove very helpful in this respect.</seg>
<seg id="4080"> 8.2.4 LossAversion and Endowment Effects</seg>
<seg id="4081"> Again, a fascinating and relevant effect (as documented for example, by Bewley (see Chapter 5, this volume) for the quick development, following an increase in pay, of a feeling of entitlement).</seg>
<seg id="4082"> On the other hand, a key challenge for the theory of loss aversion and endowment effects is that of the determination of the reference point.</seg>
<seg id="4083"> As Honkapohja noted above (Chapter 5, this volume), empirical observations cannot be interpreted through the lens of an application-specific reference point.</seg>
<seg id="4084"> Rather, some broad principles are called for.</seg>
<seg id="4085"> My hunch is that a cognitive approach building on self-signaling and social signaling (and sometimes on informed principal theory) is likely to be relevant.</seg>
<seg id="4086"> But this is largely unexplored territory at this stage.</seg>
<seg id="4087"> Furthermore, other approaches, for example, building on neuroscience, are likely to be relevant as well.</seg>
<seg id="4088"> Other examples come to mind.</seg>
<seg id="4089"> For instance, hyperbolic discounting is a tractable reduced form capturing bounded willpower, but one that for some applications will prove rather unsatisfactory.</seg>
<seg id="4090"> (Incidentally, the classical economistsˇŻ rendering of bounded willpowerŇ»for instance that of Smith, Bohm-Bawerk, or JevonsŇ»was richer than that of behavioral economists.)</seg>
<seg id="4091"> My guess is that, as for the rest of economics, behavioral economics must build on a back-and-forth interaction between careful and parsimonious modeling combined with structural tests in the field and in the laboratory.</seg>
<seg id="4092"> 8.3 Comment by Timothy D. Wilson</seg>
<seg id="4093"> It is gratifying to see how ideas in social psychology have been applied to behavioral economics and real-world problems.</seg>
<seg id="4094"> The two disciplines share a common goal: to understand how people process information and make decisions in ways that impact their lives and the lives of others.</seg>
<seg id="4095"> It is important, though, to point out differences between the disciplines of social psychology and behavioral economics that have, perhaps, kept them apart.</seg>
<seg id="4096"> Behavioral economics has largely been concerned with understanding policies and real-world applications, whereas social psychology has focused more on basic researchˇŞon what makes people tick.</seg>
<seg id="4097"> There are also important methodological differences.</seg>
<seg id="4098"> Psychology is dominated by laboratory experiments, whereas behavioral economics relies more on field studies.</seg>
<seg id="4099"> There are, of course, many exceptions to these rules in both disciplines, but you are more likely to find a social psychologist doing basic laboratory studies with college students, and more likely to find behavioral economists doing large-scale field studies of important real-world problems.</seg>
<seg id="4100"> I think each field can learn from the other and that a lot of progress will be made if we join forces.</seg>
<seg id="4101"> How might we facilitate such interactions and what form might they take?</seg>
<seg id="4102"> I suggest that we collaborate on small-scale studies that employ the experimental method in applied settings.</seg>
<seg id="4103"> Social psychologists have been reticent to do field research for a number of reasons.</seg>
<seg id="4104"> One is that it can be difficult to know how the processes we study in laboratory studies, in which we focus on a small number of independent variables, will play out in natural settings.</seg>
<seg id="4105"> Armchair thinking on how intervention might work is not much help here.</seg>
<seg id="4106"> We need to take our best shot in small-scale interventions, with randomly assigned control groups, much as argued by Ross and Nisbett (1991).</seg>
<seg id="4107"> Admittedly, it is not easy to conduct well-controlled experiments in natural settings.</seg>
<seg id="4108"> With a little cleverness, however, we can do it, as with the following examples.</seg>
<seg id="4109"> Ayres (see p. 145) mentioned a fascinating correlational study he has done in New Haven on tipping taxicab drivers.</seg>
<seg id="4110"> Taxi passengers gave lower tips to African- American drivers than to white drivers.</seg>
<seg id="4111"> As with any correlational study, the causal interpretation of this finding is unclear.</seg>
<seg id="4112"> To make a stronger causal inference, the study could be done experimentally.</seg>
<seg id="4113"> The driver of the taxi could obscure his face with a hat or hood, making his race unclear.</seg>
<seg id="4114"> Then, the researcher could randomly assign passengers to race of driver, by changing the picture of the driver that is posted in the back seat.</seg>
<seg id="4115"> With such an experiment we would know for sure whether the race of the driver is causing people to tip differently (the driver, of course, should not know which picture is posted during any particular ride).</seg>
<seg id="4116"> This would fall in the domain of social psychology that looks at context effects on peopleˇŻs prejudice attitudes, in particular their uncontrolled prejudiced attitudes.</seg>
<seg id="4117"> Second, Mullainathan (seep. 86) described the problem of low school attendance in developing countries.</seg>
<seg id="4118"> It has been suggested that building in incentives such as feeding kids at school at lunch time might be a way to solve the problem.</seg>
<seg id="4119"> That could be tried experimentally with a small intervention, in which incentives are offered in some school districts but not in others.</seg>
<seg id="4120"> There are practical and ethical problems but the power of such designs would probably warrant the experiment.</seg>
<seg id="4121"> Third, many universities and businesses are instituting diversity training programs, in which the attendees are asked to undergo some sort of educational exercise to enlighten them about diversity and presumably to change their behavior in more egalitarian direction.</seg>
<seg id="4122"> Little is known about how these programs affect people.</seg>
<seg id="4123"> If you ask the organizers how they evaluate whether the program has the intended effects, they often point to the responses of the people who participate in the program.</seg>
<seg id="4124"> This might have some value, but a much more powerful design would be test these programs experimentally.</seg>
<seg id="4125"> There is a big industry offering diversity training services but, for obvious reasons, they are not always interested in in-depth evaluation of their effectiveness.</seg>
<seg id="4126"> However, it would not be difficult to come up with control groups that are randomly assigned to different treatments in order to evaluate their true effects.</seg>
<seg id="4127"> Fourth, think of a new drug for cancer.</seg>
<seg id="4128"> We all agree that it should be tested very carefully for harmful effects before marketing.</seg>
<seg id="4129"> We seem not to have the same attitude toward psychological interventions.</seg>
<seg id="4130"> In Canada, for example, the law now requires that cigarette packs have pictures of people with cancer and other scary images to dissuade people from smoking.</seg>
<seg id="4131"> To my knowledge, this intervention was never experimentally tested to see whether the pictures have any effect, desirable or not.</seg>
<seg id="4132"> When it comes to psychological interventions, we rely more on common sense about what we think will happen and, unlike testing how a drug will work, see little reason to test these interventions experimentally.</seg>
