<seg id="4133"> I suspect that the pictures on cigarette packs in Canada do have some effect, but that is just my common sense talking, and I think it is important to find out empirically what those effects are.</seg>
<seg id="4134"> One often hears from social psychologists that they would like to do more applied research but there are all kinds of practical and institutional barriers.</seg>
<seg id="4135"> This is one area in which social psychologists should learn more about neighboring fields such as behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="4136"> I have been very impressed with the kind of field studies many economists are conducting, often using experimental designs to study important real- world problems.</seg>
<seg id="4137"> There is a natural collaboration here.</seg>
<seg id="4138"> Social psychologists know a lot about social influence and cognitive processing, and many are eager to see how these processes play out in the real world.</seg>
<seg id="4139"> By partnering with economists who have much more experience studying real-world problems, substantial progress could be made.</seg>
<seg id="4140"> 8.4 COMMENT BY PETER DIAMOND</seg>
<seg id="4141"> I share the hope, expressed above by Tirole, that in time behavioral economics will cease to exist.</seg>
<seg id="4142"> Currently, a first-year economics graduate student learns statistics, math, micro, and macro.</seg>
<seg id="4143"> At some point, one of these students interested in behavioral economics studies psychology in some form, taught by an economist or, if the student is lucky, by a psychologist.</seg>
<seg id="4144"> Integration of the underlying intellectual basis for behavioral analysis into the first-year core is the long-term hope.</seg>
<seg id="4145"> In designing this conference we wrestled with the question of planning a conference and a volume that would enhance behavioral economics.</seg>
<seg id="4146"> This issue was addressed explicitly by Camerer (see Chapter 7), who said that behavioral economics really started with hammering away at anomalies.</seg>
<seg id="4147"> This is a lot easier to do when relevant data are present.</seg>
<seg id="4148"> I think it is always important to keep anomalies in mind and I think everybody recognizes that merely hammering at anomalies does not move paradigms all by itself.</seg>
<seg id="4149"> More than that has to be done.</seg>
<seg id="4150"> I hope that the conference and this book can generate an increasing respect towards the potential for psychology in economics.</seg>
<seg id="4151"> I think this is helped by having all the excitement generated by brain pictures in neuroeconomics.</seg>
<seg id="4152"> However, to go from these brain images to outcomes is both hard and a long route.</seg>
<seg id="4153"> But I think that through experiments we can increase awareness of the potential contributions of social sciences to better policy generally. And that will make psychology more acceptable inside economics as well.</seg>
<seg id="4154"> A primary target for generating excitement for behavioral economics is obviously Congress.</seg>
<seg id="4155"> Congress votes to provide money for building bridges, but it does not vote on a blueprint for the bridge.</seg>
<seg id="4156"> Yet when it comes to designing a pension system, Congress lays out the blueprint.</seg>
<seg id="4157"> If we can get the idea of the importance of experiments more embedded in the public consciousness, i.e., more of the sense that there is real expertise in social science,</seg>
<seg id="4158"> then maybe Congress will rely more on that expertise, as they do with bridges.</seg>
<seg id="4159"> How do we advance behavioral economics beyond the anomalies and generate more respect?</seg>
<seg id="4160"> I think we need to convince a lot of people, and that means primarily young people, that this is a good research opportunity.</seg>
<seg id="4161"> That is what this volume is trying to do.</seg>
<seg id="4162"> So what does it take to make a good research opportunity? A first element is that it has to include the type of work that people like to do.</seg>
<seg id="4163"> An example is the kind of extremely complex simulations that are perhaps best exemplified by the work of Tom SargentŇ»this really appeals to a set of people who like to get into the code and have the computer skills to do very complex calculations.</seg>
<seg id="4164"> If it is not fun for you to do that kind of research, you are not going to get caught up in that style of work.</seg>
<seg id="4165"> Some people like to do hard theory where they use their math techniques.</seg>
<seg id="4166"> There were some references in the conference to work by Gul and Pesendorfer, which I think has much potential to help people recognize that there is scope in behavioral economics for doing this kind of work.</seg>
<seg id="4167"> I say this even though I think the psychology in their work is not well-grounded.</seg>
<seg id="4168"> We need to recognize that different people like to do different kinds of research and the research endeavor benefits from different people doing different kinds of research.</seg>
<seg id="4169"> And here, with some reluctance, I may disagree a little with Tirole.</seg>
<seg id="4170"> I think that sometimes the insights from reduced-form models are worth having as long as one does not over interpret them.</seg>
<seg id="4171"> But this goes with the basic point that the real distinction is not between a good model and a bad model, but between a model that is good for some question and one that is bad for that question.</seg>
<seg id="4172"> A second element in having a good research opportunity is that there is an audience.</seg>
<seg id="4173"> Most people do not write for themselves (although a few do).</seg>
<seg id="4174"> They want an audience so that they can think they are going to be read, think they are going to be applauded.</seg>
<seg id="4175"> I think this volume can make it clear that there is a range of areas here with audiences and potential audiences.</seg>
<seg id="4176"> The way we picked topics was to stay away from the fields that are already well-developed or well-advertisedˇŞbehavioral finance does not need this kind of advertising and behavioral macro just had all the fuss from a Nobel prize to a practitioner.</seg>
<seg id="4177"> There was no behavioral industrial organization covered at the conference because there is not yet an audience there.</seg>
<seg id="4178"> A third element for a good research opportunity, and the one that gives me the most pause, is the ability to judge the quality of a contribution.</seg>
<seg id="4179"> Most people do not want to write for an audience if the bulk of the audience is not going to like what is written.</seg>
<seg id="4180"> A researcher needs a sense of what it takes to do good work.</seg>
<seg id="4181"> One of the things that bothers me a lot in behavioral economics is that it is much harder to judge what will be well-received than it is in a well-established field, where a researcher can judge whether a potential paper seems more interesting than similar ones that have been publishedˇŞ</seg>
<seg id="4182"> and so it is easy to see where the subject can go.</seg>
<seg id="4183"> I feel very nervous when talking to graduate students who are thinking about behavioral research;</seg>
<seg id="4184"> I think the risk, inherent in doing any research, is larger in behavioral economics than in well-developed fields.</seg>
<seg id="4185"> The chapters in this volume can help since they spell out what some researchers like.</seg>
<seg id="4186"> And of course, the bottom line for the definition of a good research opportunity is from the jobs that follow from research, to which economists pay a lot of attention.</seg>
<seg id="4187"> It is easier for hot prospects who will end up with jobs in top schools to find a supportive environment than it is for the average job candidate.</seg>
<seg id="4188"> Long before coming to the conference, I had the feeling that one should never use the term ˇ°preferencesˇ± without a modifying adjective, whether it be real preferences, underlying preferences, or choice preferences.</seg>
<seg id="4189"> Preference, I think, needs to be modified since it is so easy to slip verbally from one interpretation of ˇ°preferenceˇ± to another.</seg>
<seg id="4190"> One of the things I learned at this conference is that the same should be done with ˇ°incentives.ˇ±</seg>
<seg id="4191"> We have financial incentives and economic incentives.</seg>
<seg id="4192"> But we also have other ways of changing behavior that are changes in the nature of incentives.</seg>
<seg id="4193"> I think we need some terminology here remembering that economists have great interest in incentives as well as in outcomes and in equilibria.</seg>
<seg id="4194"> Economics and psychology are different disciplines for the good reason that they try to do different things.</seg>
<seg id="4195"> Sometimes having different disciplines is important for getting results.</seg>
<seg id="4196"> Sometimes they create barriers, but barriers that one tries to break down.</seg>
<seg id="4197"> The participants at the meeting are working at this.</seg>
<seg id="4198"> I think it is useful to remind fellow economists that we tend to use psychology differently from psychologists.</seg>
<seg id="4199"> Sometimes the difference is a good idea and sometimes it is not.</seg>
<seg id="4200"> Another goal of the conference was to show that there is a wide range of psychology that can be useful in economics: that is why we wanted diversity in the type of psychologists in this meeting.</seg>
<seg id="4201"> This conference was great fun for us; let us hope it will be good for others.</seg>
<seg id="4202"> 8.5 GENERAL DISCUSSION</seg>
<seg id="4203"> I. Ayres</seg>
<seg id="4204"> We should promote the possibility of carrying out experiments about social or economic issues more aggressively to policy makers, and require the testing of laws before they are implemented.</seg>
<seg id="4205"> An example of an arena where this would be highly desirable is in firearms legislation.</seg>
<seg id="4206"> Ch. Engel</seg>
<seg id="4207"> An experiment in law is very different from an experiment in medicine because of the Heidelberg Principle: in the case of law, performing the experiment changes the environment.</seg>
<seg id="4208"> T. D. Wilson</seg>
<seg id="4209"> If you start with a small experiment, this concern about general equilibrium effects is not so serious, and we will still obtain valuable results.</seg>
<seg id="4210"> P. Diamond</seg>
<seg id="4211"> Politicians may not agree to evaluation through experiments because many politicians would worry that the results of experiments would undermine support for the legislative outcomes they want to push.</seg>
<seg id="4212"> From audience: what is the role of evolutionary psychology?</seg>
<seg id="4213"> E. Shafir</seg>
<seg id="4214"> We do not know much about evolutionary psychology, because there are no fossils.</seg>
<seg id="4215"> It is good for developing intuitions, but it is not enough for supporting and explaining findings.</seg>
<seg id="4216"> Evolutionary psychology is a bit like psychoanalysis.</seg>
<seg id="4217"> It can explain anything but is very difficult to test.</seg>
<seg id="4218"> Its value, perhaps, is in generating new hypotheses that can be tested experimentally.</seg>
<seg id="4219"> I think the number of novel hypotheses it has generated about social behavior, however, is very small.</seg>
<seg id="4220"> A. Rangel</seg>
<seg id="4221"> I believe in neuroscience and I want to work on this.</seg>
<seg id="4222"> It is not only about brain imaging, it is about trying to understand which processes are activated.</seg>
<seg id="4223"> How the brain makes decisions is very relevant to economics.</seg>
<seg id="4224"> In the case of addiction, we know how particular substances activate processes in the brain.</seg>
<seg id="4225"> I am surprised that psychologists are even less enthusiastic than economists.</seg>
<seg id="4226"> Is this because of competition about resources?</seg>
<seg id="4227"> I want to come back to animal studies such as the studies on bees that Shafir talked about.</seg>
<seg id="4228"> There are two kinds of animal studies.</seg>
<seg id="4229"> Some are about processes, such as monkey studies, and I find those studies valuable.</seg>
<seg id="4230"> Other studies are about choices, and those are less useful for economics because humans have a much more complex brain circuitry to make choices and hence animal studies are of limited use here.</seg>
<seg id="4231"> E. Fehr</seg>
<seg id="4232"> I can imagine psychologists in the 1950s saying that social psychology was not useful.</seg>
<seg id="4233"> I think there is good research and bad research in all fields.</seg>
<seg id="4234"> There is serious research going on in neuroscience, and it is a legitimate field of inquiry.</seg>
<seg id="4235"> It has turned out to be very useful for animal and primate research, so it has a potential for understanding human behavior as well.</seg>
<seg id="4236"> Neuroscience might be hysterical, but it is intrinsically interesting, it will be done, and it might generate very important findings.</seg>
<seg id="4237"> It is important for economists to play a role, because they ask different questions</seg>
<seg id="4238"> (in the same way as economists ask different questions to psychologists now).</seg>
<seg id="4239"> N. Stern</seg>
<seg id="4240"> Can you give examples in neuroscience?</seg>
<seg id="4241"> This might be easier than in economics.</seg>
<seg id="4242"> I tried to convince politicians for a long time; and I realized that one good example is worth a thousand regressions.</seg>
<seg id="4243"> Politicians have very short time horizons.</seg>
<seg id="4244"> Therefore, a more promising route to affect policies is to work with government agencies such as the U.S. Department of Education or the World Bank which have much longer time horizons.</seg>
<seg id="4245"> S. Mullainathan</seg>
<seg id="4246"> This is already well underway.</seg>
<seg id="4247"> For example, the U.S. Department of Education now requires randomized experiments and careful evaluation before implementing new policies.</seg>
<seg id="4248"> This is often driven by the person at the head of the institution, and those top administrators are often more open to using randomized trials than [are] elected officials.</seg>
<seg id="4249"> There is a tension between psychology and economics:</seg>
<seg id="4250"> economists like generality, to be able to make predictions, while psychologists are often reluctant to extend their findings beyond the specific context of their study.</seg>
<seg id="4251"> This difference is probably not that stark.</seg>
<seg id="4252"> Psychologists are also willing to make predictions about the real world;</seg>
<seg id="4253"> it is really about their willingness to go out and test these predictions in the field.</seg>
<seg id="4254"> B. Holmstrdm</seg>
<seg id="4255"> This is a completely a theoretical conference.</seg>
<seg id="4256"> One concern that comes up for a theorist is that you listen to examples, and your temptation is to just go with experiments, which will work for a while, but eventually you will have to organize facts using theories (as Tirole emphasized earlier).</seg>
<seg id="4257"> In this sense, this conference has been disappointing because it has not yet reached the stage of proposing theories.</seg>
<seg id="4258"> E. Fehr</seg>
<seg id="4259"> There are some domains in behavioral economics where we try to sort theories out: this is the case, for example, with social preferences, where theories are proposed and then tested with experiments.</seg>
<seg id="4260"> I hope that the cycle between proposing theories and testing those theories with empirical or experimental evidence will be short (weeks), as it is in physics, rather than long (decades) as was the case in economics in the past.</seg>
<seg id="4261"> J. Tirole</seg>
<seg id="4262"> Everybody uses reduced forms and they are indeed often very useful.</seg>
<seg id="4263"> For example, although the IS-LM theory in macroeconomics is a very reduced form, it has played a tremendously useful role.</seg>
<seg id="4264"> Later on, research based on new developments in imperfect competition, costly price readjustments, search theory or for corporate finance ended up capturing aspects of the IS-LM reduced form predictions.</seg>
<seg id="4265"> Yet, our aim should be to develop unifying theories which are not context dependent, so that they can be used for normative purposes.</seg>
<seg id="4266"> If we want to become behaviorally more sophisticated, I think that, at least at our current level of understanding,</seg>
<seg id="4267"> we need many different theories and that developing a unifying theory to explain everything is an impossible dream.</seg>
